# 岱史

**The *Tai History***

**Title:** *Tai History*. Compiled by Ming dynasty scholar Kui Zhilong. Eighteen fascicles. Based on the *Wanli Continuation of the Daoist Canon*.

**Preface to the *Tai History***

Of all the mountains that encircle the central lands, there are myriad, yet none surpass the Five Peaks; of the Five Peaks, none surpass Mount Tai—hence it is called the *Tai Zong* ("Ancestral Peak"). From the time of the Yellow Emperor and Emperor Shun onward, no title has been more revered. Thus, since antiquity, no dynasty that rose by Heaven’s mandate failed to perform rites upon Mount Tai.

Yet the practices of the Qin and Han dynasties are matters gentlemen avoid. Only our Great Ming has wholly rejected the deceptive custom of *feng* and *shan* sacrifices, casting aside such falsehoods as unworthy of the Dao. Moreover, the rituals of distant reverence, the quantities of jade and silk offerings, the prayers of ascent, and the ranks and titles conferred—all have been purged of the errors of recent ages and restored to ancient purity. Of all who have performed rites upon Mount Tai since time immemorial—seventy-two rulers in all—none surpass our Great Ming.

In past ages, Mount Tai’s distinction lay merely in its strange marvels and prodigious omens, manifesting numinous wonders to proclaim the world’s harmony. But under our Ming, the imperial capital was established at the northern pole, and this mountain stands sentinel at the empire’s eastern gate, where the Wen and Ji Rivers converge. From the realm below, tribute and taxes flow like fish in a stream, numbering in the tens of thousands, yet all these waters are channeled through the mountain’s springs. It is as though the mountain’s spirit, like a prime minister, gathers the tributes of the Nine Provinces and conveys them to the celestial treasury. Thus, Mount Tai’s blessings upon the state and its protection of the people—even for ten thousand generations—are unmatched, and none surpass those of our Great Ming.

Since Mount Tai is the greatest of the Five Peaks and the mightiest in the central lands, and since our Ming’s rites upon it, as well as its beneficence toward our dynasty, are unparalleled in history, then its other wondrous and singular sights—its traces of ascent and contemplation, recorded in immortal classics, gazetteers, and the fragments of broken steles—though they lend weight to lesser mountains, are but trifles beside Mount Tai. For what makes Mount Tai supreme among mountains, and our Ming supreme among dynasties, lies not in such things, but in what the chronicler must now set forth in detail.

In the past, I was entrusted with the imperial seal to oversee governance in the Ji region. In the course of my duties, I ascended this mountain and gazed upon its expanse, seeking the vestiges of ancient sage-kings and divine emperors. Yet the ages had stretched too far, and no trace could be found—thus I sighed in lament. Moved by the gradual decay of earlier records, I charged Assistant Commissioner Zha to compile and renew them.

Commissioner Zha then selected from old compilations, refined them with his own judgment, and modeled his work on the *Three Histories*. Drawing from a hundred schools, he produced several fascicles of annals, tables, records, and treatises. As for the wondrous and singular sights, the traces of ascent and contemplation recorded in immortal classics, gazetteers, and the fragments of broken steles, he gathered them meticulously, omitting nothing. Yet he gave special attention to the rites performed upon this mountain from the time of the Yellow Emperor and Emperor Shun onward, as well as the Ming dynasty’s ceremonial protocols and titles—how they rejected Qin and Han corruptions and restored ancient traditions. These he arranged under distinct categories, fulfilling the duty of grand and particular commemoration. Thus, he renamed the work the *Tai History*, and its significance is now manifest.

As this history was nearing completion, the Emperor, in response to calamities and to pray for a bountiful year, issued a decree of virtue, dispatching high ministers to offer sacrifices to the Five Peaks and invoke blessings for the people. The rites began with Mount Tai, and it is fitting that auspicious omens should respond to the imperial will. Let later scholars of marvels and anomalies recognize that in this sagely and divine age, the myriad spirits receive their charge—none more so than under our Emperor. Then this history will not only gain luster, but the mountain itself will be further ennobled.

With reverence, I inscribe these words at the beginning to offer my humble contribution. As for the work’s other principles and methods, Commissioner Zha has provided his own account, which I need not elaborate.

On the auspicious first day of the new year in the ding-mao year of the Wanli reign, Tan Yao of Dongguan in Lingnan completed this inscription.

*Public Notice of The Mount Tai History*
From Zha Zhilong, Associate Commissioner of the Bin-Le Division of the Shandong Provincial Salt Transport and Distribution Bureau, regarding the compilation of the supplementary gazetteer:

On the eighteenth day

**I.**
The title *Dai History* derives from "Dai Zong," the sacred designation of Mount Tai, first recorded in the *Canon of Yao* and originating with the Yellow Emperor. Thus, we adopt its ancient name, "Dai." The work follows the conventions of historical writing, hence the term "History." Earlier scholars once titled their records *Lu History*, but here we proceed differently.

**II.**
The old maps of Mount Tai were exceedingly crude. I personally traversed the mountain, marking its strategic and scenic sites, then commissioned artisans to render them. The new map expands fourfold upon the old, which is also preserved for comparison. Where the old map sketched only a general outline, the new one details its topography and landmarks. Most other maps are newly created.

**III.**
Three sections are devoted to *Investigations*:
- *Illustrated Investigation*
- *Astrological and Terrestrial Investigation*
- *Topographical Investigation*

The term "investigation" (考) denotes verification, entailing cross-referencing with classical texts, histories, and the works of the Hundred Schools. These subjects were largely neglected in previous gazetteers; here, we adopt the model of *Comprehensive Investigations* to present them anew.

**IV.**
Two sections are presented as *Tables*:
- *Mountains and Waters Table*
- *Territorial Boundaries Table*

The term "table" (表) signifies "to display and clarify." The scenic expanses of mountains and rivers, along with the shifting borders of jurisdictions, demand explicit delineation. Thus, we follow the historical convention of chronological tables to illuminate them.

**V.**
Four sections are recorded as *Annals*:
- *Annals of Imperial Hunts*
- *Annals of Imperial Sacrifices*
- *Annals of Historical Relics*
- *Annals of Sacred Temples*

The term "annals" (纪) means "to record." Histories feature imperial annals, where momentous events are inscribed with particular prominence. Here, we chronicle the numinous traces of sages and deities, the creations of emperors, and—most resplendently—the imperial compositions and prayers of our present dynasty. These are distinguished from ordinary records.

**VI.**
Five sections are compiled as *Treatises*:
- *Treatise on Palaces and Temples*
- *Treatise on Local Products*
- *Treatise on Incense Taxes*
- *Treatise on Auspicious and Inauspicious Omens*
- *Treatise on Ascents and Prospects*

The term "treatise" (志) means "to record," preserving achievements and details lest they be forgotten. These treatises address the diverse aspects of human culture and natural principles. Though drawing from earlier gazetteers, they correct errors and supplement omissions where time and circumstances have altered. The *Treatise on Incense Taxes*, absent in prior records, is newly established here.

**VII.**
Each volume begins with a preface summarizing its main themes and the intent behind its composition. Where my observations touch upon contemporary benefits or harms, I have appended brief remarks, though I cannot presume their worth.

**VIII.**
By nature, I am crude and ill-informed, and my duties leave little leisure. When time permits, I borrow rare books from scholar-officials to search for references, though such works are scarce. Though I have personally visited many of the mountain’s scenic sites and ancient relics, I cannot claim to have seen all. As for the poems and inscriptions of esteemed officials and literati of our dynasty, some are retained from old gazetteers, others gleaned from steles and plaques, or collected from anthologies. Where my knowledge falls short, omissions are inevitable—not from deliberate exclusion, but from the limits of my reach. A broad and thorough compilation remains but a speck of dust.

*Written by Zha Zhilong of Haichang, Xizhe, in the Lixia Retreat of Reclusion.*

**Third Fascicle (Character *Gui*)**

**Fourth Fascicle (Character *Shen*)**

**Volume Eleven**   *Records of Official Residences*
**Volume Twelve**   *Records of Local Products*

**Fifth Fascicle (Character *Er*)**
**Volume Fifteen**   *Records of Ascents and Views* (Poetry Through the Ages)
**Volume Sixteen**   *Records of Ascents and Views* (Poetry from the Dynasty’s Founding to the *Jiading Jiachen* Era)

**Sixth Fascicle (Character *Wu*)**
**Volume Seventeen**   *Records of Ascents and Views* (Poetry from the *Jiading Jiachen* Era Onward)

**Seventh Fascicle (Character *Yi*)**
**Volume Eighteen**   *Records of Ascents and Views* (Rhapsodies, Records, and Miscellaneous Writings)

The *Dai History* is divided into fourteen thematic sections, further arranged into eighteen volumes. Though their lengths vary, they are compiled into seven fascicles, each named after a phrase from the *Doctrine of the Mean*: *"Tested by the spirits, without doubt."* For *"tested by the spirits, without doubt"* is the knowledge of Heaven, and the sacred spirit of Mount Tai stands as the greatest of all spirits. In this endeavor, I have purified my thoughts, wielded my brush with reverence, climbed and searched for wonders, and pursued my task with unrelenting diligence—could this not be tested by the spirits? Yet with my humble and shallow understanding, how could I presume to know the Dao of Heaven? How could I claim to be without doubt? Here, I feel deep shame.

However, Mencius taught that to fully realize the mind is to know one’s nature, and to know one’s nature is to know Heaven. Thus, the study of Heaven’s Dao is ultimately found in the full realization of the mind. Following the example of the ancients, I have devoted myself to brush and ink in service to the luminous spirits. How dare I not exert my utmost sincerity to seek communion, striving to reach that state of *"without doubt"*? This naming is not without purpose.

Respectfully inscribed by Zha Zhilong.

**First Volume of *Records of Mount Tai*: Illustrated Study**

Shu asked: Why is it called an *Illustrated Study*?
It is the examination of Mount Tai’s strategic topography, rendered into illustrations.
When the primordial chaos first divided, the myriad things emerged, yet none surpassed the greatness of flowing waters and towering peaks. Among the myriad peaks, none surpassed the greatness of the Five Marchmounts. Among the Five Marchmounts, all stand lofty and majestic, yet none surpasses the greatness of Mount Tai.
Nearby, it spans Qi and Lu, extending to the Yangtze and Huai; afar, it commands the Nine Provinces, bestowing timely rains across the Four Seas. Is it not the grand spectacle of Hua and Yi, the sacred guardian of past and present?
Yet to encompass its full splendor within the space of a hand’s breadth—how difficult indeed!
Since ancient times, those who have surveyed lands and distinguished domains have relied upon maps. Without them, one cannot exhaust its wonders through observation, nor can records capture its details. Even if a refined scholar were stirred to journey beyond, where would they begin?
Thus, we verify the past with the present and inscribe these maps at the head of our pages.
To map Mount Tai is to grasp the axis of Earth, and so we first depict its celestial correspondences, linking it to the patterns of Heaven. We map the Temple of the Marchmount, where the spirit dwells; the Azure Cloud Palace, revered by the world; and the Bright Hall of Zhou, marking the traces of imperial governance. We append the *True Forms of the Five Marchmounts*, for Mount Tai, as the foremost of the Five, rightfully unites their magnificence.
When the Marchmounts bestowed their spirit, Confucius was born—honored for myriad generations like Mount Tai itself. We map his temple and exalt its sacrifices, honoring the sage’s legacy and the mountain’s sacred power.
Is this not Heaven’s unfolding painting, Earth’s surging colors? How could it be the work of human hands?
Through these illustrations, we emulate the workings of creation, allowing those who behold them to open the scroll and stand before the sacred peak. In an instant, they may sense the descent of divine presence, yearning to offer reverence. This is not merely to delight the eye and gladden the heart, nor solely to serve the pleasures of scenic travel.

**Second Volume of *Records of Mount Tai*: Study of Celestial Correspondences**

Shu asked: Why study celestial correspondences?
It is to examine Mount Tai’s astrological division and align it with its governing stars, for it corresponds above to the celestial patterns.
The essence of yin and yang originates in Earth and ascends to Heaven. In Heaven, the Twenty-Eight Mansions; on Earth, the Twelve Provinces—each governs its domain, even down to a single hill or mound, which mirrors the constellations.
Now, Mount Song stands as the central Marchmount, reaching toward Heaven, yet Mount Tai is the foremost of all Marchmounts. How could its celestial reflection not be grand and resplendent?
Since the office of the *Baozhang* fell into disuse, the boundaries of states have shifted over vast distances, their rise and fall spanning centuries. Those who discourse on celestial correspondences variously assign Mount Tai to the Heart and Chamber constellations, or to the Stomach and Straddler, or to the Void and Rooftop. Their words flow endlessly, leaving no consensus.
Yet though records are vast, we must seek truth in antiquity. Ancient astronomy assigned the regions of Horn and Neck to {Yan}. The *Tribute of Yu* places Mount Tai within the domain of Xu and Yan—how could it not govern the Horn?
How profound were the words of the monk Yixing: "The terrestrial forms of the realm are embodied in the Two Riverine Barriers. Observing the configurations of the Two Rivers and the beginning and end of the Milky Way, one may discern the celestial correspondences."
The *Comprehensive Records* states: "The eastern mountains stand at the convergence of rivers, occupying the central position of ascent and descent. Mount Tai lies between them—this is the enduring consensus of a thousand years."
Here, we compile records from various histories, offering them to scholars of broad learning and discernment for adjudication.

**Records of the Grand Scribe’s *Treatise on Celestial Patterns*** states:
Horn, Neck, Root: Yan Province;
Chamber, Heart: Yu Province;
Tail, Winnowing Basket: You Province;
Void, Rooftop: Qing Province.
Bing and Ding correspond to the Yangtze, Huai, and the Sea; Jia and Yi to Qi; Shen and You to Lu.

**Records of the Grand Historian’s *Treatise on Geography*** states:
The land of Qi corresponds to the Void and Rooftop constellations. To the east lie Qi River, Donglai, Langye, Gaomi, and Jiaodong; to the south, Mount Tai and Chengyang; to the north, Qiancheng, Qinghe, and the southern regions of Bohai—Gaocheng, Zhonghe, and Yangxin; to the west, Jinan and Pingyuan—all belong to the Qi division.

The Grand Astrologer of Wei, Chen Zhuo, stated: Mount Tai governs six degrees of the Horn.

**Records of Jin’s *Treatise on Astronomy*** states: Mount Tai enters twelve degrees of the Horn.

**Records of Tang’s *Treatise on Astronomy*** states: The *Jianglou* and *Xuanyuan* constellations govern Mount Tai, where Jupiter resides. The Void, Rooftop, and Straddler lie in the Zi division of Qi. The Void, Rooftop, and Straddler form *Xuanyuan*, tracing the northern slopes of Mount Tai and its surrounding peaks. Their terrain flows into the *Zouzi* region, manifesting as the Celestial Ford, cutting across the southern Milky Way. All stars governing humanity and the ranks of officials correspond to the Void and Rooftop.

The *Kui* and *Lou* constellations lie in the Xu division, corresponding to the Lu region. *Kui* and *Lou* form *Jianglou*, tracing the southern slopes of Mount Tai and its surrounding peaks, flowing into the *Zouzi* region and marking the site of *Lou* and *Wei*.

**Records of Song’s *Treatise on Astronomy*** states: The seven eastern mansions of the Azure Dragon—*Jiao*, *Kang*, *Di*, *Fang*, *Xin*, *Wei*, and *Ji*—are governed by the Green Emperor, overseeing spring, wood, and Mount Tai.

**Records of Song’s *Revival Treatise on Astronomy***: Wang Yi said: Mount Tai enters twelve degrees of the Horn.

Ma Duanlin’s *Astrological and Geographical Divisions of Prefectures and Commanderies* follows the same arrangement.

**Elucidations on the Origins of Heaven** states: The mountains of the world divide into four ranges, corresponding to the twenty-eight mansions, sustaining the maritime lands of commerce and prosperity. From the lower reaches of the Northern River south to Mount Tai lies the land of the Three Qi; from the lower reaches of the Southern River north to Mount Tai lies Zou and Lu—these are the maritime lands.

**Comprehensive Records** states: Mountains extending from the south straight to the west align with the ascending *qi* of the Milky Way; those from the north straight to the east align with its descending *qi*. Thus, the eastern mountains stand at the convergence of mountains and rivers, balancing ascent and descent.

Wang Ziqing said: According to the *Shandong Gazetteer*, citing the *Book of Jin*: The northern slopes of Mount Tai and its surrounding peaks govern *Xu* and *Wei*, while the southern slopes govern *Lou* and *Wei*. Citing the *Old Book of Tang*: Jiyin, Qi, and Lu correspond to *Xin*, *Fang*, and *Di*. Our dynasty’s *Qing Classification of Correspondences* relies on these two sources to determine the sequence of celestial regions, and the *Comprehensive Records* follows suit. Speaking of Mount Tai in terms of *Xin*, *Fang*, and *Di* is somewhat fitting. Yet *Xu* and *Wei* are northern mansions, while *Lou* and *Wei* are western—how can they be spoken of as eastern? Furthermore, consulting ancient astronomical texts: *Jianglou* corresponds to Lu, while *Xuanye* corresponds to Wu, Yue, and Yangzhou. Jupiter completes its twelve-year cycle through the heavens, having no fixed position, yet the *Tang Treatise* and charts both speak of it as governing Mount Tai—this likely refers to its spiritual association rather than its astrological division.

Wang Zheng’s alignment of governance with the celestial mechanism must be understood, though it is not easily grasped. Thus, this record adopts Wang Yi and Ma Duanlin as authoritative while treating other accounts as uncertain, awaiting those skilled in astrological observation.

**Volume Three of *Tai’shi*: An Inquiry into Strategic Geography**

Shu said: Why inquire into strategic geography? To examine the ancient and modern discourses on Mount Tai. Since the inception of heaven and earth, no terrain surpasses Mount Tai in strategic importance; thus, since the dawn of written records, no mountain has been more extolled for its geography. The earliest mention appears in the *Canon of Yao*, with the two words *Daizong*. While the *Canon of Yao* describes all other mountains by their cardinal directions, only Mount Tai omits direction, calling it *Dai* instead of *Yue* and *Zong* instead of *Yue*—for it emerges from *Zhen* (the east) to match *Qian* (heaven), where the myriad things begin their succession, and nestles within *Kun* (earth), spanning the realm. As the foremost of the Five Peaks, its strategic eminence is unmatched under heaven.

Thereafter, the *Book of Songs* praises *Luzhan*, the *Rites* calls it the *Yanzhen*, and the *Erya* names it the *Eastern Peak*—each extols its preeminence in its region. Other works—philosophical texts, histories, the hundred schools, Buddhist scriptures, and Daoist records—abound in grand descriptions, with dazzling phrases and profound words: celestial palaces, divine chambers, grotto heavens, blessed lands, and tales of golden thrones and jade tables—endless wonders. Yet even ancient scholars did not dismiss accounts of the supernatural, strange records, or unofficial histories. If later generations offer fine descriptions of strategic geography, they rarely surpass these. Thus, all that has been seen or heard is compiled here for reference.

Its climate differs starkly from the lower world, and its distances are arduous to traverse, yet these are matters the world wishes to know—so let them not be deemed superfluous.

The *Canon of Shun* says *Daizong*. According to the *Comprehensive Commentary on the Five Classics*: *Daizong* signifies that when rulers of new dynasties receive the Mandate and report their achievements, they must do so at *Daizong*. The east is where the myriad things begin their succession; *Zong* means "chief," denoting its primacy among the peaks.

The *Tribute of Yu* states: "From the sea to Mount Tai lies Qingzhou." It also says: "From the sea and Mount Tai to the Huai lies Xuzhou." The *Book of Documents* commentary notes: Qingzhou’s domain extends northeast to the sea and southwest to Mount Tai. It further states: South of Mount Tai, east of the Ji River lies Xu; north of Mount Tai, east of the Ji River lies Qing.

The *Lu Hymns* says: "Mount Tai, towering and majestic, is what Lu gazes upon." The *Garden of Persuasions* explains: Mountains disperse *qi*, giving life to the myriad things. *Tai* signifies supreme greatness. The *Erya* says: "A great mound is called a *Tai* hill." Some say *Tai* means "great," referring to the supreme harmony of heaven and earth’s *qi* unfolding in the east.

The *Rites of Zhou: Offices of Zhou* states: "East of the Yellow River is Yanzhou, whose chief mountain is Mount Tai." *Zhen* (chief) denotes its weight. The *Erya* says: "East of the Yellow River lies Mount Tai." It also says: "Mount Tai is the Eastern Peak." And: "Within it lies Mount Tai, with its five grains, fish, and basins of water." The *Book of Songs* commentary explains: *Yue* (peak) signifies a mountain’s revered status. The east governs the *qi* of heaven and earth’s generation; by cardinal direction, it distinguishes the Five Peaks as the eastern pillar of heaven.

The *Gongyang Commentary* says: "Striking the rocks, clouds rise; gathering in spans of a *cun*, they cover the sky. Before the morning is over, rain falls across the land—only Mount Tai can do this."

The *Han Officials’ Etiquette* states: "The winding path ascends in over fifty turns, passing through the Small Heavenly Gate and the Great Heavenly Gate. Gazing upward at the Heavenly Gate, it is like looking at the sky through a window from within a cave."

From the base to the ancient *feng* and *shan* ritual site spans forty *li*. At the summit’s western cliff lies the "Stone Inquiry of the Immortals," while the eastern cliff is called "Jieqiu." To the southeast stands the "Sunrise Terrace." At the Sunrise Terrace, when the first cock crows, one beholds the sun’s nascent glow, stretching some three *zhang* in length. Further southeast is the "Qin Terrace," from which Chang’an may be seen; the "Wu Terrace" overlooks Kuaiji; and the "Zhou Terrace" commands a view of Qi. The Yellow River lies over two hundred *li* from Mount Tai, yet from the temple precincts, it appears as a sash, as though nestled upon the foothills. South of the mountain stands a shrine, surrounded by a thousand *wu* trees, the largest measuring fifteen or sixteen *wei* in girth, said to have been planted by Emperor Wu of Han. At the Small Heavenly Gate stand the Five Great Pines of Qin, still extant.

The *Book of Later Han* records: When Emperor Guangwu performed the *feng* rites on Mount Tai, clouds coalesced into the forms of palace halls. The *Daoist Canon* states: Mount Tai encircles one hundred and sixty *li*, with an additional fifty *bu*, each side spanning forty *li*. Guo Pu’s writings note: Mount Tai lies northwest of Fenggao County; from its base to the summit measures forty-eight *li* and one hundred *bu*. The *Classic of Mountains and Seas* says: The encircling waters of Mount Tai flow eastward into the sea. The *Dharma Garden Pearl Grove* recounts: To the east of Mount Tai lies the Sweet Spring, shaped like a well, its body entirely of stone. Those who seek to drink must first purify their hearts, kneel, and embrace it—then the spring flows forth like a stream. Should one approach with impurity or disdain, the waters recede, for divine spirits govern it.

Zeng Gong’s *Record of the Two Halls* states: The waters north of Mount Tai, along with the southeastern rivers of Qi, converge northwestward at the Blackwater Bay, then further northwest at Wuyai. Su Dongpo’s *Preface to the Poem for Yang Jie’s Departure* recounts: Master Wuwei once ascended Mount Tai’s summit on an imperial mission, witnessing the sunrise at the first cock’s crow. Wei Zhuangqu’s writings describe the "Sunrise Peak" atop Mount Tai, where one may glimpse the sun’s bath at midnight, its golden radiance stretching as far as the eye can see—this is the sea. Amidst the faint green haze, flashes like lightning reveal the interplay of islands and streams. As the golden hue fades, the sun’s orb floats, resembling a great jade platter in the water. This is the view from the seashore, beholding the sea’s sunrise. Ascending the "Flower Crown Summit" of Tiantai, one realizes this is but a small sea, for the mountains encircle beyond it the true great sea.

Mount Tai possesses a Sunrise Terrace, where the sun is observed before its rise; a Moon Terrace, where the moon is viewed after its descent; a Chang’an Terrace, gazing westward toward the mountains of the Qin region; and a Yue Terrace, looking southward toward the mountains of Kuaiji. Mount Heng boasts seventy-two peaks, with its own Sunrise and Moon Terraces, yet these do not compare to Mount Tai’s, for it aligns with the *mao* position. The *Record of the Sacred Peak* states: Liangfu and Changbai, the two auxiliary mountains, serve as Mount Tai’s attendants. The *Comprehensive Records* notes: The mountains of Jinan form its northern foothills; those of Yanzhou, its southern foothills; the coastal ranges of Qing and Qi, its left flank; and the mountains east of the Yellow River, its right flank. Is this not the essence of Mount Tai?

Notable among its ancient relics: to the south, the *wu* trees left by Emperor Wu of Han; at the Heavenly Gate, the Five Great Pines of Qin; atop the summit, the uninscribed stele of Qin; and upon the sheer cliffs, the inscriptions of the Kaiyuan era of Tang. These rank among Mount Tai’s most distinguished vestiges. The *Biographies of Lord Mao* recounts: Among the thirty-six grotto-heavens of the immortals, Mount Tai, with its circumference of over thirty *li*, is named the "Grotto-Heaven of the Three Palaces." The *Extensive Records* states: Mount Tai is called the "Boundless Primordial Grotto-Heaven," the abode of the Peak Emperor, rising four thousand *zhang* and encircling one thousand *li*. The *Records of the Five Peaks* says: The Eastern Peak, Mount Tai, is governed by the Celestial Prince Tianqi, who commands ninety thousand immortals, both officials and maidens. The mountain spans two thousand *li*, situated in Yanzhou’s Fengfu County. The *Record of Blessed Lands* describes Mount Tai’s grotto-heaven as spanning three thousand *li*, the domain of ghosts and spirits.

The *Biographies of the Immortals* tells of a stone chamber within the Sacred Peak, suspended between heaven and earth, furnished with golden couches and jade tables. *Master Shi* states: Within Mount Tai lie divine chambers and towering pavilions. The *Compendium of Curiosities* says: Mount Tai is called the "Grandson of Heaven," for it is the grandson of the Celestial Emperor, charged with summoning human souls. As the east governs the inception of all things, it knows the span of human life. The *Daoist Scriptures* declare: The deities of the Five Peaks oversee the world’s beings, each with their respective domains. Mount Tai, as the Celestial Emperor’s grandson and the abode of the host of spirits, presides over the world’s people—offices, life and death, rank and station. The geomancers say: Where the waters at Mount Tai’s foothills converge, only the grove of Confucius flourishes, for they speak of the floating and washing of the Wen and Si rivers—was the birth of our Master truly a matter of chance? Thus, in his final moments, he sang of dragging his staff upon Mount Tai.

Since the time of Ren Gong, the lineage has thrived without interruption, a testament to the Sacred Peak’s profound favor upon the House of Kong—could such grace ever be exhausted? The *Scenic Survey* recounts: Once, in the depths of winter, I ascended the mountain through driving snow. At the Imperial Tent, clouds and mist obscured all. Upon reaching the Eighteen Turns, the sky cleared, and gazing down at the mountainside, clouds still lingered. Yet upon descent, the snow fell as before. At winter’s turn to spring, mists rise from the cliffs and valleys, so bitterly cold that at first one may still point a finger, but soon, chains and locks are enveloped, and the mountain’s form vanishes entirely. When the skies briefly clear, the streams, ravines, trees, and eaves of pavilions glisten with frozen blossoms, strung like pearls and powdered like a painting.

Once, in spring, I observed the mountainside at dawn, where clouds spread evenly, their hues resplendent in a single color, as though gazing upon the inverted heavens. After the fourth month, the mountain grows humid and uninhabitable. Even in the fifth and sixth months, the cold persists, demanding layered silk and outer quilts.

Mornings and evenings were like deep autumn. When sudden summer storms struck, winds at the mountainside whipped clouds into surging waves, while thunder and lightning emerged from below, vaguely revealing the foothills where white waves churned like a boiling sea. In an instant, clouds would ascend to the summit, and rain fell above and below alike.

Those who drank from the mountain’s streams often suffered diarrhea, save for the sweet and pure waters of Jade Pool, White Crane Spring, Water Curtain Spring, Jade Flower Spring, and Jade Maiden Spring. On the eastern cliff of the Yuanjun Temple, a crevice dripped water like pearls, yielding nearly one *hu* day and night—its flavor was especially fine.

*Tai Shi, Scroll Four: "Mountains and Waters"*
Shu asks: Why enumerate mountains and waters?
To record those that cling to Mount Tai. Tai is a mountain, yet why speak of it alongside waters? Because springs rise from its base—these are the sources of water. Thus, to speak of mountains is to speak of waters.

The names of its peaks: Feng, Yan, Dong, Ling, Ya, Tai, Men, Yu, Zhai, Yuan—all are mountains. The names of its waters: Quan, Chi, Jing, Xi, He, Wan—all are waters. Others, like bridges, arches, and inscriptions, serve only to mark the splendor of mountains and waters. In sum, all are the glories of Mount Tai.

Of the world’s finest mountains and waters, people past and present have spoken endlessly. Is this mere empty praise? These mountains and waters defy comparison. Aligned with the celestial pole, presiding over all peaks, they are where sun and moon retreat, where clouds and rain gather, where emperors first offered sacrifices, where the multitudes flock, and where the myriad beings flourish. Even a fist-sized stone here gathers spirit and nurtures life; a handful of its springs moistens the Wen and branches into the Wei and Si. Is their transformative power not vast?

Yet it is not merely their towering peaks, deep gorges, sheer cliffs, soaring pavilions, or towering gates—nor their cascading torrents, jade-green streams, rainbow-hued reflections, or the four seasons’ ever-changing splendor—that overwhelm the eye and outpace the heart’s capacity for wonder. Such is their singular magnificence.

I once remarked: Mount Tai’s mountains and waters excel in grandeur, not in mere novelty or artifice. Thus, they are called unmatched.

**Peaks and Stones**

Fenghuang Mountain lies southwest of the summit, lord of White Cloud Grotto.
Tianzhu Peak stands southwest of the summit, behind the Pavilion of Reverent Gazing.
The Summit Stone rests at the Wangdi Temple, where the late Minister Wan Gong inscribed: *"Marking the Peak of Mount Tai."*
Elephant Mountain lies below Fenghuang Mountain.
Sunrise Peak is east of the summit; at the fifth watch, one may see the sun rise over the sea. The Viewing Sea Pavilion stands here.
Viewing Sea Stone lies northeast of Sunrise Peak; at the fifth watch, as the sun rises, the sea becomes visible.
Stone Hearth Mountain is south of the prefecture, where Emperor Wu of Han performed the feng sacrifice.
Great Hanging Stone lies west of the summit.
Jieshi Mountain is fifty *li* south of the prefecture, where Emperor Zhen of Song performed the feng sacrifice.
Moon Viewing Peak is west of the summit, opposite Sunrise Peak, its name derived from the moon’s waning in the *geng* direction—here called Yueguan.
Small Hanging Stone also lies west of the summit, facing Great Hanging Stone.
Fairy Bridge Stone is west of the Cliff of Self-Sacrifice, where two sheer cliffs face each other, five *chi* apart, with three stones propping them like a beam.
Yi and Zi Mountains are fifty *li* south of the prefecture, where the Yellow Emperor performed the feng sacrifice.
Qin Viewing Peak is west of the summit.
Heart-Testing Stone lies west of Fairy Bridge Stone—two interlocking stones perched over a ten-thousand-*ren* cliff. Those who step on it feel it sway, inspiring terror. The superstitious claim only the sincere remain unmoved.
Liangfu Mountain is one hundred *li* south of the prefecture, where the First Emperor of Qin performed the feng sacrifice.
Zhou Viewing Peak: Old records suggest it should lie west of the summit, but the late Censor-in-Chief Hu Zongzhen inscribed a stone to its left at the Azure Cloud Arch.
Yi and Shi Mountains lie east of Liangfu Mountain; the seventy-two ancient lords often performed the feng sacrifice here.
Sword Case Stone is west of the Great Valley—a square stone like a case, said to have once held a divine sword, now taken by immortals.
Aolai Mountain is southwest of the summit, near Bamboo Grove Temple. Its stones rise like pillars; from the imperial tent, the view is even more wondrous.
Wu Viewing Peak’s location is lost, but it likely lies east of the summit.
Sword-Testing Stone is at the mouth of the Great Valley. Legend says an immortal drew the stone-case sword and split this rock, half of which now lies toppled. Today, the two stones’ veins appear cleanly severed.
Elder Peak is southwest of the summit, standing tall like a hoary elder.
Green Mountain lies at the southeastern foot of the mountain, northeast of the Han Bright Hall.
Fairy Shadow Stone is beside Sword-Testing Stone. Its shadow bears a headscarf and sword, one foot extended in a seated pose. The stone is flat as a grindstone, its cliff’s shadow etched as if carved—yet no human hand shaped it. Legend claims this is the immortal who drew the sword to split the stone.
Yellow Mountain is forty *li* west of the summit, named for its soil. With Green Mountain, it flanks Mount Tai like wings.
Parrot Peak stands before Aolai Peak, named for Jia Fei’s resemblance to a phoenix.
Eastern Divine Cloud Mountain is ten *li* east of the summit.
Hanging Stone Peak is southwest of the summit.
Western Divine Cloud Mountain is ten *li* west of the summit.
Returning Crow Peak is west of the summit.
Dragon Vein Stone lies on the southern slope, its veins like a dragon’s.
Stone Rear Mountain is ten *li* west of the summit, named for its many boulders.
Solitary Beauty Peak stands on the southern slope, towering and elegant, its name inscribed by ancients.
Tiger Mound Stone is also on the southern slope, shaped like a crouching tiger.
Dragon Mouth Stone: two stones leaning together, from which a clear spring gushes.

Solitary Mountain stands ten *li* north of the summit, rising steep and solitary. Lion Peak lies on the southern slope, named for its resemblance in form. Ox-Heart Stone is also named for its shape. Jade Maiden Mountain is roughly ten *li* northeast of the summit, where beneath its cliffs lies the Jade Maiden’s Cultivation Stone. Ram Pen Stone is said to be where immortals once grazed their flocks. Lotus Peak stands east of Solitary Beauty Peak, its clustered crags like a lotus blossom. Square Stone is so named for its perfectly square form. Stone Horse Mountain lies fifteen *li* northwest of the summit. Rouge Stone lies on the northern side of the mountain, its hue like cinnabar. Gull Nest Mountain is fifteen *li* west of the summit, its cliffs riddled with gull nests—hence its name. Hanging Blade Peak stands above the Western Stream to the southwest. Red Gate Stone glows red, resembling a vermilion gate from afar. Five Maidens’ Enclosure Stone lies to the northwest. Legend tells of nocturnal cries and struggles, and by dawn, the stones had formed a circle—said to be the work of five immortal maidens. Camel Mountain, ten *li* north of the summit, resembles a camel bearing its young. Hibiscus Peak also rises above the Western Stream. Crane Mountain stands ten *li* north of the summit, its verdant woods home to wild cranes—hence its name. Flying Crow Peak likewise overlooks the Western Stream. Old Crow Peak lies north of the summit, its trees thick with crow nests. Pavilion Meditation Mountain, also called Gaoli or Haoli, adjoins Sheshou to the south, three *li* from the summit. Dragon Spring Peak stands northeast of Doumu Temple, its right flank steep and striking. Three-Pointed Mountain lies ten *li* west of the summit. Lingering Han Mountain rises behind Puzhao Temple. Gentleman’s Peak stands at the summit. Zulai Mountain lies thirty *li* south of the mountain, serving as its *anshan*. Atop it lies Purple Source Pond, with Exquisite Mountain, Solitary Beauty Peak, and the eastern and western Heavenly Barrier strongholds below. Further down lie White River Bend and Bamboo Stream. Here, the Tang poets Li Bai, Kong Chaofu, Han Zhun, Pei Zheng, Tao Wu, and Zhang Shuming once retreated, known as the Six Idlers of Bamboo Stream. Later, the Tang scholar Shi Jie built a study on the old site, writing his works there—hence his title, Master of Zulai. Stone Chambers: one on Jade Maiden Mountain, another at its base, said to be where the Jade Maiden cultivated the Dao, its scenery most wondrous. Lazy Zhang’s Stone Chamber lies at the northern foot of the summit, where the Daoist Lazy Zhang once refined his practice. Stone Boats: one in White Dragon Pool, another at the southeastern foot of the mountain, said to resemble a moored boat at Jia Family Village. Eight Treasures Mountain lies a hundred *li* east of the summit in Laiwu, also called Yellow Mountain. Candle Mountain stands north of Jade Maiden Mountain, its solitary stone like a candle.

Caves and Ridges:
White Cloud Cave lies beneath Phoenix Mountain. From the Fengxi Arch, one descends southward a few paces down steep stone steps to reach it, where white clouds often emerge. The late Censor-in-Chief Li Dai inscribed it *Rain Over All Under Heaven*, alluding to the *Gongyang Commentary*: “Before the morning is out, rain falls over all under heaven.” Censor Tan Yao also carved an inscription in stone. The Fangting Pavilion was built here, commanding the finest views.

Water Curtain Cave lies above Gao Lao Bridge. Welcoming Sun Cave, also called Cloud Sun Cave, can accommodate over twenty people. Minister Zhu Heng inscribed characters on the Thousand-Stone Cliff, naming it Morning Sun Cave. Yellow Flower Cave is located north of the mountain at the Jade Maiden’s cultivation site. Lü Gong Cave, also called Golden Mother Cave, lies on the southern slope beside Jade Maiden Pool. Guodong Cave is situated south of the mountain, its deep and lofty cavern offering respite to travelers and panoramic views of the surrounding peaks. Ghost Immortal Cave lies on the shady side of Xianhao Ridge, its depths dark and secluded—none dare enter. White Crane Cave is forty *li* west of the mountain and can hold over thirty people. Huang Boyang Cave is fifteen *li* west of the summit, where Huang Boyang of the Warring States period cultivated and secluded himself. Golden Thread Cave is ninety *li* north of the mountain, capable of holding over a hundred people, and was where Qiu Changchun refined his elixir. Peach Blossom Cave lies beneath the Tang Dynasty grinding-stone cliff at the summit, with a spring nearby. Langranzi Cave is roughly a *li* east of the Imperial Tent at mid-mountain, accommodating over thirty. Guanyin Cave is behind Bamboo Grove Temple. Lou Jing Cave is a hundred *li* west of the summit, where the Han Dynasty’s Lou Jing secluded himself; a nearby cave yields saltpeter. Green Mist Ridge lies southwest of the summit. Return Horse Ridge’s stone steps grow increasingly steep, forcing riders to dismount. Yellow Slope Ridge, its soil rich in ochre, rises dark and sheer; beyond it, a single path levels out, called Joyous Three. Wild Goose Flight Ridge, also called Turning Back Peak, lies southwest of the summit, where migrating geese and crows often alight. West Traverse Ridge stretches west of the summit, its sheer cliffs spanning over ten *zhang*. Hometown Ridge, also west of the summit, is where, by local lore, the dead return in spirit, longing for their homeland. Ten Peaks Ridge stands to the right of the Jade Emperor Temple. Watershed Ridge lies east of the summit, behind the Han Dynasty’s Bright Hall and west of Yang Lao’s Garden. Ascending Immortal Ridge is northeast of the summit, at the northeastern corner of the Han Dynasty’s Bright Hall. Great Wall Ridge lies northwest of the mountain, a remnant of the ancient Great Wall’s grand defenses. Thought Valley Ridge is south of Valley Mountain Temple. Immortal Terrace Ridge is sixty *li* north of the mountain. Bright Moon Gorge lies to the north, its peaks arrayed like a screen. Recruiting Soldiers Ridge is southwest of the summit, before Proudly Arising Mountain. Little Dragon Gully, once called Little Dragon Mouth, is a stone gorge where waters converge, its cascading springs like a dragon’s spray. Great Dragon Gully was formerly known as Great Dragon Mouth. Peach Blossom Gully is twenty *li* southwest of the summit, south of Peach Blossom Cave, now thickly planted with peach trees. Immortal’s Footprint Gully, also called Horse Hoof Gully, lies a *li* behind Bamboo Grove Temple, bearing the imprint of an immortal’s straw sandal, over a *chi* long, and a horse’s leap, five *cun* in length. Stone Sutra Gully lies on the southern face of the mountain, where a flat stone half a *mu* in size bears an ancient carving of the *Diamond Sutra* in regular script, with some characters nearly eight *fen* in size, said to be the work of Wang Xizhi. Stone Wall Gully is on the southern face, its cliffs sheer as if hewn. Great Gully lies below Bamboo Grove Temple on Proudly Arising Mountain, the path of the Middle Stream, containing wonders such as White Dragon Pool, Hundred-Foot Cliff, and Heavenly Sash Spring, with Song and Yuan Dynasty poems carved on its stones. Dengdu Gully is on the southern face, traditionally held to be the underworld’s domain; today, a Dengdu Temple stands to its south. Ghost Child Gully, also on the southern face, is where, by local lore, the dead return in spirit, following Zhang Hua’s *Bowu Zhi*. Buddha Temple Gully is forty *li* northeast of the mountain, home to Valley Mountain Temple and its stone Buddha. Pepper Seed Gully lies on the southern face, named for an eccentric who once cultivated pepper there. Stream Inside Gully is on the northern face, its deep caverns topped by a gushing spring that never runs dry.

Cliffs, Crags, and Terraces:
Confucius Cliff lies southwest of the summit. To its north, connected to Benevolence Peak, three sides drop sheer for hundreds of *zhang*, with a jutting stone over a *zhang* high at its center; foolish folk often leap from it, seeking boundless merit. Minister He Qiming built a level tomb to deter such acts, inscribing the stone *Love Your Body Terrace*. East Hundred-Foot Cliff joins the summit’s southwest, its waterfall feeding Heavenly Sash Spring. West Hundred-Foot Cliff lies three hundred *bu* from the East Cliff, ten times its height, facing east while the East Cliff faces south. Immortal’s Shadow Cliff stands on Proudly Arising Peak southwest of the summit, its stone dark save for the immortal’s silhouette, pale as paper. Five-Flower Cliff lies south of the summit; from the city walls, the summit is invisible, hidden by this cliff. Quail Cliff is a sheer precipice to the north, where wild quail nest. Horse Stable Cliff is on the southern face, named for its shape. It is also called Three-Character Cliff, for three brushstrokes remain on its surface, unweathered by wind and rain, said to be Lü Dongbin’s hand. Imperial Tent Cliff stands at the Imperial Tent. Twin Peaks Crag lies west of the summit, two peaks facing each other with a chamber-like hollow below. Immortal’s Inquiry Crag—*The History of the Han* records: “The western crag of the summit is where immortals are questioned.” Flood Dragon Immortal Crag is on the southern face, where, by tradition, the Hanlin academician Wang Congzhi shed his mortal form; Yuan Haowen’s *Poem for Zhang Tianni* reads: “On the Shedding Immortal Crag, may you linger.” Ancient Cloud Crag stands on Return Horse Ridge. Moon-Playing Crag and the following three crags’ locations are now lost: Gazing Moon Crag, Lofty Crag, and Cloud-Locking Crag. Phoenix Terrace lies on the southern face. Ascending Immortal Terrace is on the summit’s southern side, where Lü Chunyang once stood. Southern Arch Terrace is on the northern face, its jagged rocks arching upward to a level expanse, hence its name. Northern Arch Terrace is also on the northern face. Yao’s Viewing Terrace is ten *li* northeast of the summit, atop Jade Maiden Mountain, where a flat stone resembles a terrace; tradition holds that Emperor Yao ascended here to abdicate.

Reading Terrace lies on the northwestern rear mountain of Universal Illumination Temple, past the Book-Throwing Ravine at the mountain’s base. There stands a stone some four or five *zhang* square, where the Song scholars Sun Taishan, Shi Zulai, and Hu Anding once read; Li Guo of Suzhou inscribed it in the *ba fen* script. Gazing-Ru Terrace is atop the Self-Abandonment Cliff.

**Gates, Gardens, and Stockades**
First Heavenly Gate bears an archway within the Yueyang precinct, a *li* or so inside the Sacred Mount Tai Arch. Red Gate lies in Yueyang, its plaque reading *Flying Cloud Pavilion* or *Cloud-Ladder*. Second Heavenly Gate bears an archway in Yueyang, also called Small Heavenly Gate—here stood the imperial encampment, for Emperor Zhenzong of Song once halted his retinue. Sincerity Gate lies half a *li* above the imperial encampment. Third Heavenly Gate, the Stone Gate, also called Southern Heavenly Gate, marks the end of the Eighteen Bends. Eastern Heavenly Gate lies on the eastern summit. Western Heavenly Gate lies on the western summit. Dark Warrior Gate stands at the northern foot. Old Yang’s Garden lies on the southern face, its stone cliffs perilously sheer; legend holds that Old Yang took refuge from war here. Medicinal Garden lies to the southeast; tradition claims that Daoist adepts once cultivated herbs here. Water Immortal Stockade lies on the western summit, capable of holding a thousand men. Immortal’s Stockade lies on the northern summit, also holding a thousand. Nine Maidens’ Stockade lies below Three Flame Mountains on the western summit; atop a lofty peak, legend says nine maidens once took refuge from war here. Soaring Han Stockade lies in Yueyang, holding several hundred. Heavenly Victory Stockade lies before Proud Arrival Mountain, holding ten thousand. Liu Penzi, Chi Fu, and others once mustered troops here; above stand the Recruiting General’s Mound, Banner-Raising Stone, and other imposing formations.

**Springs, Pools, and Rivers**
Azure Sky Spring lies east of the Hall of Vast Life on the summit. Camel-Grinding Spring lies below Crossing Man Peak; summit dwellers draw all their water from here. Heavenly Sash Spring lies below Hundred-Cliff of Proud Arrival Mountain, where a ten-*zhang* precipice sends cascading water like a hanging sash. White Crane Spring lies on the southern foot, crisp and sweet; compared to city wells, its weight differs. Imperial Escort Spring lies below the imperial encampment on Yellow Defile Ridge. Sacred Water Spring lies on Return Horse Ridge. Water Curtain Spring lies at Western Hundred-Cliff. Dust-Washing Spring lies within the Rear Earth Temple on the southern foot; travelers often scoop its water to rinse their eyes, hence its other name, Eye-Bright. Flying Simurgh Spring lies to the right of Queen Mother’s Pool, its waters clear and cold, flowing into the pool. Sweet Spring lies at Celestial Writ Abbey, the very one Minister Wang Qinruo presented to Emperor Zhenzong. Though it never runs dry, its waters are now no different from common streams. The Sweet Spring mentioned in the *Dharma Garden* is now lost. Queen Mother’s Pool, also called Jasper Pool, lies on the southern foot; its waters originate from the ravines of Mount Tai. Long ago, the Yellow Emperor built an abbey on Mount Tai and dispatched seven feather-clad, cloud-crowned attendants to tend incense and welcome the Queen Mother of the West, hence its name. Jade Maiden’s Pool lies to the right of the Yuanjun Shrine on the summit, sweet and cold, never running dry. Also called Sacred Mother’s Small Pool, it bears an inscription by Xie Tingjie of Yuzhang. White Dragon Pool lies on Proud Arrival Mountain, its bed several *xun* wide and fathomless; above stands a Dragon Spirit Shrine, where prayers for rain are always answered. Censor Huang Bie carved the words *Rain’s Origin* into stone. Feng Family Pool lies within the Feng Family Hamlet on the southern foot, fed by the lower flow of White Crane Spring. Cool River rises on the southwestern summit, where ravines converge into Western Stream, exiting through Great Ravine Pass from White Dragon Pool, flowing south into Floating River, then merging with Wen River to reach the Canal. Hair-Washing River rises on Yellow Defile Ridge, where ravine waters converge into Central Stream; passing Queen Mother’s Pool, it is named for her, then flows southeast to join Floating River and the Great Wen. Floating River rises from the waters of Peach Blossom Ravine on the western mountain, winding past the prefectural seat’s southeast for twenty *li* before entering the Wen. Wen River has three sources: one from the eastern foot of Mount Tai in Laiwu County’s Yuan Mountain, another from Laizhai Village in Laiwu, both flowing through Zulai Mountain’s waters, then south for thirty *li* to Great Wen Mouth, then southwest past Wenshang County’s north to reach the Canal.

**Streams, Ravines, Wells, and Bays**
Eastern Stream rises below Ascending Immortal Terrace in Yueyang and from Green Mountain’s waters, passing the Han Bright Hall. Western Stream rises west of the summit, where waters converge at Hundred-Cliff and White Dragon Pool’s Great Ravine. Central Stream rises below Yellow Defile Ridge, gathering ravine waters. Eagle-Fretting Ravine lies below the Ten Bends. Book-Throwing Ravine lies below Soaring Han Peak; named for Hu Anding, who once cast his family letters here. The old Eastern Lu inscription by Li Guo in *ba fen* script marks the ravine’s name in three large characters. Fragrant Well lies in the ancient Mount Tai Temple on the southern mountain, its waters exceptionally sweet and clear, unlike common wells. Zhu Rifan’s poem reads: *“Before the temple’s fragrant well, one recognizes the tossed coins,”* referring to this. Heavenly Well lies ten *li* east of the mountain, several *mu* wide, gathering waters from ravines and the Wen River; also called Heavenly Well Bay. Tortoise Bay lies in Yueyang. Blackwater Bay lies on the western mountain. Sudden Thunder Bay lies in Western Stream; when rain approaches, thunderclaps often erupt here. Horse-Watering Bay lies in Western Stream. Heavenly Well Bay lies to the north and southwest. This Bay lies above Queen Mother’s Pool, its charming rocks beloved; Lü Chunyang’s poem reads: *“The reckless flood dragons know my name.”* Gong-and-Drum Bay lies in Western Stream’s Great Ravine; legend says that on the fifteenth day of the seventh month, sounds like gongs and drums arise here.

**Archways, Inscriptions, and Bridges**
Sacred Mount Tai Arch marks the beginning of the ascent; it was jointly erected by Grand Coordinator Du Luofeng and others. First Heavenly Gate Arch bears an inscription by Assistant Administration Commissioner Long Guang, built by Sub-Prefect Wang Zhigang. Confucius’ Ascent Arch was inscribed by Yuzhang’s champion scholar Luo Hongguang.

Minister Zhu Heng built the Shangshu Arch. Censor Gao Yingfang built the Dayi Arch. The Gaolao Bridge Arch was rebuilt by Prefect Li Bochun and others, originally constructed by Vice Prefect Zhai Tao. The arches of Water Curtain Grotto, Return-Horse Ridge, Yellow Saddle Ridge, and Second Heavenly Gate were rebuilt in the same manner as the Gaolao Bridge Arch. The Yingtian Arch was repaired by the Chong Prefecture. The Xiaolongyu Arch was rebuilt in the same manner as the Gaolao Bridge. The Dalonggong Arch was rebuilt in the same manner as the Gaolao Bridge. The Eighteen Bends Arch was rebuilt in the same manner as the Gaolao Bridge. The White Cloud Grotto Arch was built by Grand Coordinator Li Dai. The Pengxuan Arch was rebuilt in the same manner as the Gaolao Bridge. The Jishi Arch, Anmin Arch, and Jingjian Bixia Arch were built by Grand Coordinator Li Fu, Censor Wu Ding, Han Yinggeng, and others. The Shengzhong Arch. The *Zhengda Guangming* inscription was carved by Dai Xi. The *Five Peaks’ Ancestor* inscription was carved by Dai Xi, with both steles located beneath the Sacred Mount Tai Arch. The *World’s Marvel* inscription was carved by Gentleman of the Outer Court Yang Keda on a stone north of First Heavenly Gate. The *Foremost Mountain* inscription was carved by Censor Li Fuchu beneath the Tianzeng Arch. The *Pillar of the Midstream* inscription was carved by Presented Scholar Chen Ganyu on a stone cliff. The *Here Begins the Marvelous* inscription was carved by Hanlin Academician Ma Yilong on a stone cliff. The *Celestial Pine* inscription was carved by Fan Zongwu on the right. The *Here Grows More Marvelous* inscription was carved by Ma Yilong on a stone cliff. The *Flying Spring Waterfall* inscription was carved by Vice Prefect Zhai Tao on a stone cliff. The *Here Grows Ever More Marvelous* inscription was carved by Ma Yilong on a stone cliff. The *Wind, Waves, Clouds, and Gorges* inscription was carved on a stone cliff. The *Mighty Pillar Between Two Peaks* stele stands outside Third Heavenly Gate. The *Gazing Down Upon the Six Directions* stele stands in the same location as the previous. The *Panorama of Mountains and Rivers* inscription is carved beneath the Pengxuan Arch. The *Marvel of the Sea and Sun* inscription, written by Minister Wu Tongchun, is on the southeastern precipice of Sun-Viewing Peak. The *Northern Recesses of Mount Tai* inscription, written by a former scholar, is on the cliff of Yellow Flower Grotto.

The Gaolao Bridge lies five *li* above Red Gate; legend says a scholar of the Huang-Lao tradition surnamed Gao first opened this path. The Zhuishuiliu Bridge lies below Water Curtain Grotto. The Shengxian Bridge lies below Horse Stable Cliff. The bridge in Xiaolongyu Gorge. The Butian Bridge lies at Second Heavenly Gate. The Shuwang Bridge lies along the path of Stone Sutra Valley, its name inscribed on stone. The Queen Mother Bridge lies above Queen Mother’s Pool; to its east is Lü’s Grotto, and to its west is the former site of the Sacred Mount Tai Temple—this is the foremost scenic spot. The Naruo Bridge spans the Naruo River at the river ford southwest of the prefectural city, also called the Gold and Silver Bridge.

*Gazetteer of Mount Tai*, Scroll Five: *Territorial Register*
A note asks: *What does “registering the territory” signify?*

The *Territorial Register* records the domains under the jurisdiction of Mount Tai. These domains are subordinate to prefectures, commanderies, and circuits. Now, Mount Tai stands tall as a singular convergence, its numinous efficacy flowing through the eight directions, nurturing the myriad forms and storing the essence of a thousand ages—how could it be confined to a single domain or territory? Yet since antiquity, lands and surnames have been bestowed to establish states, and boundaries have been demarcated to govern domains, always relying on famous mountains and great rivers as their borders. Thus, the *Tribute of Yu* states that the sea and Mount Tai mark the bounds of Qingzhou among the Nine Provinces. The *Rites of Zhou* ranks the sacred peaks, with Yanzhou’s daystar being Mount Tai—this is the origin of territorial demarcation. From the Qin and Han onward, governors were appointed and provinces divided, territories carved and rearranged, with countless changes in administration. The Han’s Fenggao, the Tang’s Qianfeng, and the Song’s Fengfu were all counties named in relation to the lands of Mount Tai. When the August Ming dynasty established its capital in the north, Mount Tai alone among the Five Peaks stood closest, majestically anchoring the eastern lands and shielding the imperial capital like the stars encircling the North Star. Moreover, the lifeline of the empire’s grain transport and state finances also depended on this region—was this not the very foundation of unshakable stability? The lands of Mount Tai were thus elevated from a county to a prefecture, entrusted with the annual sacrifices, and the prefecture was named Tai’an—this was no accident. Here, we exhaustively record the evolution of its administrative divisions through the ages, along with their respective durations, as presented below:

**Dynasty** | **Circuit** | **Commandery** | **Prefecture/County**

From the *Yu Gong*: In the Tang and Yu dynasties, the regions of Qing, Hai, and Dai belonged to Qingzhou. During the Xia and Shang, and under the Zhou, they pertained to Yanzhou. The *Zhou Li* records the provinces, while the *Lu Song* states, "Tai—its sacred peak is called Dai," and "The mountains stand towering."

During the Warring States period, the state of Qi established Boyi. Under the Qin, it belonged to Qi Commandery as Boyi, later becoming part of Taishan Commandery. In the Han, it initially fell under Jizhou. During the Western Han, under the jurisdiction of Yanzhou, Emperor Wu established Fenggao County in the first year of the *Yuanfeng* era (110 BCE). In the Eastern Han, still under Yanzhou’s Taishan Commandery, Fenggao County was visited by Emperor Guangwu in the twenty-second year of his reign (46 CE) and by Emperor Zhang in the second year of the *Yuanhe* era (85 CE).

Under the Jin, it remained part of Yanzhou’s Taishan Commandery as Fenggao County. During the Southern Song, it continued under Yanzhou’s Taishan Commandery as Fenggao County. In the Northern Wei, it was part of Yanzhou’s Taishan Commandery as Fenggao County, later transferred to Lu Commandery as Daishan County.

In the Sui, under Yanzhou, it was initially reassigned to Lu Commandery. In the sixth year of the *Kaihuang* era (586 CE), Fenggao was renamed Daishan County. By the early *Daye* era, it belonged to Yanzhou’s Lu Commandery as Qianfeng County.

During the Tang, under the Henan Circuit, Emperor Gaozong renamed Daishan County to Qianfeng County. Under Yanzhou’s Xuqing Prefecture, it became Fengfu County. In the Song, under the Jingdong Xilu Circuit, the Eastern Peak was first sacrificed to at Yanzhou. In the first year of the *Xiangfu* era (1008 CE), Emperor Zhenzong renamed Qianfeng County to Fengfu County, bestowing upon it the title "Great Qi" and honoring the peak as "Ren Sheng Emperor."

In the Jin, under the Shandong Xilu Circuit, Taian Prefecture was elevated from a military prefecture to a state in the twenty-third year of the *Dading* era (1183 CE). Under the Yuan, within the Shandong Dongxi Circuit, the title "Tianqi Dasheng Ren Sheng Emperor" was added to Mount Tai.

In the August Ming dynasty, under the Shandong Provincial Administration Commission, it fell under Jinan Prefecture as Taian Prefecture. The Hongwu Emperor standardized the titles, formally designating the deity of the Eastern Peak. Fengfu County was abolished and merged into the prefecture.

*Tai History, Second Volume, Character Section Catalog*

**Volume Six of *Records of Mount Tai*: *Annals of the Royal Hunt*, with Appended Accounts of Feng and Shan Sacrifices**

The Master said: Why record the *Annals of the Royal Hunt*?
To chronicle the grand rites of the Son of Heaven’s seasonal progresses. Yet why include accounts of the feng and shan sacrifices?
To record their deviations—also to expose the extravagance of rulers and the flattery of ministers, as a warning for posterity.

The *Canon of Yao* prescribes a royal hunt every five years, while the *Rites of Zhou* detail a progress every twelve. In the second month of spring—the season of growth—and at Mount Tai—the land of growth—the sovereign would sit in luminous judgment, summon the feudal lords, and examine their governance, all for the sake of the people. How unlike later ages, where rulers carved stone and inscribed mountains to boast of their achievements, or sealed golden tablets and jade caskets to curry favor with spirits! Indulgence and oppression of the people, vast processions of a thousand chariots and ten thousand riders—yet falsely invoking the precedent of seventy-two ancient rulers. Even the so-called sagacious emperors since Qin and Han could not escape this folly.

How wise was our Emperor Chengzu, the Wen Emperor of the Ming, who saw through this and resolutely rejected it, establishing a model for our enlightened age! Does this not surpass all antiquity?

Here, I append a selection of feng and shan texts from former dynasties, concluding with the critiques of earlier worthies, that readers may examine and reflect upon them.

In the Tang and Yu eras, during the second month, the emperor made his eastern progress, arriving at Mount Tai. He offered burnt offerings and observed the rites of the mountains, then received the eastern lords in audience. He harmonized the seasons, months, and days; unified weights and measures; refined the five rituals; and, like the five sacred vessels, conducted a grand progress every five years, summoning the feudal lords from the four directions to court.
*(See the *Canon of Shun*. The Xia dynasty followed this precedent.)*

In the Zhou dynasty, the emperor made a grand progress every twelve years. In the second month, he conducted his eastern progress, arriving at Mount Tai, where he received the feudal lords in audience at the Bright Hall. *(See the *Rites of Zhou*. For the Bright Hall, see *Records of Remnant Traces*.)*

In the twenty-eighth year of his reign, the First Emperor of Qin journeyed eastward through his commanderies and counties. He ascended Mount Zou and Feng, erecting a stone stele, and consulted with the Ru scholars of Lu to compose an inscription extolling Qin’s virtue. They deliberated on the rites of feng and shan, as well as the sacrifices to mountains and rivers. He then ascended Mount Tai, where he erected a stone altar for the feng sacrifice and performed the shan rites below. A sudden storm arose, and he took shelter beneath a tree, which he later enfeoffed as the "Five Great Men." He performed the shan sacrifice at Liangfu, and the inscription on the stele was carved. *(See the* Records of the Grand Historian*. Wang Zhongyan remarked that the feng and shan rites were not ancient but originated in the Qin and Han dynasties, hence his account begins with Qin.)*

The inscription in Li Si’s seal script survives in twenty-nine characters. *(For details, see* Records of Remnant Traces*.)*

*"To serve Heaven with ritual, to establish oneself with righteousness,
To serve one’s father with filial piety, to perfect others with benevolence.
Within the Four Seas, all is governed by commanderies and counties;
The barbarians of the four directions all come to offer tribute.
The people multiply, and Heaven’s blessings are everlasting.
Now the Emperor ascends the throne, establishing clear laws,
While his ministers cultivate themselves in good order.
In the twenty-sixth year, he first unified all under Heaven,
And none dared resist or disobey.
He personally inspected the distant lands and the common folk,
Ascending this Mount Tai, surveying the eastern extremity.
His attending ministers, reflecting on the origins of his achievements,
Reverently recited his merits and virtue.
The governance of the Dao flows forth, and all things flourish,
Each following its proper pattern.
Great righteousness shines brightly, to be transmitted to posterity,
In accord with the succession of change and renewal.
The Emperor, sagely in person, having pacified all under Heaven,
Never slackens in governance.
Rising early and retiring late, he establishes enduring benefits,
Issuing teachings and instructions,
Disseminating the classics and ensuring their reach,
So that near and far are all well-ordered.
All uphold the sagely will:
Nobles and commoners are clearly distinguished,
Men and women observe proper rites,
Diligently fulfilling their duties.
Illuminating inner and outer realms, none fail to be pure and upright.
These deeds shall be bestowed upon later generations,
And their transformative influence shall extend without end.
Following the late Emperor’s testamentary edict,
He eternally bears its solemn admonitions."*

In the first year of the *Yuanfeng* era, Emperor Wu of Han conducted an eastern progress to the seacoast. In the summer, the fourth month, he performed the feng sacrifice on Mount Tai and descended to sit in the Bright Hall. His proclamation read:

*"I, with my humble self, have inherited the supreme position, trembling with awe. My virtue is meager, and I am unenlightened in ritual and music. Thus, I have performed the eight sacrifices. Yet Heaven and Earth have bestowed their favor, manifesting wondrous signs, as if I had heard them with my own ears. Overawed by these marvels, though I wished to desist, I dared not. I therefore ascended Mount Tai to perform the feng sacrifice, proceeding to Liangfu before ascending the altar. Solemnly, I renewed myself, desiring to begin anew with the officials and scholars. Henceforth, the tenth month shall be the first month of the* Yuanfeng *era."*

In the regions he traversed—Bo, Fenggao, Sheqiu, Licheng, and Liangfu—he remitted the land taxes and debts of the people, and those already paid were forgiven. He also bestowed two bolts of silk upon every orphaned or widowed elder over seventy.

The four counties shall remit this year’s taxes in full. The Emperor bestows upon all commoners a rank of nobility, and to every hundred households of women, an ox and wine.

He further issued an edict: "In ancient times, the Son of Heaven made a grand tour of inspection once every five years, performing sacrifices at Mount Tai. The feudal lords had their lodgings prepared along the way; let it now be decreed that each lord establish his residence at the foot of Mount Tai."

In the second autumn, a Bright Hall was constructed on Mount Tai. In the winter of the tenth month of the first year of *Taichu*, the Emperor journeyed to Mount Tai. In the spring of the third month of the fourth year of *Taishi*, he again traveled to Mount Tai. On the *renwu* day, he sacrificed to the High Ancestor in the Bright Hall, offering him as associate to the Supreme Lord, and there received the annual accounts. On the *guiwei* day, he sacrificed to Emperor Jing in the Bright Hall. On the *jiashen* day, he renewed the feng sacrifice. On the *bingxu* day, he performed the shan rites at Stone Hearkening.

In the spring of the third month of the fourth year of *Zhenghe*, the Emperor plowed at Great Marsh and, on his return, visited Mount Tai.

*An Abridged Account of the Feng and Shan Sacrifices by Sima Qian*

Since ancient times, which of the emperors who received the Mandate did not perform the feng and shan sacrifices? Some undertook the rites without due signs, yet none who beheld auspicious omens failed to ascend Mount Tai. Duke Huan of Qi, having established hegemony, convened the feudal lords at Kuiqiu and desired to perform the feng sacrifice. Guan Zhong said: "Of old, seventy-two rulers performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at Liangfu, and I, Yiwu, have recorded twelve of them. In former times, the Lord of No-Concerns performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at Cloud Cloud; Fuxi performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at Cloud Cloud; Shennong performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at Cloud Cloud; the Flame Emperor performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at Cloud Cloud; the Yellow Emperor performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at Tingting; Zhuanxu performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at Cloud Cloud; Emperor Ku performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at Cloud Cloud; Yao performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at Cloud Cloud; Shun performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at Cloud Cloud; Yu performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at Mount Kuaiji; Tang performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at Cloud Cloud; King Cheng of Zhou performed the feng at Mount Tai and the shan at She Shou—all had received the Mandate before they could perform the rites."

Duke Huan said: "I have campaigned north against the Mountain Rong, passing through Guchu; west against Daxia, crossing the Flowing Sands; eastward, my horses were suspended and my chariots hoisted to ascend Mount Beier; southward, I advanced to Shaoling, climbing Mount Xiong’er to gaze upon the Yangtze and Han. Twice I convened the feudal lords with chariots of war, and six times with carriages of peace. Nine times I assembled the lords, unifying the realm under Heaven, and none dared defy me. How is this different from the Three Dynasties who received the Mandate?"

Thereupon, seeing that Duke Huan could not be dissuaded by words, Guan Zhong sought to deter him with practical concerns: "In ancient feng sacrifices, the millet from Haoshang and the grain from Beili served as offerings; the triple-ribbed rush from between the Yangtze and Huai served as the mat. The Eastern Sea yielded the paired-eye fish, and the Western Sea the paired-wing bird—only then did the other fifteen auspicious things arrive unbidden. Now the phoenix and qilin do not come, the sacred grain does not grow, while weeds and brambles flourish, and owls and harpies arrive in droves. Under such conditions, to perform the feng sacrifice—is this not inadvisable?" Duke Huan thereupon desisted.

Over a hundred years later, Confucius discoursed on the Six Arts and transmitted a summary of the *Changes*, stating that more than seventy kings had received the Mandate, performed the feng at Mount Tai, and the shan at Liangfu. Yet the rituals of their sacrificial vessels remain obscure, and the matter is difficult to expound.

Three years after ascending the throne, the First Emperor of Qin toured the eastern commanderies and counties, sacrificing at Mount Zouyi and extolling the achievements of Qin. He then summoned seventy erudites and scholars from Qi and Lu to Mount Tai. Some of the scholars argued: "In ancient times, the feng sacrifice was performed with a rush chariot to avoid damaging the mountain’s soil, stones, grass, and trees. The ground was swept clean for the sacrifice, and the mat was made of artemisia and couch grass, signifying ease of compliance." Hearing these divergent opinions, which were difficult to implement, the First Emperor dismissed the scholars. He then cleared a chariot path, ascending from the southern slope of Mount Tai to its summit, where he erected a stele extolling the virtue of the First Emperor of Qin and proclaiming his right to perform the feng sacrifice. Descending by the northern path, he performed the shan rites at Liangfu. The rituals were largely adopted from those used by the Grand Invocators in sacrificing to the Supreme Lord at Yong, but the details of the feng and shan rites were kept secret, and the world has no record of them.

As the First Emperor ascended Mount Tai, he encountered a violent storm midway and took shelter beneath a great tree. The scholars, having been excluded from the rites of the feng sacrifice, mocked him upon hearing of the storm. Twelve years after the First Emperor performed the feng and shan sacrifices, Qin perished. The scholars, resentful of Qin’s burning of the *Book of Songs* and *Book of Documents* and its persecution of men of letters, and the common people, embittered by its harsh laws, rebelled against it. All falsely claimed: "The First Emperor, when he ascended Mount Tai, was struck by a violent storm and could not perform the feng sacrifice." Was this not a case of undertaking the rites without possessing the requisite virtue?

Now, since the present Emperor obtained the sacred tripod, he has consulted with the feudal lords and ministers regarding the feng and shan sacrifices. These rites are rare and long discontinued, and none know their proper forms. The scholars have drawn upon the *Book of Documents*, the *Rites of Zhou*, and the *Royal Regulations*, as well as the archery and ox-sacrificing ceremonies of the *Odes*. A man of Qi, Ding Gong, over ninety years of age, said: "The feng and shan sacrifices are the names of the rites of immortality." Thereupon, the Emperor commanded the scholars to practice the ox-sacrificing rites and draft the protocols for the feng and shan sacrifices. After several years, the time for the rites drew near.

The Emperor, having heard the words of Gongsun Qing and the fangshi, that the rulers from the Yellow Emperor onward had performed the feng and shan sacrifices and summoned strange creatures to commune with the gods, desired to emulate the Yellow Emperor and those before him, to ascend to the immortals of Penglai, to elevate his virtue above the Nine August Ones, and to embellish the rites with Confucian learning. Yet the scholars, unable to clarify the details of the feng and shan sacrifices, were constrained by the ancient texts of the *Odes* and *Documents* and could not exercise their ingenuity. The Emperor then displayed the ritual vessels for the feng and shan sacrifices to the scholars and dismissed them all, making no further use of them.

In the third month, he journeyed east to Mount Tai. The grasses and trees of Mount Tai had not yet sprouted leaves, so he ordered men to carry stones and erect them at the summit. The Emperor reflected that the scholars and fangshi had each offered different accounts of the feng and shan sacrifices, none of them canonical or practicable.

The Son of Heaven arrived at Liangfu and performed the rites to honor the local earth deity. On the day *yimao*, he commanded the attendant scholars to don *pibian* caps and *shen* sashes, then sacrificed an ox to conduct the ceremony. At the eastern foot of Mount Tai, he performed the feng sacrifice in the manner of the suburban rites to Taiyi. The feng altar measured one *zhang* and two *chi* in width and nine *chi* in height; beneath it lay a jade tablet with inscribed text. Once the secret rites were completed, the Emperor ascended Mount Tai alone with the Attendant-in-Ordinary and the Master of the Imperial Carriage, Hou. There, too, a feng sacrifice was performed. All details of the proceedings were forbidden from disclosure. The following day, he descended by the northern path.

On the day *bingchen*, he performed the shan sacrifice at the northeastern base of Mount Tai, solemnly offering to Houtu. That night, a radiance seemed to emanate from the feng altar, and by day, auspicious clouds arose from its midst. The Emperor returned from the shan sacrifice and took his seat in the Bright Hall, where his ministers took turns offering toasts. The following summer, a drought occurred. Gongsun Qing remarked, "In the time of the Yellow Emperor, when the feng sacrifice was performed, there was a drought that lasted three years." The Emperor then issued an edict: "The heavens are parched—could this be due to the feng sacrifice?" He ordered the rites of the fifth year to be conducted as before, with the addition of a shan sacrifice at Stone Gate. Stone Gate, located south of Mount Tai’s base, was said by many fangshi to be a gateway to the immortals; thus, the Emperor personally performed the shan rites there.

The Grand Historian remarks: I accompanied the imperial progress to sacrifice to the gods of heaven and earth, the famous mountains and rivers, and to perform the feng and shan rites. I entered the Longevity Palace to attend the divine dialogues and thoroughly examined the intentions of the fangshi and ritual officials. Upon my return, I arranged and recorded the practices concerning ghosts and spirits from antiquity to the present, revealing their inner and outer aspects. May future gentlemen find this worthy of perusal. As for the details of sacrificial vessels, jades, and silks, or the rites of offering and response, these remain the province of the relevant officials.

Ban Gu’s *Baihu Tong* summarizes: Why must a ruler who changes the dynastic surname and receives the Mandate ascend Mount Tai to perform the feng sacrifice? It is to fulfill the duty of announcing the succession. At the inception of receiving the Mandate, he reforms the institutions in response to heaven. When the realm attains great peace and his achievements are complete, he performs the feng and shan sacrifices to proclaim this peace. Why must this be done at Mount Tai? It is the place where all things undergo transformation. Why must it be performed atop the mountain? To ascend high and address the high, following the principle of like kind. Thus, the feng sacrifice elevates the height, while the shan sacrifice at Mount Liangfu broadens the foundation. Inscribing stone to record the reign title is to manifest one’s merits and present them for posterity. Heaven is honored for its height, earth for its depth; thus, augmenting Mount Tai’s height repays heaven, while reinforcing Liangfu’s base repays earth, making clear the Mandate of heaven and earth. When achievements are complete and affairs concluded, benefiting heaven and earth, it is as though the high are made higher and the deep made deeper.

Some say the feng sacrifice involves sealing with golden mud and silver cords; others say stone mud and golden cords, sealed with the imperial seal.
