Confucius said: "Ascending Mount Tai, one may observe the more than seventy rulers of different surnames who could be counted." The term *feng* means "to broaden." The term *shan* signifies the transmission of success. Why is it performed at Liangfu? Because the Three Sovereigns performed the shan sacrifice at Mount Yiyi, proclaiming their success and declaring that the virtuous would inherit it. *Yiyi* conveys the sense of perpetuity. The Five Emperors performed the shan sacrifice at Mount Tingting, demonstrating the careful measurement and clear manifestation of virtue. The Three Kings performed the shan sacrifice at Mount Liangfu, for *liang* means "trust" and *fu* means "assistance"—assisting the Dao of heaven and earth in their course.

Only when great peace is achieved is the feng sacrifice performed to announce it to heaven. Why must it be at Mount Tai? To make clear the change of surname, inscribe stone to record the reign title, and proclaim this to the hundred kings. The *liao* sacrifice to heaven embodies the principle of repayment. The *Odes* says: "Oh, the glorious Zhou of this time, / Surveying its lofty mountains." This refers to the Zhou dynasty’s attainment of great peace and its feng sacrifice at Mount Tai.

In the twentieth year of *Jianwu* (26–56 CE), Emperor Guangwu of the Eastern Han embarked on an eastern inspection tour and arrived at Mount Tai. In the thirtieth year, his ministers submitted a memorial requesting the feng and shan sacrifices. The Emperor issued a decree: "Having reigned for thirty years, the grievances of the people fill my ears—whom do I deceive? Do I deceive heaven? It is said that even Mount Tai is not as discerning as Lin Fang; why should I defile the records of seventy-two generations?" When Duke Huan of Qi desired to perform the feng sacrifice, Guan Zhong opposed it. "If officials from distant commanderies are sent to offer felicitations, heaping empty praises, they shall be shaved and compelled to cultivate wasteland." From then on, his ministers dared not speak of it again.

In the thirty-second year, the Emperor read the *He Tu Hui Chang Fu* by night, which stated: "The ninth of the Red Liu shall meet its fate at Mount Tai." Moved by this, he ordered Liang Song to examine the apocryphal texts for references to the ninth generation’s feng sacrifice. Liang and others submitted a memorial, and the Emperor granted permission. Seeking precedents from the *Yuanfeng* era, the officials deliberated on the implements for the feng and shan sacrifices. They memorialized that square stones should be stacked for the altar, with jade tablets, jade seals, golden mud, and stone seals, all according to prescribed measurements. The Emperor, deeming the use of stone too laborious and wishing to perform the feng sacrifice by the second month, issued an edict to Liang Song to utilize the existing hollowed-out feng stone and merely add to the sealing. Liang submitted a memorial in protest, but it was not heeded.

In the first month, the Emperor arrived at Fenghao and dispatched the Palace Attendant and the Prefect of the Orchid Terrace, along with artisans, to ascend the mountain and inscribe the stone. On the day *xinmao*, he performed the feng sacrifice at Mount Tai; on the day *jiawu*, he performed the shan sacrifice at Liangfu. On the day *jimao* of the fourth month, he issued a general amnesty, renaming the thirty-second year of *Jianwu* as the first year of *Jianwu Zhongyuan*.

The inscription on the stone erected by Emperor Guangwu reads in part: In the thirty-second year of *Jianwu*, the second month, the Emperor conducted an eastern inspection tour and arrived at Mount Tai. He performed the *chai* sacrifice, offered due reverence to the mountains and rivers, and sacrificed to the host of spirits. He then received the Eastern Marquises. Among his attending ministers were the Grand Commandant Xian, the Acting Minister over the Masses and Special Advancer, Marquis of Gaomi, Yu, and others. The descendants of the two royal houses of Han were in attendance, as was the Marquis of Baocheng, descendant of Confucius, who was ranked among the Eastern Marquises. Twelve vassal kings also came to assist in the sacrifices.

The *He Tu Chi Dai Fu* states: "Liu Xiu raised troops to punish the unrighteous; the four barbarians gathered like clouds, dragons battled in the wilds. At the conjunction of four and seven, fire shall reign." The *He Tu Hui Chang Fu* states: "The ninth of the Red Emperor shall inspect and attain the center. The ninth of Emperor Liu shall meet its fate at Mount Tai. If this is truly heeded, deceit and falsehood shall not arise."

The virtue of the Red Han flourished, its ninth generation met prosperity, and its imperial tours to Mount Tai were all fated. Heaven and Earth aided the ninth, an eternal principle of the revered classics. The great flourishing of the Han followed the Dao of the ninth-generation king. He performed the feng sacrifice upon Mount Tai, carved stone to record his deeds, and performed the shan sacrifice at Liangfu, then withdrew to reflect upon and examine the Five Constants.

As recorded in the *He Tu He Gu Pian*, the *Wei Shu Zhen Yao Du*, and the *Xiao Jing Gou Na*—texts transmitted through the classics and prophecies: Long ago, Emperor Yao, wise and discerning, yielded his throne to the commoner Shun, and his descendants held the jade scepter. Wang Mang, from the family of an empress’s brother, wielded the power of the Three Excellencies, balancing like the legs of a tripod, and usurped the authority of the Grand Steward. Relying on the pretense of the Duke of Zhou and Huo Guang’s precedent of aiding the young and returning governance, he seized the throne through rebellion, usurped the imperial title, and established himself. The ancestral temples were destroyed in wrath, the altars of soil and grain perished, and for eighteen years, they received no sacrificial offerings. The three provinces of Yang, Xu, and Qing first rose in rebellion, with warfare raging unchecked, spreading even to Jing Province. Heroes and strongmen merged their forces, gathering in camps of a hundred *li*, and many usurped titles. The northern barbarians launched raids, leaving a thousand *li* without smoke, without the cries of parrots, the barking of dogs, or any sound of life.

Heaven turned its gaze, and the Emperor, though of humble birth, received the Mandate to revive the dynasty. At twenty-eight, he raised his armies, and thus, over more than ten years of successive campaigns, the guilty were punished. The common people returned to their fields and dwelled in their homes. Writing was unified in script, carriages in track, and people in moral order.

Wherever boats and carriages reached, wherever people drew near, none failed to offer tribute. He established the Bright Hall, erected the suburban altars, raised the Spirit Terrace, and founded schools. He standardized pitch-pipes, measures, weights, and balances. He restored the Five Rites, the Five Jade Tablets, the Three Silks, the Two Sacrificial Animals, the One Death Offering, and the Assistants’ Ceremonies. Officials each attended to their duties, returning to the ancient canons.

He reigned for thirty-three years, reaching the age of sixty-one. Daily he turned back with diligence, never daring to indulge in ease, traversing peril and hardship, personally inspecting the common folk. He revered the gods and spirits, showed compassion to the elderly, governed the people in accordance with antiquity, and listened with wisdom, fairness, and forbearance.

The Emperor took great care with the true texts of the *River Chart* and *Awl Script*. In the month’s *xinmao* day, he performed the *chai* ritual and ascended Mount Tai. On the *jiawu* day, he performed the *shan* rite at Liangyin. Thus he received the auspicious omens, securing blessings for the people, unifying all under heaven, and bequeathing this legacy to posterity. All officials and ministers, prefects and masters, were granted enduring blessings without end.

In the second year of *Yuanhe* during Emperor Zhang’s reign, in the first month, he made an eastern inspection tour and performed the *chai* rite to announce to the Peak of Tai. He visited Fenggao and sacrificed to the Five Thearchs at the Bright Hall above the Wen River.

In the third year of *Yanguang* during Emperor An’s reign, on the *bingzi* day of the second month, he made an eastern inspection tour. On the *xinmao* day, he performed the *chai* rite to announce to the Peak of Tai.

Emperor Ming of Wei made three eastern inspection tours to the Peak of Tai, inquiring after the elderly, relieving the sick and poor, and at times bestowing grain and silk.

In the ninth year of *Kaihuang* during Emperor Wen of Sui’s reign, the court and the people all requested a *feng* and *shan* ceremony. His edict briefly stated: “To inscribe one’s name upon the sacred mountain with meager virtue, or to petition the Supreme Emperor with empty words—this is not what I have heard. Henceforth, all proposals for *feng* and *shan* rites shall be strictly prohibited.”

In the fourteenth year, officials submitted memorials firmly requesting the *feng* and *shan* rites. The Emperor ordered Niu Hong and others to devise the ceremonial protocols. Once completed, the Emperor reviewed them and said: “This is a matter of great significance. What virtue do I possess to warrant it? I shall merely make an eastern inspection and offer sacrifices to the sacred mountain.”

At the beginning of the *Zhenguan* era during Emperor Taizong of Tang’s reign, all ministers requested a *feng* and *shan* ceremony, but only Wei Zheng opposed it. In the sixth year, civil and military officials, citing the recent pacification of the Türks, the flourishing of virtue throughout the realm, and the repeated bumper harvests, memorialized for the *feng* rite at Mount Tai. Emperor Taizong addressed his courtiers: “Whenever I see the consensus, I find that *feng* and *shan* are deemed grand affairs, and many have urged me to perform them. Yet in my heart, if the realm is at peace, households are provided for, and the people are content, even without the *feng* and *shan* rites, one may still be compared to Yao and Shun in virtue. If the people are impoverished and the barbarians invade, then even if the *feng* and *shan* ceremonies are performed, how does one differ from Jie and Zhou? In the past, the First Emperor of Qin was a tyrannical ruler, while Emperor Wen of Han was a virtuous sovereign. By this reasoning, there is no need for *feng* and *shan*. Moreover, the rites state: ‘Sweep the ground and sacrifice to demonstrate utmost sincerity.’ Why must one ascend a distant mountain and seal three feet of earth?”

Though Emperor Taizong accepted Wei Zheng’s counsel, his vassals still submitted memorials urging the rites. In the fifteenth year, as he traveled east to Luoyang, a comet appeared, and the plans were abandoned.

In the second year of *Linde* during Emperor Gaozong’s reign, he visited the Eastern Peak. In the first month of the *Qianfeng* era, he performed the *feng* rite at Mount Tai and the *shan* rite at Sheshou, with Empress Wu serving as the secondary presenter and the Princess Dowager of Yue as the final presenter. Their curtains and canopies were adorned with brocade and embroidery. Many officials, gazing from afar, secretly mocked them. The jade tablets bore the prayer texts...

The reigning Son of Heaven of the Great Tang, Your Subject [Name], dares to make this solemn declaration to the August Heavenly Thearch:

When the Sui Dynasty’s fortune fell into peril, its allotted span exhausted and its path obstructed, the people suffered in misery, and the imperial throne collapsed. Our August Ancestor raised the Yellow Dragon Banner to deliver the black-haired masses, bestowed the Dark Jade Tablet to rescue those drowning in chaos. Taizong’s achievements surpassed the mending of heaven’s rift, pacifying the realm with but a wave of his hand; his deeds rivaled the severing of the giant turtle’s legs, draining the vast sea in a single breath. I, unworthy, have inherited this legacy and reverently received its accumulated blessings, enabling the Kunlun Mountains to rest their torches and the scorching seas to settle their waves. Though my merit derives from the flourishing virtue of my ancestors, it is in truth the descent of divine favor from the boundless heavens.

Today, I humbly report this accomplishment to the Eastern Peak and return its glory to the Supreme Mystery. May the great treasure flourish, the grand foundation endure forever, its fragrant essence congealing for the myriad peoples, its transformative power molding the eight directions. Lu Binwang’s *Memorial from the Elders of Qizhou Requesting to Participate in the Feng and Shan Rites*:

The round heavens array their constellations, the Purple Palace communicates with the North Star’s majesty; the Great Thearch’s eternal plan unfolds the dark mysteries of the eastern tour’s rites. Thus we know that when the Dao’s radiance and grace abound, jade is gathered upon the cloud terraces; when the legacy of ancestral merit is extended, gold is smeared upon the Sun-Viewing Peak.

I prostrate myself in awe of Your Majesty, who rides the Qian hexagram and grasps the pivot, inheriting the resplendent light of the Three Epochs; who ascends the throne and assumes the axis, responding to the accumulated sagacity of a thousand spirits. Thus the Five Elders emerged from the Yellow River, revealing the red script of the imperial era; the Four Spirits of the seas presented the cinnabar texts of the royal assembly. Auspicious omens appeared in the triple-ridged grain, and propitious signs suffused the five-colored clouds.

Thereupon, we collated the ancient texts of the *Zongzhang* and revived the precedents of the *Biyong*. The mist-like vapors bore the jade imperial carriage to Liangyin; the moon-like wheels concealed the golden cords within the tracks of Mount Tai. We, your subjects—mere straw dogs in substance, shadows beneath the mulberry and elm—are fortunate to dwell in an age where the mirror of Yao shines brightly, casting its lingering light upon the adjoining stones, and where the vast expanse of the Xuanyuan Chart’s destiny allows us to pursue the grand rites of the golden *cong*.

Yet the ancient states of Zou and Lu, along with the former customs of Linzi, have all been nurtured by the transformations of the Two Zhous and universally acclaim the singular change wrought by this feng rite. Our lands border the green fields, overlooking the plains where the unicorn was captured; our mountains open into emerald ridges, obliquely connecting to the peaks where the horse was discerned. How can we allow the descendants of the Jixia scholars, lingering in their furrows, to be momentarily excluded from the rites of participation, or the elders of the central plains to be the sole ones offering the ceremonies of completion?

Thus, turning toward the sun, we bare our crimson hearts and retreat three paces in reverence; gazing at the clouds, we unfurl our white silks and cry out to heaven, making our voices heard through the ninefold gates. If our humble sincerity is deemed worthy and we are permitted to partake in the great rites, then even the faintest breath of a dying dream may yet hear the echoes of immortals’ songs, and the fading souls of those nearing the coffin may leap with joy upon visiting the Sacred Peak.

In the twelfth year of *Kaiyuan* during the reign of Emperor Xuanzong, many officials spoke of performing the feng and shan rites, and the Grand Chancellor Zhang Yue repeatedly petitioned. Thereupon, an edict was issued to conduct the rites at Mount Tai in the thirteenth year. The emperor composed and personally inscribed the *Inscription Commemorating Mount Tai*.

Jade Tablet Prayer Text:
The reigning Son of Heaven of the Great Tang, Your Subject [Name], dares to make this solemn declaration to the August Heavenly Thearch: Heaven ordained the Li clan, its fortune rising with the virtue of earth. Gaozong and Taizong received the mandate and established the ultimate standard. Gaozong ascended to the center, and the Six Directions flourished; Zhongzong restored the dynasty, yet the succession remained unstable. The Thearch, in his benevolence, bestowed upon me loyal martial prowess, pacifying internal strife and supporting the sacred father. Reverently inheriting the great treasure for thirteen years, I have respected the will of heaven, and the Four Seas are at peace. Performing the feng rites at Mount Tai, I offer thanks for this accomplishment to heaven. May my descendants enjoy a hundred blessings, and may the black-haired people receive fortune.

*Preface and Inscription Commemorating Mount Tai by Emperor Xuanzong*:

Carved upon the mountain’s stone, polished into the cliff’s surface—details are recorded in the *Account of Remnant Traces*.

Having occupied the imperial throne for fourteen years, I reflect upon my lack of virtue, my ignorance of the supreme Dao, entrusting what should not be entrusted, and securing what should not be secured. In this, I know not whether I have offended the realms above and below. My heart is vast and turbulent, as if fording a great river.

By the grace of the Supreme Thearch, I have received the accumulated blessings of my ancestors. My ministers and officials, in concert, have cultivated the imperial standard. The Four Seas are united, the Five Constants flourish, the years have yielded double harvests, and the people enjoy great harmony. The myriad officials have gathered in counsel, proposing that I perform the feng and shan rites, declaring that no filial piety surpasses revering one’s father, and no ritual exceeds reporting to heaven. Heavenly signs have arrived, and the people’s hopes have accumulated. Though I have firmly declined, I could not persist in refusal.

Together with my ministers, I examined the canons of Yu, interpreted the institutions of Han, and marshaled the Six Armies, shaking the Nine Regions. Banners were arrayed in order, soldiers and horses remained silent. Solemn and majestic, reverent and harmonious, we ascended to the summit of the Sacred Peak.

The *Erya* states: “Mount Tai is the Eastern Peak.” The *Rites of Zhou* says: “The mountain that guards Yanzhou is truly the grandson of the Heavenly Thearch, the abode of the host of spirits. Its direction marks the beginning of the myriad things, hence it is called *Dai*. Its position presides over the Five Peaks, hence it is called *Zong*.”

In ancient times, when a new dynasty received the mandate, it was here that heaven and earth were opened, merits were offered, genealogies were recorded, and dynastic names were inscribed. Inheriting the legacy of the former kings, I now follow these rites, truly desiring to repay the favor of the Dark Heaven’s mandate and pray for blessings for the black-haired people. How dare I exalt myself above the ages or compare myself to the Nine August Ones?

Thus, I established the altar below the mountain to receive the assistance of the assembled lords in the sacrifices. I personally performed the feng and liao rites upon the mountain, hoping that a single offering might reach the spirits. This also embodies the principle of honoring heaven by ascending the heights and enlarging earth by broadening the base.

On the *gengyin* day of the mid-winter month, the rites were conducted at the Eastern Peak, with sacrifices offered to the August Heavenly Thearch and our August Ancestor as the associate. All the spirits in heaven descended without exception. On the following day, the shan rites were performed at Sheshou, with sacrifices offered to our sagely predecessors and the August Earthly Thearch. All the spirits on earth were duly honored.

On the *renchen* day, I received the feudal lords. The dukes and princes advanced, saying: “The Son of Heaven has received the heavenly signs and accepted great fortune.” The assembled ministers bowed, touched their foreheads to the ground, and proclaimed “Long live the Emperor ten thousand years!” They celebrated with joy and reverence, presenting their sincerity and virtue. The great cosmos was measured, the constant norms were restored. The Three Dukes and the hundred officials have accomplished their tasks; the myriad things follow their courses, and the people prosper. The regional governors and assembled ministers have fulfilled their duties.

The brothers, one and all, are deeply bound in filial piety and fraternal love, extending their virtue to the myriad states—how splendid is this! Our Confucians have established rites, our historians have composed music; heaven and earth are harmonized and compliant—how splendid is this! The Man, Yi, Rong, and Di tribes, translating their tongues repeatedly, come to offer tribute; through the transformative power of successive sages, what need have I to admire them? The five numinous creatures and the hundred treasures arrive day by day and gather month by month; in this era of grand flourishing, what cause have I for doubt?

From this day forward, those in office must not grow arrogant. Unify the royal standards, standardize the laws, abolish outdated regulations, and amend deficiencies in governance. Preserve simplicity, discard complexity and harshness, and strive to establish the ultimate human norm—only then will the celestial principles be manifest in every household.

Alas! Heaven gives birth to the multitudes, and the ruler is their timely guide, able to benefit all under heaven with great blessings—thus serving Heaven is made clear. Earth, thick and nurturing, bears all things, and the ruler is their timely assistant, able to enrich the lives of the people—thus serving Earth is made evident. When Heaven and Earth are thus illuminated, the spirits and gods become manifest.

Our cultured ancestor and my late father, their refined essences dwelling in Heaven, must surely say: “How admirable is this young grandson of ours, who can offer sacrifices to the Supreme August Lord with timely and fragrant reverence!” And they will grandly proclaim: “May our cultured and martial descendant receive a renewed mandate, inheriting our former legacy, forever preserving the royal blessings, and may his descendants perpetuate them.”

This humble one dares to respond to and extol the August Lord’s gracious mandate. Together with all officials, I shall pacify the multitudes, striving to surpass past achievements and avert future calamities. If even a single man remains unprosperous, the fault lies with the myriad regions—I, with a single heart, shall see this through to the end. May the Supreme Heaven know my resolve.

I cherish the practice of three virtues: compassion, frugality, and humility. Compassion is to tread the path of unforced auspiciousness; frugality is to honor the teachings for posterity. Those who are self-satisfied invite loss from others, while those who are self-effacing gain from Heaven. If this is so, then the laws at the borders will be easily followed, the foundations and structures easily maintained. Carve these words upon stone and inscribe them upon metal, so that later generations may hear the words and perceive the heart, observe the branches and know the root.

**Inscription:**
Heaven gives birth to humanity and establishes the ruler to govern.
The ruler receives the mandate and serves as the Son of Heaven.
The departed do not linger; the living come without end.
Those whose virtue is frozen perish; those whose Dao is lofty rise.
Resplendent was our August Ancestor, radiant was the Great Ancestor.
They reformed the governance of Sui and encompassed the myriad states.
They extended the realm to the limits of Heaven, opened frontiers to the ends of Earth.
In martial prowess, all was brought to order; in civil virtue, the times were harmonized.
The High Ancestor studied antiquity, his virtue reaching far and wide.
□ The vast Nine Yi were subdued at a single stroke.
Rites were perfected in the Feng and Shan sacrifices; achievements equaled those of Yu and Shun.
Majestic was Mount Tai, where our mysterious spirits flourished.
The Central Ancestor continued the mandate, renewing the old state.
The Illustrious Ancestor succeeded in brilliance, and all under Heaven returned to benevolence.
Governing with reverent self-restraint, his rule was pure and transformative.
The rites of completion were bequeathed to posterity.

In this distant age, this humble one builds upon the foundations of five sages.
Not boasting of achievements, not priding in virtue,
I reverently follow the ancestral canon, grandly inheriting the eternal mandate.
Utmost sincerity moves Heaven, bringing fortune to the myriad people.

Of old, seventy-two rulers performed the Feng sacrifice on Mount Tai.
Some performed the Shan sacrifice at Sheshou, others at Yunyun.
Though their nearness is unseen, their names may still be heard.
Reverently following the cultured ancestors, their illustrious merits shine forth.
The fangshi’s empty deceptions, the Confucians’ pedantry—
later rulers sought immortality, falsely claiming divine jade tallies.
Qin suffered wind and rain; Han sullied its records.
Virtue unaligned with Heaven brought only disgrace.
The Dao lies in observing governance; fame does not follow desire.
Carve these words upon the sheer cliffs, proclaiming them to the assembled peaks.

In the eighth year of *Taiping Xingguo* of Emperor Taizong of the Song, over a thousand elders from Mount Tai journeyed to the capital to request the Eastern Feng sacrifice. The Emperor modestly declined. Ministers from within and without presented odes and repeatedly extolled his merits, petitioning for the Feng and Shan sacrifices in numbers beyond count. In the first year of *Yongxi*, officials submitted three memorials, and an edict was issued to perform the rites at Mount Tai in the eleventh month of that year. However, due to the fires at the *Qianyuan Wenming* and *Erdian* halls, the edict was rescinded, and the Feng and Shan sacrifices were postponed.

In the first year of *Dazhong Xiangfu* of Emperor Zhenzong, Lü Liang and 1,277 elders from Yanzhou presented themselves at the capital to request the Feng sacrifice. The Emperor said: “This is a great matter; it cannot be lightly discussed.” Shao Hua, the prefect of the state, along with his subordinates and the *jinshi* Kong Wei of Yanzhou, prostrated themselves at the palace gates to petition. Prime Minister Wang Dan led the civil and military officials, generals, local administrators, tribal leaders, monks, Daoists, and elders—24,070 in total—to the Eastern Upper Gate to request the Feng sacrifice on Mount Tai. The imperial decree refused. After four further memorials, an edict was finally issued to perform the rites at Mount Tai in the tenth month of that year.

Previously, Palace Censor Zhao Xiang had petitioned for the Feng sacrifice, but the Emperor clasped his hands and remained silent. Wang Dan and others said: “The Feng and Shan sacrifices have long been neglected. Without a sagely age of peace, how could they be revived?” Earlier, Wang Qinruo had slandered Kou Zhun for the treaty concluded under duress, leaving the Emperor discontented. One day, the Emperor asked Qinruo: “What should be done now?” Qinruo, sensing the Emperor’s weariness of war, falsely replied: “If Your Majesty were to take You and Ji with military force, this disgrace could be erased.” The Emperor instructed him to consider alternatives. Qinruo then proposed the Feng sacrifice to pacify and awe the realm, impressing the barbarians. The Emperor asked: “Would Wang Dan object?” Qinruo conveyed the Emperor’s intent to Dan, who reluctantly acquiesced.

On the *xinmao* day of the tenth month, the Emperor departed from the capital. On the *dingwei* day, he arrived at Fenggao. On the *gengxu* day, he ascended the mountain by palanquin. On the *xinhai* day, he sacrificed to the Supreme August Lord at the altar on the summit, where the Minister of the Secretariat read the jade tablet inscription. That same day, he returned to the Fenggao Palace. Five-colored clouds appeared in the sky. All the rare birds and exotic beasts presented from the four directions since the previous reigns were released at the foot of the mountain.

On the *guichou* day, the Emperor ascended the *Chaoyin* altar, proclaimed a general amnesty, and renamed Qianfeng County as Fengfu, and the summit of Mount Tai as Taiping Peak.

**Jade Tablet Prayer:**
The heir of the Song, the Son of Heaven, [Name] humbly proclaims to the Supreme August Lord: As the era unfolds in great harmony, the Song has received the mandate. The Great Ancestor laid the foundation, achieving order and stability...

Emperor Taizong inherited the celestial chart, his reign flourishing in ever-greater splendor. Profoundly mindful of the common people, he grandly succeeded the sagely lineage. Twelve years have passed, and from all four directions, omens have converged—black millet of exceptional rarity, the six treasuries manifesting their signs. The seasons harmonize, the years yield abundance, and all living beings attain their fulfillment. Thus, the great feng sacrifice was convened to reverently offer this solemn observance. He personally scaled the perilous heights of the sacred peak to present himself before the Supreme August Heaven. Praying for blessings to extend below, he honored the spirits and revered the ancestors. Heavenly favor knows no end, and numinous blessings truly follow, ensuring the prosperity of the myriad chariots and seeking the preservation of pure bestowals.

**Preface and Inscription by Emperor Zhenzong:**
I have heard that to expand virtue and teaching across the realm, to pacify all beings and achieve supreme peace—this is the great enterprise of the state. To examine the finest rituals and offer utmost sincerity, to ascend the sacred peak and respond to pure bestowals—this is the solemn observance of the kingly way. From the time of knotted cords, all is vast and unknowable; in the records of bamboo and silk, all is clear and attainable. None have failed to inaugurate blessings for posterity, accumulating and storing felicity, perpetuating their auspiciousness, and seeking to bestow upon their kind. Thus, they could sacrifice within to the Supreme Lord and extend their audience to the feudal lords, tracing the distant footsteps of the Eight and Nine, fulfilling the joyous hearts of the millions. Therefore, King Wu exterminated the tyrant and unified the great mandate, and King Cheng thereby made his eastern tour; Emperor Gao pacified the three rebels and inaugurated Heaven’s favor, and Emperor Wu thereby performed the feng sacrifice.

In the past, the Five Dynasties declined, and the realm fractured. The nine domains cried out, gazing at their shadows in search of survival; the teeming millions sighed, calling to Heaven in supplication. Without divine martial prowess, how could the many difficulties be quelled? Without civil enlightenment, how could the ultimate governance be restored? Reverently considering the Great Ancestor, who inaugurated the mandate and established the ultimate standard—heroic and martial, sagely and civil, his divine virtue and mysterious merit: the Filial Emperor accumulated goodness from the beginning, receiving boundless mandate. Tested by hardship, he ultimately ascended to the imperial throne. His majestic spirit shook the distant lands, his mysterious grace flowed boundlessly. None turned away unanswered; all who came received response. He delivered the people from suffering, elevating all things to a springtime terrace. Enabling the myriad states to emulate the great prosperity of Tang, proclaiming the nine virtues like King Wen, he inaugurated the mandate before and bequeathed prosperity after.

Emperor Taizong, of utmost benevolence and in accord with the Dao, possessed divine merit, sagely virtue, civil and martial brilliance. The August Emperor of Broad Filial Piety grandly continued the foundation. The radiance of his reign was like the rising sun, illuminating all within the four seas; like the vastness of Heaven, covering all living beings. Human culture was perfected, and the divine way established its teaching. He honored the worthy and esteemed virtue, following the martial path and lightening punishments. The sounds of metal and stone summoned the bright spirits; the rites of jade and silk brought the distant barbarians into harmony. The written scripts and axle tracks were unified; the rituals and ceremonies lacked nothing. The Supreme August One bestowed his gaze, like the warm reverence of Yu Shun; the myriad people knew no change, like the transformations of Tang Yao. The flourishing of his reign surpassed all antiquity.

The earlier edicts on the feng sacrifice had already been issued as a completed mandate. To hesitate in its execution would be the greatest humility. Yet I, this unworthy one, have been entrusted to guard the grand enterprise. My virtue is unequal, my aspirations unclear. Unable to sustain its flourishing, I know not how to achieve success. Now, as the yang spring reaches its season, the numinous texts bestow their blessings. Thus, the elders from beyond the Yellow River and the scholars of Zou and Lu urged me with divine grace and entreated me to perform the feng and shan sacrifices. They traveled a thousand *li* without hesitation to reach the imperial court. I was alarmed and could not bear it; their resolve was firm and unyielding.

Soon after, the princes, regional governors, ministers, and generals submitted five petitions for the feng sacrifice, with over ten thousand voices in agreement. They believed that the celestial mandate was renewed, and precious omens amassed—what Heaven wills cannot be refused, and the people’s hearts cannot be rejected. To defy Heaven’s will, how could one be said to follow the Dao? To suppress the people’s hearts, how could one be said to comply with them? Thus, it is fitting to ascend the great mound and complete the grand rite.

Though admonitions were earnest and entreaties insistent, I reflected deeply: Heaven and Earth bestow their protection, the ancestral altars store their blessings. Auspicious vapors fill the ritual grounds, and a modest peace is achieved. The borders are tranquil, weapons stilled; wind and rain are timely, harvests abundant. Without alarm or terror, the people are numerous and prosperous—all is the gift of Heaven, not my own merit. Though I proclaim success and inscribe my virtuous title, my frozen virtue is unworthy. Yet to record the celestial charts and respond to extraordinary omens is not something I, in my humility, dare refuse.

At the Sun-Viewing Peak of the Celestial Grandson and the Immortal Halls of Liangfu, the ancestor of the Five Peaks and the origin of all things, the rites of ascending the central altar and offering the beacon fire remain as of old. Following the intentions of the ancestors and adhering to the established model, reverently serving the circular altar and solemnly accompanying the ancestral spirits with filial piety—thus, I ordered the ministers to arrange the proceedings and commanded the scholars to study and practice. Those responsible for the sacrifices were all provided for; those attending to my person were not compelled to ascend. Therefore, the jade, silk, sacrificial animals—these I diligently prepared; the feathered regalia and imperial attire—these I carefully selected. With sincere intent and unwavering resolve, rising early and retiring late in vigilance, my utmost sincerity and clarity penetrated all, and manifest responses repeatedly appeared.

At Heaven’s bestowed grace, I personally ascended the cloud-borne chariot to perform the ritual and offer the celestial tally. How dare I rest in governing the realm? In the early days of late autumn, I reverently offered at the ancestral temple, announcing the paired sacrifices. On an auspicious day in the first month of winter, I devoutly ascended Mount Tai, presenting myself in reverence. Placing the precious records at the left of the throne and elevating the ancestors to join the offerings—this is the correctness of ritual and the beginning of filial piety. Then, I performed the shan sacrifice at She Shou, its forms all proper.

At that time, the heavenly spirits all descended, and the earthly deities all ascended. The sacrificial smoke and flames were as expected, the offerings as if visible. Though the presentation was brief, its brilliance was in luminous virtue; the response was truly in utmost sincerity. Moreover, I poured libations following the precedents of Deng Palace, observed the accounts at Ganquan, and received the jade pendants of the feudal lords and the jade tablets of the five ranks. Thus, I promoted benevolence, encouraged good governance, examined institutions, and gathered folk songs. The brilliance of culture and learning thereby extolled the grand achievements of the Two Saints; the joyous bestowals of blessings thereby comforted the longing hearts of the people.

Moreover, these are the pure roots of the Two Modes, the lingering blessings of the Seven Temples, the flourishing beauty of the state and family, and the great felicity of the multitudes—how could one so solitary and dull as I bring them about? Only by rising early and retiring late in reverent awe, by remaining vigilant day and night, by never presuming self-satisfaction nor indulging in ease, by comforting and pacifying the myriad states, and by nurturing and fostering all orders of society, may I respond to the august mandate of the vast heavens. I shall inscribe this record upon the mountain’s slopes to perpetuate it for all ages.

**Inscription:**
The peaks of Mount Tai stand tall in the east,
Where all things begin, and dark omens unfold their patterns.
Since the time we received the mandate,
We have returned to honor the azure vault.
Here, seventy-six kings offered fán and chái.
Yet I, humble and unworthy, reverently succeed this grand enterprise.
With ceaseless diligence and trembling awe,
Though blessed, I dare not rest.
The primal talisman shines forth its bestowals,
And lingering blessings flow afar.
Pressed by the sentiments of all,
I complete this grand rite in due form.
The former kings’ illustrious deeds
Were proclaimed in this very month.
I, so young and unrefined,
Bear a name undeserved.
Ever mindful of their protective grace,
I revere the radiant spirits.
Thus, I exalt the solemn sacrifices
To convey my utmost sincerity.
Auspicious signs gather in abundance,
And the bells and chimes hasten forth.
Where rites are unmarred,
The spirits indeed draw near.
Sealing the stone with layered earth,
Carving words upon the mountain’s charm,
May the people forever prosper,
And boundless blessings ever descend.

---

**Sun Shou’s Memorial:**
After Your Majesty has just completed the eastern feng sacrifice, you now contemplate a western journey—this does not align with the ancient kings’ cautious practice of divining expeditions only once every five years. At present, the state’s construction projects have continued unceasing for years, while floods and droughts have wrought havoc, and famine prevails. To further burden the people with sacrifices to the spirits—will they truly accept such offerings?

He further stated: Today’s treacherous ministers, citing the late emperor’s past feng and shan sacrifices, urge Your Majesty to continue his legacy. Yet when the late emperor sought to pacify Youzhou and Shuofang and reclaim lands from Jiqian, these ministers never offered a single strategy. Instead, they humbled themselves with lavish gifts to sue for peace with the Khitan, debased the state’s dignity, and appeased Baoji with titles—treating the principle of “a ruler’s disgrace demands a minister’s death” as empty words, while deceiving their superiors became their duty. That Your Majesty would turn the hard-won achievements of the ancestral founders into tools for these ministers’ self-serving gains—this is why I sigh in deep sorrow and regret.

In the fourth year of Zhenghe during the reign of Emperor Huizong, the prefectures of Yanzhou, Zou, Pu, Kai, Dezhou, Xing, Ren, and Yingchang, along with the commanderies of Zhengzhou, Guangji, and Yongxing, reported that local elders wished to petition the court for the emperor’s feng sacrifice. An edict declined the request. At the time, Cai Jing held power and sought to stage a feng and shan ceremony to glorify the era of peace. He had already prepared golden cords, jade seals, and all necessary ritual implements, even constructing four thousand boats. Rain gear alone numbered in the hundreds of thousands, and other provisions matched this scale—yet the ceremony was never carried out.

During the Yongle reign of the illustrious Ming, officials of the Court of Imperial Sacrifices presented an ode requesting the feng sacrifice on Mount Tai, but Emperor Chengzu firmly rejected it.

**Wang Zhi, “On the Feng and Shan Odes and Medicinal Remedies”**

The *Ode on Declining the Feng and Shan Sacrifices* was composed by Hu Guang, the late Minister of Rites and posthumously honored as Duke Wenmu, formerly a Hanlin Academician.

During the reign of Emperor Taizong, a minister of the Court of Imperial Sacrifices remarked: “In ancient times, when a sage ruled the world and his virtue and achievements reached their zenith, he would invariably perform the feng and shan rites to announce his success to the divine and leave a legacy for all ages. Now, as Your Majesty ascends the throne, the heavens are clear, the earth tranquil, the people and all things flourish, and your sagely virtue and divine merit surpass all predecessors. Yet why have you not discussed the feng and shan sacrifices to emulate the greatness of past emperors?” He requested that the Ministry of Rites proceed with the rites.

The emperor replied: “The feng and shan sacrifices are not ancient practices. The renown of emperors in later ages rests on virtue, not on such ceremonies.” He refused the request.

When Hu Guang learned of this, he said: “This is a matter of utmost virtue, yet some ministers secretly support it.” Fearing the emperor might be swayed, he composed this poem based on the argument that the feng and shan sacrifices were not ancient traditions, along with the reasons why later wise rulers and worthy ministers rejected them.

The emperor, whose sagely nature was lofty and enlightened, possessed true insight sufficient to dispel age-old errors and serve as a bright mirror for all kings. Upon reading the poem, he exclaimed with delight: “This reflects my own thoughts!” Thus, those who advocated for the rites were silenced, and the feng and shan sacrifices were ultimately abandoned.

In honoring one’s ruler, a true gentleman strives to guide him toward the right path—such was Hu Guang. Those like the First Emperor of Qin and Emperor Wu of Han, who indulged their extravagant ambitions and disturbed both heaven and earth, were met with divine disdain. To advocate for such rites is precisely why a state must distance itself from flatterers—and this was Hu Guang’s deepest concern.

---

**Discourses on the Feng and Shan Sacrifices by Confucian Ministers Through the Ages**

Sima Biao of the Han dynasty said: “Since the time of the Supreme Sovereign, seventy-two dynasties have performed the feng sacrifice on Mount Tai. When a new dynasty arose, the sacrifice was altered to mark the beginning of a new era, making it clear that the rite was not inherited. Successive kings who conducted imperial tours merely repaired the altars to offer sacrifices.

The First Emperor of Qin and Emperor Wu of Han performed the feng sacrifice on Mount Tai, but their motives stemmed from a fondness for immortality and belief in the words of alchemists. Their creation of stone seals and the act of sealing them were misguided. The Way of Heaven values sincerity and simplicity, avoiding waste. Thus, calves were used as offerings, and vessels were made of clay and gourds. There is no need for sealing rituals or the laborious carving of stone.

Xia Shaokang and King Xuan of Zhou, who restored their fallen dynasties, did not alter the feng sacrifices. Emperor Guangwu wished to follow Emperor Wu’s precedent, but Liang Song vehemently opposed it, arguing that only a new sacrifice would align with Heaven’s will. After performing the rite, no blessings followed, and Liang Song was later executed. Though his death was due to his own crimes, it was also a consequence of falsely attributing blame to the gods.

The reason emperors achieve great renown in later ages lies in their virtue extended to the people—not in the performance of the feng sacrifice.”

Cui Ling’en said: “Before the Zhou dynasty, those who performed the feng sacrifice built earthen altars. It was only with the First Emperor of Qin and Emperor Wu of Han that stone seals were first used.”

Yuan Hong said: “One who receives the throne through abdication must possess supreme virtue in the world, while one who receives the mandate through conquest must achieve great merit for all things. Thus, when a king first establishes his rule, he performs the feng and shan sacrifices to announce his success to the divine.”

The East is where the myriad things begin; mountains and peaks are where the numinous qi resides. Thus, to seek the root of things, one must go to their origin; to obtain what is universally shared, one must go to where it dwells. To honor the altar and grounds is called *feng*; to declare the rise of a new dynasty is called *shan*. Therefore, the *feng* and *shan* sacrifices are the grand rites by which a king inaugurates his enterprise.

Without virtue that suffuses all, one may not presume to discuss this matter; without merit that broadly delivers, one may not approximate this ritual. It occurs but once in ages, its Way supremely exalted. From the Yellow Emperor, Yao, and Shun down to the Three Dynasties, each performed the *feng* and *shan* but once—none in the interim ever undertook its rites. Even succeeding rulers who possessed merit and virtue merely restored former practices, augmenting the policies of their predecessors; they could not aspire to the level of those who founded states and transformed all things.

The divine Way is truly one, its function unburdensome; Heaven and Earth are simple and unadorned, their rites prizing plainness. Thus, white cogon grass is used for offerings, valuing its purity and simplicity; vessels are made of earthenware and gourds, for their ease of use. The rites of *feng* and *shan* should indeed be simple and unelaborate. As for stone caskets and jade tablets, these are not in accord with the nature of Heaven and Earth.

Xu Maolian of Liang said: “When Shun offered sacrifice on Mount Tai, it was part of his imperial tour.” Yet Zheng Xuan, citing the *Xiaojing Gouming Decade*, states: “He performed the *feng* on Mount Tai, reviewing merits and offering flame; he performed the *shan* on Liangfu, inscribing stone to record his reign.” This is the distorted claim of apocrypha. Among the seventy-two rulers before Suiren, the age was simple and the people pure—how could they have used gold and jade for seals? They governed by knotted cords—how could they have carved inscriptions to proclaim their achievements? Such fabrications are egregious.

If a ruler is sagely, he has no need of *feng* and *shan*; if he is mediocre, he ought not perform them. The First Emperor of Qin once performed the *feng* on Mount Tai, and Sun Hao once performed the *feng* on Mount Guoshan. Both were cases of rulers seeking fame above and ministers flattering their wills below—these were not acts of supreme virtue and should not be taken as models.

Hu Zhitang of Song said: “Apocrypha trace their origins to the Five Classics but have lost their way, particularly in matters of ghosts and spirits, the obscure and the manifest. Spirits bring no calamity; when the Dipper’s stars descend to the palace hall, the Five Blessings are bestowed: long life, wealth, health, love of virtue, and a natural end. We bow our heads in reverence to the Most Wondrous and True Dao, the Radiant and Ever-Shining Heavenly Venerable. The assembly wholeheartedly prostrates before the Nine Heavens’ Golden Gate, the Illuminated Dao and Penetrating Virtue Great Immortal of Lingji Palace, the Manifest and Universally Delivering Pure Subtlety Cavernous Mystery, the Profoundly Responsive and Compassionate Protector of the State and Shelter of the People, the Augustly Blessed and Vastly Potent Perfected Lord. The Nine Heavens’ Jade Gate, the Propagating and Upholding Teaching Supreme Immortal, the Illustrious and Efficacious, the Lofty and Tranquil, the Profound and Serene, the Wondrously Responsive and Benevolent, the Assisting State and Aiding People, the Abundantly Blessed and Vastly Potent Perfected Lord. The divine immortals and all spirit officials within the palace reverently kindle the Seven Luminaries’ divine lamps, arraying the celestial bodies to manifest the image of the sun.

We humbly acknowledge that the Dao resides in the beginning of the Great Ultimate, unseen in its concealment, manifest in its subtlety. The sun is the essence of all yang, accomplishing all without exception, perceiving all without obscurity—its wonder is unfathomable. What endures is its spirit; its majesty blazes forth, to be held in awe by all, like Zhao Dun. Radiant above, the people all look up to it, like the brilliance of the Duke of Zhou—how abundant was his virtue! Pure and white, his integrity shone like the moon. The sun’s chariot, solemn and complete, revolves in the celestial palace; the sun god’s majestic carriage graces the Hall of Universal Yang. Its rays extend for ten thousand miles, illuminating the three thousand worlds. Entering our gate, one harbors no doubt of the mortal realm; following our Way, one eradicates the ills of the dusty path.

We reflect with compassion on all beings, sunk in the mire of worldly dust, vainly chasing shadows in exhaustion, like hail and dew—fleeting and insubstantial—leaving only sighs for hidden faults. From this day forth, turn inward in reflection, and abide with the Dao in constant clarity. We pray that those who dwell in markets may prosper in trade and commerce; those who labor in the fields may peacefully till the soil and dig wells; the skilled may measure shadows with jade tablets; the diligent may burn the midnight oil; scholars may compose resplendent five-colored prose; and officials may extend their martial prowess far and wide. If all conduct is thus, how could the golden vessel of the state ever be flawed?

Zhang Junqing said: “Those who deem *feng* and *shan* not of antiquity include Wang Zhongyan; those who consider them unorthodox include Li Taibo; those who find them untrustworthy include Su Ziyou. The Six Classics contain no text on *feng* and *shan*, nor do the sagely kings record such affairs. Those who expounded on it—Guan Zhong traced its source, Sima Qian dredged its flow, Ji Zhong rode its waves, and Zhang Yue fanned its currents. Those who indulged in it—the First Emperor of Qin puffed its smoke, Emperor Yuan of Han sealed its embers, Emperor Yang of Sui kindled its fat, and Emperor Xuanzong of Tang ignited its flames. The *feng* and *shan* rites served only to indulge desires and display ostentation.

When measured against the clear texts of the Six Classics and the actual deeds of the sagely kings, the confusion of later ages only deepens. Take a single mountain: some say it lies in Dongshan, others in Mengyin. A single name: some say it is in Mouyin, others in Juping. A single site: some say it is in Juping, others in Boxian. The theories of Fu Qian, Jin Zhuo, and Ying Shao diverge. Some say Fuxi and Shennong performed the *shan* at Yunyun; others say the Three Sovereigns performed it at Yiyi. Some say Emperor Ku, Yao, and Shun performed the *shan* at Yunyun; others say the Five Emperors performed it at Tingting. Some say Yu performed the *shan* at Kuaiji and Tang at Yunyun; others say the Three Kings performed it at Liang. Even the accounts of Guan Zhong and Kong Yingda are inconsistent. With such a tangle of conflicting claims and no definitive evidence, did the ages of Yao, Shun, and the Three Dynasties truly witness such events? Can the seventy-two rulers be trusted? Even if such events occurred, how could the Six Classics and their transmitted texts fail to record them?”

Alas! How might Fan Zh

The aspiration to emulate the Yellow Emperor and commune with the immortals of Penglai finds its parallel in the *Records of the Grand Historian* as "the desire to follow the Yellow Emperor in reaching the divine immortals of Penglai." "Difficult to implement" in this context appears in the *Records* as "difficult to put into practice." The passage describing the solemn sacrifice to the Earth Deity at the northeastern base of Mount Tai is rendered in the *Records* as "performing the sacrifice to the Earth Deity at the northeastern foothills of Mount Tai, where the land rises like a reverent mound." The character in question is recorded in the *Records of the Grand Historian: Treatise on Pitch Pipes* as "white." The term "father" in the *Book of Han: Treatise on Sacrifices* appears as "civil." "Multitudes" in the *Book of Later Han: Treatise on Sacrifices* is written as "to avoid." The reign of thirty-three years and sixty-one years of age is noted in the *Book of Later Han* as "thirty-two years on the throne at the age of sixty-two."

*Annals of the Sacred Mount, Volume Seven: The Rites of Prospect*
The Master inquired: "What is meant by *wang* (prospect)?"

The appellation of the sacrifices conducted by emperors and kings. Why are these recorded? To establish the order of sacrifices and exalt ritual propriety. Of the great affairs of the state, none surpasses sacrifice; of the great affairs of sacrifice, none surpasses those to Heaven and Earth and the Five Peaks. Mount Tai, as the foremost of the peaks, is always the first to receive the imperial hunt and prospect. How could this not be revered and esteemed?

Yet in the *Book of Yu*, the prospect sacrifice is called *zhi* (ordered); in the *Rites of Zhou*, the Four Prospects are likewise called *zhi*, ranked alongside the Three Dukes—distinct from the sacrifices to Heaven and Earth, lest one transgress ritual to curry favor. Descending to later ages, extravagance and deceit proliferated: the Tang styled itself "king," the Song styled itself "emperor," presenting jade tallies and memorials, inscribing jade tablets and ascending cloud terraces, all in hopes of flattering the gods to secure their blessings. Yet do they not reflect that the gods are luminous, upright, and impartial? Can such flattery truly win their favor?

Our August Emperor, in reverting to antiquity to establish the sacrificial canon, first rectified the names, designating the deity as "Spirit of the Eastern Peak, Mount Tai." For the suburban altar, the announcement of rites, and the prayers of thanksgiving, all were ordered with ritual propriety, in accord with the imperial classics and royal statutes. The enlightened successors upheld this luminously, forming a model for ten thousand generations. As for the dukes, ministers, and all officials involved in the sacrifices—all acting for the state and the people—their deeds are duly appended here.

In the second month of the year, the sage-kings of Tang and Yu made their eastern inspection tour, arriving at Mount Tai. They kindled the fire and performed the prospect sacrifice to the mountains and rivers, as recorded in the *Canon of Shun*. The Grand Master of Ceremonies of Zhou offered blood sacrifices to the Five Peaks and performed the Four Prospects, ranking the Five Peaks alongside the Three Dukes, as seen in the *Rites of Zhou*. The Duke of Zhou was enfeoffed at Qufu, and his son, Bo Qin, became the Marquis of Lu, thereby assuming the rites of Mount Tai, as recorded in the *Annals of Lu*. In the summer of the thirty-first year of Duke Xi of Lu, the fourth divination for the suburban sacrifice was unfavorable, so the sacrificial animal was released, yet the Three Prospects were still performed, as noted in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*. The *Gongyang Commentary* asks: What are the Three Prospects? They are the prospect sacrifices—sacrifices to Mount Tai, the Yellow River, and the sea.

In the third year of Duke Xuan of Lu, in the spring of the royal first month, the ox for the suburban sacrifice injured its mouth. The divination was repeated, the ox died, and the suburban sacrifice was abandoned, yet the Three Prospects were still performed, as recorded in the same text. When the Ji clan made a private sacrifice to Mount Tai, Confucius asked Ran You: "Could you not have prevented this?" Ran You replied: "I could not." Confucius exclaimed: "Alas! Can it be that Mount Tai is less discerning than Lin Fang?"

In the third year after ascending the throne, the First Emperor of Qin journeyed east to the sea, sacrificing to the famous mountains, great rivers, and the Eight Spirits. The second of the Eight Spirits was the Earth Master, to whom sacrifices were offered at Mount Tai and Liangfu. The origins of the Eight Spirits’ sacrifices are unknown; they may have existed since the time of Zhou’s ancestors, and the First Emperor continued them. When he later performed the feng and shan sacrifices, he also sacrificed to Mount Tai.

During the reign of Emperor Gaozu of Han, until the time of Emperor Wu, Mount Tai lay within the territory of the King of Jibei, who personally conducted its sacrifices. In the first year of the *Yuanshou* era, the king, knowing the Son of Heaven was about to perform the feng and shan sacrifices, submitted Mount Tai to the throne. Thereafter, the sacrifices were officially administered by the emperor. In the fifth year of the *Yuanfeng* era, the emperor arrived at Fenghao, performed the feng sacrifice, and sacrificed to Mount Tai. Thereafter, the feng sacrifice was performed every five years, totaling five such sacrifices.

In the first year of the *Shenjue* era of Emperor Xuan, an edict ordered the Minister of Ceremonies to establish the regular sacrificial rites for the Five Peaks, all to be conducted by envoys bearing the imperial tally. Only Mount Tai and the Yellow River received five sacrifices annually; the others received three. In the first year of the *Shenping* era of Emperor Guangwu, the northern suburban sacrifice to the Five Peaks was established. In the thirtieth year of the *Jianwu* era, in the third month, the emperor declined the proposal for feng and shan sacrifices, visited Lu, and retired to sacrifice to Mount Tai. In the thirty-second year, in the second month, the emperor performed the feng and shan sacrifices, offering to Heaven at the southern foot of Mount Tai. He also presented a single sacrificial animal at the regular sacrificial site of Mount Tai to announce the rites.

In the second year of the *Yuanhe* era of Emperor Zhang, in the second month, he visited and sacrificed to Mount Tai. The *Comprehensive Meaning of Customs* states: At the Temple of Mount Tai, on the day of *he dong* in the tenth month, the day of *hu dong* in the twelfth month, and the day of *jie dong* in the first month, the prefect always attends the sacrifices.

In the third year of the *Huangchu* era of Emperor Wen of Wei, sacrifices were offered to the Five Peaks. In the eighth year of the *Xianhe* era of Emperor Cheng of Jin, the northern suburban sacrifice was established, with the Five Peaks included in the rites. During the Liang dynasty, it was decreed that commanderies and states possessing the Five Peaks should appoint three sacrificial officials to perform rites in the first and eleventh months.

Emperor Jingmu of Northern Wei established temples for the Five Peaks north of the Sanggan River, dispatching sacrificial officials annually to each peak and garrison to offer prayers. Emperor Xiaowen’s prayer to Mount Tai read: "The creative transformations intermingle, giving rise to the Two Modes. Once the dark and yellow were separated, mountains and rivers emerged. The Four Streams contain the numinous; the Five Peaks embrace the earth spirits. Together they encompass the directions and images, receiving and dispensing the gaze of the sun and moon. Mount Tai stands lofty and majestic; Liangfu coils and towers. Green hills teem with life; spring’s roots surge and flourish. They give birth to the myriad kinds, inaugurate the radiance of all things. Above, they unfold divine work; below, they harmonize the numinous order. In accord with the patterns of transformation, the Four Seasons overflow. The hundred kings carved their achievements here; none failed to dwell in this sanctuary."

During the *Wude* and *Zhenguan* eras of the Tang, on the days of the Five Suburban Sacrifices to welcome the qi, the Five Peaks were sacrificed to. Additionally, the Eastern Peak was sacrificed to in Yanzhou. In the early *Kaiyuan* era of Emperor Xuanzong, the Five Suburban Sacrifices to welcome the qi were conducted as before. The prayer text is recorded below.

In the thirteenth year of the *Kaiyuan* era, the spirit of Mount Tai was enfeoffed as the King of Heavenly Unity, with ritual rank elevated one degree above the Three Dukes. At that time, the Daoist priest Sima Chengzhen of Mount Tiantai remarked: "The present sacrifices to the Five Peaks are for the spirits of the mountains and forests. Each of the Five Peaks has a grotto-palace, where Upper Clarity Perfected Ones descend to assume their offices. They govern the mountains and rivers, wind and rain, yin and yang, and the sequences of qi. Crowned and robed, attended by divine immortals, all have names and numbers. I request that separate fasting altars be established for them." Emperor Xuanzong, intrigued by his proposal, ordered that Perfected Lord shrines be established at each of the Five Peaks. Thus, Daoist scriptures now refer to the Perfected Lord of the Eastern Peak as the Great Numinous Azure Prime Controller of Destiny.

In the early *Kaiyuan* era, the regular sacrificial prayer was established, reading: "The succeeding Son of Heaven, [Name], respectfully dispatches [Official Title and Name]: Daring to solemnly announce to the Eastern Peak, Mount Tai: The spirit assists in nurturing the ten thousand kinds, securing one region. As spring begins, with jade and silk, pure offerings, music, and abundant tribute, we humbly present this morning offering to the Eastern Peak, Mount Tai."

May the spirits partake in the offerings.

In the first, seventh, and eighth years of the *Tianbao* era, officials were dispatched to sacrifice to the Five Peaks upon each year’s harvest. In the second year of *Zhenyuan* under Emperor Dezong, an edict was issued to the Minister of Rites Pei Yu and ten others, each to conduct sacrifices to the peaks within their respective regional commands.

At the beginning of the Song dynasty, sacrifices to the Eastern Peak, Mount Tai, were held in Yanzhou. In the first year of *Dazhong Xiangfu* under Emperor Zhenzong, following the Feng and Shan rites, an edict was issued to bestow upon the Heavenly-Aligned King of Mount Tai the additional title of Heavenly-Aligned Benevolent and Sagely King. In the fifth year, the title was further elevated to Heavenly-Aligned Benevolent and Sagely Emperor. The emperor commanded Academician Chao Chong to compose the *Stele Inscription of the Heavenly-Aligned Benevolent and Sagely Emperor*, which remains preserved in the mountain’s temple.

**Stele Inscription by Chao Chong**

Your servant has heard that mountains are formed by the accumulation of earth, embodying the boundless generosity of the great foundation. Elevated in name as the *Yue* (Peaks), they stand as the lofty mounds that secure the realm’s reverence. Among them, Mount Tai, rooted in primordial *qi* to achieve its singular formation, rivals the four seasons in primacy and serves as the eastern guardian of the Central Kingdom. Distinguished among the Five Directions, its position is supreme, akin to the heavens’ grandeur. Depicted in the Eight Trigrams, it stands as the marker of the *Zhen* (Thunder) region. Its profound depths connect to the celestial palaces, and its convergence of forces earns it the title of the abode of spirits.

In its majesty, it embodies the *Gen* (Mountain) trigram, holding dominion over the axis of *Kun* (Earth). Its rugged peaks and towering heights rise at dawn, with the Gate of Heaven marking the boundary of the lush azure expanse, and the Sun-Viewing Peak overlooking the radiant sun. Immortals, near and far, dwell in its tranquil stone chambers, while the golden caskets of the *Yongming* preserve the blessings of eternal life. It nurtures the hundred plants and fosters all living kinds, regulating the ebb and flow of thunder and rain, exhaling and inhaling wind and clouds. Thus, its sealing (*feng*) unites with the primordial tally, and its ascent (*shan*) humbles the world entire.

Nearby, it adjoins the ancestral altars of Liang; afar, it gazes upon the vastness of Qin. It commands the seas and the Yi River, girded by the Wei and Si Rivers. The people of Zou revere it, and the state of Lu venerates it as their guardian. It initiates the transformations that give life to all things, serving as the domain of longevity and uprightness.

From ancient sage-kings to the illustrious emperors of old, those who received the celestial mandate, unfolded the imperial charts, studied the sacred texts, announced their achievements, and rendered great offerings—all inscribed their names and auspicious omens upon its stones. From the era of Wu Huai to the reign of Emperor Ming of Tang, records of their ascents and sacrifices fill the annals, none absent from this sacred peak.

In the thirteenth year of *Kaiyuan*, the deity was first enfeoffed as the Heavenly-Aligned King, with rites elevated above those of the Three Dukes. Through the five dynasties that followed, its renown faded into obscurity. Yet with the rise of our august dynasty, propitious fortune flourished, fulfilling the people’s hopes and the era’s mandate for renewal. Emperor Taizu, wielding his heroic might, subdued the cosmos, toiling through four campaigns to quell the tumult and unify the realm. Emperor Taizong inherited and exalted this grand legacy, establishing the imperial throne, harmonizing virtue and power, and uniting heaven and humanity, thereby pacifying the lands beyond the seas.

Yet the arduous task of founding the dynasty, the accumulation of strategic foresight, and the pressing demands of governance left little respite to pursue loftier endeavors. The weight of ancestral bequests and the succession of generations made clear the destined path. Then came the August Emperor, *Chongwen Guangwu Gantian Zundao Yingde Qinming Shangsheng Renxiao Huangdi*—Your Majesty—who inherited the stabilized foundation, ushering in an era of profound peace. Displaying benevolence to nurture all things and filial piety to honor the ancestors, you have disseminated prosperity, perfected governance, pacified the frontier, and soothed distant lands. With the realm’s fortunes flourishing and the celestial will manifest in purity, you have ascended to greater heights, fostering abundance and well-being. Upholding the reverence due to the ancestral temples and contemplating the arts of emperors and kings, you guide the realm with a long rein, the grand Dao lying smooth and unobstructed before you. Thus, the celestial thoroughfares yield bountiful offerings, and the teachings of virtue spread far and wide.

The sun and stars are aligned in their courses, and the era of Great Peace and Great Tranquility has united all under the shared script and cart. With the emperor governing effortlessly above and the people joyfully tilling the earth below, you have sought counsel from the wise and talented, consulted the reclusive and profound, deliberated on classical rites, and embraced the arts and letters. Extending the lineage of a thousand years, it is fitting to illuminate the rites of *feng* and *shan*, and at the zenith of all things, to solemnly announce these deeds to the divine.

Yet ever modest, you have amplified your glory; embodying the ceaseless vigor of the *Qian* trigram, you conceal your brilliance and ponder the Dao in profound stillness. Suddenly, celestial blessings stirred, and the Supreme Deity looked upon you with favor. Auspicious signs arrived in abundance, and the divine will made its choice, gathering the august mandate to awaken the ministers and the people. The golden slips and jade characters of the sacred texts rivaled the treasures of the River Chart and Luo Writ. Receiving these secret revelations, you enacted their blessings, bestowing prosperity upon all under heaven. You established a new reign title, renamed the capital, and resplendent omens spread, astonishing all who heard.

At that time, the elders of the eastern lands presented memorials at the palace gates, while the southern ministers and officials jointly submitted petitions, urging the revival of the ancient rites of *feng* and *shan*. Unable to refuse their earnest pleas, you acquiesced, seeking both to exalt the grandeur of heaven and earth and to glorify the pure virtue of your ancestors. Provisions for the grand undertaking were borne entirely by the imperial treasury, and the preparations—from construction to ritual—spared no labor from the people.

In the winter of the first year of *Dazhong Xiangfu*, the tenth month, the ritual implements and solemn guards were arrayed, the imperial retinue assembled, and the sacred peak ascended. With purified hearts and reverent forms, you carried the tally to perform the rites. The officials bustled in preparation, and the hundred ceremonies were meticulously observed. Gathering at the summit’s sheer cliffs, you drew in the luminous *qi* of the heavens. The altar stood in pristine solemnity, the sacrificial animals and vessels immaculately prepared. Jade and silk were presented in due order, and the ritual vessels stood in formation. The offerings were made with utmost reverence, and the music and chants resounded. The feast delighted the Three Spirits, and the earthly sacrifices honored the Two Sages. The beacon fires were lit, and the smoke rose high, displaying the jade tablets for all to witness and erecting the cloud-piercing terrace.

The rites at She Shou followed in due course, adhering to ancient precedents without embellishment. The court convened, amnesties were granted, and the blessings flowed like a cleansing tide. The people’s praises were gathered, their hardships addressed, and their merits recognized. The imperial virtues were extended, and the institutions of former kings were adopted, illuminating the achievements of the illustrious emperors. The grand design was fulfilled, with the divine subtly aiding its realization.

Thus, from beginning to end, the auspicious signs grew ever more radiant. The historians’ brushes scarcely paused in recording them: the fragrant vapors swirled, the sun shone warmly, and the immortal fungi, though rootless, blossomed with elegance.

The sweet springs welled forth without source, surging with beneficent flow. Auspicious radiance and restful qi gave rise to fine grains and wondrous trees, to the progenitors of scaled and shelled creatures, to the myriad species that soar and swim. Marvels appeared in profusion, unmatched in ages past—could this not be the sign of tribute duly rendered, of blessings bestowed in response?

It is said that this mountain towers above all peaks, its merit a companion to creation itself—no falsehood here. The court, following the *Yu Shu*’s precedent of the Four Tours and the Han’s reverent reenactment of the Five Sacrifices, proclaimed the state’s rites, reaffirmed the solemnity of worship, and honored the virtues of uprightness, wisdom, and clarity. With wholehearted devotion and reverent awe, they prayed for the people’s welfare, sharing the nation’s joy. After the feng rites upon the peak, the mountain’s sacred title was further ennobled to "Celestial Paragon, Benevolent and Sage King of the Equal Sky"—a name that captures the numinous workings of creation and elevates the rites of veneration. How profound its meaning!

To be called *benevolent* is to embody the creative power that gives life to all beings; to be called *sage* is to manifest the supreme mystery of divine efficacy. Such exalted and resplendent titles are indeed the utmost. Resolving to further adorn the temple’s appearance and magnify its awe-inspiring majesty, the court entrusted the grand artisans with their duty and deliberated upon a design of renewal. Envoys were dispatched to oversee the work, coordinating the auspicious timing and pooling their efforts. Laborers and craftsmen were assembled, materials supplied, cloud-ladders raised, and wind-borne axes set to work. Local resources were marshaled to meet the deadline, while master artisans from across the realm displayed their skills. The planning was flawless, the joy in labor eclipsing fatigue. Completed within the year, the project surpassed all expectations: the halls grew grander in splendor, the divine images more dignified in bearing. Wherever one turned, reverence deepened.

In the fourth year, the sacrifice to the Earth at Fenyin was performed, completing the rites of cosmic reciprocity. The statutes were meticulously observed, and the exchange between heaven and earth was felt. Once the pure silk offerings were bestowed, great rewards followed. From the round-headed to the square-footed, all sang and danced in celebration. How splendid! With each passing season, harmony unfolded in radiant brilliance. The emperor’s luminous achievements rivaled the sagely works of old, soaring alongside the flourishing talents of the age. The resplendent blessings of the sacred were proclaimed, and extraordinary favors recorded. These grand occasions were all committed to verse.

Like the sun and stars in their brilliance, like the nurturing rain and clouds, the inscriptions were carved on phoenix-washed stones and turtle-borne steles, spreading the hymns of praise far and wide, fulfilling the highest aspirations of governance. Yet the emperor’s aspirations extended further: seeking the elusive *Xiangwang*, he ascended Kongtong, opening the gates to the myriad wonders and expanding the abodes of the perfected. This was not merely to divine longevity at the suburban altars but to guide both the refined and the common into the realm of *Huaxu*.

Moreover, as the Great Unity and its Five Assistants derive from heaven, and the Five Sacred Peaks of Great Tranquility from earth, their positions mirror one another, and their august names may stand as equals. Thus, the Five Peaks were each honored with imperial titles, and the deity of Mount Tai was elevated to "Celestial Paragon, Benevolent and Sage Emperor." A decree was issued to establish a new Five Peaks Imperial Temple east of the imperial highway, aligned with the *xun* direction of the imperial residence, to express the court’s reverence.

The emperor composed a *Fengshen Shushao* hymn, summoning his attendants to explain the intent behind the creation. Behold the grandeur of the sacred text: as the great bulwark of the eastern peak, Mount Tai nurtures all things with its gentle might, fostering the flourishing of life and the flow of vital essences. Its numinous presence is most fitting, for it governs the hidden and the manifest, its portents confirmed in valleys and gorges, its blessings relied upon by the teeming masses. The records of old are replete with such accounts, and former kings paid them due regard: the rites of enfeoffment were inscribed, the sacrifices solemnized, the number of offering villages increased, and the prohibition on logging and gathering enforced—all to honor the unfathomable majesty and manifest the boundless wonders of the divine.

Now, with weapons stilled and the realm united, with harvests bountiful and no pestilence to afflict the land, these blessings flow from the great responses of heaven, aiding the eternal vision. It is fitting to consult the wisdom of the past and advance these exalted titles. In the eras of Tang and Yu, the deities were called *Emperors*; in the ages of Shang and Zhou, they were called *Kings*. Yet the kings of Shang and Zhou were mortal ministers, while the emperors of Tang and Yu were divine beings—what doubt could there be? Moreover, their regalia, guards, and ceremonial attire were prepared with utmost care, their splendor matching their dignity. To complete such rites without deviation is to wish for enduring blessings, extending to all living things.

In the emperor’s spoken words and sagely decrees, his impartiality and magnanimity were evident, as were the clarity of his principles and the abundance of his beneficence. Through his nurturing governance, he brought the people to prosperity and longevity, revealing the heart of a sage. Thus, the record was engraved in stone and erected in the courtyard of the Five Peaks Temple, following the counsel of his close ministers.

That winter, envoys were dispatched in parallel to each of the sacred peaks. An auspicious day was chosen, and the officials prepared. The emperor, clad in ritual robes, ascended the Chaoyuan Hall as music played. There, he oversaw the envoys’ departure, who bore the jade tablets and imperial regalia. The processions followed in order, and the onlookers stood like a wall, marveling at the unparalleled splendor of the ceremony and its renown. Once the envoys completed their tasks, they returned on schedule. The solemn grandeur of the occasion surpassed all antiquity.

The following year, the emperor ordered his five ministers to compose inscriptions, each to be erected at the respective peak’s temple. As for this minister, bathed in the emperor’s grace and steeped in his pure radiance, I was fortunate to join the ranks of the imperial entourage, entering the dragon-and-phoenix halls. Though unworthy, I was entrusted with the imperial command and dared to offer these humble words. Overwhelmed by the favor, I recorded the glorious rites, humbly adorning the sacred stele at Mount Tai and embellishing the enduring stone at Mount Lu. Dazzled by the cascading honors, I am ashamed of my empty learning. With dry lips and moistened brush, I respectfully inscribe this inscription:

Lofty Mount Tai,
spanning the vast east,
where qi congeals and spirits dwell,
foremost among the Five Peaks.
Its might intertwines with rolling ridges,
its beauty rises above Gui and Meng.
It towers beyond the seas,
the very center of heaven.
High it brushes the heavens,
looking down upon the sun’s abode.
Its divine plan is profound,
its prosperous fate foretold.
Nature’s essence gathers in benevolence,
the people dwell in fortune.
Ever constant in the land of Lu,
its great virtue resides in wood.
A deep abyss of a hundred spirits,
a cavern heaven of three palaces.

Amidst the hidden immortals of Jiqiu,
the mushroom lads array as transcendents,
white deer yoked in tandem,
flying dragons summoned in script.
Dwelling in this sublime realm,
they near the great span of years.
The mountain’s elder is honored as the Sun’s Ancestor,
the year’s turning is called the Sacred Peak.
Gazing up at its jagged crags,
it founds and upholds this earth.
The realm of longevity is ample,
the divine intelligence bears responsibility.
Sacrifices flourish in grandeur,
reverence for its majesty as if present.
A thousand years of flourishing fortune,
