the eight directions’ foundation laid.
Martial deeds pacified and settled,
cultural teachings gathered and brightened.
The feng and shan rites of the imperial seal,
ancestral precedents duly established.
Under the sagely successor,
the grand design was at last fulfilled.
The emperor reverently received the mandate,
the deity aided in virtue.
Vast achievements congealed,
the imperial plan was truly fulfilled.
Auspicious responses arrived in mystery,
august omens defied measure.
Vast and boundless, abundance flourished,
the common people multiplied and thrived.

Oh, the majestic temple,
commanding a famed region,
with refined music renewed and composed,
it assumed its complete form.
With unified hearts overseeing the labor,
ample resources met the workforce.
Craftsmanship was never lacking,
thunderous progress spread like stars.
The grand edifice was wholly renewed,
the multitude of changes startling to behold.
Its solemn and majestic demeanor,
its pure and immaculate altars.
Rites and renovations were fitting,
sacrificial offerings duly presented.
Meritorious deeds matched heaven’s work,
blessings flowed like wandering clouds.
In harmony with the people’s hopes,
radiantly repaying the divine favor.
This exceptional title was thus offered,
unmatched by any in ancient times.
May the multitudes of millions find peace—
how could this be mere pacification?
The grandson of the Heavenly Emperor,
once more ascended as emperor.
Emerging from the Palace of Thunder,
overlooking the sun’s frontier.
No affair was greater,
yet the principle lay in hidden accord.
This verdant stele was erected,
its fragrance soaring for a hundred generations.

Each year, the emperor personally inscribed the prayer tablets,
commanding the local governor to attend the sacrifices.
In the twenty-eighth year of the Zhiyuan era of the Yuan dynasty, the title "Heavenly Equal, Great Life-Giving, Benevolent and Sagely Emperor" was conferred upon Mount Tai.
Annually, an official was dispatched to offer sacrifices at the mountain’s shrine.

In the sixth month, the twentieth day of the third year of the Hongwu era of the Great Ming, the emperor decreed:
To the west of the vast Eastern Sea, in the east of the Central Kingdom, where numinous essence gathers and vital force is born with heaven and earth, its form and majesty towering—since ancient times, emperors have ascended it to observe the azure seas, examine the land’s advantages, and secure the people’s peace. It is called Mount Tai; in reverence, it is fitting, and in ritual, it is proper. Since the Tang dynasty, divine titles have been bestowed upon its spirit, a tradition upheld through successive dynasties to this day. Formerly, the Yuan ruler lost control, the realm was in turmoil, the people suffered in misery. I, rising from common cloth, received the mandate of Supreme Heaven and Earth, aided in secret by the hundred spirits, quelling violence and restoring order, rightfully assuming the throne. It is fitting to honor heaven and earth, to offer sacrifices to the spirits, to unify the people in accordance with the times, and to follow ancient precedent.

Now that the realm is at peace, I have maintained the sacrificial rites. Given the deity’s titles conferred through the ages, I, who rose from humble origins, have deliberated again and again, fearing I dare not follow suit. For the spirit shares its origins with the vault of heaven, its numinous power presiding over a region since time immemorial. The source of its divine influence over humanity cannot be fathomed, nor can the extent of its mandate from Supreme Heaven and Earth. How could a ruler presume to interfere? Fearing to add further titles, I have thus named the mountain after the spirit of the Eastern Peak, offering seasonal sacrifices. May the deity deign to witness this.

In the spring and autumn, during the second month of each season, the Tai’an Prefecture shall record the rites and offer preliminary sacrifices one day prior.

The *Imperially Composed "Ode to Mount Tai’s Loftiness"* states:
Oh, lofty Mount Tai!
Its height—who knows how many thousands of *ren*?
Its roots coil through Qi and Lu—
who knows how many thousands of *li*?
Its shadow stretches over the Eastern Sea,
towering as a pillar of heaven.
Benefiting the common folk,
it stirs clouds and exhales mist,
where divine dragons emerge.
When blessings descend, sweet dew drips from the pines;
in bountiful years, torrents soak the fertile earth.
In winter, cold winds issue from its cliffs and ravines,
resounding in a clamor—
rivers and forests roar like thunder.
The earth’s foundation,
the foremost of mountains,
Mount Tai’s northern reach
is like the eastern frontier of Yang Valley.
At times, ascending its summit,
one hears the myriad sounds of heaven.
Oh, the height of Mount Tai!
Its pillar-like might—can it be described?

Suddenly, winds rise from ten thousand ravines,
clouds surge from the peaks,
thunder rumbles through the rivers,
lightning flashes like a thousand-mile rainbow—
the spirit of Mount Tai has arrived.
In its presence, awe and numen are fully manifest,
such is the divine’s creation.
Soon, the winds calm, clouds disperse,
lightning ceases, thunder stills,
and the rivers return to tranquility—
how surpassingly lofty is Mount Tai!

Its verdant pines,
born with heaven and earth,
leaning against the crimson cliffs to grow.
The pines’ longevity—
they do not vie with the cliffs in height,
yet soar ten thousand *ren*.
From the most ancient times to the present,
the pines sweep the crimson cliffs,
where moss does not flourish;
the cliffs reflect the pines,
their five hues intertwining in radiance.
Apes cry from the treetops in the clouds,
cranes soar east of the Sun-Watching Peak,
roc and magpie circle the deep valleys—
though borne by whirlwinds, they cannot ascend the summit.

Alas!
To climb Mount Tai is to belittle the world,
to cross the great sea is to diminish rivers and lakes—
truly so!

In the tenth year of the Hongwu era, the cyclical year *dingsi*, the eighth month, the first day of the *dingwei* cycle, the emperor respectfully dispatched Duke Li Wen Zhong of Cao, the Daoist Wu Yongyu, and Deng Zifang to offer sacrifices to the spirit of the Eastern Peak, Mount Tai, saying:
I, bearing the favor of Supreme Heaven and Earth and the efficacious aid of the spirits, have pacified the warlords, quelled disasters, ruled the black-haired people of the Central Kingdom, and governed the barbarian lands. Now, ten years have passed, and China enjoys peace. Yet regarding the sacrifices to the spirits—if one speaks of the ancient rulers, the sovereign offered prayers for the people, observing the spring supplications and autumn thanksgivings. At such times, there were hopes, and sacrifices to the spirits; there were imperial tours, and offerings at each locale. Now, ten years have passed since I founded the dynasty, and the people have only just found peace. Thus, I have not been able to personally attend the sacrifices in each place.

Here follows the translation:

---

I now specially dispatch the founding loyal minister Li Wenzhong, Daoist Wu Yongyu, and Deng Zifang to act in my stead. They shall present sacrificial animals, prayer texts, and silk offerings at the shrine below, thereby repaying the efficacious spirits. From this year onward, in the mid-autumn season, I shall send envoys to the shrine to perform the sacrifices—may the spirits deign to witness this. Partake, I pray.

In the eleventh year of Hongwu, Daoist Wu Yongyu and official Xing Xingfu were dispatched to proclaim: The spirit, abiding in this cardinal peak, has concentrated the essence of earth and heaven, presiding over the people with truly great merit. Now, in mid-autumn, when rites of thanksgiving are due, I specially command envoys to present sacrificial animals, prayer texts, and silk offerings for the sacrifice—may the spirit deign to witness this. Partake, I pray.

In the twenty-eighth year of Hongwu, Daoist Yue Benran of Shenle Abbey and National University student Wang Ji were dispatched to offer sacrifice, saying: When the Yuan’s mandate was nearing its end, heroes arose in multitudes, and the people suffered the scourge of war. At that time, I too joined the ranks of those heroes, mustering troops to protect the people. The Supreme Lord silently aided me, and mountains and rivers received their mandate, lending their efficacious power—wherever we went, we prevailed. After more than five years of campaigning, the weapons were stilled, the people found peace, and all rejoiced in the pursuit of life’s sustenance. The realm has now enjoyed twenty-eight years of great peace. Recently, in the fourth month of the *yihai* year of Hongwu, the Guangxi Administration reported that the tribal chieftains Zhao Zongshou of Longzhou and Huang Shitie of Fengyi Prefecture had defied civilizing governance, betrayed the state, and brought calamity to the people. The matter of military action is grave indeed; having resolved upon it, I dare not proceed without first making this announcement.

The reason for this announcement is this: when an army of a hundred thousand marches, each soldier is parted from parents, wives, and children. Along the way, they endure hunger and fatigue, and the miasmic vapors of mountain mists and pestilential airs may afflict them—such are the hardships of war. When the army enters enemy territory, the innocent people suffer harm. Moreover, where great armies pass, not even thorns grow, and the people, alarmed and uncertain, cannot escape injury. This is why I make this announcement. My prayer is that the noxious vapors be transformed into clear, pure *qi*, that the ringleaders be swiftly subdued, the good people allowed to live in peace, and the soldiers returned quickly to their homes, where they may reunite with their families and care for their parents.

Yet I dare not presume to address the Supreme Lord directly—may the spirit deign to witness this and convey my plea. This I reverently announce.

In the thirtieth year of Hongwu, Daoist Zhu Duoru of Shenle Abbey and National University student Gao Xi were dispatched to offer sacrifice, saying: When the Yuan fell and warfare raged, countless lives were lost. I, bearing the favor of August Heaven and the efficacious power of the mountain peaks, riverine guardians, and all the spirits of the land and sea, saw my generals execute their duties, stilling the weapons and pacifying the people. Now, thirty years have passed, and in the aftermath of war, the people have only just found stability. Recently, however, the generals stationed to guard the southwestern frontiers have failed to manifest benevolence and authority, knowing only how to enrich themselves and oppress the people. This has driven the various tribal and Miao peoples to distress and fury, leading them to attack the garrisoned troops and harm the good people under their protection.

I do not presume to resort to arms, but circumstances leave me no choice but to command my generals to lead troops in a punitive campaign. Yet the mountains and rivers are perilous and distant, and the enemy’s lands are dense with vegetation, shrouded in mists and clouds, their thick, swirling *qi* inhaled with every breath—thus, many fall ill with pestilence. These soldiers, each bidding farewell to their grandparents, parents, wives, and children, venture into danger and distance to pacify the frontier barbarians and secure the Central Kingdom. I humbly pray that the spirit convey my plea to the Supreme Lord, bestowing clear, pure *qi* to dispel the miasmic vapors, swiftly subduing the tribes, and hastening the return of the troops to their camps, where they may reunite with their grandparents, parents, and families. This is my prayer.

As the army sets forth on the twenty-sixth day of the ninth month, I specially dispatch envoys bearing incense, silk, sacrificial animals, and libations to first reverently announce this at the spirit’s abode. Partake, I pray.

In the fifth year of the Yongle reign, Daoist Fusheng and student-official Zhang Li were dispatched to offer sacrifice, proclaiming:

Of late, the rebellious traitor of Annam, Lê Quý Ly, and his descendants, Lê Sát and others, have wantonly unleashed their violence, repeatedly ravaging the frontier and seizing the lands of Lộc Châu and other regions in Tư Minh Prefecture. Though I extended leniency and refrained from mobilizing troops to punish their crimes, I sent envoys to admonish them, demanding the return of the occupied territories. Yet the Lê rebels resorted to cunning evasions, returning lands that were not their original holdings. Even after their restitution, they reoccupied Tây Bình Châu, further encroached upon Ninh Viễn Châu, coerced local officials, seized control of the populace, plundered wealth, and slaughtered or enslaved men and women. The people along the border endure their cruelty, the people of Annam suffer their harm, and their exactions know no end, leaving the old and young in perpetual distress. Even the lands of Champa have endured years of their pillaging.

I have repeatedly sent envoys to admonish them, hoping they would mend their ways, but their wickedness has only deepened, showing no hint of repentance. As the sovereign of all under heaven, how could I bear to witness the people drowning in suffering and not come to their aid? Thus, I have commanded generals to lead the army forth, to condemn their crimes and launch a punitive campaign. My intent is to deliver the people from affliction, not to wage war wantonly—this course is truly one of necessity. By the grace of the August Heaven and Earth, the sacred mountains and rivers, and the divine seas, and through the valor of our officers and soldiers, we have swept away their vile factions and pacified the good and loyal.

Yet I remain mindful that our troops endure hardship in the field, parted from their parents, wives, and children. The mountains and rivers are treacherous, the roads long and arduous, and now the weather grows sweltering, with miasmic vapors rising in thick mists. Their daily routines disrupted, they are vulnerable to illness. Day and night, this weighs upon my heart, robbing me of peace in sleep and appetite. I earnestly pray that the spirits, perceiving my sincerity, convey my plea to the Supreme Lord, bestowing great blessings to silently dispel pestilence, sending down clear, pure *qi* to ensure the health and safety of our troops, sparing them from all afflictions.

I now dispatch envoys bearing incense, silk, sacrificial animals, and libations to first reverently announce this at the spirit’s abode. Partake, I pray.

In the tenth year of the Xuande reign, cyclical year *yimao*, fifth month, first day *renshen*, fifteenth day *bingxu*, the Emperor respectfully dispatches Hu Zongmin, Prefect of Tai’an Prefecture, to offer sacrifice and announce to the Spirit of the Eastern Peak, Mount Tai:

I have inherited the great throne of my ancestors, governing the people below, ever diligent day and night, with nurturing the people as my duty. I earnestly pray that the spirits, in their hidden and manifest assistance, ensure timely rain and sunshine, prevent disasters and calamities, bring the hundred grains to fruition, and let the people thrive in well-being, the nation enjoy peace and prosperity, forever relying on your divine blessings. With fragrant silk, I convey my utmost sincerity—may the Spirit perceive and accept it. Partake, I pray.

In the first year of the Zhengtong reign, cyclical year *bingchen*, fifth month, first day *dingmao*, fifteenth day *xinsi*, the Emperor respectfully dispatches Che Xun, Supervising Secretary of the Ministry of Personnel, to offer sacrifice to the Spirit of the Eastern Peak, Mount Tai:

This eastern land is graced by the lofty Mount Tai, where the people and all things are securely governed, its merit truly abundant. Having inherited the great mandate, I reverently perform this sacrificial announcement—may the Spirit accept and forever protect our state and homeland. Partake, I pray.

In the third year of the Zhengtong reign, Hu Zongmin, Prefect of Tai’an Prefecture, is dispatched to announce:

I, in reverent governance of the people, ever mindful of their protection, pray for the growth of the hundred grains in due season. I earnestly hope that the luminous spirit will bestow abundant blessings, averting disasters and harm, ensuring timely rain and sunshine, yielding a bountiful harvest, and bringing prosperity to the common people. Partake, I pray.

In the ninth year of the Zhengtong reign, Xi Jianyan, Academician of the Hanlin Academy, is dispatched to announce:

I, entrusted by Heaven to nurture the people, am ashamed of my inadequate virtue, which has brought prolonged drought and suffering to all living beings. Day and night, I reflect upon myself with deep remorse. You, Spirit, who preside over this sacred peak, share in this sorrow. Rain must come in its season, and you bear this hidden duty. I now offer this prayer, hoping for a response—may sweet rains be generously bestowed to bring abundant harvests. This blessing is not of my making but yours, O Spirit. Partake, I pray.

In the third year of the Jingtai reign, Wang Wen, Grand Guardian of the Heir Apparent and Censor-in-Chief of the Censorate, is dispatched bearing fragrant silk and a grand sacrifice to announce:

Recently, the rivers have overflowed, from Jining Prefecture southward to the Huai’s northern reaches, submerging homes and fields, leaving the people in dire straits, their rescue beyond our means. This grieves me deeply. I govern to benefit the people, just as you, Spirit, release springs to nourish all things—both by Heaven’s command. Now, the floods surge through the Huai and Si rivers, bringing disaster to both public and private realms. Whose responsibility is this? Surely, it stems from my lack of virtue, yet how can you, Spirit, alone evade blame? Only when the waters flow appropriately, benefiting rather than harming the people, shall each fulfill their duty—looking up, I bear no shame; looking down, no remorse. May this sincere plea move you to respond and ease my deep concern. I reverently announce this.

In the sixth year of the Jingtai reign, Xue Xilian, Minister of Justice, is dispatched bearing fragrant silk, sacrificial animals, and libations to announce:

Reverently upholding Heaven’s mandate, burdened with the weight of governance, I am the people’s support, upon whom fortune and misfortune depend. My resolve is ever inward, yet external events often stray. Whether cold or heat arrives untimely, or rain and sunshine falter, fields lose their yield, and grains fail to ripen. My heart aches for the people, and the nation’s plans suffer. Tracing the cause, I find it lies here. Though disasters arise from my faults, which I cannot shirk, turning misfortune into fortune is your sacred duty. Heaven bears blame without merit—my transgressions are mine alone. But who can match your power to transform calamity into blessing? I earnestly offer this plea, hoping it meets your gracious regard. I reverently announce this.

In the sixth year of the Chenghua reign, Li Xi’an, Minister of Rites concurrently in charge of the Court of Imperial Sacrifices, is dispatched to announce:

Recently, in Shandong, from last autumn to this summer, the heavens have withheld rain for too long. Springs have run dry, summer wheat has failed, autumn fields remain unplanted, and the Grand Canal is too shallow for ships to pass. My heart is anxious, deeply troubled by this. You, Spirit, who stabilize this region, upon whom the people rely—seeing this drought, how can you not be moved? I now dispatch an official bearing fragrant silk to announce to you, hoping you will embody the Heavenly virtue of cherishing life, silently activate the mechanisms of transformation, and abundantly bestow rain. May the fields be thoroughly drenched, the waterways flow freely, easing my concerns and greatly fulfilling the people’s hopes. May your glorious deeds be everlasting, forever unceasing—may the Spirit perceive this. Partake, I pray.

In the thirteenth year of the Chenghua reign, Chen Yin, Left Provincial Administration Commissioner of Shandong, is dispatched to announce:

The state reveres the divine, strictly maintaining its sacrifices, hoping for the silent operation of transformative power to protect and bless the people. Yet in recent years, the heavens have been unseasonable, the earth unsettled; thunder and lightning have behaved abnormally, rain and sunshine have strayed from their proper times...

At times, malevolent omens arise, and pestilence spreads unchecked. Far and near, the people suffer frequent famines, wandering in destitution and hardship—words cannot express their anguish. My heart aches with sorrow, yet I know not how to act. As you, O Spirit, preside over this land, upon whom the people rely, how can you not take these calamities to heart? Thus, I have prepared incense and silk and dispatched an official to offer sacrifice. I humbly pray that you embody the virtue of the Supreme Lord’s love for life, perceive my compassion for the people of this region, and turn the mechanisms of creation to manifest your mighty power. Ward off misfortune, avert disaster, and transform calamity into blessing. May the people’s lives flourish, and may your eternal recompense be boundless. May the Spirit perceive this. Partake, I pray.

In the twenty-first year of the Chenghua reign, Sheng Xian, Left Vice Censor-in-Chief of the Shandong Surveillance Commission, is dispatched to announce:
Since the dawn of time, O Spirit, you have guarded the eastern realm, summoning clouds and rain to nurture all things each year. Your numinous radiance shines forth, upon which the people depend. Thus, through the ages, the rites of offering have been upheld without fail. Yet this year, between the second and third months, the earth has trembled several times. It must be that human affairs have fallen into disorder, disturbing your peace. As a local official has reported this, my heart is filled with dread. I have prepared incense and silk and commanded an official to proceed with the sacrifice, gazing reverently toward the lofty peaks to pray. May you deign to witness this and stabilize your abode, aiding the governance of creation and bestowing great fortune upon the people. From ancient times to the present, all who look up in hope do so here; all that the state reveres is found here. I respectfully announce this.

In the fourth year of the Hongzhi reign, Yuan Shouzhi, Left Director of the Directorate of Ceremonial, is dispatched to offer sacrifice, saying:
Since last winter brought no snow, and this spring’s weather has been marked by severe drought, the rains have failed to arrive on time. The fields lie withered, and the common people are filled with dread. I am deeply alarmed and have turned inward in reflection, offering earnest prayers. May you, O Spirit, take pity on the people below and turn the great workings of creation, sending timely rains to nourish the crops and relieve the people’s hardship. May the people enjoy abundant harvests, and may you, in turn, receive boundless offerings. I respectfully announce this.

In the sixth year of the Hongzhi reign, Wang Ji, Left Assistant Censor-in-Chief of the Shandong Surveillance Commission, is dispatched to offer sacrifice, saying:
Since last winter brought no snow, and this spring has seen little rain, the fields remain unplanted, and the common people are gripped by fear. I am deeply alarmed and have turned inward in reflection, offering earnest prayers. May you, O Spirit, take pity on the people below and turn the great workings of creation, sending timely rains to nourish the crops and relieve the people’s hardship. May the people enjoy abundant harvests, and may you, in turn, receive boundless offerings. I respectfully announce this.

In the seventh year of the Hongzhi reign, Li Xing, Eunuch of the Directorate of Palace Attendants; Chen Rui, Grand Guardian and Earl of Pingjiang; Liu Daxia, Right Vice Censor-in-Chief; and Xiong Shi, Right Assistant Censor-in-Chief, are dispatched with incense and silk to announce:
Recently, the Yellow River has abandoned its former course, flooding Zhangqiu and flowing eastward into the sea. It has ruined the people’s fields and obstructed the transport routes. I have specially dispatched civil and military officials, both within and beyond the court, to inspect the breached embankments and oversee the repairs. May you, O Spirit, silently assist in completing this work. Let the farmers not abandon their labors, and let the state’s finances not suffer loss. This is my earnest and sincere hope. I respectfully announce this.

In the *Jiwei* year of the Hongzhi reign, the Imperial Edict on the Stele for the Reconstruction of the Eastern Peak Temple:

We have heard that since ancient times, the rites by which the Son of Heaven repays and sacrifices to spirits and deities are recorded in the classical canons. Below the suburban sacrifices to Heaven and Earth, there are also the sacrifices to famous mountains and great rivers. For mountains and rivers are the grandest of all physical forms between heaven and earth; where form is grand, the *qi* that gathers there is also grand, and spirits must dwell therein. Thus, they bestow the moisture of rain and dew, the generation of wealth and goods, and the prayers for harvests, floods, and droughts—all to benefit living people. This is the origin of the rites of repayment and invocation.

Now, Mount Tai stands in the ancient Yanzhou, corresponding to the east among the directions and to spring among the seasons. As spring governs the inception of all things, Mount Tai is the foremost of the Five Peaks and is thus called the *Daizong* (Supreme Peak). In ancient times, when the Son of Heaven made his imperial tours of inspection, he first performed the seasonal sacrifices in the east. The feudal lords within their domains were also permitted to offer sacrifices, though their rites were ranked below those of the Three Dukes, distinguishing them from the sacrifices to Heaven and Earth. In later ages, however, it was exalted with honorific titles, and by the time of emperors, its status became indistinguishable from that of Heaven and Earth.

When Our August Ancestor, the Supreme Emperor Gao, first established the realm, he issued an edict to standardize the sacrificial canon, restoring its title as the Spirit of the Eastern Peak, Mount Tai. The rites of solemn repayment were established, and the ordinances have endured to this day. The vision of this Great Sage far surpasses the ordinary by ten thousandfold. Not only must We reverently uphold it, but even Our descendants for ten thousand generations shall not alter it.

Temples to the Eastern Peak exist throughout the realm, but the one on Mount Tai is the principal site of worship, revered through the ages. Thus, its scale and system surpass those of other regions, and its incense and offerings are especially abundant. Since the founding of the dynasty, Our Ancestral Sages have observed the rites of repayment with utmost care, and the temple structures have been repeatedly repaired. Yet, as the years have passed, wind and rain have eroded them; beams and rafters have decayed, tiles and bricks have crumbled, and the vermilion and azure decorations have faded and worn away.

In the winter of the *Jiwei* year of the Hongzhi reign, mindful of Our Ancestors’ reverence for the spirits, We dispatched Miao Kui, Eunuch of the Directorate of the Imperial Stud, to oversee the sacrificial rites and repairs, deeming it fitting. We then allocated over eight thousand taels of silver from the imperial treasury, along with the accumulated incense funds stored at the temple, and commanded Miao Kui to collaborate with the military and civil officials of Shandong in the restoration. Before long, however, the western frontier raised an alarm, and Miao Kui was dispatched to supervise the military campaign, delaying the completion of the work.

In the winter of the *Renzu* year, We again sent him to oversee the sacrifices. He then conferred with Xu Yuan, Right Vice Censor-in-Chief and Grand Coordinator, and entrusted Yu Jun, Right Administration Vice Commissioner, to supervise the project exclusively, while Wang Zongxi, Surveillance Vice Commissioner, assisted in its oversight. Through the diligence of the officials and the excellence of the work, the project was completed by the summer of the following year. From the three halls to the palace courtyards, the corridors, gates, and walls—all that had collapsed was replaced, all that was ruined was restored, and all that had faded was renewed and purified. The temple now gleams with gold and azure, its majesty profound and solemn. The luminous spirit of Mount Tai, now honored and at peace, shall henceforth secretly assist and openly bless Our dynasty’s posterity and the people of this region, ever more reliant upon its boundless favor.

Miao Kui and the military and civil officials all memorialized the grandeur of this undertaking, deeming it worthy of record for posterity. Thus, We inscribe these events at the end of the stele and append a poem:

Before the vast chaos was divided,
*Qi* was one and undifferentiated.
The pure ascended, the turbid descended,
And thus began Heaven and Earth.
Of all that received form,
None was greater than mountains and rivers.
Where *qi* gathers with form,
There the spirits reside.
Majestic Mount Tai,
Pierces the clouds and mist.
The myriad peaks revere it,
None dare rival its height.
Since ancient times, the Son of Heaven
Has performed its sacrifices with utmost reverence.
Its rites ranked with the Three Dukes,
As the canons have transmitted.
From the Tang and Song onward,
The rituals were passed down in succession.
With exalted names and honorific titles,
Its worship grew ever more solemn and exclusive.
Our August Ancestor,
Resolved to restore antiquity.
His sacrificial edicts,
Surpassed all former records.
We inherit His legacy,
Our sincerity ever more steadfast.
The sacrificial rites are renewed,
The temple’s splendor enhanced.
May the spirit’s heart be pleased,
Bestowing blessings without end.
May Our posterity flourish,
And Our civilizing influence shine.
Vast and tranquil,
For a hundred million years.

In the fifth year of the Zhengde reign, We dispatched Qiao Yu, Left Vice Minister of the Ministry of Revenue, to announce:
Recently, the seasons have been unseasonable, and rain has fallen sparingly. Springs and streams have dried, and the transport routes have become arduous. We suspect that Our governance may have strayed, offending the *qi* of Heaven. Our heart is filled with alarm, and We have reflected inwardly. Thus, We have ordered the officials to attend to their duties. Now, in the lands of Qi and Lu, where springs and sources abound, and where famous mountains and great rivers are the abodes of spirits, We respectfully present incense and silk and specially dispatch a court minister. We humbly beseech the luminous spirit to turn the great transformations, to send forth sweet rains and dredge the river courses, that the state’s provisions may flow unimpeded, the fields’ crops may flourish, the people’s livelihood may be secured, and the foundation of the realm may endure without limit. We respectfully announce this.

In the sixth year of the Zhengde reign, We dispatched Xu Yong, Right Administration Vice Commissioner of the Shandong Provincial Administration Commission, to announce:
Since last year, Ningxia has rebelled, and We commanded officials to suppress it. The traitors were captured, the internal disorder was pacified, and the realm, both within and without, was settled. Without the great protection of the spirits, how could this have been achieved? Yet, to this day, We have not offered thanks. Recently, the realm has faced many trials: floods and droughts have followed one another, corpses of the starved line the roads, the people suffer, bandits gather, and suppression has not yet succeeded. Reflecting on the causes of these calamities, We are filled with trepidation. We humbly pray that the divine mercy may perceive Our sincerity, secretly assist the workings of transformation, dissolve disasters before they arise, and summon auspicious omens in response. May it protect Our state and forever shelter the people. We respectfully announce this.

In the eleventh year of the Jiajing reign, We dispatched Li Cheng, Magistrate of Tai’an Prefecture, to offer sacrifice, saying:
The spirit, infused with numinous essence and nurturing excellence, has long presided over and stabilized this region, secretly assisting the state. We, in Our humility, have reverently received the Mandate of Heaven for eleven years now, serving the spirits with utmost care, never daring to slacken. Yet, the heir to the throne has not been established, and this weighs heavily upon Our heart. Thus, We have specially prepared sacrificial animals, silk, ale, and grain, and dispatched an official to offer sincere prayers.

May it please the spirit to manifest its divine power and bestow upon Us an imperial heir, that Our state may enjoy boundless blessings and the spirit itself reap eternal felicity. Humbly we pray, may the offering be accepted.

In the seventeenth year of the Jiajing reign, We dispatched Ding Fang, Magistrate of Tai’an Prefecture, to offer sacrifice, saying:
In recent years, We have repeatedly commanded officials to pray for an heir before the spirit. On the auspicious day of early winter in the bing-shen year, We gratefully received the Heaven-sent heir to the throne, a blessing also aided by the spirit’s unseen support. Now, in gratitude, may the spirit deign to accept this offering and continue its silent protection. May the offering be accepted.

In the twenty-second year of the Jiajing reign, cyclical year gui-chou, on the first day of the intercalary third month, ding-wei, the thirty-eighth day thereafter, jia-xu, the Emperor dispatched Shen Yinglong, Right Vice Censor-in-Chief of the Office of the Censor-General and Inspector of Shandong, to offer sacrifice to the Spirit of Mount Tai, saying:
We reverently uphold the Mandate of Heaven and govern the myriad people, ever vigilant day and night, never daring to rest. Recently, the regional officials of Huaixu reported: since last summer, the Yellow River has repeatedly overflowed, causing extraordinary floods. Embankments have collapsed, homes and fields have been swept away, and the Grand Canal has silted up. The people suffer from hunger and cannot sustain themselves. We are deeply grieved upon hearing this. As the spirit presides mightily over this region and eternally receives its due sacrifices, may it embody the Supreme Emperor’s virtue of cherishing life, averting calamity and repelling disaster. May the dredging and construction be completed, the waters return to their former course, the people resume their livelihoods, and the grain transport be unobstructed. By the spirit’s manifest efficacy, We present these offerings with utmost sincerity. May the spirit deign to accept them. Respectfully we announce this.

In the thirty-third year of the Jiajing reign, cyclical year jia-yin, on the first day of the second month, ren-shen, the eighteenth day thereafter, ji-chou, the Emperor dispatched Shen Yinglong, Right Vice Censor-in-Chief of the Office of the Censor-General and Inspector of Shandong, to offer sacrifice to the Spirit of Mount Tai, saying:
Previously, due to the overflow of the Yellow River and the obstruction of the grain transport route, We had already dispatched an official to make sacrificial announcements. Now, through the spirit’s manifest response, the dredging has been completed, the river flows freely, and the transport routes are open, greatly benefiting the state’s affairs. We hereby offer our gratitude. May the spirit deign to accept this offering. Respectfully we announce this.

In the thirty-third year of the Jiajing reign, cyclical year jia-yin, on the first day of the fourth month, xin-wei, the twelfth day thereafter, ren-wu, the Emperor dispatched Shen Yinglong, Right Vice Censor-in-Chief of the Office of the Censor-General and Inspector of Shandong, to offer sacrifice and announce to the Spirit of Mount Tai, saying:

We, upholding Heaven’s mandate, nurture the myriad people, hoping for abundant harvests, timely harmony, and the absence of disasters. Recently, in various regions, floods, droughts, warfare, and famine have befallen the people, with peril and ruin filling the roads, and calamities unprecedented. Our heart is filled with anxiety and trepidation.

As the spirit, in reverence to the Emperor’s command, stabilizes and aids this region, We trust you will show compassion. Thus, We have commanded the purification of the sacred site, provided fragrant offerings and silks, and specially dispatched Our inspector to prepare the rites, proceeding with utmost reverence to the shrine to offer sacrifice. We hope the illustrious spirit will manifest great efficacy, subtly influencing the transformative mechanisms of nature, that the sequence of qi may be harmonious, rain and sunshine timely, disasters and afflictions pacified, and universal abundance and prosperity bestowed. Thus may We together fulfill Our duty to Heaven in nurturing the people, and the spirit, too, shall enjoy boundless blessings.

Respectfully we announce this.

In the third year of the Longqing reign, the Emperor dispatched Jiang Tingyi, Right Vice Censor-in-Chief of the Office of the Inspector-General, to offer sacrifice to Mount Tai, with the following proclamation:

Of late, extraordinary floods have brought calamity upon the common people, deeply troubling Our heart. We now specially dispatch an official to perform the sacrificial rites, praying that the spirit may grant protection and bestow eternal blessings upon the state and its people.

Respectfully we announce this.

In the first year of the Wanli reign, the Emperor dispatched an envoy to sacrifice to the Spirit of Mount Tai of the Eastern Peak, proclaiming:

The spirit, nurturing essence and concentrating numinous power, has long presided over the eastern lands, securing the peace of the people and all living things, upon whom all generations depend. Now that We have inherited the great mandate, We reverently perform this sacrificial announcement. May the spirit deign to accept and witness it, and bless Our nation.

May the offerings be enjoyed.

Through the ages, officials have composed prayers and supplications:

Wei Gao Yun’s *Sacrificial Text to Mount Tai*:
Here stands the axis of earth, the guardian of the eastern lands, aligning with the Two Modes to ensure eternal stability, exalting supreme virtue to match Heaven. Thus it draws upon primordial qi to create all things, harmonizing yin and yang in transformation. Its towering ridges and deep valleys teem with divine marvels and swift spirits, exhaling wind and cloud, nurturing the myriad beings. It is the refuge of life and the gathering place of auspicious omens. For this reason, emperors of successive dynasties revered it, inscribing their achievements through feng and shan sacrifices to proclaim their accomplishments. Seventy-two sovereigns have come here—none but those whose deeds rivaled creation itself, whose resonance matched the natural order, could achieve such a station.

Since Our dynasty has reverently upheld the sacrifices, embracing and pacifying the hundred spirits, within Our domains, all rites are duly observed. Though once the celestial path remained untraversed, and sacrifices were offered from afar, now that the great transformation is complete and Our sway extends over the lands of Huai and Tai, We reverently present offerings to the spirit of this sacred peak, that the people may partake of its blessings.

Second year of the Huangxing reign.

Ouyang Xiu’s *Sacrificial Text to the Eastern Peak*:
I, Xiu, having recently been relieved of my post, was permitted to return to my hometown. As I prepared my carriage to depart, the season of summer rains had begun. With sincere devotion, I dared to beseech the great workings of creation. By the spirit’s grace, my journey was not hindered: a cool breeze dispersed the gloom, and the autumn sun cleared the path. Thus, I encountered no obstacles on the road and safely returned to my humble abode. The depth of this virtue is beyond repayment; with a single cup of pure wine, I humbly offer my gratitude.

Zeng Gong, Prefect of Jinan, *Proclamation to Mount Tai*:
In times of drought, prayers were offered to the spirit, and blessed rains descended, yielding a bountiful harvest. This spring, for the river works, twenty thousand laborers were conscripted, with double that number aiding in rotation. Nearly half the people of Qi were thus mobilized. As they returned home at the onset of summer, the toll of their toil was great, and the season for planting was disrupted. The people may enjoy a good harvest only once in several years; to exhaust them thus is grievous. Now, as the winter wheat begins to form ears, scorching heat has turned to frost. Though officials bear the blame, I dare not advance further. What wrong have the people committed? We rely on the spirit’s enduring benevolence to bring forth clouds and rain—this power rests with the spirit alone. May it take heed.

Dare we proclaim:
The spirit, embodying virtue and embodying benevolence, presides over the eastern lands, summoning clouds and rain to bestow blessings across the eight directions. Now, this land of Qi lies at the mountain’s foot, suffering from prolonged drought in the height of summer, its wheat withering. Local officials, mindful of their counties, have hastened to every sacred site, but their words, humble and lowly, fail to move the heavens. Scattered clouds have repeatedly dispersed, and nearly twenty days have passed. These weary people, burdened by taxes and levies, even in prosperous years struggle to fill their bellies. A single disaster drives them to the brink of the ditches. O spirit, whose might and mercy shelter all living things, should not this province be the first to receive your grace? How could your divine favor abandon them? Thus, We dispatch the people with sincerity, humbly beseeching your compassion. If a single rain falls abundantly, their plight may be relieved. May you deign to witness this and not bring shame upon the divine.

From ancient times to the present, those who ruled the realm have journeyed here to perform the feng and shan sacrifices, inscribing their achievements to proclaim their reigns—none have failed to come. Some, though they longed to set foot here, never could; its grandeur and singularity make it a place of divine luminosity, revered by all under Heaven. The *Two Classics* record its radiant power and awe-inspiring might, a legacy that endures for ten thousand generations—what other mountain could compare?

The lands of Qi and Lu, gazing upward in reverence, have long depended on its hidden blessings for their sustenance and raiment. Now, this province has seen no rain since the fourth month; the ponds have dried to dust, and the millet withers in the fields, while locusts multiply. Though governance may be flawed, sufficient to bring this upon us, what crime have the people committed to deserve such suffering? Even the humblest hills and smallest streams can bring fortune to the people, bestowing years of plenty. How much more so Mount Tai, which with a single morning cloud can bring evening rain to the entire realm—an effort as easy as turning a wheel? Yet in recent times, it has shown no compassion. Perhaps none have made their pleas known, or their offerings were not made with reverence. The lives of the people are fraught with hardship: no stores or reserves, heavy taxes and endless demands. A single poor harvest drives many to the brink of death. The ignorant and strong may seize the moment, gathering to plunder, only to face execution. Even the law-abiding farmers, tilling their fields, perish alongside them—how can this not grieve Us?

I, Gong, entrusted by the Son of Heaven to govern this eastern domain, dare to approach with solemnity and proclaim to the sacred temple: May the spirit take pity on the people’s suffering and aid the year’s harvest. Let rain fall abundantly, transforming barrenness into abundance. May these people, with their oxen, sheep, millet, and grain, forever offer their devotion.

The above three texts all beseech rain.

I, your humble servant, act against the times, my words dismissed as trivial, few companions, little support—my toil and honor slight. So estranged from others, how dare I hope to be trusted in the unseen realm? Yet, as I journeyed eastward, I came to pay homage, entrusting this text to convey my sincerity. Gazing upon the ancient lands of Qi and Lu, I lean upon this towering peak. As the drought demon rages, I fear the harvest will fail, and the people will perish in the ditches or fall to the sword.

May the luminous and discerning [deity] deign to accept and respond, sending down nourishing rains from the heavens, their words solemn as they ascend in supplication—yet my heart trembles in silent dread. Though I cannot harmonize with the world, how then may I move the majestic spirit?

Yet I know the gods walk with the Dao/Way: though their treasured silence defies clarity, their emptiness of heart ensures all things that come are met with response; their impartial hearing transcends the common passions of favor or resentment. The great may call themselves great, the small may be deemed small—yet I seek only goodness. Ever steadfast in equanimity, their subtle sincerity ascends, and their beneficence spreads far: now a sigh becomes wind, now a tremor becomes rain; thunder rumbles unseen, clouds gather in misty ascent. Sweet showers fall through the night, dispelling calamity without a trace.

Thus, from the western reaches to the royal domains, from the eastern lands to the boundless sea, the people’s hopes are fulfilled, all things thrive. Millet sways in luxuriant tassels, grain burgeons in verdant abundance. Seasonal treasures flourish, the year’s labor bears fruit; the people feast on plentiful harvests, offerings overflow in sacred rites. Folk dwell in peace among their fields and villages, officials set aside coercion and punishment.

Such boundless grace defies repayment, such supreme virtue eludes description. Thus, my heart surges with profound gratitude. Adrift in the world’s trials, I am sustained by the unfailing gaze of divine insight, knowing loyalty and sincerity may be relied upon. I dare offer these words in humble thanks, ashamed that my thoughts lack refinement.

**Right: Prayer of Thanksgiving for Rain.**

During our dynasty, the Censor-in-Chief Chen Fengwu’s *Address to the Sacred Mountain*:

Among the renowned mountains under heaven, the Five Peaks hold the highest reverence. The deity of the Eastern Peak, who presides over the growth of all things, is especially exalted among them. From the Tang dynasty to our sagely reign, successive eras have honored its worship, its majestic power resplendent—for the peace and peril of the realm rest in its governance.

In the autumn of the *xinsi* year, Fengwu, then serving in the eastern provinces, paid homage at its shrine and prayed for a bountiful year. Later, by the grace of imperial favor, he was appointed as provincial governor, laboring day and night to pacify and cherish the people. In the spring of the *renwu* year, when ancestral rites were performed in autumn, timely rains and sunshine prevailed, a testament to the deity’s benevolence—thus, faith was confirmed.

That autumn and into winter, bandits suddenly arose in the region of Guimeng, ravaging the people. Though pursued beyond the borders, the remnants fled south across the river, growing ever more audacious. Recently, by the deity’s protection and the convergence of military forces, they were eradicated, and the Central Plains now enjoy tranquility.

Yet within the lands of Qi and Lu, deep valleys and dense forests harbor hidden malice, their threats ever-present. Relying on the deity’s awe-inspiring might, may evil be uprooted and violence transformed. Let the regions between the Ji, the sea, and Mount Tai know no banditry or drought, but flourish in abundance and well-being. Thus may Fengwu’s duties be somewhat eased—and how dare he forget the rites of reverent repayment?

With utmost sincerity, he presents sacrificial offerings and humbly tenders this address. May the deity deign to witness it.

**Respectfully submitted.**
**Second year of the Jiajing reign.**

**Address to the Sacred Mount by Xu Yingyuan, Magistrate of Tai’an:**

I have heard that when clouds gather and qi spreads, rain falls across the land in less than a morning—only the spirit of Mount Tai can achieve this.

I, Xu Yingyuan, unworthy and dull, have been appointed to govern this land. Upon entering its borders, I inquired of the people’s desires, and they spoke of years of drought: half the fields lie barren, their grasses withered. If rain were to come, life might yet revive.

Yet at this time, my spirit and will were divided, my robes unclean. I dared not approach the radiant spirit, but waited in held breath for ten days and more. The fine grain remains unsown, the wheat seedlings wither, and the long-awaited rains have yet to arrive—does this not show the spirit’s unceasing punishment upon this land?

The people, exhausted and weak, cannot endure further suffering. As the one charged with this land, I have failed in my duty and deserve to bear the calamity. Those hapless masses, how pitiable they are!

Thus, I humbly lead my officials in offering our utmost sincerity to pray to the radiant spirit. May the spirit bestow its grace at last, sparing the roads from desolation. How could we ever fully repay the spirit’s virtue?

**May the offering be accepted.**
**Twelfth year of the Jiajing reign.**

Left Provincial Administration Commissioner Yang Weicong’s *Address to Mount Tai*:

Of all mountains under heaven, none are more revered than the Five Peaks, yet among them, only Mount Tai is honored as the *Ancestral Peak*, its name most illustrious. Rising through the clouds, its vapors gather in mere inches, and before the morning ends, rain falls across the land—its merit is greatest. Standing in the East, it is truly the treasury of all living things—its virtue most abundant. Within the four seas, no place is too distant or remote for those who turn toward it—its spirit most efficacious.

Thus, since ancient times, sage emperors and enlightened kings have shown it utmost reverence—how much more should those who serve as officials? I, Weicong, have received the Son of Heaven’s mandate to govern this eastern land, overseeing the sacred peak. Upon assuming office, I fulfilled my duties by passing beneath its shrine, offering sacrificial animals and sweet wine—how could I dare be anything but reverent?

Yet I reflect deeply: my own lack of virtue has burdened me with this weighty responsibility. Where governance remains unjust, where the people suffer want, where winds and rains come unseasonably, where locusts and pestilence arise—all these are the failings of officials. I, Weicong, dare not shirk this blame. Yet I look to the spirit with hope: if the spirit’s grace silently aids us, it will be the people’s blessing—not a boon I, Weicong, could claim as my own.

Respectfully addressed.
Fifteenth year of the Jiajing reign.

**Lu Wenzhi, Surveillance Vice Commissioner of Jinan Circuit, "Sacrificial Text to the Sacred Peak":**

Of the gods, none surpass in merit the one who stabilizes and guards this realm; of officials, none bear greater duty than I, who govern and pacify this land. Our duties stand distinct, yet in principle, our bond is one of mutual resonance. Recently, during my circuit inspections, I was moved by compassion for the farmers. I reflected: the disaster of scorching drought is the fault of those who, like me, have been negligent toward Heaven and indifferent to the people. Thus, I examined my conduct, issued warnings, and led all officials—high and low—in reverent supplication. We bore the weight of hempen robes in solemn offering, and the gods’ grace descended in radiant response. Upon the mountain, clouds arose at the touch of stone, coalescing from the smallest measure, and before the morning had passed, rain fell. The starving people now have hope, and the tax collectors no longer fret. How swift and vast is this divine work—truly, it is the manifest blessing of the eastern lands. As I stand in the wind, my heart swells with gratitude; my gaze toward the peak is all the more earnest. I have commanded the officials to elevate the sacrificial vessels and, with reverence, repay the gods’ favor. May the spirits partake.

**Sixteenth year of the Jiajing reign.**

---

**Duan Tingjie, Censor-in-Chief, "Address to the Sacred Peak":**

Vast is the earth, and the Five Peaks rise in majesty; among them, the gods hold sway as sovereigns. Their numinous power shines resplendent, upholding the celestial order above, ensuring timely winds and rains. Below, they grasp the axis of the earth, presiding over plowing and harvest, expanding the august rule of our dynasty. The realm’s longevity is secured, the people sheltered; profound and boundless is this divine work, enduring as of old.

Tingjie, entrusted with the mandate to govern this land, comes in due ritual to pay homage to the gods. At this first audience, how dare I presume to petition the divine? Yet my plea is neither frivolous nor self-serving—it is to beg for the people’s sake. The summer floods brought ruin; the winter drought has grown severe. Yang encroaches while Yin lies subdued; the imperial spring decree is thwarted. The winter wheat withers, and the people suffer. The gods rely on the people, and the people rely on sustenance—how can we bear to see them brought to such extremity? If the gods are angered, or if the fault lies with this governor, then let the blame rest with me alone. What guilt do the people bear? I humbly beseech the hidden workings of transformation to manifest their numinous bounty, sending clouds and snow to quench this desperate thirst. Should punishment be due, I, the governor, accept it willingly—let not the people endure further hardship. I cry out in anguish; may the gods heed my plea. May the spirits partake.

In the second month of the twenty-third year of the Jiajing reign.

*Memorial of Thanksgiving to the Sacred Peak*:

I, the governor, have failed in my duties and offended the numinous powers, bringing about this winter drought. Thus, in restless distress, I prostrated myself beneath the temple eaves to plead for the people’s sake. By the grace of the gods, the blazing sun was soon veiled, thick clouds gathered in layers, sleet and snow fell first, followed by gentle rains, drenching the land in abundant moisture. The response was as swift as an echo—yet the officials and people know not how to repay this favor, and I, Cheng Cai, can only express my humble gratitude.

The fault lies with me, the governor; what virtue do I possess to move the gods? What sincerity could I offer to touch their hearts? Yet the august gods, upright and benevolent, refused to punish the people of this remote western land on my account. Their glorious and mighty deeds must be duly acknowledged with thanksgiving.

Having received the gods’ forgiveness, from this day forward, I shall redouble my efforts to refine my conduct, eradicate my flaws, and earnestly inquire into the people’s sufferings, striving never to bring shame upon the gods. May the gods continue to watch over and protect us.

The offering is made. May the spirits partake.

Second month of the twenty-third year of the Jiajing reign.

**Memorial to Mount Tai by Zheng Yun, Surveillance Commissioner and Censor-in-Chief of Shandong:**

Of the gods, none so profoundly shapes the workings of creation, manifesting benevolence and might to match Heaven’s grandeur. Your virtue stands peerless among the Five Peaks, your towering merit a counterpart to the eight rivers’ flow. From ancient times to the present, your sacred titles have been exalted, and you remain the revered gaze of Qi and Lu. Five portents respond in season, six treasuries rest in tranquility.

I, Yun, humbly receive the imperial charge, guiding the reins of this land, upholding the standards of governance as your guiding principle. In spring’s life-giving bounty and autumn’s measured restraint, in the purging of corruption and the fostering of virtue, even to the regional and judicial offices, your luminous gaze descends. In silent accord with the spirit, how dare I not approach with solemn reverence?

Thus, I present these offerings of meat and wine, adhering to time-honored rites. With clarity and sincerity, I make this offering, beseeching your aid. May your celestial merits flourish, your decrees shine resplendent. May the land below forever depend on your glory, your radiance made manifest.

May the spirits partake.

**The twenty-third year of the Jiajing reign.**

---

**Memorial to Mount Tai by Wang Shu, Governor and Censor-in-Chief:**

It is said that the records of antiquity extol your majesty, your splendor and brilliance reigning supreme among the Five Peaks—this is the great work of the gods. From the touch of stone, clouds arise; from a mere inch, they gather, and in less than a morning, rain falls upon all under Heaven—this is the gods’ power. That I, Wang, traverse the realm, accumulating merit and bestowing favors to benefit this province, is also the gods’ singular grace.

Thus, timely rain and sunshine are rightly the gods’ first duty, and the private hope of this region. Yet no rain has come. From spring to the cusp of midsummer, across all four borders, three seasons have suffered. The wheat harvest is lost, and how can the grain crops hope for the year? By night, we lament the Milky Way; by day, we suffer the dust-laden wind. The people sigh in distress, and Heaven above bears resentment. Is it not a grievous failure that the gods hasten to aid this land?

Moreover, I, Shu, was commanded to govern here, tasked only with safeguarding and ordering, exhausting my heart and strength. If I were to purchase rain through bribes, I would still look to the august gods—such is the nature of my station. Yet if I or my officials have secretly harbored deceit, straying from sincerity, then let the gods’ punishment fall upon us alone. What guilt do the people bear? Why should those who serve in office endure this scourge?

With solemn purification and reflection, I dispatch Instructor Hu Dabing to perform the *yu* rites. May the gods heed the morning’s divine power, bestowing the blessings of a thousand *li*. Then not only shall I and the people of this region find solace in answered hopes, but the gods’ trustworthiness before all under Heaven, their radiance through past and present, may also be at stake.

May the gods heed this plea.

May the spirits partake.

In the fifth month of the thirty-first year of the Jiajing reign, Censor-in-Chief and Regional Inspector Shen Yinglong’s *Address to Mount Yue*:

Is the numen of the mountain merely to be revered for its soaring cliffs, jagged ridges, valleys that cradle winding streams, and its towering, perilous heights that serve as a bulwark for the region? Nay, it is also entrusted with unlocking the mysteries of the unseen, stirring the primordial essence, warding off calamities, repelling misfortune, bestowing blessings upon the people, and safeguarding the state. Consider the spirit of Mount Tai: it stands preeminent among the peaks, embodying the divine work of life’s governance, its fragrance lingering through the ages, drawing homage from all directions—is it not the foremost of the sacred mountains under Heaven?

Yet this spring, unrelenting drought has blighted the winter wheat; this summer, ceaseless rains have left the people scarce of grain. Where is the prosperity that heralds auspicious omens? Why have the seasons reversed their bounty? Is this but the travail of natural disasters, which even the most sagely of eras cannot escape?

I, Yinglong, unworthy as I am, have been charged with pacifying and governing the eastern lands, and the spirits of these mountains and rivers have been graciously entrusted to my care. Thus, I have traversed sheer crags, pushed through dense thickets, ascended verdant hills, and journeyed through spring fields to perform the rites, to pray for abundant harvests, to seek blessings for the people, and to ensure I do not fail in the sacred duty of the Son of Heaven, who cherishes and honors the myriad spirits with reverence and solemnity. This is no mere antiquarian pursuit or quest for rare marvels, nor an idle delight in gazing upon the vast emptiness below or the boundless expanse above.

O Mountain, whose majesty mirrors Heaven itself, may you aid my resolve, manifest your benevolent protection, and bring great peace to this land. May you secure the sacred mandate as firmly as the roots of the mulberry, and fortify the imperial legacy for eternity. Then shall the wonders of Mount Tai, famed throughout Qi and Lu, be ever more revered, and the veneration it has received from successive sages be proven true.

May the spirit heed this plea.

**Second Prayer for Rain:**

Heaven and Earth give life to the myriad things, and it is the office of the spirits to sustain and enact this. The sagely Son of Heaven nurtures the ten thousand people, and it is the office of ministers like us to sustain and enact this. Now, in the various prefectures of Shandong, the drought has grown dire. The wheat seedlings wither, and the people are in panic. Signs of famine spread, and warnings of parched ponds arrive daily. We, the ministers, pass sleepless nights in dread, at a loss for what to do.

We, your humble and dull servants, have failed in our duty to safeguard and govern, bringing suffering to the people—a crime we cannot evade. Yet you, O Spirit, are wise, luminous, upright, and able to summon clouds and rain. Can you, too, neglect your duty and willingly accept Heaven and Earth’s punishment?

We, the ministers, reverently uphold the imperial command and solemnly perform these rites, humbly offering this plea to beg for the people’s lives. We pray you look upon our sincerity, turn the workings of creation, and send forth a deluge of sweet rain to soak the four fields. Let the withered revive, and the Three Nurturers’ hopes be fulfilled. May you dispel disaster before it takes shape and transform calamity into blessing.

We, the ministers, will forever bear the boundless grace of your luminous power, and you, O Spirit, shall know boundless glory. Hear this plea. Partake of the offering.

Second month, Jiajing thirty-second year.

Memorial from Supervising Censor Feng Jian of Shandong, *Prayer to the Sacred Mountain*:

In the lands of Lianyu in the East, conditions fall far short of antiquity. Taxes and corvees weigh heavily; even in years of harvest, the people scatter and flee. Recently stricken by floods, they are left destitute, unable to sustain themselves—elders and children languish on the roads, while the strong gather in restless bands. Their suffering is too grievous to witness.

The Sagely Son of Heaven, moved by the hardships of the times, has dispatched eminent ministers to distribute famine relief. Thus, life on the brink of extinction revives, yet all still pin their hopes on this year’s autumn harvest. But now the wheat withers in drought, and farming lies abandoned. Public and private coffers are exhausted—how shall we endure?

You, O Spirit, are the famed Sacred Mountain of the East, able to summon clouds and rain to give life to all things. From ancient times to the present, your worship has been revered in the rites. If the people suffer, those who govern this land deserve censure—but I fear even you, who preside over this soil, cannot remain at ease. May you, in your majesty, heed the benevolence of our sagely ruler and grant the plea of this humble servant. Bestow your numinous essence in abundance to alleviate the people’s afflictions.

Your virtue and grace shall flow endlessly.

Respectfully offered.

In the second month of the thirty-second year of the Jiajing reign. *Memorial to the Sacred Mountain* by Yu Dan, Surveillance Commissioner and Imperial Censor of Shandong:

With reverence, we present sacrificial offerings of meat and wine, and dare to make this solemn declaration to the Spirit of the Eastern Sacred Mountain, Mount Tai:

O august and radiant Founder of the East, your rites of worship endure unchanging. Your divine power is vast in its bestowal, nurturing all things and manifesting your numinous efficacy. You establish the supremacy of the East and adorn the peace of the earth. Through the ages, local offerings have been made in gratitude, repaying your virtue and celebrating your accomplishments.

In our present imperial dynasty, the rituals of worship remain luminous. We, undeserving, serve in this land and now approach with solemn offerings, seeking to invoke your bright and fragrant presence. May your responsive power be manifest, fortifying our grand enterprise and perpetually elevating us to abundance.

May you partake in these offerings.

On the thirtieth day of the [missing character] month in the thirty-third year of the Jiajing reign. *Memorial to the Sacred Mountain* by Duan Guyan, Surveillance Commissioner and Imperial Censor of Shandong; Xu Wentong, Left Assistant Administration Commissioner of the Provincial Administration Commission; Wang Shizhen, Li Song, Vice Commissioners of the Provincial Surveillance Commission; Wang Lin, Zhang Shifen, Administration Commissioners; and Li Chunhua, Acting Brigade Commander of the Regional Military Commission:

We have heard that the great virtue of Heaven and Earth is the giving of life, and that the primordial qi of ceaseless generation, in its fullness and concentration, is called Mount Tai.

For "Tai" signifies "Great Harmony"—the qi of Heaven and Earth in perfect harmony resides wholly therein.

Thus, when this [primordial qi] unfolds in time, it is spring, and the Divine Sovereign presides over it; when it spreads in space, it is the East, and the Divine Sovereign holds it exclusively. How vast is the creative power of Heaven! How supreme is the receptive power of Earth! Both originate and complete, begin and end here—this is what is called the treasury of Heaven and Earth’s love for life, and the foremost ancestor of the Four Sacred Peaks. As for its towering cliffs and boundless expanse, these are matters beyond discussion.

Therefore, from the time of the Lord of Yu to the present illustrious dynasty, all have performed solemn sacrifices and offered reverent gratitude—rightly so. I, your humble servant, now inspect the Qi-Lu region, charged with maintaining order, rooting out evil, and suppressing violence, duties that seem akin to the life-giving Way of the Great Spirit. Yet can my heart, my essence, my bearing, my sincerity truly move the Spirit to respond? Alas! I know it cannot be so.

I have heard that spring gives life and autumn brings death—such is the flow of dark transformation. That good is rewarded and evil punished is the numinous power of the enlightened Spirit. To suppress wickedness and manifest virtue is the governance of the Great Lord, modeled on Heaven. Thus, when the Way of life employs death to sustain life, life becomes inexhaustible; when the mechanism of fortune employs misfortune to secure fortune, fortune becomes inexhaustible.

From this, I understand that the Great Lord’s manifest governance, aided by hidden forces, perfects transformation. And I, your humble servant, observing customs and holding the reins of office, am but carrying out the sovereign’s mandate to bring it to the people—is this truly different? Ah! The Spirit does not accept sacrifices that violate propriety; the noble person does not blindly worship a Spirit that does not respond. May the Great Spirit deem my words not unworthy.

I humbly pray that You discern my sincerity, accept my offerings, and withhold Your censure. Respectfully submitted.

The twenty-seventh day of the fifth month in the summer of the *wuwu* year of the Jiajing reign.

Governor and Censor-in-Chief Zhang Jian’s *Offering Text to the Eastern Marchmount* states:

Tracing the lands of the east, the domains of Qing and Yan were first established, the old borders of Qi and Lu, where the transformations of sages, worthies, kings, and hegemons flourished, and the methods of ritual propriety and prosperity thrived. Since the August Ming assumed the throne, this has become the left flank of the capital’s hinterland, bounded by the eastern sea and the western river defenses. O resplendent and luminous Spirit, You indeed preside over the *Zhen* direction.

I, Jian, have received the enlightened mandate to govern this region. Thus, my tour of inspection and Your awe-inspiring power both hold the charge of upholding the principles of the seen and unseen. Reflecting on the eastern lands, my anxious heart wanders in distress. Famine and dearth have befallen us; official conduct has faltered. Excessive exactions, banditry, and disease have brought darkness and inundation, flight and exile. Household registers have dwindled; fields and paths lie desolate. Comparing what my eyes now witness to the prosperity of ancient times, I am moved to sorrowful indignation.

Since assuming office, I have risen early and retired late in trepidation. Looking up, I have received Your divine aid: timely rains and sunshine, abundant harvests of the hundred grains, and fields lush with ripened crops. Not only have the people not been forced to gather every last stalk, but even the gleanings left behind have sufficed to reveal the hidden stores of the wealthy and leave the poor with surplus. This is the blessing You have bestowed upon our people—thereby ensuring the well-being of one in my position.

Those who remain now enjoy the joy of fruitful years, but how can those who have departed be without longing for their native soil? With mulberry and catalpa ever in mind, the debts owed to them are difficult to repay. Those who think of returning do not come; those who come do not prosper. In summoning them back, reclaiming the land, and opening new fields, who would dare rest idle? Thus, I have adjusted taxes by household, equalized levies by land division, petitioned the Son of Heaven above—who has graciously granted assent—and held accountable the local officials below. Should any among them harbor deceitful hearts or trifle with the law, enriching themselves at the people’s expense, the majesty of the law will be made manifest, and they will bring ruin upon themselves.

Were it not for the luminous Spirit repeatedly granting us years of plenty and nurturing us with harmonious *qi*, the people would be pressed by poverty, disease, and epidemics—how could they then enjoy lasting prosperity in their homeland? My governance and beneficence have been clear and thorough, yet Your hidden and silent aid has been the true radiance before which I stand in awe.

My aim is neither to betray the sacred intention of the Son of Heaven in shepherding the common folk nor to fail my lifelong devotion to public duty and concern for the state. When the lanes of the countryside and the wilds of the suburbs are fully restored to their ancient prosperity, it will also redound to the glory of the luminous Spirit of the East. With this in mind, I respectfully present sacrificial offerings, accompanied by black and yellow silks. Having purified myself and maintained ritual purity, I reverently proclaim this address: in gratitude for the fruitful years past, in supplication for the timely blessings to come, and in earnest repayment of my sincerity—words cannot encompass its depth. May You reward the good and protect the upright, O Spirit, with Your auspicious favor.

I, Jian, with a humble heart full of earnestness, know that Your presence will be vast and abundant. May You deign to accept these offerings.

In the forty-first year of the Jiajing reign, Censor-in-Chief and Provincial Governor Fu Xizhi’s *"Sacrificial Address to the Sacred Peak"* states:

Recently, the Grand Canal has overflowed its banks, obstructing the vital transport route. By special imperial decree, a high minister has been appointed to oversee its dredging and restoration. O Spirit, You majestically guard this region, warding off all calamities. Whether in the confluence of rivers or the springs that issue from the mountains, whenever officials are dispatched to perform rites and offer prayers, we humbly beseech You to witness our solemn resolve. Unburden our deepest concerns, swiftly quell the surging floods, and grant success to this great endeavor, that the transport of grain may flow unimpeded and the people’s prosperity endure without end. This we reverently proclaim.

In the sixth year of the Longqing reign, Censor-in-Chief and Provincial Governor Lu Shude’s *"Address to the Sacred Peak"* states:

O Spirit, You preside over the venerable peaks, embodying the primordial force of generation. Across the vast expanse of heaven and earth, who does not revere and depend upon Your grace? Within these boundless lands, Your qi is dispensed and Your essence spread—every act, every breath, relies upon and rests in You.

Yet of late, the skies have withheld their rains, and the people’s cries grow ever more desperate. As governor, I have grieved over this and hastened to pray in the courtyard. I have also issued orders to all officials, urging them to join in earnest supplication. But the rains have lingered, slow to arrive, while the heavens remain distant and inscrutable. Is it due to the many flaws in governance, or the lack of sincerity in our prayers?

Just as I prepared to dispatch an envoy to present this address at Your shrine, clouds gathered before the draft was even completed, and rain fell before the messenger could depart. O Spirit, Your compassion for the people is as swift as an echo answering a call. How fortunate I am, undeserving of such honor! With reverence, I have purified the sacrificial offerings and sent an official to present these words. Humbly, I beseech Your radiant power: may Your boundless virtue and grace descend without fail. Let the rains come in due season, from the weak streams of the He to the eastern reaches of the Qi, covering all without exception, drenching all without stint. Thus may the withered flourish, the parched be tilled, the people’s anxieties dissolve, and the land know abundant harvests. May You deign to accept these offerings.

The Censor-in-Chief and Provincial Governor Li Fu’s *"Address to the Sacred Peak"* states:

O Spirit, You preside over the eastern lands, sheltering all beneath the heavens. The rise and fall of the state’s revenues lie within Your governance, and the weal and woe of the living people rest in Your sovereign hand. Your awe-inspiring power has shone through the ages, and Your blessings have long secured the peace of this realm.

Yet in Shandong these past years, yin and yang have fallen into discord, and disasters and crop failures have followed one upon another. Winter brought no snow, spring scarce rain, and now as summer arrives, ceaseless winds prevail. The wheat, though in bud, withers; the grain, though timely, lies unplanted. Farmers wander in distress across the fields, while officials and clerks tremble in their offices. Though prayers have been offered for days on end, still the clouds gather not, nor does the rain fall.

I cannot but reflect: the people of Shandong are already crushed beneath the wheels of fate, and if this year’s harvest fails again, how shall they endure? The taxes of Shandong are as dense as autumn thorns, yet their households stand empty as upturned vessels—how shall they meet their obligations? Though we officials have failed in our duties, what guilt do these common folk bear?

I humbly beseech Your august presence, O Spirit, Whose divine radiance is ever near, Whose gaze pierces the finest thread. If fault lies with us, then let punishment descend upon me in the unseen realm. But if this is merely the travail of the times, I dare implore Your compassion: release the yin to restrain the yang, stir the clouds to spread the rain, that our hundred grains may flourish, the lowlands teem with abundance, and carts groan beneath the harvest. Bestow Your grace upon this multitude, that women and children may laugh through the year.

If not—if our pleas go unanswered—how then shall Your power be called mighty? If our prayers go unfulfilled, how then shall the people praise Your blessings? I, unworthy as I am, have poured forth my sincerest entreaties.

With reverence, I present this address.

In the thirteenth year of the Wanli reign, Prefect Li Bochun offered the address on behalf of the people; as the prayer concluded, rain fell.

Li Fu, Censor-in-Chief and Governor, composed *An Address to the Sacred Peak*:

This year, from the onset of spring through the fourth month of summer, no rain has fallen. From the imperial capital to the Huai River plains, parched lands stretch beyond a thousand li. Here in the eastern provinces—Your Majesty’s left flank—the people suffer ever more grievously. Though earlier rains brought some relief, the moisture barely penetrated the soil. Now, the seedlings fail to ear, and those that do bear no grain, withering once more. The people murmur in distress, crying out to Heaven without cease, near and far alike.

The Son of Heaven grieves and toils: he has set aside rich viands and silenced music, issued edicts of self-reproach, and left the imperial roads unswept. From dawn, he walks on foot from the palace to the suburban altars, standing barefoot in the mulberry groves to pray, pleading for the people’s lives, sparing no effort in his devotion.

I, entrusted with governance in these eastern lands, have failed in my duty—how can I shift blame? I think of these ignorant folk, soon to perish in ditches and gullies, and my heart aches without respite. As the lord’s sorrow is the minister’s shame, I prostrate myself upon the mats, unable to rest even for a moment.

O Spirit, whose sacred seat upholds the Great Peace and whose office sustains all living things—how can You bear to watch these people daily perish in such numbers? I, Li Fu, humbly lead my subordinates to present our sincere supplications before You. May Your divine clarity punish us officials for our failure toward the people, yet spare the harvest from further calamity. Let but a hand’s breadth of rain fall across the land before noon, that our millet and sorghum may flourish, and the long-standing toil of our sovereign be comforted. Then these remnant people may yet offer abundant sacrifices, filling the vessels with rich offerings to sustain Your rites without want.

O Spirit, look upon my earnest devotion and grant me a sign of favor. I await Your decree with trembling reverence.

Respectfully offered on the twenty-fifth day of the fourth month in the yiyou year of the Wanli reign. May the sacrifice be accepted.

**Memorial to the Sacred Peak by Li Fu, Censor-in-Chief and Provincial Governor:**

Since the transition from spring to summer, fierce winds have wrought havoc, and the scorching qi of drought has burned like fire. The summer wheat hangs withered, and the autumn grain remains unsown.
