I, Fu, having received the imperial mandate to govern and pacify the eastern lands, look upon the people’s hardship with deep sorrow and a heart heavy with grief.

Earlier, I purified my intentions and prepared offerings to present my supplications before You. By Your divine clarity, You deigned to witness my sincerity and bestowed Your numinous blessings. Twice I offered prayers, and thrice the rains came. The withered wheat was half-revived, and the new grain began to flourish. Each response was as swift as the echo to the drum, ever more confirming Your unfailing presence; each drop fell like rich ghee, a boundless outpouring of compassion. Yet while the timely rains have eased the hopes of the farmers, the moisture has not been sufficient, and the fear of renewed drought still lingers.

Moreover, from the capital’s outskirts to the distant provinces, it is said that for three seasons not a drop of rain has fallen, and across thousands of li, not a grain of food remains. This has caused Our Sovereign profound distress, and all officials to stand vigilant. It concerns the altars of state above and the lives of the people below—how could my own trepidation alone be cause for alarm, when Your divine heart must also be moved to pity?

Thus, I do not weary of repeated supplication but prostrate myself anew, offering thanks for past blessings while entreating future favor. I humbly beseech You to manifest Your great power, to send forth hidden clouds and boundless rains. Let the sweet showers know no borders, falling in torrents everywhere; let Your vast benevolence blanket the realm, bringing joy to all. Sweep away the scorching fiends of drought, and turn our faces toward the songs of a bountiful year. I, Fu, await Your decree with a heart full of gratitude and reverence.

Respectfully offered. In the bingxu year of the Wanli reign, Vice Prefect Zhao Chengfang presented this memorial on my behalf. As the rites concluded, the rains arrived.

---

**Memorial to the Sacred Peak by Li Dai, Censor-in-Chief and Provincial Governor:**

Of all the renowned mountains under heaven, the Five Peaks stand supreme; among the Five Peaks, none is more revered than the Sacred Peak of the East. Firmly rooted in the earth’s foundation, it presides over the eastern lands, its divine efficacy matched only by Heaven, its vast power nurturing all life. Here in Qi and Lu, we ought to bask in its abundant grace.

I, Dai, have received the imperial command to govern the eastern regions. Upon entering this land, I found the ravages of drought—fields parched, people gaunt—and my heart filled with dread. I personally visited Your sacred shrine to pray for the people’s well-being. By Your gracious regard, You showed me Your boundless favor: as soon as the words of my prayer were spoken, the numinous rains began to fall. The following day, as my carriage turned homeward, clouds gathered and rain descended in abundance—a great deluge of sweet showers, a magnificent display of Your divine power. The farmers’ hopes were answered, and all things flourished. Yet how could my gratitude ever repay such merit? Your towering virtue defies description.

I have dispatched an official to perform the rites in my stead, offering pure sacrifices to fulfill this act of thanksgiving and to express my humble devotion. I humbly pray that wind and rain may come in due season, that no calamities arise, and that the people may forever sing of prosperity and abundance. I, Dai, await Your decree with boundless joy and reverence.

Respectfully offered.

On the fifth day of the fifth month, the fourteenth year of Wanli.

Li Dai, Censor-in-Chief and Provincial Governor, addresses the Spirit in this *Announcement to the Sacred Peak*:

Of all divine powers whose transformative influence extends across the Nine Regions, whose rank surpasses the Hundred Spirits, who enriches the people and sustains the realm—none shines more resplendently in the imperial court than You. Here in the eastern lands, incense and offerings rise in ceaseless fragrance; every household invokes Your name, every home relies upon Your protection. Yet why has Heaven shown no mercy? Why has such great calamity descended? For three springs, no rain has fallen, and the wheat has withered away; for nine summers, no rain has come, and the grain stands barren of ears. The nation’s resources and the people’s livelihood now face utter depletion.

Our sagacious sovereign, grieving for the afflicted, has fasted and forgotten meals; the roads are filled with displaced multitudes, the common folk emaciated and broken. If Your heroic spirit retains awareness, how could You bear to witness this? Your boundless compassion and universal salvation are our sole hope, reverently entrusted to Your crimson altar.

I, Dai, possess no extraordinary means to relieve this famine, and my heart aches as if in prayer. Day and night, I have purified myself and fasted, beseeching on behalf of the people. May the Spirit heed this plea and grant us Your cherishing protection. Let timely rains and sweet showers revive this withered land.

Respectfully announced.

On the twentieth day of the sixth month, the fourteenth year of Wanli.

Assistant Vice Commissioner of the Shandong Salt Distribution Bureau, Zha Zhilong, in *A Prayer for the Compilation of the Tai History*, states:

From my earliest years in the family school, reciting the teachings of Confucius and Mencius, I heard the saying that "ascending Mount Tai makes the world seem small," and never failed to feel my heart stir and my gaze lift, longing for the joy of setting foot upon its slopes. In the ren-shen year, I passed through on official business and briefly climbed its heights, but soon returned to my post, resigned to parting forever from the mountain’s spirit. How could I have hoped for the fortune of Cheng Bozi, who, while serving as magistrate of E, compiled a record of its scenic wonders?

Unexpectedly, after a twelve-year interval, I was reassigned to a post within a hand’s reach of the mountain’s spirit. When the provincial inspector, Academician Tan of Lingnan, was sent to survey the sacred peaks, he sought local gazetteers and entrusted me with the task of compiling them. Fortunately, in this era of clear governance and retiring officials, I found myself free from daily duties, able to wander in thought among its forests and streams. Did the mountain’s spirit intend this leisure to fulfill my long-held wish?

I thought to make use of this opportunity to offer at least a drop of service. Though my rank is humble and my talents meager, I dared not remain idle. Bowing my head to the task, I labored without regard for cold or heat, burning midnight oil at my dawn-lit desk, gathering the ancient and the modern. After nearly four months, the draft was complete.

I conducted three investigations: *An Illustrated Study*, *A Study of Celestial Correspondences*, and *A Study of Strategic Topography*. I composed two tables: *A Table of Mountains and Waters* and *A Table of Boundaries*. I recorded four chronicles: *A Chronicle of Imperial Hunts*, *A Chronicle of Imperial Vistas*, *A Chronicle of Relics*, and *A Chronicle of Sacred Temples*. I compiled five treatises: *A Treatise on Palaces and Halls*, *A Treatise on Local Products*, *A Treatise on Incense Taxes*, *A Treatise on Disasters and Portents*, and *A Treatise on Ascents and Views*. And I bound these together under the title *Tai History*.

The name "Tai Zong" dates back to the Yellow Emperor, and today it is fitting to return to its ancient origin. The method of "recording" is the exclusive province of historians, and I have modestly followed its conventions. Yet I fear that my insights are shallow and my vision narrow; that my travels have not reached every remote crag or hidden vale; that broken steles and missing texts have eluded my gaze. These shortcomings fill me with deep trepidation.

At the auspicious altar below the temple, I respectfully present this draft to the divine, intending to ascend its perilous heights and survey its expanse. Dare I presume to rival the achievements of ancient worthies or to summon the clouds of Mount Heng? I can only hope that the divine will look upon me with favor and silently aid my endeavor—dispelling clouds and revealing the sun, unveiling every marvel, that I may face the scene with brush in hand, as if inspired by heaven. Then, perhaps, I will not betray the mountain’s spirit in obscurity, nor fail the trust placed in me, nor waste the toil of leaning on my desk and unrolling my scrolls. For this, I rely upon the unseen assistance of the divine.

Respectfully announced.
On the fourteenth day of the tenth month, the fourteenth year of Wanli.

After the prayer was completed, I ascended the summit of Mount Tai. For three days, the skies remained clear, allowing me to traverse all its strategic sites, just as I had hoped.

*Chronicle of Mount Tai, Volume Eight: Record of Relics*
The Master said: What is meant by "recording relics"? It is to document the splendid traces of antiquity, those that remain visible to this day and have not yet vanished. As eras and times change, things transform with the age. In examining the present to understand the past, how could one do so without relying on traces?

This Mount Tai—so grand in the institutions of emperors and kings, so flourishing in the culture of sages and worthies—abounds in splendid relics, too numerous to enumerate in detail. Among the most significant: the Hall of Abundant Brilliance evokes the glorious governance of Yu and the Zhou; ascending the Terrace of the Feng Sacrifice stirs reflections on the extravagant ambitions of rulers through the ages. Reading the Qin seal script stele or the cliff inscriptions reveals the calligraphy of masterful hands; standing where Confucius once stood, one grasps the vastness of his "smaller than all under heaven" vision. Treading the Fields of Wenyang or passing the City of Tutu, one admires the sagely strategies of military preparedness; recalling Yan Yuan’s gaze upon the fulling mallet or Zengzi’s song at Liang Mountain, one draws near to the assembly of outstanding disciples at the gate of the Sage. Investigating Sun Mingfu’s lecture stone or Shoudao’s staff and sandals, one remembers the refined conduct of masters and disciples of old.

All these sites remain intact, their records preserved. Their lingering influence and residual elegance endure alongside Mount Tai itself—such is the power of what moves across a thousand years to stir the heart. Here too are the wanderings of the Daoist immortals, ever present in the grotto-heavens and blessed lands, and thus they are included as well.

**Imperial Relics**
*Jade Slips*: The *Fengsu Tong* states: "Atop the Peak of the Eastern Sacred Mountain lies a golden coffer and jade slips, which reveal the length of a person’s lifespan." Emperor Wu of Han drew a slip and obtained the number eighteen; reading it backward as eighty, he indeed lived to eighty. In the early Hongwu reign of our dynasty, a local resident discovered a jade casket in the mountains containing sixteen jade slips. The authorities presented them to the court, where their inscriptions were verified as the text of Emperor Zhenzong of Song’s sacrifice to the Earth Goddess of Mount Tai. Later, in the autumn of the *renyin* year of the Chenghua reign, a jade slip was washed out by rainwater beneath the Peak of Sunrise Observation. A eunuch envoy on a mission to the eastern vassals hastened to present it, and it was ordered to be returned to its original place. The present Temple of Sunlight Observation was built there.

*Stone Chamber*: The *Han Shu* records: "Emperor Wu of Han performed the Feng sacrifice on Mount Tai and the Shan sacrifice at Stone Chamber." Ying Shao notes: "Stone Chamber lies south of Mount Tai, where alchemists claim immortals dwell." The *Record of Mount Tai* states: "The western cliff at the summit is the Stone Inquiry of the Immortals." Its original site is no longer identifiable.

*Terrace of the Feng Sacrifice*: There are two. One stands at the summit, traditionally said to have been built by ancient emperors for the Feng sacrifice. It now serves as the terrace of the Jade Emperor’s Temple, with a small stone tablet below inscribed *Ancient Altar of the Feng Sacrifice*. The other is on the Peak of Sunrise Observation, said to have been constructed by the Song. It is a stone coffer roughly a *zhang* square, also inscribed *Ancient Altar of the Feng Sacrifice*. Both mistakenly conflate the Feng and Shan sacrifices. Locals call it the "Treasure Repository," claiming it houses golden texts and jade slips.

Guanzi states: "The Lord of No-Year sacrificed on Mount Tai and carved stone to record his merit." Guo Pu’s *Rhapsody on the Capital* says: "The Yellow Emperor convened his ministers on Mount Tai and composed the music of *Qingjiao*." The *Records of the Grand Historian* notes: "In antiquity, seventy-two rulers performed the Feng sacrifice on Mount Tai and the Shan sacrifice at Liangfu, of which Guan Zhong recorded twelve." Long ago, the Lord of No-Year, predating Fuxi, performed the Feng sacrifice on Mount Tai and the Shan sacrifice at Liangfu. Li Qi remarks: "The Lord of No-Year was the first to perform the Feng and Shan sacrifices." The *Huainanzi* states: "The kings of high antiquity performed the Feng sacrifice on Mount Tai and the Shan sacrifice at Liangfu over seventy times." An ancient poem inscribed on Mount Tai reads: "A realm of ghosts and spirits three thousand *li* wide, where seventy-two lords performed the Feng and Shan sacrifices." The *Brush Notes of Master Jingwen of the Song* records: "When Confucius ascended Mount Tai, he saw the scripts of seventy-two rulers, each in a different hand."

From these accounts, it is clear that the Feng and Shan sacrifices originated with the ancient emperors, not beginning with the Qin. Yet Master Wenzhong claimed that the Feng and Shan sacrifices were not ancient, and the Confucians all criticized him. Were those earlier accounts mere fabrications to flatter the extravagant ambitions of later rulers? Here they are preserved to mark the ancient traces.

*Zhou Hall of Abundant Brilliance*: Located northeast of the peak, its valley stretches forty *li*, and its ruins remain today. Nearby stands Gushan Temple, whose scenic beauty is fully described in Academician Ke’s *Temple Record*.

*Han Hall of Abundant Brilliance*: Situated southeast at the foot of the peak, ten *li* from the prefectural seat. Emperor Wu of Han built it in the *yuanfeng* era based on a diagram presented by Gong Yudai of Qi. Atop it, Yuan-era inscriptions read *Former Site of the Hall of Abundant Brilliance*.

The terrain here stretches expansively before rising abruptly—a solitary stone ridge, its summit flat and towering some four *zhang* in height, with a circumference of roughly three *li*. Behind it, a spur of the mountain’s foothills serves as a natural screen, while to its left and right, the landscape appears as if arrayed in escort. A ravine’s waters wind and coil before merging southward into the Wen River. From afar, the peaks of Zulai and other mountains resemble a row of folding screens and low tables. The scale of imperial assemblies of old remains vividly before one’s eyes.

*Zhang Junxiang’s Discourse on the Hall of Abundant Brilliance*:
The Hall of Abundant Brilliance originated with the Yellow Emperor. During the reign of Emperor Wu of Han, Gong Yudai of Jinan presented *Yang’s ‘Rituals of Sacrifice and the Hall of Abundant Brilliance’*, but its contents were deemed unorthodox and thus excised. Yet his description of a thatched roof with water channels aligns closely with the *Rites of Dai the Elder*. Moreover, the *Artificers’ Record* notes that the Xia dynasty’s "Hall of the Ancestors," the Shang’s "Double Roof," and the Zhou’s "Hall of Abundant Brilliance" evolved from simplicity to refinement, from narrowness to grandeur. Extrapolating from this, if the Yellow Emperor indeed had a Hall of Abundant Brilliance, it would have been a single hall without walls, roofed with thatch—a true embodiment of ancient frugality.

Furthermore, Emperor Wu, seeking immortality and longevity, was often misled by the extravagant claims of magicians who fabricated tales of the Yellow Emperor to peddle their doctrines. Such fabrications—like the construction of the Five Cities and Twelve Towers, the enshrinement of myriad spirits in the Hall of Brilliance, or the casting of the cauldron from copper mined at Mount Shou—were gross exaggerations designed to flatter the extravagant ambitions of the ruler. Only Gong Yudai’s proposed design for the Hall of Abundant Brilliance was so unadorned and simple. Though unorthodox, it serves as a corrective to the excesses of palaces with thousands of gates and myriad chambers. Thus, it should not be dismissed merely because it emerged during an era of feng and shan sacrifices and the pursuit of immortality.

*The Great Wall and Massive Dikes*:
Located west of Mount Tai, these fortifications stretch along the Green River for over a thousand *li*, extending to Langye Terrace where they meet the sea. Su Dai, addressing the state of Yan, remarked, "Qi possesses the Great Wall and massive dikes." Han Feizi noted, "The Great Wall and massive dikes suffice as foundations." The *Records of Qi* state that King Xuan of Qi built the Great Wall atop mountain ridges, running east to the sea and west to Jizhou for over a thousand *li* to defend against Chu. The *Comprehensive Gazetteer of Geography* records that the Great Wall begins northwest of Pingyin County in Jizhou, follows the Green River along the northern ridges of Mount Tai, passes through Lichuan in Jizhou, then southwest to the north of Bocheng County in Yanzhou, and finally east to Langye Terrace in Mizhou, where it enters the sea. The *Bamboo Annals* state that in the twentieth year of King Hui of Liang, King Min of Qi constructed dikes to serve as a Great Wall. Today, the ridges northwest of Mount Tai retain the name "Great Wall," and their ruins remain visible.

*The Inscriptionless Stele*:
Standing at the base of the Terrace of Imperial Ascent atop the mountain’s summit, this stele was erected by the First Emperor of Qin. Some call it a stone marker, others a spirit tablet, while still others claim it conceals golden writings and jade slips within—a stone casket. Its purpose remains inscrutable, a testament to the Qin’s cunning. The stele’s stone is yellowish-white, smooth and lustrous, free of moss, and not native to Mount Tai. Transporting it from another mountain must have exacted an immense toll in labor—a stark example of the Qin’s ruthless exploitation of its people.

The *Records of the Grand Historian* mentions that Emperor Wu of Han placed five cartloads of stone on the mountain, though their whereabouts are now unknown. Thus, Pu’an’s poem laments:
*"The five carts of stone of Han Wu are lost to time,*
*Yet the Inscriptionless Stele of Qin still barks at the wind."*

The deeds of Qin are not worth recounting, but it is lamentable that Emperor Wu of Han emulated them.

*The Qin Seal Script Stele*:
Located behind the Western Official’s Residence above the Jade Maiden’s Pool, this stele bears the inscriptions of Li Si, eulogizing the virtues of the First Emperor and the Second Emperor of Qin. When Liu Ba of the Song dynasty compiled the *Catalogue of Qin Seal Scripts on Mount Tai*, 222 characters remained. Today, erosion has left only twenty-nine. Though the Qin dynasty was tyrannical, its achievements were unparalleled, particularly in calligraphy, where none in later ages could rival it. Such works should not be discarded.

Shen Yue inscribed the stone, calling it the "Broken Stele of Qin." In *Ouyang Xiu’s* *Collected Antiquities*, my friend Jiang Linji, demoted to an official post in Fengfu, once ascended Mount Tai’s summit and examined the site of the Qin inscriptions. He remarked that the stone was too hard to carve, yet the Qin somehow managed it. The area was barren of vegetation, untouched by wildfires, which preserved the stele for so long. Yet wind and rain have eroded it, leaving only a few dozen characters.

*Postscript to the Catalogue of Qin Seal Scripts*:
The *Records of the Grand Historian* notes that both the First Emperor of Qin and the Second Emperor toured the commanderies and counties, erecting steles with inscribed texts. Today, the only legible Qin seal script on Mount Tai is the Second Emperor’s inscription, comprising some fifty characters. The First Emperor’s inscriptions are said to have vanished, leaving no trace. When Lord Ju of the Song dynasty governed Dongping, he dispatched artisans to Mount Tai to create ink rubbings. In the year *qingli* *wuzǐ* (1048), he had the text carved onto a new stele and personally wrote a postscript, totaling forty-eight characters. Ouyang Xiu’s *Collected Antiquities* also records that his friend Jiang Linji, while serving as magistrate of Fenggao, visited the stele in person and found only a few dozen characters remaining.

In the spring of the second year of *Daguan* (1108), I ascended Mount Tai with several fellow villagers and spent the night at the summit. Seeking the Qin seal script, I lingered by the stele. The stone was partially buried in the earth, standing no more than four or five *chi* tall, its shape roughly square but irregular, with uneven sides left unpolished. The fifty-odd characters were inscribed on the slightly smoother southern face, where people often sat, making them more visible to scholars and officials. The other three sides were more eroded and obscured, drawing little attention. Upon close inspection, I discerned faint traces of characters. Scraping away the grime and erosion, I attempted to make rubbings, gradually revealing the text. Encouraged, I intensified my efforts, though I remained unsatisfied.

In the autumn of the third year of *Zhenghe* (1113), I returned to the mountain’s summit and personally oversaw the process, using felt and mallets to create rubbings. Comparing them with other versions, I finally achieved a complete and accurate reproduction. The stele’s four sides were fully inscribed, totaling twenty-two lines, each with twelve characters. The text begins on the southwest side, proceeding in order to the north, east, and south. The western face contains six lines, the northern three, the eastern six, and the southern seven. At the end, the phrase "Imperial Decree" (*zhì yuē kě*) appears, followed by three characters on the southwest edge. Each line maintains twelve characters, though the number of lines per side varies due to the stone’s uneven dimensions. The twelve lines constitute the First Emperor’s inscription, while the remaining ten belong to the Second Emperor.

Comparing this with the *Records of the Grand Historian*, the meaning is fully preserved, and calculating the missing portions, the character count aligns precisely. Thus, the seal script of Mount Tai is now complete. The Song scholars Ouyang [Xiu] and Jiang [Shao] had never visited the site themselves, relying solely on the accounts of artisans—no wonder their records were flawed. Many trusted the accounts of high officials and thus did not examine the text closely. Having obtained the inked copy and documented the stele’s form and structure, I returned home. Relatives and friends, upon hearing this, came to inquire, and weary of repeated explanations, I compiled this record.

In total, there are over two hundred and twenty seal-script characters, of which one hundred and forty-six remain legible. These I have transcribed in seal script. The seventy-six damaged or obliterated characters, no longer visible, are supplemented with the text from the *Records of the Grand Historian* and annotated below. The record complete, I displayed it on the wall. The once obscure and cryptic text is now clear and evident.

The Qin dynasty’s lack of propriety is not worth discussing, yet Li Si’s small seal script has been revered through the ages. After more than thirteen hundred years, its reemergence is truly remarkable. For instance, *qīn* (亲) was miswritten as *qīng* (轻), *lí* (黎) as *lí shǐ* (远黎史), *zuò* (作) as *xiū* (休), *sì* (嗣) as *shì* (世), *tīng* (听) as *shèng* (圣), *chuí* (陲) as *chuí* (垂), *tǐ* (体) as *lǐ* (礼), and *kūn* (昆) as *hòu* (后)—these are all errors in historical records, which should be corrected based on the stele.

The title "Imperial Censor Grandee" (*Yùshǐ Dàfū*) refers to *Dàfū* (Grandee). As *Zhuangzi* states: "Moreover, it was entrusted to the *Dàfū*." Wei Hong noted: "In ancient script, a single character could have two names." Thus, I have annotated accordingly. In the *Records of the Grand Historian*, the inscription at Langye Terrace lists the names of accompanying officials. Among the fragmentary Langye characters in my collection, the term *wǔ fū* (五夫) also appears—perhaps *fū* (夫) was omitted after *dà* (大) to avoid redundancy.

There are two cliff inscriptions on the mountain: one behind the Eastern Peak Temple at the summit, written in the *bafen* script by Emperor Xuanzong of Tang in *Record of the Inscription on Mount Tai*, with characters about five *cun* in size, vigorous and admirable; the other on the right side of the cliff, inscribed with Tang scholar Su E’s *Eulogy on the Eastern Sacred Ceremony*, characters over an inch in diameter, resembling Jin-dynasty calligraphy. Recently, Lin Dun of Fujian overlaid it with four large characters—*Loyalty, Filial Piety, Integrity, and Righteousness*—nearly obliterating the original.

Fenghuang Terrace, located outside the Dengfeng Gate, is named for the phoenixes that alighted on Mount Tai during the reign of Emperor Xuan of Han. Wangxian Terrace, thirty *li* northeast of the prefectural seat, was built by Emperor Wu of Han. Wuhu Terrace was constructed by Emperor Gaozong of Tang. According to the *Old唐书*, Emperor Gaozong built the Feng Sacrifice Altar four *li* south of Mount Tai, modeled after the Circular Mound, and also erected steles for the Dengfeng, Jiànchán, and Cháojìn ceremonies. He named the Feng Sacrifice Altar *Wuhu Terrace*, the Dengfeng Altar *Wansui Terrace*, and the Jiànchán Altar *Jingyun Terrace*. Today, all lie in ruins, and the steles have vanished into obscurity.

Zhang Yue’s preface to the eulogy states:
In the sixth generation of the illustrious Tang, the Divine Martial Emperor of Kaiyuan, having received the Mandate anew and achieved great peace, performed the Feng sacrifice at Mount Tai and the Shan sacrifice at Mount She. The golden steles recorded his reign, and celestial signs shone forth. Confucian ministers, moved by his virtue, erected monuments at the altars. It is said: At the dawn of humanity, when goodness arose among rulers and ministers, the Dao was dim and customs simple. As times changed, fortunes rose and fell, and all faded into oblivion, leaving no trace.

Later sages, drawing from celestial patterns and establishing ranks and titles, distinguished them with robes and regalia, fortified them with armor and weapons—thus rites and music emerged, and records were preserved. Returning to the source, they paid reverence to Heaven and Earth; repaying their origins, they offered homage to spirits and gods. Hence, the Feng and Shan sacrifices were the means by which emperors, having received the Mandate of Heaven, proclaimed their achievements.

Reviewing the arcane teachings of ancient sages and examining the universal methods of past sovereigns—who could match Heaven and yet fail? How could one turn from the Dao and not err? From this, we deduce that the meaning of the Feng and Shan rites comprises three principles, while the strategies of emperors encompass seven. What are these seven? The tradition speaks of virtue/power, benevolence, propriety, wisdom, and trust. To follow them is to be called sagely and wise; to oppose them is to be deemed reckless and perverse.

The three principles are:
First, one’s position must align with the sequence of the Five Phases in the cosmic records;
Second, the time must coincide with the era of universal peace;
Third, one’s virtue/power must embody reverence, luminosity, literary grace, and profound thought—only then does one harmonize with Heaven, and one’s name shall not perish.

Should any of these be lacking and one still claim the right to perform the Feng and Shan, not only would people withhold their approval—how could Heaven permit it?

Those who recount ancient histories declare: Among rulers, none surpassed Yao of Tang and Shun of Yu; among ministers, none excelled Gao Yao, Hou Ji, or Qi. These three ministers, though of subordinate rank, were endowed with virtue/power sufficient to govern the realm. Confucius, in compiling the records of emperors and kings, linked the oaths of Zhou and Qin, clarified that the Duke of Lu used royal rites to sacrifice to the Duke of Zhou, and noted that Qin inherited the legacy of Bo Yi to succeed the Zhou. Was this not the subtle intent of the Sage? Yet Qin’s merit in unifying the realm was great, though its enjoyment of Heaven’s favor was brief. Had Heaven not remembered the virtue/power of the House of Ying, the great Mandate would not have returned to the imperial house.

Does Heaven’s favor toward the Tang arise without precedent? In its rise, dark omens stirred, the Yellow Thearch gazed in contemplation, and destiny aligned with righteous action, bringing renewal and prosperity. Tang did not temporarily exile Xia, nor did Wu wage war to replace Yin. The High Ancestor founded the dynasty, and the Four Ancestors carried forth its radiance. Their virtue/power reached Heaven, and their blessings permeated the earth—springs welled forth, clouds billowed, dew descended, and even the lowliest creatures thrived. All was enveloped in the nurturing warmth of qi, as if Heaven and Earth covered and bore all, as if the sun and moon shone upon them. All things under form returned to their brilliance, and all beyond the external found their place in the heart. For one hundred and eight years, this has endured. Now, the Emperor has quelled internal strife and inaugurated a new Mandate, honoring the Ruizong Emperor while restoring the old order. The cosmos has been renewed, and the court has assumed its rightful place.

When the Fuxi and Xuanyuan clans first crafted the imperial plan, the nine kinship groups were harmonized, the hundred clans enlightened, the myriad states united in peace, and the common people transformed. They established the earthen gnomon to measure the calendar and reformed the bronze armillary to align the heavens. When the Tang and Yu clans expanded the imperial Dao, Heaven and Earth, the four seasons, and the six ministries were ordered by ritual. The well-field system divided the land into three grades, and the five domains established taxation. They enlarged the nine ancestral temples to honor forebears and fixed the six pitch-pipes to harmonize with the spirits. When the Three Dynasties instituted the royal system, martial might wove its warp, civil arts its weft; sagely counsels guided, and numinous transformations perfected. Yet still, they remained vigilant, day by day growing more cautious. They heeded admonitions to advance virtue, employed the loyal and capable to replace the unworthy, studied the texts of non-action, and deliberated in the Hall of Assembled Worthies. They ennobled scholars, esteemed classical learning, and arrayed their court like cranes and phoenixes in formation, while their frontier generals stood resolute as tigers and leopards. Human harmony spread far and wide, and divine treasures arrived in abundance. Heavenly omens and earthly treasures—thousands of kinds, ten thousand forms—overflowed the charts and records, unheard of in former times.

Our Sovereign regards human omens as paramount and does not concern himself with material portents. The dukes, ministers, and officials solemnly advance, saying: "How splendid, Your Majesty! Your filial piety reaches Heaven, and your governance aligns with the Dao. Last year, when you offered prayers to the Earth Thearch, the people received Heaven’s expiation. In the year before, when you sacrificed at the Circular Mound, the sun did not obscure the new moon. Auspicious signs were invoked to seek sagely wisdom, and the four seasons were observed to discern Heaven’s will. Heaven and humanity are in accord—its pattern is not distant. Perhaps the lofty peaks have swept their paths, awaiting the arrival of Your Majesty’s green canopy; the Supreme Thearch has stored up favor, long awaiting the jade tablet rites. How can we monopolize this honor and abandon the ancient merit?"

The assembled ministers repeatedly urged the Emperor, acknowledging their fault two or three times. The Emperor then embraced them, saying: "Revering the Dao of Heaven and humbly following virtuous counsel, I fear my virtue/power may not suffice—how dare I shrink from diligence? Let the rites of the imperial progress be composed, and the origins of the Feng and Shan sacrifices sought."

Thereupon, the ritual officials prepared without prior warning, and military affairs were harmonized without prior consultation. The celestial maidens wove the sunlit days, and the imperial guard sprinkled the roads. The Six Jia spirits marshaled their ranks, and the Eight Formations guarded the imperial procession. In the mid-month of early winter, the imperial carriage set forth. A thousand banners trailed like clouds, ten thousand halberds marched like a forest. Gleaming armor and flying flames radiated brilliance, shaking the plains and stirring the grasses and trees as if by wind. Passing through commanderies and counties, the Emperor inquired into local customs, questioned the aged, revived neglected sacrifices, restored fallen institutions, and repaired decrepit policies. Those who had longed for their ancestors rejoiced in their households, and the myriad regions flocked to pay homage—thousands of miles stood like a wall. Cities and towns united in celebration, and hills and mounds gathered in dance. Among them, white-haired elders wept with joy, never imagining that the souls of the Yellow Mound would witness the Feng rites of the Qian era again.

The clouds of Yao departed, and the sun of Shun returned. Divine splendor and numinous radiance suffused the vast expanse between Heaven and Earth. In that month, the Emperor arrived at Mount Tai. He purified himself in the fasting palace, cleansed his spirit in the quiet chamber, and concentrated his mind in profound contemplation, preparing to commune with the Great Unity.

As for Mount Tai—it is the palace where the sagely emperors receive the Mandate of Heaven, the abode where the celestial grandson and virtuous men dwell in spirit. Since ancient times, countless states have been established, yet only seventy-two have ascended its summit to proclaim their reigns. Our High Ancestor was the sixth, and now this is the seventh. Is this not because of the exalted position, the flourishing era, and the unparalleled virtue/power of this age?

Before the great Feng sacrifice, they piled earth upon the high ridge and built the Tai Altar at its southern base. The Heavenly Altar had three tiers, and the twelve steps of the celestial staircase were arranged in order. All spirits were reverently ranked, seated in their proper places. Tall banners and feathered standards, flutes and chimes, drums and bells—all were suspended within the double eaves of the altar’s canopy.

Amidst the solemn rites, thousand-year bells resounded as halberds and spears stood in formation, encircling the four gates. The sacred vessels of state, heirlooms of unbroken lineage, were arrayed in the central courtyard. The jade imperial carriage and golden-wheeled chariot, adorned with kingfisher plumes and yellow canopies, flanked the processional way. All officials and feudal lords, along with Qiang, Yi, Man, and Mo tribes, gathered in reverent assembly after the accolades were bestowed. The guests of abdicated kings stood in ordered ranks upon the sacred precinct. Crossbowmen with shaven heads, iron-armored steeds, and gilded arrowheads stood clad in snow-white armor, their banners blazing like fire, encircling the pristine imperial grounds.

Thereupon, at the celestial-aligned first month of the year, the imperial procession advanced in measured pace. A thousand chariots massed upon the level road, while the assembled ministers were left at the valley’s mouth. The Emperor, driving six dragons, ascended ten thousand fathoms, accompanied only by one or two elders and attendants. Emerging from the Heavenly Gate, he stood at Sunrise Peak, lodged at the Misty Expanse, and spent the night upon jagged crags. The crimson clouds seemed within reach, while white mists lay below. On the *gengyin* day, he sacrificed to the High Ancestor at the summit altar, pairing him with the Supreme Emperor, while commanding the assembled officials at the lower stations to offer homage to the host of spirits.

The Emperor, clad in ceremonial cap and robes, ascended the altar to present offerings and bow in reverence. Bronze bells resounded, feathered dancers performed, the great *Huangzhong* bell was struck, and the *Dalü* hymn was sung:
*"The gates of heaven swing open, as the Son of Heaven speaks with the divine.
Pure and steadfast, he upholds the Dipper’s measure,
Establishes the imperial standard, and labors for the common folk.
Illuminating blessings before signs appear,
Averting calamity before its sprouts.
Above and below, the ritual proceeds in perfect accord,
Music resounds in full measure.
Fortune and longevity converge,
As the gods bestow their sacred decrees."*

Then, jade tablets were concealed at the summit’s peak, and kindling was kindled to send smoke billowing toward the high heavens. Thus, the people’s sincerity ascended, borne aloft by flames and smoke. As the sun’s reins turned, the spirits’ favor lingered. Five-colored clouds arose, brushing the imperial steed and following the procession. Cries of "Long live the Emperor!" echoed from heaven to earth.

The following day, the Emperor honored the Illustrious Ancestor, pairing him with the Earth Spirit, and performed rites at the Earth Altar. Grand music was performed as he received the eastern lords. The customs of the land remained unchanged, with no demotions or revocations of noble ranks. The king’s grace renewed, heralding the joy of disaster’s end and great blessings. Before a full day had passed, transformation harmonized with the people’s hearts; before the morning had advanced, boundless fortune blanketed the realm. Thereafter, the golden casket was sealed within the ancestral chamber, and the jade phoenix carriage returned to the imperial capital. How resplendent were the sagely court’s achievements, embodying the grandest virtues of the most illustrious ages!

At this time, peoples of all customs—Hua and barbarian alike—though their tongues differed, sighed in unison: *"When armies gather in great numbers, even mountains grow clouded. Yet when a million-strong host marches a thousand *li* to its appointed task, on the day of the rites, the heavens remain unblemished, the earth unstirred by dust. The depths of winter transform into spring’s warmth, and biting winds yield to gentle breezes. Who but one of utmost virtue could move heaven to such perfect compliance?"* As the ancients said: *"From west to east, from south to north, none do not submit."* Now we truly know: when the sage acts, all things behold him, and their hearts yield in reverence.

Some may ask: *"Is it not improper for the Emperor to first sacrifice at Mount Tai and later at the Earth Altar?"*
The answer: *"This is not a matter of propriety, but of ritual. The king serves Heaven as a father and Earth as a mother. Honoring the spirits is an act of filial piety—what impropriety could there be? Moreover, the outer rites of the Heavenly Kindling are presided over by the Emperor, while the inner rites of seasonal offerings are assisted by the Empress. This establishes the proper order of human relations, reforms corrupt customs, and revives the laws of a hundred kings. Thus, for a thousand generations, people behold its lingering radiance, hear its fading echoes, savor its sweet fruits, and drink its fragrant nectar. It refines the profound mysteries of the Dao, flowing endlessly as a virtuous legacy. Your servant composes this ode to proclaim it to the spirits. Though the canons of the Four August Ones and the Six Imperial Classics—even the great *Jifu*—cannot fully capture its essence, I borrow the words of the common folk’s ode:
*'Great indeed is Heaven’s majesty!
The Emperor follows its model,
Leading our myriad states to receive Heaven’s mandate.
For a hundred generations,
May men and gods together preserve it,
Thus we pray.'"*

The Earth Altar lies two *li* south of the mountain, upon the foothills of Sheshou Mountain, which adjoins Haoli. Many former dynasties performed their earth sacrifices here. Emperor Zhenzong of the Song ordered Wang Qinruo to compose an inscription for the altar’s stele.

The Feng Sacrifice Altar, built by Emperor Zhenzong of the Song, lies five *li* south of the mountain. The Chaojin Altar stands in [a location south of] the prefecture. Emperor Zhenzong, during his eastern Feng sacrifice, received the assembled ministers at this site. The Wind, Cloud, Thunder, and Rain Altar occupies its former location. Chen Yaosou composed its stele inscription, which now stands at the altar’s base, alongside the *Celestial Completion Hall Stele* and the *Heavenly Equal Benevolent Sage Emperor Stele*. All were written in the calligraphic style of Chu Suiliang, resembling the *Sacred Teachings Preface*.

The Yin-character Stele stands outside the Qianfeng Gate of the prefecture, over a hundred paces to the east. It records Emperor Zhenzong’s gratitude for the Heavenly Scriptures and the virtues of the Two Saints. Its inscription is called "Yin-character" because the text faces north, following local custom. Some call it the "Golden Stele," claiming the characters were gilded at the time. Others name it the "Lipá Stele," as its refined intent honors the sacrifices. These names, though phonetically similar, share a common meaning. It is said that during Emperor Zhenzong’s eastern Feng sacrifice, he halted at the Huizhen Palace, hence the stele’s screen-like design, with its north-facing inscription. The palace once stood over a hundred paces south of the stele, but while the palace now lies within the city walls, the stele remains outside, so few understand its original purpose. In the *guiyou* year of the Wanli era, Inspector Wu Congxian built the Taiyin Pavilion north of the stele.

The Imperial Tent Site lies along the mountain’s central path, the same place where the First Emperor of Qin planted five pines. Emperor Zhenzong of the Song halted here during his eastern Feng sacrifice, hence its name. The stone foundations of the tent hall remain visible.

Tutu Hill lies on the mountain’s northern slope. In the eleventh year of Duke Yin’s reign, as recorded in the *Zuozhuan*, the Duke spoke to Prince Hui of building a retreat at Tutu for his old age—this is the place.

The Red Pavilion stands within Tai’an Prefecture. In the eighth year of Duke Zhao’s reign, as recorded in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, a grand review was held at Hong—this is the site.

The White Mule Mound lies one *li* north of the Feng Sacrifice Altar. When Emperor Xuanzong of Tang performed the Feng sacrifice at Mount Tai, the prefecture of Yizhou presented a white mule of remarkable stature. The Emperor rode it personally, finding it well-suited for ascent and descent.

Upon concluding the rites, the Emperor descended the mountain to rest, when officials reported that the mule had choked without illness. The Emperor marveled at this and posthumously ennobled it as "General White Mule," ordering officials to prepare a coffin and construct a stone mound for its burial.

Bocheng lies at the eastern foot of the mountain, thirty *li* from the prefectural seat. It was known as Fenghao in Han times, Wenyang in Sui, and Qianfeng in Tang—all referring to the same place. Today it is called Old County.

Yingcheng stands at the eastern foot of the mountain, fifty *li* southeast of the prefectural seat. Established as a county under the Han, it belonged to Taishan Commandery; under the Tang, it was part of Eastern Taizhou before being absorbed into Bocheng.

Xieguo City lies at the southeastern foot of the mountain, beside the Han-era Bright Hall. It is the site where the states of Qi and Lu met at Jiagu and later where Qi made amends—hence its name. The area southeast of the mountain is called Guli, the ancient Jiagu. The Guiyin Fields were those returned by Qi to Lu in apology. The Wenyang Fields lie south of the mountain along the Wen River. In the second year of Duke Cheng’s reign, the *Spring and Autumn Annals* records the seizure of Wenyang Fields; in the eighth year, it notes that the Marquis of Jin sent Han Chuan to declare the return of Wenyang Fields to Qi.

**Traces of Sages and Worthies**
Confucius passed by the side of Mount Tai and heard a woman weeping bitterly at a grave. The Master leaned forward in his carriage to listen and sent Zilu to ask her: "Your weeping suggests deep sorrow." She replied: "Indeed. Once my father-in-law was killed by a tiger, then my husband, and now my son." The Master asked: "Why do you not leave?" She answered: "There is no harsh governance here." The Master said: "Take heed, my disciples—harsh governance is fiercer than a tiger." (See *Liyun* in the *Book of Rites*.)

Confucius traveled on Mount Tai and encountered Rong Qiqi in the wilds of Lang, clad in a deerskin robe, a hempen belt, plucking a zither and singing. Confucius asked: "What brings you such joy, Master?"

Rong Qiqi replied: "My joys are many. That Heaven generates the myriad things, yet only humans are noble—this is my first joy, for I have attained humanity. Between men and women, men are honored while women are lowly; thus, to be a man is noble—this is my second joy, for I have attained manhood. There are those who never see the sun and moon, who never leave their swaddling clothes; yet I have already lived ninety years—this is my third joy. Poverty is the constant lot of the scholar, and death the end of all men. To dwell in constancy and meet one’s end—what sorrow is there in that?"

Confucius said: "Excellent! He knows how to console himself." (See *Liezi*.)

Yan Yuan followed Confucius up Mount Tai. Confucius pointed toward the gate of Haojian and asked: "Do you see the gate of Haojian?" Yan Yuan replied: "I see it—it appears as if bound by a white silk ribbon." Confucius covered Yan Yuan’s eyes and stopped him, then descended the mountain with him. Yan Yuan’s hair turned white, his teeth fell out, and he soon died of illness. (See *Jintong Wanlong Gu*. *Han Shi Waizhuan* records a similar account. Today, southwest of the prefectural city, there stands a high slope called Yanzi Slope.)

Master Zeng wore tattered clothes and tilled the fields at the foot of Mount Tai. When the heavens sent snow and the cold grew severe, he was unable to return home for ten days and nights. Longing for his parents, he composed the *Liangshan Song*. (See *Zengzi Xiaoshi*.)

When Mencius was born, his mother dreamed of a divine being riding clouds from Mount Tai, who was about to alight at Yi. His mother gazed intently for a long while, when suddenly a wisp of cloud descended, and she awoke. At that time, the lanes and alleys all saw five-colored clouds covering the Meng residence. (See *Tongzhi*.) Later generations praised Mencius for possessing the towering majesty of Mount Tai, which fittingly matched the omen of the clouds—truly so.

Bo Ya traveled to the northern slopes of Mount Tai when he was suddenly caught in a violent storm. Taking shelter beneath a cliff, his heart grew sorrowful, and he took up his zither and played. First, he composed the *Melody of Endless Rain*, then shifted to the *Sound of Crumbling Mountains*. With every piece he played, Zhong Ziqi grasped its essence. Bo Ya set down his zither and sighed: "Excellent! Excellent! Your listening—your spirit and imagination—are as my own heart. How could I hide my music from you?" (See *Liezi*.)

When Yanling Jizi journeyed to Qi, on his return, his eldest son died and was buried between Ying and Bo. Confucius said: "Yanling Jizi is one well-versed in ritual from Wu." He went to observe the burial: the pit was not dug to the spring, the body was encoffined in timely garments, and after interment, the mound was shaped to match the pit’s dimensions, its height sufficient to conceal a person. After the sealing of the grave, he bared his left shoulder and circled the right, then thrice cried out: "Flesh and bone return to the earth—such is fate! But the soul and qi go everywhere without bound." Then he departed. Confucius said: "Yanling Jizi’s observance of ritual is fitting indeed." (See *Liji*. Both Ying and Bo belong to the eastern lands of Mount Tai in Taian. Mencius was buried in Qi and returned to Ying, which is the same region.)

The tomb of Liuxia Hui lies a hundred *li* southeast of the prefectural seat. To this day, many villagers in the area bear the surname Zhan, and the place is called Liuli Village.

Yang Hu City is the fiefdom granted to Yang Hu of the Jin, located ninety *li* southeast of the prefectural seat.

Sun Fu, styled Mingfu, was a native of Pingyang in Jinzhou. In his youth, he sat for the imperial examinations but failed. Retreating to the southern foothills of Mount Tai, he studied the *Spring and Autumn Annals* and wrote *Exalting the King and Revealing the Subtle*. In Lu, there were many scholars, among whom Shi Jie, a man of exceptional worth and Dao, was the most eminent. From Shi Jie downward, all took him as their teacher.

At the age of forty, Sun Yu remained unmarried due to poverty. Chancellor Li Di sought to betroth his younger brother’s daughter to him, but Sun hesitated. Shi Jie and his fellow disciples then advised him: “For a long time, high officials have not deigned to associate with scholars. Now the Chancellor, undeterred by your poverty and low status, wishes to entrust his kin to you—this is a tribute to your virtue and integrity. You should accept this union to enhance the Chancellor’s reputation for wisdom.” Sun thus consented.

Kong Daofu, a *Giving Instructions* official, was a man of unyielding integrity, stern demeanor, and strict reserve, admitting few into his company. Upon hearing of Sun’s reputation, he visited him. Shi Jie carried his staff and shoes, attending at his side. Sun, seated, felt ashamed to stand, and whenever Kong rose, bowed, or took his leave, Shi Jie assisted him. When Sun later returned the visit, the same courtesy was observed. The people of Lu had long revered these two men, and from this time forward, they came to recognize the proper rites between master and disciple. All marveled at the sight, and Li Di and Kong Daofu gained renown among the scholar-officials for their conduct.

Later, when Shi Jie served as a school official, he declared in court: “Master Sun is no recluse; he merely seeks the right path to serve.” In the second year of the Qingli era, Vice Military Affairs Commissioner Fan Zhongyan and Academician of the Resourceful Power Fu Bi recommended his moral character and classical learning as worthy of the court. He was summoned and appointed *Collator of Texts* and *Direct Instructor* at the Directorate of Education. Once, he was called to the *Near Brilliance Pavilion* for an imperial audience, and the emperor intended to appoint him as *Imperial Lecturer*. However, detractors claimed his teachings deviated from those of earlier Confucians, and the appointment was halted.

In the seventh year, Kong Zhiwen of Xuzhou was arrested for reckless scheming. A search of his home uncovered poems containing Sun’s name, and Sun was implicated and demoted to *Supervisor of Commerce Taxes* in Qianzhou. He was later transferred to Sizhou, then appointed *Magistrate of Changshui County*, *Acting Vice Prefect* of Yingtianfu, and *Administrative Assistant* of Lingzhou. Over ten officials, including Hanlin Academician Zhao Yao, petitioned that Sun’s conduct served as a model for the age and his classical learning made him a teacher of men—he should not be exiled to distant lands. He was thus reinstated as *Direct Instructor* at the Directorate of Education.

In the second year of the Jiayou era, he died at home in the seventh month at the age of sixty-six, having attained the rank of *Gentleman for Court Discussion*. During his tenure at the Imperial Academy, he had been appointed *Assessor of the Court of Judicial Review*. When the emperor visited, he was granted a purple robe and a silver fish tally. Upon hearing of his death, the emperor was grieved and bestowed one hundred thousand coins upon his family. High officials, scholar-officials, and students of the Imperial Academy gathered to mourn and contribute to his funeral expenses.

In his study of the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, he did not rely on commentaries or twist the text with forced interpretations. His words were concise and clear, illuminating the merits and faults of feudal lords and ministers, assessing the rise and decline of the times, and deducing the order or chaos of the ruler’s Dao. His insights aligned closely with the fundamental meaning of the classic. As he lay ill, Military Affairs Commissioner Han Qi informed the emperor, who dispatched scribes with paper and brushes. His disciple Zu Wuze visited his home and collected fifteen of his writings, which were transcribed and stored in the imperial archives.

*(See Ouyang Xiu’s epitaph for further details.)*

Hu Huan, styled Yizhi, was a native of Hailing in Taizhou. Together with Sun Mingfu and Shi Shoudao, he studied on Mount Tai, enduring hardship and eating plainly, staying awake through the night. For ten years he did not return home; when a letter from his family arrived, he saw only the words "safe and sound" before casting it into a ravine, never unfolding it again. Today, people call the place where he discarded the letters *Tou Shu Jian* (Letter-Discarding Ravine).

Xu Heng, styled Shenping, was a native of Henei. During the chaos of the Jin dynasty’s wars, he took refuge at Zulai on Mount Tai, where he fortified his encampment and named it *Lu Lei* (Lu Rampart). Initially, he traveled with Yao Shu and Dou Mo, through whom he encountered the teachings of Cheng Yi and Zhu Xi, and thus became a renowned Confucian scholar. Kublai Khan of the Yuan summoned him to serve as Educational Commissioner of Jingzhao, and he was later promoted to Academician of the Jixian Institute, concurrently serving as Libationer of the Directorate of Education and Director of the Bureau of Astronomy. He was further elevated to Left Vice Director of the Secretariat and posthumously enfeoffed as Minister of Works, with the posthumous title *Wenzheng*.

**Legends of Immortals’ Traces**

The Old Man of Mount Tai, whose surname and given name are unknown, was encountered by Emperor Wu of Han during his eastern feng sacrifice. The emperor saw the old man by the roadside at the foot of the mountain, his head emitting a white light several feet high. Intrigued, the emperor questioned him, and the old man replied: "When I was eighty-five, I was withered and near death, until I met a man of the Dao who taught me to abstain from grains, subsisting only on wood-infused well water and resting my head on a divine pillow. Within the pillow were thirty-two substances: twenty-four corresponded to the twenty-four qi, and eight to the eight winds. Following this practice, I was rejuvenated from old age to youth, and now I can walk three hundred li in a day. I am now one hundred and eighty years old." The emperor received his formula and bestowed upon him jade and silk. Later, the old man entered the mountains and departed, returning to his hometown only once every five or ten years. After three hundred years, he never returned again. *(See* Leilin Zazhi *and* Ge Hong’s* Shenxian Zhuan.*)*

Ji Qiu Jun was a Daoist of the foothills of Mount Tai. His white hair turned black again, and his fallen teeth regrew. During the reign of Emperor Wu of Han, he was rewarded for his Daoist arts in defense of the emperor. Later, he withdrew from service. When the emperor performed the eastern feng sacrifice, Ji Qiu Jun played the zither and bowed in welcome, urging the emperor not to ascend the mountain, warning that he would surely injure his foot. The emperor ascended several li before his left toe broke. Ashamed, he returned and established a shrine for Ji Qiu Jun. The eulogy reads:
*Ji Qiu’s insight was profound,*
*Cultivating the Dao on the sacred mountain.*
*Refining his form, fearing decay,*
*He reversed age, restoring youth.*
*When Han Wu journeyed forth,*
*He came bearing his zither to greet him.*
*He warned against ascending the land,*
*Foretelling what was yet to come.*
*(See* Liexian Zhuan *and* Jishi Yuanhai.*)*

An Qisheng was on Mount Tai when he encountered Li Shaojun. While gathering herbs in the mountains, Li fell gravely ill, but An Qisheng gave him a spoonful of *Shenlou San*, and he recovered.

Cui Wenzi was a native of Mount Tai who, for generations, had been devoted to the teachings of the Yellow Emperor and Laozi. He lived at the foot of Gangshan, later crafting *Huang San* (Yellow Powder) and *Chi Wan* (Red Pills), which he sold in the marketplace. He claimed that three hundred days later, a great plague would strike the people. The local magistrate, Wen Suo, begged for his aid. Cui brandished a red whip, striking the *Huang San* as he paraded through the streets. Those who drank the powder upon entering a house were immediately cured, and the number of lives saved was in the tens of thousands. Later, he departed and sold *Huang San* in Shu, which is why the world treasures Cui Wen’s *Chi Wan* and *Huang San*. The eulogy reads:
*Cui attained the Dao,*
*Mastering its secrets and mysteries.*
*When pestilence descended, bringing death,*
*His compassionate heart grieved.*
*With a red whip, swift as lightning,*
*His divine medicine arrived in an instant.*
*Those saved in that moment*
*Would bear witness for all time.*
*(See* Liexian Zhuan *and* Jishi Yuanhai.*)*

During the Jin dynasty, Zhang Zhong, styled Juhe, was a native of Zhongshan. Amid the chaos of the Yongjia era, he withdrew into seclusion on Mount Tai, practicing purity and emptiness, ingesting *qi*, and consuming *lingzhi* mushrooms and minerals. Fu Jian, having heard of his virtue, summoned him to Chang’an. Zhang appeared in rustic attire, and Fu Jian said: “Master, your cultivation of personal perfection is more than sufficient, yet your merit in aiding the world remains wanting. Thus, I have humbly invited you from afar, intending to entrust you with the role of Grand Preceptor of Qi.” Zhang replied: “In the past, fleeing the turmoil, I took refuge on Mount Tai, living among the birds and beasts. The title of Grand Preceptor is not one I dare presume. I wish only to return to my remaining years on the sacred mountain.” Fu Jian sent him off in a carriage, and as they passed Mount Hua, Zhang sighed: “I, a Daoist of the Eastern Peak, shall perish on the Western Peak—such is fate.” Soon after, he departed this world, and was posthumously titled Master of Peaceful Dao.

During the Tang dynasty, Lü Yan, styled Dongbin, inscribed a five-character quatrain at the Pool of the Queen Mother on the twenty-first day of the third lunar month in the eighth year of the Tiansheng era of the Song (a *wuyin* year). He signed it “Huixi’s inscription.” On the eighteenth day of the sixth lunar month in the sixth year of the Zhenghe era (a *bingshen* year), he again inscribed a seven-character quatrain, signing it “Master Hui’s second inscription.” The calligraphy of both inscriptions resembled that of Yan Lugong, while the characters “second inscription” (*zaishu*) resembled those in the engravings of the Pavilion of the Late Worthies. None could decipher them, but enthusiasts copied his authentic traces at the Hall of Meeting the True. From his poems, it is known that Pure Yang Zi visited Mount Tai thrice. The poems are recorded in the *Record of Ascents and Views*.

Zhang Yi, of unknown origin, arrived at Mount Tai during the Kaiyuan era to study the Dao alongside a certain Li. After some time, Li, being of imperial lineage, returned home and later served as a secretary in the Court of Judicial Review. When the An Lushan rebellion erupted, he fled with his family to Xiangyang, and while on a mission to Yangzhou, he encountered Zhang Zi on the road. Zhang invited Li to stay the night, and his estate was grand, his attendants resplendent. Among the female musicians, Li noticed one playing the *zheng* who bore a striking resemblance to his wife. When the gathering dispersed, Zhang summoned the *zheng* player and tied a *linqin* ornament to her sash before they parted. The next day, Li returned to find the gates overgrown and desolate, with no sign of human presence. He asked a neighbor, who said: “This was the residence of Liu Daoxuan, but no one has lived here for over a decade.” Upon returning to Xiangyang, Li examined his wife’s sash and found the *linqin* ornament. When questioned, she recounted a dream in which five or six men pursued her, saying Zhang had summoned her to play the *zheng*. As they parted, he struck her sash with the *linqin*. Thus, Li knew Zhang had attained the Dao.

Qiu Changchun, of unknown origins, once resided at Changchun Abbey south of Mount Tai. He embraced the Quanzhen teachings, and during the Yuan dynasty was bestowed the titles Divine Immortal and Supreme Master of Non-Action and Daoist Cultivation, with the sobriquet Master Changchun. Later, he departed, leaving behind traces of his transcendent transformations on the two peaks of Mount Feng.

Zhang Zhipu, styled Master Tianni, was a native of Bao Village in Tai’an. At six, he could recite the Five Classics; at twelve, he entered the Daoist gate and dwelled at Huizhen Abbey for several years, his Daoist practice surpassing his peers. Originally named Zhiwei, the Yuan emperor later renamed him and granted him the title Grand Master of Revered Authenticity and Preserved Virtue, presenting him with purple robes. He oversaw the reconstruction of Daiyue Abbey, Shengyuan Abbey, and the Upper Sacred Peak Temple. In the early years of the Yuan, Wang Yi of Caizhou composed a poem in his honor:

*"The lineage of Red Pine stretches far,
Yet here on the sacred peak, he becomes an immortal."*

A note appended: *"This man lived to one hundred and twenty, thrice meeting emperors."*

The scholar Xu Shilong inscribed his portrait:

*"Though his form was frail, in the Dao he was robust;
Though his nature was aloof, to all things he was equanimous.
Embodying the essence and function of Confucianism and Mohism,
He glimpsed the subtle signs of Heaven and Earth."*

Du Renjie remarked: *"His learning was of Laozi and Zhuangzi; his aspirations, of the Yellow Emperor and Qi Bo."*

Wang Tianting of his prefecture said: *"Externally pure as ice and frost, internally radiant as sun and stars."*

And further: *"Leaping between mountains and marshes, luxuriant in virtue and power—calm and unhurried, such was this man called Master Tianni."*

In his later years, he composed a daily chant:

*"Shed this mortal coil of flesh,
Now joyously light and free.
Advance beyond the hundred-foot pole,
To the grotto-heavens of Penglai, come and go."*

Come and go—his past-life accumulated virtue, a physician-monk, now no mere minor talent of the Daoist gate.

Xu Shilong, styled Master Fuzhai, was also of unknown origins. A Hanlin Academician of the Yuan, he had long been a companion of Tianni of Mount Tai. During the late Yuan chaos, he changed his name, wandering up and down Mount Tai, often residing in the Sacred Peak Temple or the City God’s Temple. Three youths studied under him: one was taught swimming and leaping, another *The History of the Han*, and the third, named Wang Gui, received instruction in the imperial examination curriculum. After a long time, he took his leave, foretelling that the three would attain brief yet illustrious success.

Later, the one who had studied *The History of the Han* went blind, relying on storytelling for sustenance. The swimmer encountered the siege of Guan City in Jinan; by swimming, he broke the encirclement, and by leaping, he escaped death, thereby earning rank and reward. Gui passed the imperial examinations and received official rank. All three fulfilled his words wherever they went.

When Gui was around eighty years old, Shilong came to see him again. During the Tianshun reign, he once more visited Mount Tai, where only an elderly Daoist at Shengyuan Abbey recognized him—his appearance remained as it had been in childhood. Later, his whereabouts became unknown.

Upon examining the collected works of *Wenshan*, I found a letter addressed to "Shilong, Minister of Rites, from Dongping." The poems mourning Wenshan reveal him to have been a man of the late Song. He lived through the rise and fall of the Yuan, yet men of our dynasty still encountered him—how extraordinary!

Chengxing the Perfected, of unknown origin, achieved Daoist transcendence on Mount Tai. See *The Illustrated Explanation of the True Forms of the Five Peaks*.

Wang Congzhi, also of unknown origin, first served as a Hanlin academician before retiring to Mount Tai. There, he sat in meditation and transformed, and the place was later called *Tuixian Rock* ("Rock of the Shed Transcendent").

Haiyin, a native of Tai’an Prefecture, resided at the Great Brightness Chan Monastery in Zou County. From childhood, he awakened to the Dao, often dreaming of an extraordinary monk who guided him. One day, he summoned his disciples and said, "My illusory body will not long remain in this world." Having written his final admonitions, he sat upright and transformed. For three days, his countenance remained as if alive. Above the monastery, a purple mist radiated brilliance, piercing the heavens. The county magistrate, witnessing this, declared him a true Buddha.

**Record of Sacred Precincts, Volume Nine of *The History of Mount Tai***

**Preface:**
What are "sacred precincts"? They are the dwellings of divine spirits. Why begin with the Temple of the Sacred Peak? It is the established ritual for the imperial sacrifices, where officials, following the imperial mandate, offer annual worship—such is propriety.

The Temple of the Sacred Peak comprises three: upper, middle, and lower. The lower temple is the most grand and magnificent, situated due north within the prefectural city. It has been the site of imperial sacrifices and renovations across dynasties. Its vermilion gates and golden doors, dragon pillars and oyster-shell eaves, screen walls and towering gates—all evoke the grandeur of an imperial palace. Han mulberries and Tang scholar-trees twist beneath cloud-like canopies, while imperial steles and ancient tablets stand in dense forests, attesting to its antiquity.

The Jade Emperor Abbey, the Azure Emperor Abbey, the Hall of the Earth Deity, and the Hall of Wealth—these house the celestial and terrestrial deities. They are found at both the summit and the foothills, for it is said that heaven and earth concentrate their numen here, and the spirits must have their abodes.

The Azure Radiance Divine Response Duke’s temple is dedicated to the Heavenly Immortal and Jade Maiden, Azure Radiance Primordial Sovereign. Her temples fill the summit and foothills, but the one at the peak, built with imperial patronage, stands uniquely vast and majestic. It is said that her divine efficacy brings peace to the people and prosperity to the state.

Confucius is the Mount Tai among men. The Two Masters of Lu are the teachers of a region—thus, their temples here are fitting.

Other temples of Buddhism and Daoism, along with the abodes of various spirits, dot the cliffs and verdant valleys. Most were established between the Sui, Tang, Song, and Yuan dynasties, yet fewer than half remain today, and even these are in severe disrepair. Only the abbeys at the mountain’s summit continue to receive solemn offerings without interruption. Yet, with no side chambers to house the yellow-capped Daoists, they remain impoverished, scattering to the foothills in search of sustenance. What then becomes of the incense and offerings to the spirits? How can the caretakers secure their livelihood? This is why the temples swiftly decay after repairs. I propose that the authorities use surplus funds from incense taxes to construct side chambers on available land at each summit abbey, housing only those yellow-capped Daoists of disciplined conduct. Their names should be registered with the government, and they should be tasked with maintaining the altars and sweeping the grounds. This would prevent the abbeys from falling into rapid disrepair, save annual renovation costs, and—by relieving the people while honoring the spirits—achieve both ends.

The Eastern Peak Temple stands at the northwest corner of the prefectural city. *Fengsu Tong* states: "The Temple of the Sacred Peak is in Boxian County, thirty *li* northwest of the city, guarded by the Mountain Warden." According to the stele of the *Shengyuan Abbey of the Sacred Peak* from the Zhiyuan era, the temple was originally on the southern foothills. The two abbeys before *Shengyuan* likely mark the Han site. During Empress Wu Zetian’s usurpation of the Tang, it was moved to its current location—though some say it was relocated during the Song. Subsequent dynasties saw cycles of decline and restoration, as detailed in various stone inscriptions.

The temple’s layout features a crenellated wall, two *zhang* high and three *li* in circumference, with eighteen gates. Five face south: the central one is the *Gate of the Sacred Peak*, the eastern side is the *Gate of Lofty Aspiration*, and the western side is the *Gate of Grand Vision*. One gate faces east: the *Eastern Flourishing Gate*. One faces west: the *Western Flourishing Gate*. The remaining gates are the *Rear Gate* and the *Minister’s Gate*. Each gate has a tower, and there are four corner towers: the southeast is the *Xun Tower*, the northeast the *Gen Tower*, the northwest the *Qian Tower*, and the southwest the *Kun Tower*.

Entering through the *Gate of the Sacred Peak*, one passes through successive gates: first the *Gate of Heavenly Accord*, then the *Gate of Benevolent Peace*, and finally the *Hall of Benevolent Peace*. Two terraces, high and low, connect in sequence. Before the hall, between the two flanking structures, stands the bell and drum tower. Behind the tower lie the vegetarian halls. Behind the main hall is the *Sleeping Palace*, with side chambers on either side. The scale is vast and lavish, befitting an imperial residence.

To the left of the *Gate of Heavenly Accord* is the *Shrine of the Three Spirits*, and to the right, the *Shrine of the Grand Marshal*. Before these, the eastern auxiliary hall is the *Hall of Illustrious Numen*, and the western is the *Hall of Extended Felicity*. Three stele pavilions stand: one between the *Gate of the Sacred Peak* and the *Gate of Heavenly Accord*, and two flanking the bell and drum tower. All three pavilions house imperial steles from our dynasty.

Before the main hall grow dozens of ancient pines, their trunks twisted and branches sprawling—unmatched elsewhere. Before the *Hall of Illustrious Numen* stands a Han parasol tree, and before the *Hall of Extended Felicity*, a Tang scholar-tree, both extraordinary. Even the finest paintings could not capture them. The largest ginkgo tree measures three *ren* in girth, hollowed by fire within. As *The Scripture of Yin Convergence* says: "Fire is born from the tree"—how true!

Nine intricate rocks stand, their surfaces bearing ancient inscriptions, revealing they were offerings from distant lands. Among the forest of steles, those notable for calligraphy include: Zhang Shizhi’s regular script *Record of the Xuanhe Temple*, and Minister Qiao Yu’s seal script *Imperial Prayer Text*.

Before the temple is the *Pavilion of Distant Reverence*, and before it, the *Gate of Distant Reverence*. Beyond lies the *Imperial Avenue*, the site of the Song dynasty’s eastern imperial procession and the route for the imperial guard. In truth, it serves as the temple’s first gate.

The old plaque read *Gate of Preliminary Reverence*. Within the gate stands a terrace, and atop the terrace a pavilion. The pavilion has double eaves and sixteen corners on all four sides, its soaring elegance and intricate beauty unmatched. In former times, all who had business with the Sacred Peak would first bow at this pavilion before entering the temple—hence the name *Preliminary Reverence*. Today, when the officials perform the rites of prayer and thanksgiving at the temple, upon concluding, their ceremonial procession exits through the pavilion gate, preserving the ancient custom. The pavilion now displays screens and mirrors, one square and one round, each over six *chi* in height. Before the mirrors, carved pedestals hold images of the Primordial Lady. Pilgrims from all directions who come to pay homage at the temple also bow here first—thus it is also called the *Pavilion of Distant Reverence*.

**Excerpt from *Record of the Restoration of the Sacred Peak Temple* by Academician Yuwen Cuizhong of the Song Dynasty**
In the ninth month of the fourth year of the Xuanhe era, officials reported the completion of the Sacred Peak Palace and Temple. An imperial decree commanded Academician Yuwen Cuizhong, the Hanlin Academician-in-Attendance, to record the occasion.

Yuwen Cuizhong bowed twice, touched his forehead to the ground, and spoke: "I have heard that since ancient times, those who received the Mandate to become emperors all possessed manifest virtue, resplendent in the celestial court, uniting the hearts of the Four Seas and Nine Provinces as lords of Heaven, Earth, the altars of soil and grain, and the hundred spirits. Thus, they established altars and jade tablets to symbolize these entities, erected palaces and temples to house them, offered sacrificial animals, wine, and grain to present their purity, and employed prayers, tablets, and proclamations to convey their sincerity. Though distant in form, their intent could be discerned; though solemn in demeanor, their sincerity could be reached—scarcely different from human sentiments.

Therefore, the Yellow Emperor established ten thousand states and enfeoffed seven thousand divine domains. Through the eras of Yu, Xia, Shang, and Zhou, though cultural refinement and simplicity alternated, and their preferences differed, their reverence for the spirits to protect the people shared a single principle: they invariably placed the Sacred Peak foremost. The majesty of its divine power, its radiance and splendor, as recorded in histories and witnessed by all, form an unbroken succession.

When the Song received the Mandate and established its capital at Bian, the Sacred Peak stood to the east, scarcely ten post stations away. Emperor Zhangsheng initiated the rites of enfeoffment and personally offered reverence at the temple. Moved by the extraordinary responses of the divine, he recalled that since the Tang’s Kaiyuan era, the Sacred Peak had been enfeoffed as a prince, its rites elevated one rank above the Three Dukes—yet this was still insufficient to match the glorious responses. Thus, he elevated the Five Peaks together, bestowing upon them imperial titles. From then on, the palace and temple were further restored, and offerings grew more abundant. From the four corners of the realm, over ten thousand *li* away, officials and commoners hastened to present offerings, prayers, and thanksgivings, their devotion ever-renewing with each passing day.

Our present emperor, wise, benevolent, and filial, radiates brilliance above and below. The spirits move in harmony with Heaven, and his virtue extends universally. Having already raised the people to prosperity and longevity, he now extends his devotion throughout the realm. In all matters of revering the spirits and exalting the sacrifices, he exhausts his will and prepares every offering to the utmost. From the year *xinsi* to *renyin*, imperial edicts repeatedly descended, expanding and restoring the palace halls. The enclosing walls stretch far, their wings spreading like thoughts taking flight, towering as if the Azure Capital and Purple Ultimate—all who behold them know this to be the dwelling of the divine. In total, there are eight hundred and thirteen bays, comprising halls, sleeping chambers, gatehouses, pavilions, storehouses, guest quarters, towers, observatories, and corridors. No funds were drawn from taxes, nor labor conscripted through summons, yet the structures stand majestically complete, as if fashioned by Heaven and Earth. The spirits rest in contentment, and blessings respond like echoes.

Ah! This is truly a testament to sagely virtue. Beholding this palace and temple, its earthen and wooden structures adorned with resplendent craftsmanship, one knows the wealth and prosperity of the prefectures and counties. Gazing upon the dazzling curtains and canopies, the gleaming robes and crowns, one knows the abundance of silk and hemp. Seeing the sacrificial animals filling the courtyards and the fine wines and grains offered daily, one knows the success of agriculture and husbandry. Hearing the flutes and drums filling the streets and the songs and cheers along the roads, one knows the harmony of customs. May the spirits heed these offerings and present them to the Supreme Emperor, bestowing these blessings and fortunes. Then the altars of soil and grain will stand firm, the dynastic succession will stretch far, and prosperity will flourish without end."

**Excerpt from *Record of the Sacred Peak Temple* by Li Xian of the Ming Dynasty**
The *Book of Documents* refers to the Sacred Peak as *Daizong*, for the east corresponds to spring, the season of all things’ renewal—hence *Dai*. As the foremost of the mountain peaks, it is called *Zong*. But why is it worshipped? The records state: "It brings forth clouds, wind, and rain," and "It is the source from which the people derive their livelihood."

Before the Three Dynasties, sacrifices were made only at altars. For example, the Zhou rites prescribed four pits and an altar for the worship of mountains, forests, hills, and mounds. From the Qin and Han onward, with the rise of divine immortals and the rites of *feng* and *shan*, temple shrines were established. In ancient times, the Five Peaks were regarded as equivalent to the Three Dukes, meaning the number of sacrificial animals and vessels was determined by the scale of the offerings, not that the peaks were enfeoffed with the rank of the Three Dukes. Later generations, however, enfeoffed the spirits of the Five Peaks as kings and emperors, dressing them in regal robes and crowns, making them appear as solemn as human spirits.

Our sagacious ancestor, the Hongwu Emperor, possessed wisdom and insight unmatched through the ages. At the beginning of his reign, he issued an edict to the realm, declaring: "The sacred peaks, mountains, seas, and rivers are all lofty mountains and vast waters.
