From the opening of Heaven and Earth to the present, the essence of their numinous *qi* has gathered to form spirits. How could mere titles bestowed by the state be added to them?" Thus, in accordance with ancient regulations, he abolished the titles conferred by previous dynasties. For the Eastern Peak, he designated it simply as: *Spirit of the Eastern Peak, Mount Tai*.

Ah! This act corrected the errors of extravagant and improper rites, embodying what is meant by "consulting the spirits without doubt" and "awaiting the sages of a hundred generations without confusion."

Prefect Chen Quan of Jinan wrote to me in the capital, saying: "The temple of the Spirit of the Eastern Peak, Mount Tai, has fallen into disrepair. An imperial edict has been obtained for its restoration. Provincial Governor Jia Quan and the officials of the Three Departments have jointly planned and overseen the project. Surveillance Commissioner Wang Tie, in particular, has taken great care in the endeavor. I have been entrusted with managing the affairs. Construction began on the twenty-sixth day of the eighth month of the fourth year of the Tianshun era and was completed on the first day of the ninth month of the fifth year. The temple has been entirely renewed, inspiring even greater reverence among the people of the region than before."

Here the translation follows:

---

The assembly declared: "This must not go unrecorded. We humbly request that you compose an inscription to be carved, so that it may endure for posterity."

I reflect: When Mount Tai emits clouds, they gather from the smallest measure and rain upon all under heaven before the morning is out. Its virtue and power are indeed vast, yet it is the people of this region who benefit most from its grace—how much more so when they draw upon its resources for tiles and materials?

Though it is recorded in the court’s sacrificial canon and thus especially revered in this region, now the provincial governor, the censorate, and other high ministers have united in purpose to restore the temple of the spirit. Moreover, the prefect has faithfully carried out his commission, embodying the court’s intent to honor what the people revere. Truly, they may be called the parents of the people. Those who neglect the spirits and oppress the people—how can they not feel shame when they see this?

*A Brief Record of the Xueqiong Temple of Mount Tai*

The spirit of Mount Tai, the Eastern Peak, has long had a temple on its southern slope. Whenever the court performs great rites or undertakes major affairs, it dispatches envoys to make offerings there. Over time, the temple buildings had fallen into disrepair, many collapsing or leaking without repair.

Previously, local officials had memorialized the throne to request reconstruction, but the work was never completed. In the *jimao* year of the Tianshun era (1459), the magistrate of Tai’an Prefecture reported the matter to Jinan Prefecture, which in turn petitioned the court. An imperial edict granted permission for repairs.

At the time, Provincial Governor Nian Gongfu was planning the project but was transferred before it began. His successor, Left Vice Censor-in-Chief Jia Quan, arrived and, together with the surveillance commissioner, the provincial administration commissioner, and the judicial commissioner, deliberated on the matter. They selected a capable official to oversee the work and further entrusted Jinan Prefect Chen Quan to make monthly inspections to manage the project.

Upon arriving in Tai’an, Prefect Chen declared: "Restoring the temple of the Eastern Peak is indeed a way to reverently comply with the imperial command and honor the great spirit. Yet we must also prioritize conserving the people’s resources and valuing their labor. If the people are impoverished and weary, the task cannot succeed." He then inquired of the temple keepers, who reported that the offerings accumulated over decades were substantial. Thus, he dispatched agents to purchase timber of all sizes and other essential materials for the repairs.

When the work commenced, all craftsmen and laborers were drawn from official ranks, so that the common people remained unaware of the burden. Chen managed the project with methodical care, neither hasty nor sluggish. Construction began in the autumn of the *gengchen* year (1460) and was completed in the fifth month of the *xinsi* year (1461). The halls, surrounding corridors, gates, towers, and enclosing walls were all fully restored. All present wished to carve an inscription to commemorate the event.

Wang Yun, Surveillance Commissioner of Shanxi and a native of Jinan, wrote to me seeking a composition for this purpose. I reflect: The spirit of Mount Tai, the Eastern Peak, stands foremost among the sacred peaks. The sagely court has righted its name and ranked its sacrifices, yet if its temple were left untended, how could the responsible officials ensure timely repairs? Now, the provincial governor, the censorate, and other high ministers, along with officials like Prefect Chen, have managed the project without exhausting the people’s resources, achieving a complete renewal. All this may be said to reverently uphold the court’s illustrious mandate and bring harmony between the people and the spirits.

Thus, I set forth the events and inscribe this verse:

One principle governs the myriad affairs,
The two qi intermingle as root.
The pure and light drift boundless,
The turbid and heavy settle and endure.
The yielding flows, the firm stands tall,
Rivers surge, peaks tower.
Of all these, Mount Tai,
Is where creation’s essence gathers.
Dragons soar through the vast void,
Its weight presses deep into the earth.
Not only does the state of Lu gaze upon it,
It is the greatest of all sacred peaks.
Its depths are unfathomable,
Its blessings measureless.
From a hand’s-breadth of dark clouds,
Sweet rain falls across the eight directions.
Its merit is already vast,
Its recompense should be made manifest.
A temple stands on its southern slope,
Newly restored by imperial command.
High ministers carry out the task,
The prefect fulfills his duty.
The people are neither impoverished nor weary,
The work is completed in full.
Halls, corridors, gates, and towers,
Rise majestically, winding and grand.
High and low, all meet the proper measure,
Neither too lofty nor too low.
The spirit’s qi circulates and harmonizes,
Flowing and abiding here.
In ancient times, mountains and rivers
Were honored with proper rites.
But in later ages,
Extravagant titles proliferated,
Elevating peaks to heaven,
Ranking them with the Three Dukes.
In our sagely era,
The Dao returns to ancient ways.
False and presumptuous claims are cast aside,
All follows the great mean.
The sacred peaks and rivers,
All have their names restored.
Of these, the spirit of Mount Tai,
Is most fittingly revered.
Now that this work is done,
All wish to carve it in stone.
I set forth the principles and continue the words,
To endure without end.

*Brief Record of the Restoration of the Eastern Peak Temple by Li Qin*

In the winter of the *dingwei* year of the Jiajing era (1547), the temple of Mount Tai was struck by disaster. The halls, corridors, guest quarters, meditation halls, gates, and towers were all reduced to ashes. Only the three rear halls and the Bingling and Yanxi Palaces remained.

The local officials reported the calamity to the throne, and the emperor was deeply alarmed for some time. He ordered the punishment of the negligent officials and immediately initiated plans for reconstruction. However, due to successive years of poor harvests, the project could not commence for nearly a decade.

In the eighth month of the *xinyou* year (1561), on the occasion of the emperor’s birthday, the Court of Imperial Sacrifices was commanded to prepare silk and offerings, reverently presenting them to the spirit of Mount Tai with solemn rituals. The local officials prepared the altars and performed the rites at the appointed time. Surveillance Commissioner Zhu Zhenshan, bearing the imperial mandate, presided over the sacrifices. Fearing the inadequacy of the offerings, he consulted with the provincial administration and judicial commissioners, urging swift action. He specially commissioned Jinan Prefecture Vice Prefect Zhai Tao to oversee the project, having selected him for his ability.

Vice Prefect Zhai then issued orders to the relevant authorities, procuring timber, metal, stone, and other materials, as well as craftsmen and laborers, all funded by the temple’s incense and silk offerings, without imposing the slightest burden on the people. Construction began in the spring of the *renxu* year (1562) and was completed in the summer of the *guihai* year (1563). The temple was fully restored: ruined structures were rebuilt, leaning ones replaced, collapsed ones repaired, and faded or soiled ones refreshed and cleansed. The vermilion and green pigments were vividly applied, their brilliance dazzling.

Surveillance Commissioner Zhang Shizhou performed the sacrificial rites at the temple, rejoicing in its completion. On that day, auspicious vapors overflowed, and divine radiance gazed down. The spirit of Mount Tai, now honored and at peace, shall henceforth bestow hidden blessings and manifest aid. Rain and sunshine will come in due season, yielding abundant harvests; disasters and illnesses will vanish, and the people’s qi will harmonize. Thus may our nation forever enjoy boundless prosperity—a merit of the spirit that shall endure with heaven and earth.

The gentleman declares: the merit of Master Zhai in serving the spirit is truly magnificent!

*Brief Record of Vice Commissioner Xu Tianzeng’s Renovation of the Dai Temple*

At the foot of Mount Tai once stood a temple named Dai Zong. Located on the southern slope of the peak, it was relocated to its present site during the Song dynasty. Its design includes a surrounding wall with eight gates, towers at the four corners, a front hall, a rear chamber, and side halls—solemn as a royal residence.

Our dynasty inherited the legacy of the former regime. Since the Tianshun era, repeated imperial edicts have mandated its repair, as attested by surviving steles. The Filial Emperor personally composed an inscription, its imperial brilliance resplendent for ten thousand generations to behold.

In recent renovations, only the two courts were addressed, with funds drawn from accumulated incense taxes, obviating the need to invoke imperial decrees or draw from the inner treasury. In the Jia-Shen year of Wanli (1584), the temple had again fallen into disrepair. Thereupon, Vice Commissioner Tu petitioned the two courts, securing approval to appoint Zhang Shichen, Vice Magistrate of Jinan Prefecture, to oversee the construction, with Su Tianshou, Registrar of Tai’an Prefecture, assisting. I arrived to supervise with even greater diligence, and by the eighth month of autumn in the Yi-You year (1585), the project was completed. The total expenditure amounted to over nine hundred taels of silver, achieved without burdening the people’s wealth or labor, yet the grand work was accomplished.

I have heard that sacrifice is a ritual, and the state’s great sacrifices must be conducted with reverence. The sages established rites to honor the Five Peaks, pairing them with Heaven. Their sacrifices were offered on altars, not in halls; their spirits were represented by tablets, not images. Throughout the realm, from prefectures to counties, seasonal sacrifices to mountains and rivers within their borders universally follow this principle. To restore ancient practices would only confuse the people.

This temple preserves the precedents of former dynasties, with its halls and images left unchanged. Was this because the primordial chaos of creation had not yet reached them? Or was a deeper meaning concealed within? During the Hongzhi era, the imperial voice admonished as much.

Thus do I recognize the sages’ intent in revering the spirits and guiding the people: to instill awe, inspire the heart toward goodness, and restrain the impulse toward evil. Through mutual exhortation, this contributes to governance. As the saying goes, “What does not harm governance, the sage does not alter for the people.”

Now the temple’s edifice stands majestic, its divine might radiant, as though the spirit watches from above. When men and women from all directions come to pray for blessings or seek absolution, entering the temple to gaze and bow, they are moved to solemn reverence, none failing in devotion. Might this not aid the sagely transformation, if only by a ten-thousandth part?

As for the teachings of Buddhists and Daoists—claiming that spirits, like kings, wield power over fortune and misfortune, life and death, and that good and evil yield karmic retribution—such notions lack canonical basis, and thus Confucians refrain from discussing them.

*Record of Li Qin’s Renovation of the Ancient Well at the Yue Temple*

In my old age, I have retired to the forests and springs, ashamed of my lack of contribution to the world. Each month, I gather over a hundred villagers, young and old, at the Yue Temple to expound the Sacred Edicts, presuming to elucidate their meaning, hoping to transform customs and restore the ancient Way of Lu.

Two men, Li Jin and Lu Shiqing, bowed and addressed me: “The temple has an ancient well, its water once clear and pure, relied upon for ritual ablutions and daily use. Over time, silt and mud have choked it, forcing us to draw water from outside for offerings, which risks impurity. We have pooled our resources to excavate and purify it, dredging it thoroughly, and the water has returned to its former clarity. We request that you inscribe the year and month on stone.”

I replied: “This reflects the spirit of today’s village compact. Goodness is innate to the human heart, not borrowed from without. When private desires obscure it, its original nature is lost. Consider the well: when clogged with mud, the spring cannot flow, yet its clear source remains. With a single dredging, it is restored. Likewise, when our people are clouded by selfish desires and lose their fundamental hearts, should we not guide them?

The village compact exists precisely to remove the ‘mud’ of our people and restore their innate goodness. Moreover, Mount Tai is the foremost of the Five Peaks, and this water draws from its true vein. Offered to the divine, its use is noble and weighty.

We who dwell in the land of Lu have long been neighbors to sages and worthies. If each person truly understands self-renewal, a single transformation can lead to the Dao. But if one is submerged in selfish desires and abandons what is noble and weighty, how can one face others without shame?”

Thus do I record this to serve as an example for all.

The Upper Temple of the Eastern Peak stands before the cliff inscriptions at the summit. During the Zhiyuan era of the Yuan dynasty, Director Zhang Zhichun once renovated it. In the Jiajing era, Grand Coordinator Zeng Xian oversaw its restoration, and Provincial Governor Chen Ru recorded it.

Chen Ru’s Record states:

Mount Tai, the most revered of the Five Peaks, stretches across Qi and Lu, bridging the Jiang and Huai regions. Towering into the clouds, its radiance outshines the sun and moon—how majestic and lofty! Is it not the most wondrous and singular spectacle under heaven?

When our August Ming Emperor ascended the throne, he offered sacrifices to the mountains and rivers and honored the host of spirits, declaring: “Let this peak assist our state.” Later, in the year *xinchou*, when northern barbarians rebelled, he commanded the frontier ministers: “Fortify your ramparts and bastions to defend our nation and secure my reign—once more, let this peak serve its purpose.” Alas! How great is the timely utility of this mountain!

At that time, Zeng Xian, Right Censor-in-Chief of the Censorate from Guangling, received the imperial commission to oversee the eastern lands. Upon arrival, he encountered famine and first regulated his own expenditures to relieve the people. He restored the sacrificial rites and ensured every measure for their welfare was taken. Thus, he petitioned the court to reduce the capital’s grain reserves by tens of thousands of *shi*, and likewise, the land taxes by tens of thousands. Addressing the provincial and prefectural officials, he declared: “The majestic power of Mount Tai stirs the realm. From military preparedness to disaster relief, from the sustenance of officials to every public need—all depend on it. Does it not shelter our eastern mountains?”

Learning that the temple’s structures had fallen into disrepair, he asked: “How can we offer sacrifices without restoring them?” In the fifth month of summer, he consulted with Surveillance Commissioner Yang Benshen of Guanzhong. Together, they resolved: “Mobilize official resources without wearying, act without delay, and complete the work to honor its completion.” He issued orders to the various offices:

To Provincial Governor Chen Ru and Wang Hongcan: “You shall oversee the planning and resolve all difficulties.”
To Surveillance Commissioner Yu Huan: “You shall supervise the labor, ensuring no disorder arises and all tasks are executed with reverence.”
To Prefect Liu Xi and Assistant Prefect Wang Jingju: “You shall gather and direct the workers.”
To Magistrate Ma Fengbo: “You shall lead the efforts, ensuring all laborers work diligently, ever mindful of their duty.”

Thus, construction commenced in the fifth month of summer in the year *renyin* and concluded in the third month of spring in the year *guimao*. The Eastern Peak Hall was rebuilt with four bays, facing south. The Azure Cloud Archway was erected, also facing south, as was the Taiyue Archway, while another faced west. The Eastern Officials’ Hall was built with six bays, and the Western Officials’ Hall with four, both facing south. The bell and drum towers, restored opposite the ruined shrine of the Yuanjun, stood east and west as originally designed.

Upon completion, that year brought timely rains and sunshine, and the eastern lands enjoyed peace. The wise declared that the spirit of Mount Tai had manifested. Zeng Xian then requested that a stele be erected at the mountain’s summit, and the task of recording the event was entrusted to me. I bowed deeply and proclaimed:

“Alas! When heaven sends seasonal rains, mountains and rivers yield clouds. As desires arise, omens appear first—such is the marvel of the divine. Behold this Eastern Peak, pregnant with essence and vitality, foremost in the world! Is there any limit to how it stabilizes our people and upholds our state?”

The Central Temple of the Eastern Peak lies on the mountain’s southern slope, east of the Queen Mother’s Pool. Formerly called the Daiyue Abbey, its local name is now the Lord Lao Hall, where only a small Three Pure Ones shrine remains. According to Li Kua’s *Record of the Jasper Pool* from the Song dynasty, the abbey originated with the Yellow Emperor and is situated here. Within the ruins stand an ancient bridge and over ten ancient pines, their branches twisting like children or dragons. Gao Hui of our dynasty recorded that Emperor Wu of Han planted them during his eastern pilgrimage—these must be the same. In the rear garden’s grotto, a stone tablet bears the inscription “Han Minister,” traditionally attributed to Zhao Mengfu, and the calligraphy appears authentic.

Three ancient steles remain. Two bear inscriptions by Daoist followers from the Tang and Later Zhou dynasties, written in running script with archaic elegance. The paired stones stand side by side, sheltered by an eastern canopy, and locals call them the Mandarin Duck Steles—a truly rare design. The third stele records prohibitions from the first year of the *Zhiyuan* era of the Yuan dynasty. Below is its text:

**Prohibitions of the Tai’an Prefecture**
*Tai’an Prefecture, in accordance with the directive from the Eastern Peak’s Supervisor of Repairs, states: The Eastern Peak has three temples—Upper, Middle, and Lower. The Daiyue Abbey north of the prefectural city is the Middle Temple. During the Tang dynasty, Empress Wu Zetian converted it into a Daoist abbey. The Queen Mother’s Hall on its left flank was destroyed, though the Yanyan Pavilion above remains, where emperors rested during their feng and shan sacrifices. Below lies the Queen Mother’s Pool, followed by the Lü Xi Cavern, where sacred images endure. A stone tablet by the pool’s terrace reads: By order of the prefectural authorities, all are forbidden from defiling the pool’s surroundings. Violators will be punished with eighty strokes. This office, having received the directive, hereby issues this notice to enforce the prohibition.*
*Posted on the __ day of the ninth month, the twenty-ninth year of the Zhiyuan era.*

The Jade Emperor Temple, also known as the Supreme Clarity Palace, stands at the mountain’s summit, the site of the ancient *feng* altar. Once ruined, it was rebuilt in the nineteenth year of the Chenghua era using funds from the imperial treasury. During the Longqing era, Minister Wan Gong relocated the temple to the summit’s north, excavating the peak’s stone to mark it with an inscription: *“Marking the Summit of Mount Tai.”* Wan Gong himself composed the record.

**Minister Wan Gong’s Stele Marking the Summit of Mount Tai**
In the spring of *renshen* of the Longqing era, the Yellow River overflowed, blocking transport routes. The Emperor, deeply concerned, commanded me: “Go and govern the waters.” By summer, the river was tamed, and all channels restored. The Emperor, pleased, then ordered: “Go and pacify the east.” Thus, in the eighth month, I ascended Mount Tai to report the completion of my duties. I traversed its sheer cliffs, scaled its perilous heights, examined the Qin Stele, and ascended the Sunrise Pavilion. Finally, I reached the summit and paid homage at the Celestial Palace.

Suddenly, a black-robed servant, eyes wide and feet stumbling, beckoned to me and said: “This is the summit stone of Mount Tai.” I found it strange. Upon climbing it, the surface was as smooth as if sealed; beneath, the steps were like any other—how could one know this was the summit of Mount Tai? And how could one know this was the summit’s stone?

I sighed deeply: “Mount Tai commands the reverence of the Four Sacred Peaks, and this summit stone commands the reverence of Mount Tai itself. Yet we have built over it, leveled it, and trodden upon it, suppressing its nobility and concealing its numinous wonder. This is my fault—my fault indeed.”

I immediately ordered Ji Ban and Wang Zhi Gang to dismantle the Supreme Clarity Palace, relocating it to the rear. I commanded: “Simply excavate the earth to reveal the summit. Do not alter its shape, do not destroy its form, do not cut what Heaven has wrought. Restore Mount Tai’s truth, and be done.” The attendants then removed the soil, exposing the summit. The summit stone measured eleven *chi* in breadth, fourteen *chi* in thickness, and rose three *chi* in height. Atop it rested a living stone: to the east, two *chi* and five *cun* wide, one *chi* and three *cun* thick; to the west, one *chi* and eight *cun* wide, eight *chi* and five *cun* long. To think that the eastern summit of Mount Tai is already so extraordinary—yet atop it rests this stone, the supreme peak of the celestial realm, the dark crown of the Azure Emperor.

Leaning against the living stone, I surveyed the expanse of ten thousand *li*. Gazing above and below across the eight wilds, I saw afar: to the east, the sun rising over Fusang; to the west, the winds blowing from Kunlun. Nearby, the Qin Stele stood like a standard of rectitude, clustered stones like a gathering of jade tablets. The hills of Qi and Lu took forms resembling the Five Phases: round as metal, straight as wood, sinuous as water, sharp as fire, square as earth. They cradled the crimson gates of the Azure Radiance’s palace and overlooked the secluded abode of the Primordial Lady. Here, the grand vista of the six directions was complete.

This summit stone had remained concealed for countless millennia. Now revealed, it restores Mount Tai’s truth and preserves its dignity. May future visitors refrain from altering, destroying, or cutting what Heaven has shaped, ensuring its truth and dignity endure for ten thousand generations, lest they offend the spirit of Mount Tai.

As for the black-robed servant—his mind seemed elsewhere. When I asked his origin and who had sent him, he remained silent. Alas, I understood: the black robe received his charge from the Azure Radiance, who received it from the Primordial Lady, who received it from the Supreme Clarity, who received it from the Primordial Beginning, who received it from the Ancestor of the Void and Obscurity.

The Jade Emperor Temple stands at Huima Ridge on the mountain. Investigating Censor Chen Cha composed its record. Before the temple, a cliffside staircase, over three *zhang* high, leads to the Ascension Pavilion. Chen Cha’s *Record of the Temple* briefly states:

While traveling from Zhejiang’s judicial circuit to Jin, I passed through Qi and Lu, arriving at this mountain. Ascending its perilous heights, I traversed cloud-wreathed crags and wind-swept ravines, winding through a thousand turns. Midway up the slope, I suddenly beheld a magnificent new shrine. Behind it lay the Auspicious Immortal Crag; to the left, the Ten Peaks Ridge; to the right, the Nine Peaks Mountain. With its sheer cliffs and extraordinary formations, the temple occupied the finest site. Its statues gleamed with gold and jade, their robes and crowns solemnly arrayed. Bells and drums resonated with brilliance. Its deity was the Jade Emperor of the Vast Heaven.

I reflected: no mountain is more renowned than the Five Peaks, and none more majestic than Mount Tai. For from a single vein of Kunlun, it stretches far and rises here as a colossal bastion—the primal source of all mountains. Since my youth, I have traveled the two capitals and the provinces of Jiang and Zhe, returning to dwell for a decade on the narrow slopes of Mount Wu. Later, I rose again to gaze upon the Nine Doubts, passed Hengyang, visited the Auspicious Grove and Yuejun, traversed the ruins of the Meng and Duan clans, reached the Golden Sand River, glimpsed the Bronze Pillar, crossed the Yellow and Luo Rivers, journeyed through the passes and Shaanxi, and traversed the three regions of Shu, Fujian, and Guangdong. My eyes have feasted on countless wonders, yet none compare to this mountain’s towering grandeur.

So lofty it pierces the heavens, it compels people from across the realm to unite in devotion, fasting and purifying themselves, hastening in reverence. Their spirits converge, their voices tremble with awe, as if the deity himself were present. How could this be mere coincidence? There must be a reason.

Thus, this place has this temple. I cannot judge its rightness or wrongness, but I recall how pilgrims travel thousands of *li* to reach this summit—how wearying their journey. Here, the temple offers respite amid the rugged terrain: halls and courtyards, springs and tea. The weary may rest, the hungry and thirsty find solace, those buffeted by wind and rain find shelter. Even the simple folk, prostrating themselves in trembling devotion, seek to calm their restlessness, dispel their ignorance, and pray for relief from drought, flood, plague, and pestilence. Do they not rely on this place? Could Heaven have ordained it as an aid to the people’s welfare and longevity? How could one lightly dismiss it?

*Excerpt from the Record of the Wang Pavilion*
Mount Tai, foremost of the Five Peaks and sovereign of all valleys, stands mightily in its region, its heights brushing the Milky Way. It gathers the qi of spiritual essence, concentrates the vigor of creation—truly the premier mountain under heaven and the most revered in ancient and modern sacrificial rites. Its deity governs the inception of all things, and thus all living beings under heaven—whether long-lived or short-lived, blessed or cursed, fortunate or ill-fated—revere and worship it without exception.

Our Great Ancestor, the Emperor Gao, transformed the customs of the barbarians with the ways of Xia, illuminating the laws and rites. He esteemed sacrificial ceremonies above all, and the reverence for the mountains and rivers has never waned. Through successive dynasties, the old traditions have been upheld. Now, as the August Emperor establishes his rule, his virtue flourishes and his achievements shine. All officials follow his example, and his influence spreads across the land, nurturing the customs and lives of the people.

In the autumn of *jiachen* of the Jiajing era, when the harvest was abundant, the magistrate of Tai’an presented auspicious grains and rare wheat. The ministers, along with the nine chief ministers and all officials, offered their congratulations. The Emperor, deeply pleased, responded with the words of the Han Confucians.

It is said in the *Book of Changes*: "Harmonious qi brings forth auspiciousness." This truly arises from our sagely emperor’s emulation of his ancestors and alignment with Heaven, his reverent and luminous sacrifices evoking response—could this not be the numinous efficacy of the mountain spirits manifesting these omens? In the spring of *yisi* the following year, the monk Peng Qing, having raised alms on this mountain in Ji, returned to Huima Ridge, where the Jade Emperor Hall—long-standing but now dilapidated—was expanded to five bays, its roof clad in iron tiles and glazed tiles, its bronze statues adorned, and its corridors and hempen ropes restored. Three additional bays were added as a gate pavilion. At that time, the current governor of Shanxi, Minister Zeng Shitang, was establishing his headquarters to the left of the mountain. As official duties brought him here, he named the pavilion *Shengxian Ge* ("Ascending Immortals Pavilion"). The monk approached me, requesting a record.

I reflect: the dual qi of Heaven and Earth daily manifest as spirits, and when stirred, they respond—opening and closing, revealing and concealing, as mountains bear the earth’s numen. None is weightier than the towering Mount Tai, with its majestic and solemn spirits, unmatched by others. The sagely Son of Heaven, whose merit is lofty and virtue profound, demonstrates his reverence for Heaven and Earth through sacrifices comparable to those offered to the mountain. Wherever he turns, his sincerity is met with resonance, ensuring endless offerings—is this not fitting?

The *Huizhen Gong* ("Abode of Realization") stands at the southeastern corner of the prefectural city. Formerly called *Fenggao Gong*, it was renamed by Emperor Zhenzong of Song during his sojourn. In the present dynasty, the Jade Emperor Hall was rebuilt, housing two poems by Lü Dongbin inscribed in stone, alongside Li Taibai’s verses. Steles from all directions, all antique and refined, are worthy of admiration.

**Ren Shi’s Record (Abridged):**
Upon the completion of the Jade Emperor Hall at *Huizhen Gong*, a stele should be erected to mark the occasion. I, Ren Shi, bowing in reverence, declare: The division of the primordial qi yields the light and pure, which ascends to form Heaven, and that which governs within is called the Emperor. Confucians refer to it as *Haotian Shangdi* ("August Heavenly Thearch"), while Daoist traditions honor it as *Haotian Yuhuang Shangdi* ("August Jade Emperor of Heaven"). The halls and temples of the world are all places where incense is offered to it.

*HuiZhen Gong* lies on the southern slope of Mount Tai, its founding date unknown. Nearby, a stele from the *Xiangfu* era of the Song Dynasty praises the virtues of the Two Saints, standing a hundred paces south of the palace. The *Chaojin* ("Tribute") Stele lies a mile further south. Tradition holds that Emperor Zhenzong, during his Feng Shan rites, sojourned here. In the *Dading* era of the Jin, Fengfu County was renamed Tai’an Prefecture, placing the palace at the *xun* (southeastern) corner of the city. Through cycles of rise and decline, only three bays of the Jade Emperor Hall remained, its foundations low and humble.

When the emperor dispatched envoys to present silk and offerings to the sacred peaks, they beheld this shrine. In accordance with popular sentiment—and perhaps also due to the *Zhaozhen* Shrine atop the mountain—they petitioned for its renovation. The emperor approved, and the decree was issued to the relevant offices in the *gengzi* year. Thus, artisans were summoned from Lu, stonemasons from Ying, and timber was sourced from Culai’s pines and Xinpu’s catalpas. Measurements were taken, and construction began. In *xinchou*, the *Zhaozhen* Temple was completed; in *renyin*, the Jade Emperor Hall was finished.

How majestic! How vast! The pines and locust trees stand tall, the columns are stately, the plasterwork and paintings resplendent, their brilliance and refinement dazzling. Gold and azure gleam with the radiance of sun and moon; vermilion and green rival the splendor of Heaven and Earth. The statues, solemn and awe-inspiring, vividly manifest their numen. Truly, this hall embodies our sagely era’s reverence for Heaven and devotion to the spirits in every detail.

Before it, Mount Tai and Mount Culai stand in mutual reflection. Rare flowers bloom, perfuming the jade throne; white clouds drift, leaving the jade altar serene. Those in starry crowns and feathered robes raise their hands to their brows, praying for the sagely emperor’s longevity. Yellow-haired youths and white-haired elders prostrate themselves, beseeching peace and prosperity for this world and its people.

Ah! Heaven is not distant in its loftiness but near at hand; the Emperor is not hidden in obscurity but manifest in our clarity. Those who pass by—how can they fail to hold reverence, awe, and trepidation, lest they betray the sacred reciprocity?

The *Houtu* Hall stands on the southern slope of the mountain, three *li* from the prefectural seat. Its cypresses are sparse yet striking, and the mountain shrine is quaint and venerable.

The *Qingdi* ("Green Thearch") Temples number two: one atop the mountain, the other on its southern slope. Their founding dates are unknown. Emperor Zhenzong of Song bestowed upon the Green Thearch the honorific *Guangsheng Dijun* ("Imperial Lord of Vast Generation"). The temple atop the mountain was repaired by the provincial governor, Censor-in-Chief Li, in the *bingxu* year of the Wanli era, completed in the first winter month. The temple on the southern slope preserves an inscription by Emperor Zhenzong, his calligraphy and eulogy still extant. During the Hongzhi and Zhengde eras of the present dynasty, it was expanded; in the Jiajing era, Minister Zhu Heng further renovated it.

The Green Thearch is one of the Five Thearchs worshipped in the Han and Tang dynasties. According to the *Book of Han* and the Song *Treatise on Astronomy*, the Green Thearch is a celestial spirit, and the Eastern Marchmount is associated with him—hence the origin of this temple’s worship. In the Song Dynasty, the honorific *Guangsheng* was added by imperial edict to the Ministry of Rites:

> The Eastern Marchmount stands as the foremost of mountains, its numen upright and sacred. It governs generation in the east and assists the August Emperor in his wisdom. Rightly does its name head the registers of immortals, its virtue extending to all beings. Its sacrifices at the sacred altars are recorded in bronze and stone.

The Green Thearch, Imperial Lord of Vast Generation, oversees nurturing and growth. His Dao is profound and empty, aiding the mysterious transformations of the lofty and luminous, sheltering all life in boundless abundance. His ancient shrine, attested in local records, was honored with imperial visits. As the emperor’s solemn procession arrived, secret seals were bestowed in ritual, and the divine halls were beheld in hope of hidden blessings. The observance of these rites extols his virtues, manifesting reverence and ensuring eternal felicity. Thus was his honorific proclaimed: *Qingdi Guangsheng Dijun* ("Green Thearch, Imperial Lord of Vast Generation"). The temple was specially adorned, and this edict was issued for all to know.

**Eulogy for the Imperial Lord of Vast Generation**
*Composed and inscribed by imperial hand, with seal script heading*

> He governs the lofty Marchmount, rectifying the sacred numen. Presiding over generation in the east, he aids the August Emperor’s wisdom. Rightly does his name crown the immortal registers, his virtue blessing all beings. His sacrifices at the sacred altars are engraved in gold and stone.

Having reverently performed the investiture rites to reverently reciprocate the divine blessings, we offer this illustrious title in utmost sincerity, composing this text to extol his virtues.

**Eulogy**
He regulates the sacred Mount Tai,
establishing this eastern land.
The realm of generation and nurturance,
the abode of numinous immortals.
Here dwells the exalted Perfected,
rightly charged with the luminous mandate.
His divine power is unfathomable,
yet reverence calls forth response.
His flourishing deeds shine forth,
and auspicious omens multiply.
Thus we honor him with this noble name,
to express our profound devotion.

Engraved in stone by the Imperial Calligraphy Institute on the twenty-seventh day of the tenth month in the first year of *Dazhong Xiangfu*.

**Imperial Prayer**
On the fourth day of the eleventh month in the first year of *Dazhong Xiangfu* (a *wushen* year), the first day of the month being *wuwu*, the August Emperor of Venerating Culture, Vast Valor, Sacred Wisdom, Benevolence, and Filial Piety, respectfully dispatches Minister *Shao Ye* of the Ministry of War to make offering to the Green Thearch, Imperial Lord of Vast Generation:

We humbly acknowledge your towering merit, resplendently manifest, and your profound benevolence, subtly revealed. Your divine transformation knows no bounds, for you truly govern the realm of generation. This is the sacred precinct where the exalted Perfected dwell, the numinous domain of the lofty Marchmount.

Having reverently received the primal mandate and personally performed the grand requital, we have, with the sun’s ascent, duly conducted the supreme rites. Thus we proclaim this illustrious title of our enlightened age to express our reverence. We now present this prayer to convey our utmost devotion.

Respectfully announced.

The Three Sovereigns Temple, where Fuxi, Shennong, and the Yellow Emperor are venerated, stands at the southern foot of the Marchmount, approximately three *li* from the prefectural seat.

**Imperial Prayer**
In the sixteenth year of the Hongzhi reign, cyclical year *guihai*, on the □ day of the eleventh month, the August Emperor dispatched Eunuch Director *Qi Xuan* of the Directorate of Palace Attendants to reverently present a libation of pure wine and a feast of fine viands as offering to the Three August Sovereigns, proclaiming:

Of old, the Three Sovereigns established the ultimate bounds of civilization. The hundred herbs were first tasted, and medicinal stones were thereby employed. For all afflictions—ailments, pains, maladies, and itches—mankind relies upon them for relief. Their divine sagacity and wondrous skill remain the eternal foundation of all generations. Now, with refined vessels and utmost sincerity, we humbly present this sacrifice, praying that the spirits may deign to partake and draw near.

Respectfully announced.

The Azure Clouds Temple of Numinous Response stands approximately three *li* southwest of the Marchmount’s summit, formerly named the Illustrious Perfection Abbey. It was first erected during the Eastern Feng sacrifices of the Song Emperor Zhenzong, and in the present dynasty, its structure was expanded and its name changed to the current title. Successive reigns have maintained its upkeep without fail. Most recently, during the reign of Emperor Shizong, Minister *Chen Ce* of the Ministry of Works was dispatched to oversee the temple’s restoration, donating ten thousand *jin* of silver for the project, thereby greatly enhancing its grandeur.

During the Longqing reign, Provincial Governor Fu Xizhi and Surveillance Commissioner Wu Congxian further renovated the temple. The deity enshrined here is the Heavenly Immortal Jade Maiden, the Azure Clouds Primordial Lady. Pilgrims from all directions flock here in such numbers that they tread upon each other’s heels, offering donations of gold, silk, pearls, jade, and livestock. The local authorities daily guard and manage these abundant offerings.

In the *yiyou* year of the Wanli reign (1585), Surveillance Vice-Commissioner Han Yinggeng ordered officials to assemble workers and restore the temple to its former design. The main hall, facing south, comprises five bays. The easternmost bay is enclosed and designated the "Eastern Treasury," while the westernmost bay is similarly enclosed and named the "Western Treasury," serving as repositories for the various donations. Behind the main hall, a three-bay structure is erected and titled the "Convenience Hall." To the right of the main hall, a four-bay pavilion is built and named the "Rest Pavilion." All these were newly constructed.

Before the main hall, the left wing features a three-bay "Descendants’ Hall," and the right wing a three-bay "Eye-Brightening Hall." Between them lies an open terrace and a paved pathway. To the south of the pathway stands the three-bay main gate, flanked by an eastern drum tower and a western bell tower. Outside the gate, three ceremonial arches stand in a row: the central one is inscribed "Erected for the Azure Clouds," the eastern "Pacifying the People," and the western "Aiding the World." Before the Azure Clouds Arch, spanning a fire pond, rises a gate tower named the "Golden Portal." All these enhancements built upon the existing structures.

Carved beams and painted rafters, iridescent hues of clouds and mist—this is a divine abode, not of this mortal realm. The temple’s topography is majestic: to the east and south lie the Five Cliffs, while to the northeast and west stretch the mountain’s summit, the Cliffside Inscription Peak, and the Sunrise Viewing Peak, arrayed like a screen on three sides. Before the temple, the land resembles twin gate towers. Descending west from the temple gate along stone steps for about three *zhang*, one overlooks sheer cliffs, gazing down upon the city and its wards like garden plots. Yet from the city below, the peaks and crags obscure the temple entirely—this is truly a sacred precinct of creation, a marvel of heaven.

**Imperial Prayer Text**
In the sixteenth year of the Hongzhi reign, the *guihai* year (1503), on the first day of the first lunar month, the Emperor dispatched Eunuch Miao Kui of the Imperial Horse Bureau to offer sacrifices to the Azure Clouds Primordial Lady, saying:
*"Your virtue is vast and benevolent, your compassion boundless. You safeguard the mortal realm with longevity and bless all beings with your grace. Now, as We suffer from indisposition, We humbly beseech your sacred power for restoration to health and peace. With these offerings of incense and silk, We reverently present this sacrifice, praying that your numinous efficacy may shine forth and bestow blessings upon Our realm. Respectfully announced."*

In the eleventh year of the Jiajing reign, the *renchen* year (1532), on the thirteenth day of the second lunar month, the Empress Dowager dispatched Duke Jiang Ying of Yutian, Grand Guardian of the Heir Apparent, to offer sacrifices to the Heavenly Immortal Jade Maiden, the Azure Clouds Primordial Lady, saying:
*"The Emperor has ruled the realm for twelve years, yet the imperial heir remains unestablished, leaving the foundation of the state uncertain. All gods and people look to this with hope. We now dispatch an official to your shrine to reverently perform the jiao rites, purify the sacrifices, and offer prayers. We humbly implore your divine favor to silently activate the mechanisms of transformation, that descendants may flourish and an exemplary heir be granted soon—an eternal blessing for the dynasty and the altars of state. Our earnest devotion is boundless. Respectfully announced."*

**A Brief Examination of the Jade Maiden**
The Jade Maiden of Mount Tai has manifested her numinosity across the land since antiquity. Erroneous traditions claim she is the daughter of the Eastern Peak, but this is a misconception. The veneration of the people, passed down through generations, has perpetuated these errors. Not only the ignorant masses but even learned scholars have seldom investigated her true origins.

According to the *Prefectural Gazetteer*, atop Mount Tai lies the Jade Maiden Pool. Ma Duanlin’s *Comprehensive Examination* records that beside the pool once stood a stone statue of the Jade Maiden, its spring source muddied. When Emperor Zhenzong of the Song conducted the eastern Feng sacrifices and prepared his encampment, the spring suddenly surged upward, its flow expanding and its waters becoming so clear they mirrored reflections, its taste sweet and pure. Wang Qinruo requested its dredging, but the statue was damaged in the process. An edict ordered its replacement with one of jade. Upon completion, the Emperor and his ministers observed the work, then reverently returned the statue to its original site for worship.

Liu Yuxi’s poem "Seeing Off Daoist Zhang to the Eastern Peak" reads: *"Long have you served the Primordial Lady in the Purple Tenuity."* Li Bai’s *Poem on Mount Tai* also mentions: *"Four or five Jade Maidens, drifting in maple leaves beneath the nine heavens."* Furthermore, Li Chuo’s *Record of the Jasper Pool* states that the Yellow Emperor built the Daizong Temple on Mount Tai and dispatched seven maidens, clad in cloud crowns and feathered robes, to tend the incense and welcome the Perfected One of the Western Kunlun. From this, we deduce that the Jade Maiden must have been one of the seven maidens sent by the Yellow Emperor who attained immortality through cultivation. Later generations established her shrine on the mountain. During the Song and Yuan dynasties, her image was honored with a temple, and evidence abounds. In our present dynasty, the expansion of the halls has made her responses even more manifest—otherwise, why would such a sacred site exist atop the lofty Mount Tai? Drawing from the *Comprehensive Examination*, Tang poetry, and Li Chuo’s record, we find this account increasingly credible.

**Liu Dingzhi’s Record (Excerpt)**
Atop Mount Tai’s summit stood an ancient shrine to the Azure Clouds Primordial Lady. Due to its lofty elevation, clouds and rain caused the timber to rot swiftly, while fierce winds often tore away the tiles, leaving the shrine unable to endure. Recently, Vice Censor-in-Chief Yuan Jie, while inspecting Shandong as Provincial Governor, visited the shrine and, seeing its dilapidation, resolved to rebuild it. Bronze beams, iron tiles, and glazed eaves ensured its durability; vermilion pillars, azure walls, and painted decorations enhanced its splendor. The structure was expanded in height, breadth, and depth beyond its former scale, its costs covered by official funds without burdening the people.

In the past, donations from mountain pilgrims were entrusted to shamans and priests, leading to wasteful and unaccountable expenditures. Now, however, all is managed with order and regulation.

**Yin Long’s Record (Excerpt)**
The Illustrious Perfection Abbey, located at the summit of Mount Tai, is traditionally said to be the shrine of the Heavenly Immortal Jade Maiden, the Azure Clouds Primordial Lady. In the fifteenth year of the reign of the Sagely Emperor, Eunuch Chen Xi was dispatched to oversee the sacrificial rites. The following year, an edict ordered the renovation of the shrine, expanding it upon its original site. The main hall comprises five bays, housing the Primordial Lady, while the flanking corridors, each with three bays, serve as residences for her attendant deities.

The spirit-way has its gates, and the bell-and-drum towers stand tall. Fearing that their sheer, soaring walls would be easily eroded by wind and rain, the builders fashioned tiles from fired clay and roofing from chilled iron, ensuring utmost durability. The structures were then coated in black and red lacquer, their gold and azure hues shimmering in radiant splendor—surpassing even the former glory.

The sacred image was cast in bronze to the right and carved from stone to the left, with a core of fragrant *chenxiang* wood. Upon a throne of intricately hewn stone, the deity sits adorned in a phoenix crown draped with rosy clouds, majestic and serene in divine purity.

All expenses were drawn solely from the treasury of the deity’s own endowments, ensuring every need was met without the slightest imposition upon the people.

Since the inception of the project, those who oversaw its execution included Grand Master Wen An, Xing Biao, Surveillance Commissioner Wei Hui, Liu Zhang, and down to the devoted elders and righteous scholars—all labored diligently, honoring the imperial decree and revering the divine.

Mount Tai, the mighty guardian of the East and the most venerable of the Five Peaks, has long been the site of the feng and shan sacrifices through the ages. Under our successive sagely emperors, upon each enthronement, court ministers were dispatched to offer rites at the sacred peaks. At times, eunuchs were also commanded to attend the shrine, presenting gold, silk, cowrie shells, and other treasures as tokens of sincerity, or adorning the images with ceremonial robes, banners, and canopies. Such devotion moves the divine, for the numinous power of the deity truly responds to human reverence.

As recorded in the *Prefectural Gazetteer*, of old, ferocious tigers once plagued the land—prayers sent them fleeing. Swarms of locusts brought disaster—prayers caused them to perish. From droughts to floods, the people’s supplications were met with unfailing efficacy. Thus, when the multitudes from near and far gather in worship, a sacred breeze stirs, inspiring the wise to deepen their sincerity, the simple to awaken reverence, the loyal to steel their resolve, the wicked to abandon their schemes. Goodness is kindled within, and base desires are stilled.

Ah, how wondrous is the grace of the divine!

**Xu Pu’s Record (Excerpt)**

In the eighth year of the Hongzhi reign, during the first lunar month of spring, the Censor-in-Chief and Governor of Shandong, Lord Xiong, memorialized that the Bixia Lingying Palace atop Mount Tai had been destroyed by fire and should be restored. The matter was referred to the Ministry of Revenue for deliberation, which opined that Mount Tai, as one of the Five Peaks, possessed the power of generating life, and that all temples connected to the sacrificial canon ought not to be neglected. Moreover, as the deity was revered for its numinous efficacy, people from all directions flocked there—scholars, women, and merchants bearing gold and silk as offerings—accumulating vast quantities each year. It was thus decreed that the Surveillance Commissioner should register these contributions to fund the reconstruction. The imperial edict approved, and the task was entrusted to Assistant Commissioner Chen Kuan to oversee the registry. The Inner Treasury bestowed one hundred taels of silver, with the Vice Commissioner managing its disbursement. In total, over seven thousand three hundred taels of silver were expended, along with countless laborers, to complete the project. Lord Xiong then wrote to the capital, requesting a record of the event.

I have long heard of the splendor of Mount Tai, yet my duties at court have prevented me from visiting, much as I desired to ascend its heights. Consulting the *Gazetteer of Tai’an Prefecture*, I found detailed descriptions of its topography and temples, learning of the so-called Bixia Temple. There, the former Grand Secretary Liu Wen’an’s inscription remains, its language as towering and majestic as the mountain itself, evoking the grandeur of the scene. Reading it, one feels as though gazing upon Fusang, scaling the Sun-Viewing Peak, or drifting beyond the inverted shadows of the celestial winds.

Since the dawn of heaven and earth, mountains and rivers have existed, their extraordinary beauty forming the abodes of spirits, places where lords of the land offer sacrifices. Expanding this principle, one sees that such temples are indispensable. Moreover, the state’s sacrificial canon, which gathers the people’s hearts and extends the realm’s prosperity, draws sustenance from this. Thus, the rise and fall of such temples, the prevention of decay—are these not the duties of those in office?

The record concludes with a poem:
*Atop the peak of the Great Peak stands a temple,*
*Its golden splendor soaring high, exalted and grand.*
*Receiving Qian and embracing Kun, its vital qi is concentrated,*
*The deity Bixia—by whom was she enfeoffed?*
*Her cloud-crowned, mist-robed temple looms,*
*Her moon-star radiance, her ice-snow visage.*
*The mortal world is distant, this sight rare,*
*Yet the gates gather the five directions, chariots and horses throng.*
*Great and small, all serve in harmony,*
*The nation flourishes, the harvests bountiful.*
*Of the Five Peaks, this one lies in the east,*
*Its inscribed stone shall endure forever.*

---

**Cui Wenkui’s Record (Excerpt)**
The Supreme Ultimate divides into movement and stillness, generating yin and yang. Mountains anchor the earth’s stability, embodying stillness and yin; water arises from Heaven’s One, embodying movement and yang. Mount Tai pierces the sky, its peaks rising thousands of zhang above the earth. At its summit, springs emerge, flowing into stone pools so clear one can see their reflection—an image of yang rooted in yin. The spring is named Jade Maiden, though its origins are lost to the gazetteers. As the records state, forests, valleys, and hills that produce clouds, wind, and rain, or manifest strange phenomena, are called divine. Mount Tai nurtures spirits, its summit connecting to the celestial realm, where the Qian trigram bestows its numinous power upon the earth. Kun’s way gives rise to the feminine, and the title "Heavenly Immortal Jade Maiden" likely shares its origin with the later enfeoffment of Bixia Yuanjun.

The temple stands here, bowing to the peaks of sun and moon, embracing the beauty of layered ridges. To the left lies the towering summit of the mountain, to the right, the open expanse of the Heavenly Gate. Among all the renowned sites, none surpass the wonder of this place. Perhaps the deity was commanded by the August Heaven and Primal Earth to oversee the workings of creation—how else could its divine majesty be so vast? Truly, it cannot be fathomed lightly.

For generations, this temple has been revered, but in our dynasty, the rites of veneration have grown especially grand. Exotic incense and rare treasures are bestowed, along with resplendent crowns and brocade robes. Annually, officials are dispatched to offer sacrifices, while from across the land, heroes, scholars, women, and merchants journey here, purifying their hearts and making vows. Those who travel the roads bearing incense and silk number in the millions each year. Gold, silk, and paper money pour in like rivers and clouds, enriching the public treasury, funding annual expenditures and famine relief. Confucius spoke of spirits inspiring the people to don their finest attire for sacrifices—does this not describe such devotion?

In the year Jia-Shen of the Jiajing reign, the Censor-in-Chief, Lord Wang Yaofeng, was entrusted by the sagacious Son of Heaven to govern the eastern lands. He visited the temple to pay his respects and, seeing its dilapidated state, sighed deeply. He then issued orders for its renovation. Construction began on the twenty-sixth day of the tenth lunar month in the year Yi-You, and was completed on the eighteenth day of the ninth lunar month in the year Ding-Hai. The new temple, resplendent in red and lacquer, soars above the mist and clouds, a magnificent sight amidst Mount Tai, south of the Dipper’s handle. The deity, too, now rests in peace.

When the deity is at peace above, the people are secure below. The unseen and the manifest work in harmony, the vital breath steams and rises, rain and sunshine arrive in due season. Thus, grasses flourish, the hundred grains ripen, and the court is spared concerns over the east. This may well stem from Lord Wang’s sincere devotion, which moved the divine.

**Guo Shaotong’s Record (Excerpt)**
My friend, Pan Xitai, the Right Assistant Commissioner of Yintai, once ascended this mountain during his leisure. Moved by the offerings left behind and concerned for their safety, he reported to the Governor, the Grand Coordinator Lord Wang Nangao of Nangao. They cast four bronze drums, hollowing their centers and placing them as locks, symbolizing the four seasons and the generation, growth, harvest, and storage of the myriad things. Additionally, they fashioned a tripod and vases to channel the flow of qi—though this may not fully embody the idea of "drumming and dancing to exhaust the spirit."

Pan further wished to display moral conduct, inscribing each with an inscription:
*For the first drum:*
*The primordial qi drums, the myriad things spring.*
*Alas, Mount Tai—divine, oh divine!*
*For the second drum:*
*The qi drums again, the radiant summer arrives.*
*All things prosper—such is the divine transformation.*
*For the third drum:*
*Who drums and dances? Autumn’s harvest comes.*
*For the fourth drum:*
*The drumming ceases, winter’s storage begins.*

The storehouse of things, the repose of the divine.
Dissolve and rest—you drum, you dance.
Things return to their root, the primordial source renews.

*For the tripod:*
Great virtue arises daily, petal-offerings sustain sincerity.
Man abides in the great furnace, left and right—"harmony and peace."

*For the vase:*
What is not balanced is emotion; what is not partial is equilibrium.
Does the divine rely on man? Or man on the divine?
Alas, profound and subtle—it may subtly assist the divine luminaries.

Thus, I have recorded this to provide future generations with reference. So ends the account.

**The Transmission of Wang’s Bronze and the Jade Maiden**

The Jade Maiden is a celestial immortal and divine woman. She first appeared during the time of the Yellow Emperor and was seen again during the reign of Emperor Ming of Han.

According to the *Jade Maiden*, as recorded in Li Kua’s *Records of the Jasper Pool*, the Yellow Emperor once built the Terrace of Mount Tai and dispatched seven maidens, clad in cloud crowns and feathered robes, to cultivate and draw near to the Perfected One of the Western Kunlun. The Jade Maiden was one of these seven, and she attained the Dao through her practice.

The *Scroll of the Jade Maiden* states that during the reign of Emperor Ming of Han, in the Western Ox Nation’s Sunning Prefecture, in the county of Fengfu, a virtuous man named Shi Shoudao had a wife, Madam Jin, who gave birth to a daughter at the hour of zi on the eighteenth day of the fourth month in the seventh jiazi year of the Zhongyuan era. Named Jade Leaf, she was comely in appearance and keen in nature. At three, she understood human relations; at seven, she spontaneously heard the Dharma. She once paid homage to the Queen Mother of the West. At fourteen, moved by her mother’s teachings, she wished to enter the mountains. Guided by the immortal Master Cao, she entered the Yellow Flower Grotto of Heavenly Void Mountain to cultivate. (Heavenly Void is Mount Tai, and the grotto is the site of the Stone Chamber.) After three years, her elixir was complete, her primordial essence radiant, and she thereafter dwelled on Mount Tai.

Thus, Mount Tai came to have the spirit of the Jade Maiden. At the summit, there is a pool named Jade Maiden, and beside it stands a stone statue of her. When Emperor Zhenzong of Song made his eastern pilgrimage to perform the Feng sacrifice, he first prepared the site at the spring. Suddenly, the water surged forth, so clear and limpid that one could see one’s reflection, its taste sweet and fine. Wang Qinruo requested that it be dredged. When the statue was accidentally broken, an edict ordered it replaced with jade, and the stone was restored to its pedestal. A shrine, the Zhaozhen Temple, was constructed to honor her.

Yin Long remarked that the world’s transmission of the shrine to the Heavenly Immortal Jade Maiden, the Azure Radiance Primordial Sovereign, began here. During the Chenghua era of our dynasty, the temple was expanded and rebuilt, renamed the Palace. In the Hongzhi era, it was further renamed the Temple of Numinous Response, and in the Jiajing era, it was again renamed Azure Radiance. The title "Azure Radiance Palace" originated here. Successive dynasties enlarged and renovated it, expanding the palace’s scale, and the spirit’s numinosity grew ever more manifest. Worshippers from all directions vied to journey there, and the deity’s blessings upon the nation have been abundant.

The Neishi Clan’s Records state: I have heard that even the Grand Historian, whose learning encompassed the vastest of records, still verified his accounts against the Six Classics—the *Book of Songs* and *Book of Documents*. Though the writings of Yu and the Xia may be fragmentary, their essence remains discernible. Yet for the Jade Maiden, by what evidence shall I attest her?

For heaven and earth are but a single qi: in the heavens it becomes stars and constellations, on earth it forms rivers and mountains, and when manifest, it takes human form—all is one qi. The Jade Maiden’s transformation into a person, and from person into a deity, is no different. Thus, what can be observed in the annals and records may be examined.

To claim that the Jade Maiden was born of the Eastern Marchmount’s Golden Rainbow and Great Unity’s Primordial Father, and that her incarnate form was Avalokiteśvara in the world—how can this be reasonable? Yet her numinous power became manifest on Mount Tai, for this was the place where she once dwelled and cultivated. Her spirit lingers and moves there, like clouds and mist in the vast sky, clinging and unwilling to depart. Now, Mount Tai stands in the eastern lands, bearing the virtue of Wood, while the Jade Maiden, as Kun, embodies the Water phase. The pools there are naturally her origin, and she abides in this sacred place to display her wondrous efficacy, aiding the unending virtue of generation and transformation. This is as if ordained by Heaven.

Yet the world claims that the Jade Maiden personally received the Emperor’s decree as the Lady of Azure Perfection, forever guarding Mount Tai to preside over its sacrifices—how mistaken! How mistaken! I have thus set this forth to provide the benighted with a reliable account, and so compose this record.

The Censor-in-Chief for Patrol and Inspection, Fang Yuanyi, composed the "Prayer Text for Tai’an Prefecture":

O Spirit, whose mighty authority governs the mechanisms of transformation,
Who steers and commands yin and yang,
Aiding Heaven to nurture humanity,
Radiant in numinous brilliance, shedding light—
A single offering of profound merit,
Eight echoes resound in thunderous acclaim.
Your luminous virtue mirrors the heavens,
Illuminating the seven celestial mansions.
Lofty Mount Song among the sacred peaks,
In the Hall of Inquiry, care is bestowed.
The immortal’s chariot returns in due course,
To this dwelling of repose.
The state maintains its local sacrifices,
The people present jade tablets and silken offerings.
Manifesting your numinous power, harmonizing all,
The Four Seas come to pay homage.
Here stands the Prefect,
Daily presenting the customary offerings.
With solemn vessels arrayed,
The keys and drums resound in harmony.
As the Spirit descends,
Jade chimes ring clear and resonant.
Wind and thunder serve as your chariot,
The Milky Way as your robes.
The hymns rise in reverence,
The multitudes stand in solemn awe.
Having sheltered these people,
Grant that the hundred grains flourish.
Eternally secure the foundations of the state,
Upholding the pure and tranquil order of the cosmos.
May the sacrificial rites proceed without error,
As the Spirit nurtures and sustains all.
May the offerings be accepted.

---

The Governor and Censor-in-Chief, He Qiming, proclaimed:

In the eighth year of Wanli, as I toured the eastern regions of Yanzhou, I followed the springs of Yi and Wen, ascended the summit of Mount Tai, and rested there. Suddenly, I heard the people from all directions who had come to present incense and pay homage to the Primordial Lady—each weeping aloud, like children long parted from their parents’ embrace. Yet what is the reason for this? It is the response to the sincerity of their hearts.

The heart that serves one’s parents becomes filial piety;
The heart that serves one’s lord becomes loyalty;
The heart that treats one’s siblings becomes fraternity;
The heart that deals with friends becomes trust;
The heart that teaches one’s descendants becomes kindness;
The heart that does not seize what belongs to others becomes righteousness;
The heart that does not covet undeserved gains becomes honor.
Preserve this heart, and the incense you offer shall bear good fruit;
Preserve this heart, and your pilgrimage to the sacred mountain shall sow fields of blessing.
Should it stray, however—should the heart once turn away—
Then in the light of day, men shall condemn you;
In the hidden depths, spirits shall reproach you.
Can even Mount Tai shield you then in partiality?

Confucius said: *“To offend Heaven is to have no place left for prayer.”*
This speaks of shunning transgression.
Mencius said: *“Even the wicked, if they fast and bathe, may serve the Supreme Emperor.”*
This speaks of turning toward goodness.
How can one not stand in awe?

Thereupon, I commanded Prefect Yuan Qiang to proclaim this to the pilgrims from all directions, that the hearts of men might be rectified.

---

**Memorial for Rain by Governor and Censor-in-Chief Li Fu**

O Spirit, You who hold office beneath the august heavens,
Who wield the mysterious transformations of yin,
Who distill clouds into rain to moisten all under heaven—
Those who pray for the harvest and beseech Your numinous power
Daily hasten across the realm;
Those who perform good deeds and invoke blessings
Often gather in these lands of Hua.
Here in the eastern domain, where You have long made Your dwelling,
Where Your radiant presence ever roams,
This place is no less sacred than the altars of pine and elm.

Yet now, for four months, no rain has fallen.
The scorching sun blazes relentlessly;
A thousand *li* lie parched, the fields barren of lush grass.
This has moved the Son of Heaven to sorrow and toil,
To tread the mulberry groves and pray with bared shoulders.
I, who govern this eastern land—how shall the people be secured?
Day and night, I tremble with dread, my heart as if bound in prayer.

I have searched for fault within myself,
Willing to await punishment upon a bed of leaves.
O Spirit, have mercy upon these common folk,
Prolong their fleeting lives for but a moment,
And grant them sweet rain,
That the withered may revive and the stalks rise again.
Then Your boundless grace shall equal the vast benevolence of the imperial throne.

I, Fu, humbly lead the officials and people,
Purifying our hearts to knock upon Your gates.
O Spirit, look upon us and heed our plea,
That the manifest Dao may be glorified.
We await Your command with utmost reverence.

---

**Shengyuan Abbey**, located on the southern slope of the mountain, west of Dengdu Temple. The stone inscription of the *Memorial from the Ministry of Rites* from the Zhenghe era remains extant. Rebuilt by Zhang Zhichun of the Yuan dynasty, it was renamed *Chaoyuan Abbey*. A record by Academician Xu Shilong survives.

**Excerpt from the Memorial and Stele of the Ministry of Rites**
The Ministry of Rites memorializes: In accordance with the report from Fengfu County, Taining Prefecture, and the petition from Yizhou’s Bureau of Rites, Lou Yinqiang—having verified that beneath Mount Tai in Fengfu County lies the “Three Palaces of the Hollow Heavens,” as recorded in the illustrated classics—hereby memorializes that the abbey be granted the name *Shengyuan Abbey*. The edict is approved. Thus, the memorial is issued.

**Excerpt from Xu Shilong’s Record**
What is *Chaoyuan*? The Two Modes give rise to life, and the myriad things dwell in peace—this is the court where all ministers offer New Year’s felicitations. Thus, the Daoists took this as their model and named the abbey *Chaoyuan* (“Facing the Origin”).
The ancient hall had collapsed, and Master Dongming, Keeper of the Teachings, entrusted Tianni Zi to gather craftsmen and select timber. Though the project spanned lean years, they persevered to completion. The funds came from the donations of the *Zhenchang* Assembly at the mountain’s summit and from contributions by the Keeper of the Teachings.
Of the thirty-six Grotto Heavens and seventy-two Blessed Lands, Mount Tai is one. Its rise and fall are surely overseen by numinous spirits—how could it not be cherished and protected by divine beings?

---

**Tianshu Abbey**, also known as *Qianyuan Abbey*, now bears the plaque *Palace of the Azure Clouds Primordial Lady*, located a *li* west of the prefectural city. Built during the *Dazhong Xiangfu* era of the Song, historical records state that the Heavenly Script descended upon the southwestern slope of Mount Tai—this is the site.

**Changchun Abbey**, situated in the northwestern corner of the prefectural city. The abbey for Daoist women had long fallen into ruin, and Buddhist monks took residence there. The *Zhongtong* stele inscription by Yuan He Zi, a record for the Daoist woman Zi Shoushen, and the imperial edict of Chinggis Khan engraved by the Immortal Qiu both remain extant.
The record states that the ancestral master was called the Immortal Qiu, and *Miaozhen* was the honorific bestowed upon Shoushen.

**Excerpt from Wu He’s Record**
Teachings cannot stand without the Dao; people cannot be cultivated without teachings. All who take the Dao as their foundation and the divine as their guide strive to dispel blindness and rescue the benighted, guiding them toward the realm of eternal life.
Alas! Some hear and do not awaken; others hear and awaken—this is the fortune of a single encounter at dawn or dusk.
In recent times, by the grace of our ancestral master who established the teachings, the Seven Perfected opened the heavens. From the wise to the foolish, all were made to understand. Men practiced, women studied—all bore witness.
The Dao of *Quanzhen* is profound and ineffable.
Its teachings are subtle, wondrous, and all-encompassing.
The worthy grasp its greatness; the unworthy grasp its smallness.
Its essence lies in cutting off selfish desires, returning to simplicity, humbling oneself to serve others, and diligently cultivating virtue—this is the first step toward wandering the celestial gardens.
If one’s mind roams in tranquility and one’s *qi* merges with the void,
Neither right nor wrong, love nor hate, inwardly harming one’s life—
Then one may explore the depths of the Mysterious and the Profound.
*Miaozhen*’s cultivation and learning are beyond question.
Coming, she does not cling to gain; departing, she does not lament loss.
She does not delight in life nor fear death.
Empty yet dynamic, her heart remains unshaken.
Though heaven and earth darken, the bright sun still shines;
Though sleet and snow swirl, the warm spring unfolds—
This is the power of attaining one’s nature.
In movement, she flows with *yang*;
In stillness, she harmonizes with *yin*.
She sleeps without dreams, awakens without cares.
Empty, she awaits—thus her life is preserved.
She listens with the heart, and the *qi* of harmony fills her;
She breathes through her heels, and the Valley Spirit never dies—
This is the efficacy of preserving life.

---

**Longquan Abbey**, also called *Doumu Shrine*, located at Xiaolongkou on the mountain. Rebuilt by the Prince of De in the twenty-first year of *Jiajing*, with a stele record by Chen Jiao of Jinan.

**Altars of the Wind Earl and Rain Master**, located east of the prefectural seat. According to the *Astronomical Treatise*, the Wind Earl is the *Ji* star, and the Rain Master is the *Bi* star.

Here follows the translation:

---

Previously, the temple had fallen into ruin, leaving only a single stone tablet. In the *bingwu* year of *Chenghua* (1486), when drought persisted and prayers for rain went unanswered, clouds would gather as if to bring rain, only to be scattered by the wind. Prefect Cai Chen journeyed to the site to offer sacrifices, whereupon the wind suddenly stilled, and a great rain poured down in torrents. Thus, the temple was restored.

When the temple later collapsed, Magistrate Zheng Xiu replaced it with an altar.

**Hall of the Lofty Perfected**, located at Jinshan on the southern slope of the mountain, is also called the **Azure Emperor Temple**; later, the common folk named it the **Eye of Light Hall**.

**Three Officials Temples**—there are three: one along the central path of the mountain, called *Happy Three*; another west of the prefectural seat; and a third east of the prefectural seat.

**Dengdu Temple**, situated east of the **Ascending Origin Temple** on the southern slope of the mountain, was built in the fourteenth year of *Hongzhi* (1501). Its deity is the **Great Unity Emperor of Dengdu**, and to its left stands the **King Yama Temple**. In the *renxu* year of *Jiajing* (1552), Cui Tao, Vice Prefect of Jinan, oversaw its renovation, leaving an inscription.

**Li Qin’s Record of the Dengdu Temple at Mount Tai’s Southern Slope**

On the southern slope of the Sacred Mount stands a temple called **Dengdu**, whose deity is the **Great Emperor of the Northern Darkness of Dengdu**, accompanied by the **Ten Kings of the Underworld**. To its east is the **Hall of the Radiant Five Kings**, with the **Ten Bureaus of the Underworld** arrayed to its left and right.

According to Daoist scriptures, Dengdu governs the *yin* qi, serving as the pivot of the Nine Earths and the place where guilty ghosts and dead souls are judged and punished. Among the Ten Kings, the seventh is **Mount Tai**, as Lu Ji’s *Ode to Mount Tai* states: *"At Haoli there is a pavilion for the hidden, where ten thousand ghosts gather."* Indeed, Haoli lies but a few hundred paces from Mount Tai. Bai Juyi’s poem likewise says: *"Before and behind the Eastern Peak, souls old and new; in the northern darkness, bones fresh and ancient."* If this is so, then how could a temple to Dengdu be absent from the slopes of Mount Tai?

Yet while popular belief speaks of its power to summon, seize, and control the passage between life and death, such matters are not discussed by Confucian scholars. Nevertheless, in former times, Wu Daozi painted the full transformations of hell in Chengdu, and the people, trembling in fear of sin, abandoned the markets—even butchers and fishmongers ceased their trade. How much more, then, when the temple’s halls and terraces are adorned with depictions of the underworld’s punishments—grinding, pounding, burning, and sawing—so that those who enter grow solemn and chilled, and those who depart do so with dread, thereby awakening in them the urgency to turn from evil and avoid the torments of the afterlife? Such is the intent behind establishing teachings through the Way of the Divine—how could one dismiss its significance here?

The temple was first established by Eunuch Li Yi in the fourteenth year of *Hongzhi* (1501). Over the years, wind and rain have eroded its beams and eaves, tiles and bricks, while its vermilion and painted decorations have faded and crumbled. In the spring of the *renxu* year of *Jiajing* (1552), Vice Prefect Cui Tao of Anyang, styled *Hongzhai*, was commissioned by the two provincial administrations to oversee the restoration of the mountain’s temples. Once the major work was completed, he submitted a request to undertake the renovation of the Dengdu Temple. The administrations readily approved, and after selecting an auspicious day, construction began. Funds were drawn from incense taxes, without imposing the slightest burden on the farmers.

Work commenced in the seventh month of autumn in the *guihai* year (1553) and was completed in the tenth month of winter. The halls, corridors, gates, and enclosing walls were all thoroughly repaired. The statues of the gods and ghosts were remolded, gleaming anew. Along the western path leading up the mountain, a **Gate of the Sacred Mount** with three bays was erected. From there to the summit, additional gates were built at various sites, each inscribed with its name and adorned with couplets to enhance the scenery. The resplendent gold and azure structures now reflect upon the mountains and rivers, further magnifying the grandeur of Mount Tai.

The local gentry wished to inscribe a stele to commemorate the event. I reflect that Heaven nurtures all things in spring and restrains them in autumn, and the sage emulates Heaven by governing the people with policies of nurture and restraint through punishment. Thus it is said: *"Parents raise their children with tenderness, which is benevolence; they discipline them with warnings, which is also benevolence. Rulers govern the people with education and comfort, which is benevolence; they deter them with punishments, which is also benevolence."*

Mount Tai stands in the east, corresponding to spring, sending forth clouds and rain to bring forth all life. Yet the domain of the **Northern Darkness of Dengdu** wields the authority of autumn’s harvest and killing. Vice Prefect Cui came to oversee the mountain’s temples, yet with his remaining effort, he also attended to this temple—perhaps to admonish the ignorant and awaken in them the impulse to reform and cultivate virtue.

**Forest of Banners Hall**, with the **King Yama Temple** to its left, lies three *li* south of the mountain, between **Haoli** and **Sheshou** peaks. It houses the **Seventy-Five Bureaus** and the **Three Courts of Judgment**, each deity represented by a statue. Popular belief holds this to be the **Earthly Court**.

**Xu Shilong’s Record (Excerpt)**

Haoli was the name of an ancient dirge, composed by the retainers of Tian Heng in mourning for their lord.

The Han composer Li Yannian divided it into two melodies: *Ballad of the Dew on the Jade Sphere* for escorting princes and nobles, and *Haoli* for escorting scholar-officials and commoners. Later generations came to believe that upon death, the refined soul returns to Haoli, where spirits preside over it. Zhang Hua’s *Records of Broad Learning* and Lu Ji’s *Ode to Mount Tai* both state that after death, the soul is bound to Haoli. Bai Juyi’s poem reads: “Before and behind the Eastern Peak, souls; in the Northern Darkness, new and old bones.” Fan Dianzhi’s *Temple Record* likewise states that in life, one’s fate is cherished in Haoli, and in death, one returns to Sheshou. The Haoli Shrine lies roughly three *li* southwest of the mountain temple, to the left of the Sheshou Altar. From the Tang through the Song dynasties, its incense never ceased, and those who came to pay respects entered with solemn reverence and departed with lingering dread—could it be that people truly beheld the gods and ghosts?
