When Wu Daozi painted his *Transformation Tableau of Hell* in Chengdu, viewers were so struck with fear of sin that they turned to cultivating merit; even the butchers, fishmongers, and wine-sellers in the market saw their trade dwindle, and lawsuits decreased. Few were those who did not shudder at the sight. The shrine of Minister Zhao stands on the southern slope of Sheshou, its deity recorded in *Soushen Ji* and in the stone inscriptions of Song scholar Yuan Deng. The Three Spirits Marquis Temple, located at the Southern Heavenly Gate, honors Zhou dynasty remonstrators, Tang official Ge Zhouwu, and is also attached to the mountain temple. According to the *Comprehensive Gazetteer*, the tombs of the three lie in Gaoyuan County. The Lingzhen Marquis Temple stands east of the Piao River, southwest of the prefectural city. Its deity was originally called the Piao River General, later renamed the Tongquan Marquis. During Emperor Zhenzong’s eastern feng sacrifice, the temple was expanded, and the marquis was enfeoffed as Lingzhen. Rebuilt in the Yuan dynasty, it was recorded by Instructor Wang Zhen.

**Wang Zhen’s Record (Excerpt)**
Of Mount Tai’s three streams, only the Western Stream is secluded and mysterious, its source unfathomable. From the heights, it plunges as a waterfall straight to the mountain’s foot, where it churns and roars, emerging deep and winding, graceful and vast, until it reaches a hundred peaks and ten *li* west of the prefectural city. The locals draw from it for drinking and washing, calling it the “slippery east of the Piao.” The terrain resembles a domed tortoise, and atop it stands the temple of the Lingzhen Marquis. When Emperor Zhenzong of the Song performed rites on Mount Tai and encamped here, he was initially troubled by the drying of springs. Suddenly, the waters surged forth. Marveling at this divine wonder, he enfeoffed and established the temple. Later, when the surveillance commissioner of Tai’an and the county magistrate Sun Minxian visited the shrine together, they lamented the dilapidated halls and crumbling statues. Resolved to restore it, they had scarcely begun when the temple was swept away by a flood. The river, which had eroded the shrine’s foundations, abruptly turned westward. Having both heard of the deity’s efficacy and witnessed its power firsthand, the two officials rebuilt the temple anew to honor its worship. They entrusted me with recording this.

I have heard that mountains, forests, rivers, and marshes that benefit the people are ranked and worshipped. Mount Tai, as the foremost of the Five Peaks, is where the emperor offers sacrifices to Heaven and Earth—a place where clouds rise at the slightest breath, and rain falls within a morning’s span. Its flowing blessings nourish the people and extend even to the Son of Heaven. Its merits are manifest, and its divinity is rightly honored. Thus, I composed a hymn for welcoming and sending off the deity, to be sung in accompaniment:

Where is our marquis?
At the mountain’s foot, by the murmuring waters.
The Piao Mountain is deep, the waters resound—
there, our marquis reveals his form and voice.
His journey winds and meanders,
wind his chariot, clouds his banner.
Drums beat *kan-kan*,
water maidens sing and dance.
Our marquis arrives in joy,
his retinue harmonious, red crows in flight.
We, the people, serve him—
how abundant the blessings we receive!
When the marquis departs, how distant!
Suddenly, gloom and haze descend.
His comings and goings are unfathomable—
for ten thousand ages, we revere his divine power.

The Yuanji Duke Temple stands above White Dragon Pool on the southwest slope of the mountain’s summit. Officials offer sacrifices here in spring and autumn; in times of drought, prayers for rain have been made since the Han and Tang dynasties. The temple dedicated to the Yuanji deity was first established in the renxu year of the Yuanfeng era (1072) of the Song. Local resident Zhao He composed the *Record of the White Dragon Hall*, while Yuan native Wang Tianting wrote the *Record of Marshal Liu Li’s Miraculous Filling of the Pool*. The prefectural envoy Li Jian’s *Prayer for Rain* and prefect Lu Xiyin’s *Record of Blessings*, along with the Ming dynasty instructor Xia Jing’s *Record of Rain*, are all inscribed on stone within the temple.

**Zhao He’s Record**
On the southwest slope of Mount Tai lies a sacred spring called White Dragon Pool. Its ancient, towering stone banks enclose a bottomless abyss. The great summit looms above like a crown, while the Dayu Valley encircles it like a city wall. Above, jagged peaks resemble suspended blades; within, rushing waters roar like a hundred-zhang cataract. At dawn in autumn and dusk in summer, mists gather upon the waves in auspicious signs. Rare grasses and exotic flowers line the banks and paths, competing in fragrance. The winding current turns with the sky, embracing the peaks in its embrace—truly a majestic and wondrous place. All who ascend and behold it marvel in awe.

Since the Han and Tang dynasties through to our Song, gold dragons and jade tablets have been cast into its depths each year, and imperial incense has been offered in prayer. For the dragon commands clouds and rain, nourishing all things. How bountiful is its grace to the people! Recently, in spring and summer, drought prevailed, and the scorching sun overstepped its bounds. The worthy magistrate Yang Miao personally led the county officials and commoners in praying for rain to the White Dragon deity. By the dragon’s luminous virtue, sweet rain fell that very night, answering like an echo. Crops flourished, and the county was revived. The villagers, Huo Zhi and others, said to the magistrate: “The people are grateful for the dragon’s kindness and wish to contribute treasures to build a dragon hall in thanks.” Selecting materials and employing craftsmen, they struck a balance between grandeur and restraint. Within a month, the joyous work was complete.

The magistrate promptly reported this to his superiors and memorialized the imperial court, requesting an honorific title to commend the dragon’s efficacy. The title *Yuanji Duke* originated in the Song dynasty. During the wars, the temple was destroyed by Yan Ning, the Eastern Jurchen Duke of Jin, but within months, Ning himself perished.

In the year bingxu, Liu Li, Military Commissioner of Laiwu, was acting as Prefect of Tai’an Prefecture. To pray for rain during a prolonged drought, he performed rites by the Dragon Pool. At that time, a small white serpent emerged and played upon the incense burner. Liu took it to be a transformation of the divine dragon and bowed twice, vowing: “If rain falls within three days, I shall build a temple for the spirit.” The divination proved auspicious, and the entire army cheered, prostrating in reverence.

After several days without rain, Liu grew furious: “The spirit promised me rain—now that none has come, it must be no true spirit.” He ordered his attendants to quarry stones from the western cliff and fill the pool. As the pool neared fullness, a violent storm erupted—rain poured as if the heavens had split, and hailstones swelled to the size of parrot eggs. The crowd fled in terror. When the skies cleared, they saw that every stone cast into the pool had been swept away by the rushing currents. Struck with awe, they bowed in apology and submission. The temple that stands today was first established by Commissioner Liu.

Local elders who witnessed the event often recount it, and Prefect Zhang spoke of it in great detail. Thus, I have recorded it here alongside Li Jian’s *Prayer for Rain*.

In the gengshen year, on the first day of the second month (jihai), the nineteenth day (dingsi), Li Jian, the state companion of Tai’an Prefecture, respectfully offered a sacrifice of coarse and refined delicacies, bowing twice with reverence and touching his forehead to the ground, to the spirit of the Profound Ji River:

Last autumn, Magistrate Yan, not dismissing Li Jian as foolish or stubborn, appointed him to accompany this prefecture. At that time, the land suffered from severe drought; men and livestock fell ill with pestilence. The season for sowing wheat had already passed, yet the earth was parched and cracked, and plows could not penetrate the soil. On the day before the Double Ninth Festival, he prayed for rain here, and that very day, thunder roared and rain fell, as if in immediate response. The drought had persisted so long that only a soaking downpour could bring relief. On the fifteenth of that month, he gathered the prefect and all the officials, and despite the impropriety of his words, he once more offered prayers. That very night, rain descended, pouring for three days and nights, drenching the eastern plains for a thousand li. When the skies cleared, farmers sowed their fields, and the furrows of the countryside were filled. That the harvest of golden wheat met expectations was due to the spirit’s benevolence.

This spring, drought returned. Thus, on the day before the first *ding* day—the day when the prefect refrained from medicine—he led the officials in prayer at the spirit’s shrine. The spirit, moved by the people’s innocence, sent a sweet rain of two *cun* that very evening. Three days later, thunder and rain returned in full force, and the people rejoiced. How could we fail to gratefully accept such grace? With care, we have chosen an auspicious day, personally purified ourselves, and prepared music and offerings to present with joy in thanksgiving.

May the spirit witness this. Recorded by Lu Xiyin in gratitude for the spirit’s blessings.

Amidst the peace of All Under Heaven, within a thousand *li*, all living things—whether plant or beast—are overseen by the Inspector and the Prefect, who safeguard their prosperity and woe. When war stirs across All Under Heaven, within a thousand *li*, it is the Inspector and the Prefect who preserve the common folk, repel calamities, and achieve resonance with the divine.

Should an Inspector or Prefect lack civil and martial talent, fail in purity and stern impartiality, or neglect benevolence and uprightness, they disrupt the harmony of *qi*, and all living things in the prefecture suffer harm.

Now, the Inspector Zhu Tong and Prefect Lu Xiyin have come to govern this land. In the aftermath of war, towns lie in ruins, and the people are nearly wiped out. Upon inquiring into the cause, one cannot help but weep. Moreover, in the year *yisi*, a prolonged drought befell the land, and the people wore expressions of distress.

On the fifteenth day of the fifth lunar month, after purifying himself through fasting and bathing, the Prefect personally led his officials in prayer to the spirit of Yue Lu’s Abyssal Salvation. Repeatedly, divine responses were received. By the eighteenth day, the *xinsi* day, a great and abundant rain fell.

Daring not forget this grace, he composed a song in praise:

The dragon, as spirit,
Transforms and bends, extends and contracts,
Now vast, now minute,
Its blessings reach all living things.

Now hidden, now soaring,
Now dim, now radiant,
Clouds drift and rain descends,
All things take form in its flow.

Recorded by Xia Jing.

In the spring of the *jiaxu* year of the Jingtai reign (1454), the third lunar month passed without rain. The winter wheat withered, spring plowing lay abandoned, and the people’s hearts turned toward migration in desperation. Prefect Yang Ding, grieving for the people’s suffering as if it were his own affliction, purified himself through fasting and bathing, prepared offerings with utmost care, and invited Daoist Master Chai to conduct rites. On the first day of the fourth lunar month, he set up an altar and offered prayers with solemn devotion.

Five days passed without rain. The village elders said: "At the Yue Lu Mountains stands the shrine of the Abyssal Salvation Lord. In times past, when drought struck, those who governed here would go to pray and always receive a response." Strengthening his resolve, the Prefect, accompanied by officials and elders, traveled the muddy road to the shrine. There, they burned incense, bowed twice, and offered this prayer:

"O Spirit, you have long received offerings in this land. Surely you, in your wisdom, govern both yin and yang. If we pray in earnest and no rain comes, whose fault is it? If the harvest fails and clothing and food are scarce, how can the people find peace? If the offerings are not pure, how can you be pleased? By your power, to lash the dragon and bring rain—what difficulty is there in this?"

The words were earnest, the sincerity profound. The rites completed, as dusk fell, the prayers continued. At the appointed hour, dark clouds spread across the sky, thunder and lightning clashed, and sweet rain poured down in torrents. By the second watch of the night, it eased slightly, then fell again the next day. Within three days, rain came once more. The Prefect turned to those around him and said: "This rain comes at the right time. The right time means neither too early nor too late. Had it come five days sooner, the wheat would have suffered. Had it come five days later, how could the wheat have ripened? Without ripening, how could it bear grain? This is why this rain is timely."

Thus, what had withered flourished, what had languished revived. The spirits of all things were at ease, the people’s hearts filled with joy, and a bountiful year was assured. The Spirit’s grace toward the people was such that a grand offering of thanks was fitting. With sacrificial animals and fine wine, music and abundant offerings, they gave thanks for the Spirit’s favor, and I was charged to record this.

In an age governed by the Dao, the wind blows every five days, rain falls every ten, the year’s harvests follow their course, no blight afflicts the crops, no people die young, and the signs of Great Peace appear. Now, the rain has come at the proper time, the winter wheat has revived, spring plowing can begin, and a bountiful year is in sight. The people, blessed by this auspicious sign, leap and dance for joy, yet do not know who has brought it about. Truly, it is the Prefect’s concentrated sincerity that has moved Heaven to respond thus.

Prefect Yang, styled Zaizhi, is a descendant of the loyal martyrs of Xijiang. Through the imperial examinations, he was promoted to his current post.

This record also notes the Guandi Temples: one at Gaolao Bridge, one within the city on Yingli Street, one at the Great Stone Bridge in the Eastern Pass, and one at Xiazhang Village in the southwest of the prefecture. The last is especially numinous. Whenever great drought strikes, the people of the prefecture carry its image to the city, and upon praying, rain always follows.

*From the "Divine Edifices" section of the fourth volume of the Yue History, under the category "Spirits."*

*Volume Thirteen: Record of Incense Tax*

**Volume Fifteen: Record of Sacred Halls (from the Tenth Volume of *Tai史*)**
This section exclusively records the halls dedicated to Our Master Confucius, distinct from temples of gods and Buddhas. The shrines of the Two Masters of Lu are appended here to preserve the thread of the Dao.

**Hall of the Supreme Sage Confucius**
Located at the summit of Mount Tai, slightly west of the Jade Emperor Peak, about half a *li* distant. The site is traditionally held to be Yueguan Peak, beneath which lies Confucius Cliff. Might this be the very place where, as recounted in the *Han Shi Waizhuan*, Confucius ascended Mount Tai, gazed upon the White Horse at the Gate of Yu, and showed it to Yanzi?

During the Jiajing era, Minister Zhu Heng planned to expand the foundation here and construct a shrine to honor Confucius, but the project was abandoned due to his transfer. Between the eleventh and twelfth years of the Wanli era, former Jinan Prefecture Vice-Magistrate Hao Dayou initiated the building of a three-bay main hall, with a single-bay gate in front and enclosing walls on all sides, dedicated solely to the veneration of Confucius. However, he too was transferred before the enshrinement of the spirit tablet and the completion of the rites.

Recently, Surveillance Vice Commissioner Tan, overseeing the compilation of *Tai史*, investigated the sacred sites. The local authorities proposed restoring the aforementioned halls, enshrining the spirit tablet of Confucius, and arranging the tablets of Yanzi, Zengzi, Zisi, and Mencius as secondary honors, following the model of official academies. They also proposed conducting the *shidian* and *jimai* sacrifices in spring and autumn. Additionally, they planned to construct Daoist quarters on either side of the hall, where yellow-capped Daoists would reside to maintain the premises.

At the time, Governor Li and Surveillance Commissioner Mao approved the proposal, instructing the Provincial Administration Commission to deliberate and execute the plan. Thus, the traces of Confucius and the worthies’ visits to Mount Tai were inscribed upon the mountain, preserved from beginning to end like the military stores of Mount Chu. Visitors could now gaze upon them with delight.

**Commissioner Mao’s Prayer for the Enshrinement of the Spirit (Composed by Surveillance Commissioner Mao Zai)**
On the ___ day of the ___ month of the ___ year, we offer sacrifice to the Supreme Sage and First Teacher, Confucius:
Oh, august Master, born by Heaven’s decree,
Your essence gathered the purest *qi* of the turning eras,
Your spirit distilled the essence of the luminous peaks.
No mountain surpasses Mount Tai in grandeur,
Thus its stored essence is unmatched.
No sage surpasses the Master in sagacity,
Thus his absorption of essence is uniquely vast.
The qilin appeared at Nishan,
Nearby Jieqiu, heralding auspicious omens.
The dragon’s song echoed by the Si and Yi rivers,
Its deep currents nurturing generations of heroes.
We have heard: “Ascend Mount Tai, and the world grows small.”
To this day, your fragrant footsteps endure,
Your illustrious name resounding through the ages.
Those who behold your majesty
All long to hear your noble voice.
As if your disciples still stand upon the towering heights,
Lifting the Dipper to grasp the sun and moon,
Dispelling the blindness of the multitudes.
As if the songs of the hills and mounds resound,
Their echoes stirring the celestial winds,
Harmonizing with the pines and the cries of cranes.
As if the Six Classics of emperors and kings unfurl like brocade,
Spreading their transformative rain across the eight directions in an instant.
Though Mount Tai’s peaks are lofty,
They gain greater eminence through the Master’s presence.
Though the Master’s spiritual Dao is omnipresent,
His cherished vistas shine all the brighter.
Thus, we trace his footsteps here.
This place of ascent now hosts a revered shrine,
Where the spirit tablet is enshrined,
And the *shidian* rites are performed in due season.
Offerings of *zu* and *dou* are presented,
Not merely to evoke the reverence of “gazing up at the high mountain,”
But to inspire generations for millennia to come.
We shall witness the transformation of Qi and Lu,
Spreading effortlessly like the folds of a robe,
Gradually shaping the people of this land.
Thus, our present solemn rites
Are but a humble expression of our boundless sincerity.
We respectfully invite the Secondary Sage, Master Yan;
The Ancestral Sage, Master Zeng;
The Narrative Sage, Master Zisi;
And the亞聖, Master Meng,
To be enshrined at his side,
That their spirits may rest together.
For these four masters stood to the Master
As the lesser peaks to Mount Tai.
Their footsteps once followed closely,
Their radiant light now intertwined.
May they partake of these offerings.

**Surveillance Commissioner Mao’s Standard Prayer for Regular Sacrifices**
On the ___ day of the ___ month of the ___ year, we offer sacrifice to the First Teacher, the Supreme Sage Confucius:
Oh, Sage born by Heaven’s decree,
Spirit descended from the sacred peak.
You aided the workings of Heaven and Earth,
Your teachings guided emperors and kings.
We gaze up at Mount Tai,
Ascending with robes aflutter.
Your transformative influence touched the moment,
Your voice echoes through the ages.
We reverently behold your temple,
Where the Dao is ever renewed.
On this spring/autumn equinox,
We humbly present bright offerings.
With the Secondary Sage, Master Yan;
The Ancestral Sage, Master Zeng;
The Narrative Sage, Master Zisi;
And the亞聖, Master Meng,
Enshrined at his side,
May they partake of these offerings.

**Proposal for the Construction of the Confucian Temple at the Summit of Mount Tai (by Zha Zhilong)**
We observe that the sacred precincts of Mount Tai are the most sublime in the realm, and the revered traces of antiquity belong to the sages and worthies. Among these sages, Confucius stands as Mount Tai does among mountains. Was he not born of the mountain’s spirit? Moreover, his hometown lay in close proximity. In our distant musings, we imagine the days when the Master and his disciples ascended the heights to survey the vistas—how often they must have done so!

Thus, *Mencius* records: “Confucius ascended Mount Tai, and the world grew small.”
The *Book of Rites* (*Tan Gong*) states: “Confucius passed by the side of Mount Tai and inquired of a weeping woman, sighing that harsh governance is fiercer than a tiger.”
*Liezi* recounts: “Confucius traveled on Mount Tai and questioned Rong Qiqi on the three joys, praising his ability to find contentment.”
The *Han Shi Waizhuan* relates: “Yanzi followed Confucius up Mount Tai and saw the White Horse at the Gate of Yu. Yanzi likened it to ‘striking white silk.’”
The *Xiao Shi Lu* notes: “Zengzi tilled the fields beneath Mount Tai, thinking of his parents, and composed the *Liangshan Song*.”
All these historical accounts are well-documented and verifiable.
As for Mencius, when his mother dreamed of a divine being riding clouds from Mount Tai to hover over their home, the Song Confucians described it as “the towering majesty of Mount Tai.” How could such words lack foundation?

Viewed in this light, Mount Tai’s renowned sites were first celebrated by Confucius, and the three worthies—Yan, Zeng, and Meng—all followed in his illustrious footsteps. Yet how is it that, with the passage of time and the shifting of eras, their traces have faded into oblivion? Only the bronze statue of Confucius at the Cliff remains, half-ruined, while the whereabouts of Yan, Zeng, and Meng are lost beyond all record.

Behold the temples of the Daoist and Buddhist clergy, sprawling across the mountains and valleys, their halls resplendent with vermilion and azure—these need not be discussed. Even scholars of the Song dynasty, such as Sun Mingfu and Shi Shoudao, still offered sacrifices at the foot of the Sacred Peak. Yet sages like Confucius and worthies like Yan, Zeng, and Meng, who once trod these very grounds in life, have no sanctuary to house their spirits in death. Standing in the wind, mourning the past, how can one not feel a chill of sorrow?

Upon investigation, it was found that to the west of the Jade Emperor Temple at the summit of the mountain stand three empty halls, their walls and enclosures intact, devoid of any statues of gods or buddhas. Some two or three years prior, Hao Dayou, formerly the judicial commissioner of Jinan Prefecture, initiated plans for their construction, intending them for the veneration of Confucius. However, upon his transfer, the project remained unfinished. To this day, the halls stand vacant, gradually falling into disrepair. Yet if the existing structures are restored according to their original design, the cost would be modest, and the work could be completed in short order. Once repairs are finished, a spirit seat and tablet for Confucius shall be installed within the central hall, facing south, with those of Yan, Zeng, and Meng arranged to the east and west as attendants. Additionally, the spirit seat and tablet of Zisi shall be included, facing east and west in accordance with the layout of an academy. During the spring and autumn *ding* sacrifices, the annual incense tax shall fund the offerings: each spirit seat shall receive a table of fruits and vegetables, as is customary in prefectures and counties, while a pig and a sheep shall be presented as the principal sacrificial animals. The local official shall perform the rites on schedule. Adjacent to the halls, three rooms shall be built to house two resident Daoist priests, who shall be registered with the authorities for oversight. The pavilion housing the bronze statue of Confucius at the Cliff shall also be restored and the statue reinstated in its original location. Thus, the legacy of the sages and worthies shall endure alongside Mount Tai for all time, and the solemnity of the temple shall console the reverence of generations to come. This would not only be a blessing for the literary tradition but also a radiant honor for the mountain’s spirit. Recorded here, may these words shine resplendently in the annals of history.

Resolved on the tenth day of the tenth month in the fourteenth year of the Wanli reign.

The Shrine of the Two Masters of Lu, also known as the Taishan Academy, stands in the southeastern corner of the prefectural city, dedicated to the Song scholars Sun Mingfu and Shi Shoudao. Its rise and decline are recorded in the shrine inscriptions composed by Jin Academician Cheng Zhi (styled Huaiying) and Ming Grand Secretary Wu Kuan.

**Cheng Huaiying’s Inscription:**
The Two Masters of Lu—Sun Mingfu and Shi Shoudao of the Song—emerged during the Xiangfu and Tiansheng eras, their writings infused with the Dao of benevolence, righteousness, loyalty, and filial piety. They led the Confucian revival, earning the reverence of great scholars like Ouyang Wenzhong and Wang Wenzheng, who honored them as peers of Mencius and Han Yu. Ouyang Wenzhong even composed their epitaphs. Their contributions to the sagely Dao, their conduct in office, and their personal integrity were truly extraordinary.

Initially, the two masters built a study hall at the foot of Mount Tai, adjacent to the Grand Temple of the Sacred Peak. However, the site’s proximity to the temple’s sacred precincts proved unsuitable, so they relocated north to the mountain’s base, converting the original hall into a burial ground for scholars. They allocated annual funds for the support of students, a practice that endures to this day. Locals refer to the relocated site as the "Upper Academy."

During the Dading era, the Grand Temple burned. The following year, an imperial edict ordered its reconstruction, and the prefectural school was also renovated. Soon after, the students remarked: "The Two Masters once taught in Wenshang, and their disciples there still honor them. We, who dwell in their homeland and benefit from their virtue, can we do otherwise?" Upon hearing this, the descendants of the two masters contributed their resources to build a shrine to the left of the main gate, fulfilling the scholars’ wishes. Shi Shoudao’s grandson, Shi Zhen, dispatched his nephew Shi Lan to the capital to request his son-in-law, Cheng Huaiying, to record the events for inscription. Huaiying replied: "The Dao of the Two Masters shines as brightly as the sun and stars for posterity—what fear is there of it being forgotten? Yet permit me one observation.

When Master Sun taught the *Spring and Autumn Annals* in Lu, Master Shi became his disciple. Hearing of their reputation, Kong Daofu, a court official, journeyed from Yanzhou to pay his respects. Master Sun received the guest, while Master Shi attended him with staff and sandals, assisting him in rising, bowing, and departing—even escorting him on his return. From this, the people of Lu first learned the proper rites between master and disciple, and the moral character of scholars underwent a transformation. In recent times, scholars have prized plagiarism and shunned the bonds of teacher and friend, so that the Dao of loyalty and sincerity has long faded. Now, the state venerates the classics and nurtures scholars, aspiring to make all men like those of Zou and Lu. They must follow the great masters, delve into the profound mysteries of morality through study, and only then, when they visit the shrine of the Two Masters and partake of their lingering blessings, can they do so without shame."

**Wu Kuan’s Inscription:**
The Two Masters of Lu were the Song scholars Sun Mingfu of Taishan and Shi Shoudao of Zulai. Their shrine was first established west of the present-day Taian prefectural seat, adjacent to the Temple of the Sacred Peak. During the Jin and Yuan dynasties, it was absorbed into the temple, then relocated to the mountain’s base. Later, it fell into disrepair and was occupied by Buddhists. In the Ming dynasty, it was annexed to the prefectural school, though its cramped quarters and perfunctory rites failed to console the longing of the people of Lu.

Thereafter, Hu Xuan, a former metropolitan graduate and prefect of Deqing, petitioned Sheng Xian, Left Vice Censor-in-Chief of Shandong from Wuxi, arguing that the matter bore great weight for moral instruction. The request was forwarded to the Ministry of Rites, which approved it. The court further decreed that local officials should conduct annual sacrifices in the second month of spring, offering one sheep and one pig as a permanent rite. Hu Xuan then sought support from the provincial administration, and a site was chosen in the southeastern corner of the prefectural seat. Construction of the shrine began in the eighth month of the twenty-third year of the Chenghua reign. Before its completion, Sheng Xian retired, and Wu Xian of Meishan succeeded him, redoubling efforts to hasten its completion. Hu Xuan then wrote to request an inscription from me.

I reflect that the enduring veneration of great worthies lies beyond the grasp of petty scholars. Yet the sincerity of the request compels me to comply. During the flourishing years of the Song, the Two Masters—Sun Mingfu, who hailed from Pingyang and sojourned in Lu, and Shi Shoudao, a native of Lu—distinguished themselves in scholarship. Master Sun excelled in the *Spring and Autumn Annals*, composing *Exalting the King and Elucidating the Obscure*, a work of simplicity, impartiality, and profound fidelity to the classics’ original meaning. His learning and conduct earned him the admiration of eminent scholars, who sought his daughter in marriage or traveled to meet him. Later, Fan Wenzheng and Fu Zhenggong jointly recommended him, and he was appointed to the post of Palace Attendant, though his official career ended there.

Master Shi, born in Lu, became Master Sun’s disciple during the latter’s residence at Mount Tai. A man of moral fervor, he detested evil and composed *On Strange Doctrines*, *On the Central Kingdom*, and *Mirror of the Tang* as warnings to the age. His *Ode to the Sagely Virtue of the Qingli Era* remains widely recited. He taught the classics in his hometown, and later, at the Imperial Academy, he upheld the master’s Dao with even greater dedication, revitalizing the institution. He passed the metropolitan examinations with highest honors but advanced no further than Gentleman of the Palace.

The lives of the Two Masters are chronicled in Ouyang Wenzhong’s epitaphs and in the official histories, which attest to their eminence. Judging their words and their era, they were indeed great worthies, towering above the common herd and revered as paragons of their region. In their time, they were universally honored, and even the mountain where they dwelled was named in tribute to their virtue—a distinction rarely matched.

Though Mount Tai was recommended for office, he faced envy and was unable to fully realize his potential. As for Master Zulai, his unyielding integrity provoked calamity, nearly costing him his life and leaving his family unprotected. Such is the inevitable fate of those who cannot abide the machinations of petty men.

Six hundred years have now passed since their time. Though the world still knows of the Two Masters, it is in Lu—the land where they dwelt and were born—that the fragrance of their moral influence lingers most strongly, and where the people’s reverence for them remains undiminished. Their shrines and images still stand resplendent, never wholly abandoned, for the public conscience will not permit it. Thus, what Ouyang Wenzhong once called “the radiance of the Masters” has only grown brighter with the passage of time.

The burial sites of the Two Masters have been tended by successive local officials, who, in keeping with the court’s esteem for worthies, have bestowed additional honors and ensured vigilant protection. Moreover, both lineages have been fortunate to have descendants, and the abundance of stone inscriptions has led to the selection of their kin for enrollment as students, a measure intended to console the longing of the people of Lu. These matters are recorded here and inscribed beneath the shrines.

*Tai History, Volume Eleven: Palaces and Halls*

**Preface:**
What is the purpose of recording palaces and halls? To document the finest dwellings of Mount Tai, where travelers may linger and wander. Since ancient times, renowned pavilions, elegant halls, lofty towers, and towering pavilions have always been built upon the finest landscapes, thereby claiming the grandest vistas under heaven. And since none surpass Mount Tai in splendor, none surpass its palaces and halls in commanding such magnificence.

Here, the very branches of heaven and earth serve as its eaves and rafters; the sun and moon, its doors and windows; rosy clouds, its mats and cushions; wind and rain, its brooms and dusters. Without venturing beyond its pillars and beams, one may gaze into the far distance, survey all around, and transcend the bounds of the mortal world—an experience found nowhere else. Could this not be called the celestial capital of the upper realms?

Among these structures, the Hall of Transformative Influence preserves the fragrant footsteps of the Sage; the Taishan Academy stands upon the former grounds of the eminent scholars Sun and Shi; the Sea-Viewing Pavilion and the Pavilion of Embracing Immortals evoke the qi of bathing in sunlight and riding the winds; the Pavilion of Reverence and the Hall of Welcoming Guests serve as the gathering place for officials sent by the court to pay homage to the mountain’s lofty visitors. These are the most celebrated of all the palaces and halls.

Generally, constructing upon perilous cliffs and towering ridges is most arduous, for even the transport of timber and stone is exceedingly difficult. A single brick here costs ten times what it would on level ground. The suffering of the laborers—blood and sweat upon treacherous paths—is a sight I have witnessed firsthand. Yet with clouds swirling and mists rising, fierce winds and scorching sun, these structures decay most swiftly. Thus, many grand edifices praised in antiquity now exist in name alone.

I have often thought that construction atop Mount Tai should prioritize durability over ornamentation, maintenance over novelty, and timely repairs over waiting for collapse. Such an approach would prevent the waste of resources and the mobilization of labor, while ensuring that caretakers bear clear responsibility. This, too, is a matter of frugality and care.

**The Sea-Viewing Pavilion** (also called the Sun-Viewing Pavilion) stands on the Sun-Viewing Peak at the summit of the mountain. Fan Xianke of the Taiping era inscribed the stone plaque reading *Sun-View Pavilion*. Yang Cai erected one stele and two stone tablets, naming them *Eastern View* and *Western View* (originally intended for gazing westward toward the river’s source). Cai Shulian of the He Yuan region carved into the slope the words *First Among the Famous Mountains of the World*.

**The Pavilion of Embracing Immortals** lies west of the Sea-Viewing Pavilion at the summit. Wan Gong inscribed the plaque *Riding the Void on the Wind*. Behind the pavilion, a great stone screen bears the inscription *Where Confucius Gazed Down Upon the World*. This is the most spacious of the summit’s official residences, where high officials gather for feasts.

**The Hall of Transformative Influence** stands slightly west of the summit, at Confucius’ Cliff. Wu Qian carved into the cliff the words *Mount Tai, Lofty Peak*.

**The Pavilion of Reverence** (also the Western Official Residence) is located west of the Azure Cloud Palace, with three main chambers and flanking wings on both sides. He Qiming, former Provincial Governor and current Minister of Works, inscribed the plaque *Reverence*. Behind the pavilion lie the Heavenly Pillar Peak, the Jade Maiden Pool, and the Qin Dynasty Stele—where high officials invariably lodge.

**The Eastern Official Residence**, also west of the Azure Cloud Palace, is slightly smaller than its western counterpart and serves as the resting place for officials overseeing incense taxes.

**The Pavilion of Heaven and Earth** stands below the Great Dragon Gully. Hong Chaoxuan, Vice Minister, inscribed its current name, while Wan Gong added to the front pillars the words *Mountain of Whetstones, River of Girdles*.

**The Mid-Mountain Pavilion** is situated on the mountain southwest of the Cave of the Rising Sun.

**The Public Residence at the Gate of Sincerity** lies west of the Gate of Sincerity, comprising three chambers with flanking wings.

**The Five Pines Pavilion** has three chambers at both front and rear, with wings on the front side. It is named for the five pines planted here by the First Emperor of Qin. It is also called the *Imperial Tent*, as Emperor Zhenzong of Song once encamped here. Only two of the pines remain today. The pavilion stands midway up the mountain, where officials often dine and rest, sometimes at the Public Residence of the Gate of Sincerity.

**The Pavilion of Towering Peaks** takes its name from the *Book of Songs*. Originally located east of the Queen Mother’s Pool at the mountain’s base, it was relocated by Prefect Zhang Sheng during the Chenghua era to the left of the Water Curtain Cave.

**The Flying Cloud Pavilion**—one stood south of the mountain at Red Gate, but it collapsed long ago. Today, a stone platform supports a small structure above, with a beam path below, retaining the form of a pavilion. Fang Yuanhuan inscribed the plaque *Cloud-Ladder*, and the stone carving reads *Grotto Heaven of Penglai and Xuandu*. Xing Yifeng wrote in seal script the words *Reverence*.

**The Pavilion of High Mountains and Flowing Waters** stands at the Stone of Sunning Scriptures. Wan Gong, Provincial Governor during the Longqing era, personally oversaw its construction and composed its record.

**Wan Gong’s Inscription on the Stone Wall:**
After marking the summit of Mount Tai, I descended along the southern slope of the mountain’s base and rested at the Stone of Sunning Scriptures. The stone spans several *mu*, its surface carved with Sanskrit script in clerical style, each character as large as a dou. No one knows when it was made. Recently, a lover of curiosities engraved the *Great Learning* and *Sacred Canon* at its upper end to enhance its splendor. I then inscribed in large characters *Sunning Scriptures Stone*, each stroke nearly seven feet long and carved three inches deep, ensuring they would endure to amplify the stone’s grandeur.

The rock rises abruptly, as if cleaved by a blade. A stream cascades over the stone and hangs in midair, like ten thousand pearls suspended from the cliff. I promptly inscribed in large characters *Water Curtain*, carving deeply into the stone. The water flows gently over the characters, some partially submerged, creating a spectacle that is among Mount Tai’s most wondrous sights.

Proceeding westward through the stream, one finds a stone cliff, rising some fifteen feet high and spanning forty feet across, smooth and naturally formed, its base cradled by rock. Standing eastward before it, the Water Curtain’s spring gushes forth to the left, while dozens of peach and willow trees flourish verdantly to the right. I then built a pavilion against the cliff, constructing it entirely of stone—four pillars anchored deep into the foundation, each sunk a foot and a hand’s span, topped with stone slabs to ensure enduring shelter for countless pilgrims ascending Mount Tai.

I, fond of playing the zither, turned to my attendants and said: “Behold, this cliff rests at the foot of Mount Tai—is it not a lofty mountain? And here we stand before the Water Curtain’s spring—is it not flowing water?” I then took up my zither and plucked its strings, inviting the spirit of Mount Tai to heed the *Guangling Melody*, as if my intent soared with the high mountains and surged with the flowing streams—this is the divine masterpiece, the divine understanding. Delighted, my attendants named it the *High Mountains and Flowing Waters Pavilion*.

The *Changing Garments Pavilion* stands to the left of the Red Gate path on the mountain. All scholars and officials ascending the peak change into proper attire here, hence its name.

The *Rest Pavilion* lies west of the *Changing Garments Pavilion*, marked by three large inscribed characters.

The *Welcoming Guests Hall* is situated south of Qi, east of the mountain shrine, where officials host banquets for guests and dignitaries.

The *Sincerity and Enlightenment Hall* stands west within the mountain temple, where court-appointed ministers conducting rituals at the mountain reside.

The *Imperial Incense Pavilion* lies behind the *Sincerity and Enlightenment Hall*. Whenever the court dispatches envoys to present incense at the mountain shrine, it is first placed here, then selected and offered to the deity. The surrounding walls bear inscriptions of famous poems and prose.

The *Mount Tai Academy* rests at the foot of Linghan Peak, south of the mountain. Formerly the retreat of Tang poet Zhou Pu, it later became the teaching ground of Sun Fu, Shi Jie, and Hu Huan. During the Jiajing era, Surveillance Commissioner Lu Wenzhi erected three bays to enshrine the spirit tablets of Sun and Shi, preserving their legacy. Scholar Shao Xian wrote:
*For a thousand ages, the pure winds of Sun and Shi we revere,*
*Yet only the hall’s foundations remain by the rocky frontier.*

The *Taiyin Pavilion* stands south of the prefectural city, a relic of Emperor Zhenzong of Song’s fengshan ceremony. Censor Wu Congxian later built the pavilion and recorded its history.

*Wu Congxian’s Record of Taiyin (Excerpt)*
In the second year of my tenure in Shandong, during the late spring of the third month, I followed the path of Mount Tai, preparing to conduct affairs in Deng and Lai. Along the way, I paid homage at the mountain shrine, perilously ascending the Fengshan Terrace. Of the ancient traces that remain, only the broken steles of Qin and the cliff inscriptions of Tang endure—all else has faded into oblivion, lost to time.

Upon returning to the prefectural seat, Magistrate Li Fengyang informed me: “South of the city stands the *Yin Character Stele*, inscribed by Emperor Zhenzong of Song with his fengshan commemorative text. Located half a li from the city, it is a relic of the Eastern Fengshan ceremony from the Xiangfu era.” I accompanied my colleagues to observe it. The stele comprises five faces, joined to form a towering screen—its design unlike any other. Its reverence for heaven, respect for ancestors, and humble deference resonate through the ages. Reading its words, one cannot help but bow in solemnity and recite them with awe.

Yet as time passes and dynasties change, the once-dazzling golden seals and jade caskets now lie buried in overgrown ruins, few traces spared from erosion. Moved by this, I ordered the local officials to clear the land and build a pavilion north of the *Yin Stele*. On the fifteenth day of the ninth month that autumn, the project was completed. The pavilion spans five bays, its foundation laid with stone, overlooking a moat. With layered gates and towering eaves, the stele stands majestically before it, as if the Celestial Emperor himself presides. Pheasants strut behind, evoking the mirage of a sea market. Gazing north, Mount Tai’s sheer cliffs inspire reverence; looking south, the towering Zulai Mountain stirs thoughts of ancient sages. Compared to the once-desolate wilderness, the site now stands grand and resplendent—a magnificent spectacle not only for the prefecture but also a radiant tribute to Mount Tai, its imperial calligraphy illuminating past and present.

I then commanded artisans to inscribe *Taiyin Stele* to the south, named the pavilion *Illustrious Service to the Celestial Emperor*, and its gate *Ascending the Ancient Relics*. The city’s scholars and commoners rejoiced in its completion, lining the path with locust and parasol trees. Behind the pavilion, a canal was dug and planted with water caltrops and lotuses. At its center, a flower garden was enclosed with a fence, filled with rare blossoms and lush grasses to welcome visitors from afar. To the west, half a *mu* of land was cleared to build several rooms for travelers to rest and prepare meals. Since then, all who visit linger for days, reluctant to depart. Truly, this is a splendid achievement.

The *Ring of Verdure Pavilion* stands between Haoli and Sheshou Mountains. From here, one gazes north at Mount Tai, while the surrounding peaks—An, Niu, Zulai, and others—encircle it in emerald splendor, hence its name.

The *Gathering Beauty Pavilion* lies at the western foot of the mountain, ten *li* from the prefectural seat. Built by Jin Prefect Yao Jianrong, it was later restored by Yuan Magistrate Wang Wu. Surveillance Commissioner Xu Ying composed its record. The pavilion now lies in ruins, its inscribed stone relocated to the Confucian temple.

*Xu Ying’s Record (Excerpt)*
Of the world’s famous mountains, none rival Mount Tai in majesty and grandeur. Among its scenic wonders, none surpass the secluded beauty of West Stream. Nestled at the right foot of Mount Tai, it winds for dozens of *li*, its paths rugged beneath dense canopies. Clear springs and strange rocks abound in myriad forms, growing ever more wondrous the deeper one ventures.

Where the stream reaches its utmost limit, sheer cliffs plunge a hundred *zhang*, cascading torrents hurtling downward—seen from afar, they resemble a hanging silk sash, known as the *Celestial Girdle Spring*. To its west lies a vast ravine, where a single stream plummets from the *Heaven’s Triumph Rock*, scattering across a broad boulder before spilling over the ravine’s upper eaves like a suspended feast of water—the *Water Curtain Grotto*. Above, peaks such as *Hibiscus*, *Hanging Blade*, *Flying Crow*, and *Lion* rise in emerald sheerness, while below, the *Black Python* and *White Dragon* dwell in the divine abyss of the *Spirit Pool*. Clouds and mist exhale and swallow, shifting between obscurity and clarity; pearls leap and foam scatters, thunder roars and lightning flashes—so dazzling is the spectacle that the eye cannot take it all in. Truly, this is the very heart of mountains and waters, the profoundest recess of the great earth.

During the Jin *Dading* era, Prefect Yao of Tai’an, facing the *Water Curtain* and gazing down upon the *Celestial Girdle*, constructed a pavilion. Without moving a single wine vessel or platter, all the scenic wonders converged before it, and so he named it *Gathering Beauty*. As Su Shi’s poem says:
*"Though rivers and mountains abound in splendor,
Pavilions and terraces are hard to secure.
To ascend and not grasp the essence—
All things stand in stubborn defiance."*

How could the world possess a pavilion that so perfectly captures the essence of ascent as this one?

The *Numinous Liquid Pavilion* stood north of the *Jade Maiden Pool* atop the mountain, built during the Song dynasty and now in ruins. The *Sweet Spring Pavilion* was located at the *Celestial Script Abbey*, also a Song-era construction.

The *Encircling Chant Pavilion* lay west of the *Imperial Incense Pavilion* in the Eastern Peak Temple, enclosed by a courtyard of roughly one *mu*. On all four sides, stone carvings bore poems and eulogies by ancient and modern luminaries, hence its name. Among them were inscriptions by Han Qi, Cai Xiang, Fan Zhongyan, Ouyang Xiu, Shi Manqing, Su Zimei, and Lin Bu. The *Gathering True Abbey* poem by Zhong Fang was also present, though Fang’s carving had faded beyond recognition. Only the names of these masters remained. Over time, the pavilion fell into ruin, and the stone inscriptions lay buried in overgrowth and filth.

In the fourteenth year of the *Wanli* reign, after Surveillance Vice Commissioner Mao Yunxing’s successor, Xiang Cheng, submitted a proposal, Magistrate Sang Dongyang of Jinan Prefecture was commissioned to restore the pavilion on its original site. The handwriting of Han, Fan, Ouyang, and other masters was cleaned and renewed. Thus, the relics of these ancient worthies, spanning centuries, shone anew, serving to inspire future generations and guide them toward reverence.

*Xiang Zhilong’s Proposal to Honor the Relics of Eminent Worthies such as Han, Fan, and Ouyang*

The scenic grandeur of Mount Tai, along with its myriad historical sites for contemplation, need not be recounted in detail. Yet while the literary legacy of antiquity endures, the character of its authors is of greater urgency. Only when a person is truly awakened do their words stand firm. Even a single phrase or character, redolent with their name and fragrance, allows contemporaries to envision their bearing, while later generations, upon hearing of them, cannot but feel admiration, inspiration, and emulation. They seek to model their literary elegance and aspire to emulate their moral conduct. Thus, such relics serve to hone talent and uphold the Way of the world—hence their enduring value.

I have observed that to the west of the *Sacred Peak Temple*, beside the *Imperial Incense Pavilion*, there once stood the *Encircling Chant Pavilion*, its origins lost to time. No one knows when or by whom it was first built. Its four enclosing walls were entirely covered with stone inscriptions of poems and eulogies by renowned worthies of past dynasties, their calligraphy arrayed in succession. The pavilion stood at its center, and as one gazed upon the inscriptions, they seemed to encircle it—hence its name. Among the most illustrious of these worthies were Han Weigong, Fan Wenzhenggong, Ou Wenzhonggong, Cai Duanming, Su Zimei, Mei Shengyu, Lin Hejing, and Shi Manqing—all luminaries of the former Song dynasty, whose calligraphy must not be allowed to fade into oblivion.

Alas, in recent years, the pavilion has long lain in ruins. Fortunately, its name survives in old records. In my official capacity, I sought its former site according to these records, but none among the local gentry knew of it. After prolonged inquiries, I was at last able to locate its original foundation. Happily, the four enclosing walls and their stone inscriptions remained intact, though one corner had collapsed. Even more fortunately, the calligraphy of Han, Fan, Ou, Cai, and the others remained unharmed, though the walls were thick with dust, making it difficult to discern the inscriptions, and the accumulated filth rendered the site unbearable to approach.

I am deeply moved by this. Looking back through the ages, the responsibility lies with the gentlemen of later times. If we do not act now to honor these relics, the four walls will soon crumble entirely—not merely one corner—and how could we bear to see the centuries-old legacy of these worthies fade into obscurity in this flourishing age? Would this not be a grievous loss to our cultural heritage?

Moreover, the original structure can be restored without new construction, and the cost in labor and materials would be minimal. Should we not rebuild the pavilion on its former site, reinstating its name *Encircling Chant*? The surviving inscriptions on the four walls should be cleaned, and the collapsed corner repaired by adding inscriptions from eminent worthies of our present dynasty, thus completing the restoration. Would this not renew the legacy of the Song masters while further illuminating the literary splendor of our own era? Would it not enhance the glory of Mount Tai, ensuring its legacy endures? And might it not also serve to inspire talent and uphold the Way of the world?

This is my earnest hope.

Proposed on the twenty-fifth day of the first month in the fourteenth year of the *Wanli* reign.

The *White Cloud Pavilion* stands slightly downslope to the southwest of the summit, on the southern face of Phoenix Mountain, where the *White Cloud Grotto* is found. In the fourteenth year of the *Wanli* reign, Provincial Governor and Censor-in-Chief Li Yitao ascended Mount Tai in prayer for rain. Struck by the site’s beauty, he consulted with Provincial Inspector Han Jianfang to mark its significance. They built a pavilion and a pavilion-hall, and the project was jointly completed by Provincial Inspector Mao Jidai, as detailed in the commemorative inscription.

*Record of the White Fragrance Pavilion* by Censor-in-Chief Li Zai
This summer, I was entrusted by imperial command to assume the governorship of the eastern lands. The season was plagued by drought, and before my predecessor had departed, I hastened to pray to the spirit of Mount Tai, beseeching a single day’s grace for the people. Privately, I recalled the *Gongyang Commentary*, which speaks of clouds from Mount Tai bringing rain across the land in less than a morning—how could such clouds and rain not fulfill my prayer? Soon after, I witnessed white clouds curling and spreading to shroud the valleys, emerging from the *White Cloud Grotto*. I went to inspect it and found it on the southern slope of Phoenix Mountain, where stone steps descended precipitously. The grotto stood over a *zhang* high, its breadth proportionate. Within, a stone aperture, two *chi* wide and just over a *chi* deep, opened above a sheer, unfathomable ravine—this was the famed *Hundred-Zhang Cliff*. The aperture held water year-round, its source unseen, swelling in summer to overflow in torrents, hence the legends of the *Dragon Grotto’s Sweet Rain* and the *Cloud-Root Rain Vein*. Here, I grew certain that the claim of "raining across the land" was no exaggeration. No better words could capture its splendor.

One day, I spoke of this to Salt Commissioner Tan Xingtang, who inscribed the characters "Raining Across the Land" on the grotto’s entrance. I ordered local officials to erect a memorial arch, and slightly to its north, a single-bay pavilion. Further north, a three-bay pavilion-hall was built, its site the most wondrous of all. Backed by a stone wall like a screen, flanked by cliffs like wings, it opened grandly on one side. Leaning on the railing, one gazes down: the Great and Lesser Dragon Valleys resemble a coiled dragon spewing water, while pilgrims at the mountain’s base swarm like ants. Nearby, Mount Lizu stands to the left, Mount Aolai to the right; in the distance, Mount Yunyun, Mount Tingting, Mount Niqiu, and Mount Liangfu encircle the scene like stars, each offering its own marvels. Behold—a spectacle of the world! Having lingered in this grotto and rested in this pavilion, who would not aspire to bring rain across the land? How could this be merely a scenic retreat?

Local officials urged me to record this. The project was first proposed by former Provincial Inspector Han Xixuan and realized by current Provincial Inspector Mao Wenyuan, with other officials and staff listed on the stele’s reverse.

Written on an auspicious day in the mid-winter of the *Bingxu* year of the *Wanli* reign by Li Zai, Imperial Commissioner, Provincial Governor of Shandong, Military Superintendent, Director of Farmlands, and River Conservator, Right Vice Censor-in-Chief of the Censorate.

*Mount Tai Records*, Volume Thirteen: *Incense Tax*
A question arises: Why speak of the *incense tax*? From all directions, men and women come to pray and seek blessings, bearing incense to worship the mountain’s spirits. They make offerings, and officials record the tax to aid the state. Among all the incense taxes in the land, only Mount Tai and Mount Wudang in Huguang are comparable, yet Wudang is not a sacred peak—thus, Mount Tai alone bears this tax. As pilgrims press shoulder to shoulder, journeying from afar, climbing perilous paths in a ceaseless stream, their contributions do more than aid the state. Porters earn their livelihood, merchants profit, monks sustain their alms bowls, and even beggars survive for a time. The benefits to the region are vast indeed.

Ultimately, when pilgrims exhaust all offerings—gold, silk, pearls, jade, horses, and fine fabrics—to present to the deities, the officials daily oversee and tax these contributions, tallying the totals twice yearly. All this arises from the divine spirits’ power of summoning; how could it depend on forced levies or official demands? They gather yet do not hoard, accumulate yet can disperse—without the aid of spectral transport, the world of men naturally overflows with abundance. How wondrously does the work of creation reach such perfection!

Shandong, as the left auxiliary to the imperial capital, bears the heaviest burdens in all taxes and corvée, yet in recent years of famine, its resources have grown ever more strained. Without this revenue, how could the region meet its obligations to the throne? It must be that the deities’ protection of the state subtly aids the urgent needs of the six prefectures’ people. Initially, the taxes were recorded by the provincial administration to support local expenses, supplementing what land taxes could not cover. Later, they were redirected into the imperial treasury, with fixed annual quotas. Some argue that today, the imperial treasury holds vast sums, impossible to audit—why not allocate these funds to relieve famine in the six prefectures, extending the deities’ blessings to the lands sustained by incense and worship? Would not the spirits approve? Those who seek to alleviate the people’s suffering should consider this.

The regulations, in all their particulars, are listed as follows:

**Appointment of Officials and Quotas**
By long-standing precedent, one *Chief Inspector* is appointed from among the prefectural vice-officials to supervise the incense tax, responsible for oversight and audits. Six *Sub-Inspectors* are appointed from among the vice-officials of prefectures and counties:
- Two stationed at the *Yaocan Pavilion*, one collecting taxes from local pilgrims, the other from out-of-province pilgrims, both issuing receipts to the pilgrims;
- One at the *Xuanwu Gate*, collecting taxes from pilgrims arriving via the mountain’s rear paths, also issuing receipts;
- One each at the *Red Gate* and *Southern Heavenly Gate*, verifying receipts before allowing passage;
- One at the gate of *Bixia Temple* atop the peak, monitoring the entry and exit of pilgrims.

These posts have existed since antiquity, their origins unknown. However, in the fifth year of the Longqing reign (1571), it was decreed that one official at the *Yaocan Pavilion* and the two receipt-verifying officials at the *Red Gate* and *Southern Heavenly Gate* be abolished, leaving only three *Sub-Inspectors*—one at the *Yaocan Pavilion*, one at the *Xuanwu Gate*, and one at *Bixia Temple*. During peak pilgrimage seasons, when the *Sub-Inspectors* are overwhelmed, some argue that the earlier cuts were excessive and that the quota should be moderately increased.

**Appointment Terms**
By recent practice, the year is divided into three seasons:
- The *upper season* (first to fourth months) and *lower season* (ninth to twelfth months), when pilgrims are most numerous, see the full complement of *Chief Inspectors* and *Sub-Inspectors* deployed.
- The *middle season* (fifth to eighth months), when pilgrims are fewer, only *Sub-Inspectors* are appointed, with no *Chief Inspector*.

Each season concludes with new appointments, so the *Chief Inspector*’s tenure is irregular, and even *Sub-Inspectors* may fail to assume their posts. Given the gravity of the funds involved, strict oversight is essential. Moreover, with countless pilgrims traversing treacherous cliffs and ravines, how can officials not patrol and provide relief? Some propose that the *Chief Inspector* should be permanently stationed to ensure continuity.

**Incense Tax Regulations**
By old precedent, local pilgrims paid 5.4 *fen* per person, while out-of-province pilgrims paid 9.4 *fen*, with innkeepers subject to the same rates. Pilgrims reported to the *Yaocan Pavilion*, paid the tax, and received a receipt to ascend the mountain.

From the eighth year of the Wanli reign (1580), out-of-province pilgrims began falsely claiming local status to pay lower taxes. In response, the rates were unified: all pilgrims now pay 8 *fen*. Innkeepers package the silver, label the bundles, and store them in the *Yaocan Pavilion* treasury. During the summer and winter tax deliveries, innkeepers reclaim their bundles by name and melt the silver. However, some innkeepers substitute copper or iron for silver when paying taxes, then struggle to cover the shortfall when the silver is assayed. Some flee, while others take their own lives. These cases, witnessed firsthand, call for reform: officials should open and verify each bundle upon collection, and innkeepers should receive a small allowance for melting costs to ease their burden.

**Miscellaneous Offerings Regulations**
All pilgrim offerings—gold, silver, pearls, jade ornaments, jewelry, gold and silver figurines, copper coins, quilts, canopies, robes, gauze, silk, and fabric—are deposited at *Bixia Temple*. Each summer and winter, a prefectural vice-official is dispatched to accompany the original *Chief Inspector* up the mountain to open the temple gates. They collect, inspect, and appraise all offerings. Gold, silver, pearls, jade ornaments, jewelry, figurines, and coins are combined with the incense tax silver and delivered to the Provincial Administration Commission’s treasury for future disbursement. Quilts, canopies, robes, gauze, silk, and fabric are sent to the Jinan Prefecture treasury to supplement the salaries of provincial, prefectural, and transport officials.

**Accounting Regulations**
The annual incense tax and miscellaneous offerings vary in amount. The totals are allocated and reported to the Ministry of Revenue in a single annual submission. According to records from the thirty-seventh year of the Jiajing reign (1558), regarding imperial edicts: *Mount Tai’s temple incense funds, excluding quilts, canopies, and robes (which continue to supplement officials’ salaries), shall have all gold, silver, and jewelry delivered to the ministry in biannual shipments.* In recent years, the spring delivery has amounted to over 10,000 *taels*, while the winter delivery has reached 12,000 to 13,000 *taels*.

One portion is retained by the provincial administration to cover expenses for official hall celebrations, including memorials and congratulatory documents, sedan-chair bearers, cartage fees, public couriers, staff wages, the six offices’ records, paper, writing materials, scribes’ stipends, and the grain stipends for the various princes of the De, Lu, and Heng princely houses.

Another portion is allocated for city-wall repairs, with the silver likewise stored in the Provincial Administration Commission to fund the maintenance of city walls.

Another portion is designated for temple repairs, also held by the Provincial Administration Commission to cover the upkeep of temples on Mount Tai and other sacred peaks; five *li* out of every eight *fen* of the incense tax are reserved for this purpose.

Another portion constitutes public funds, used to provide stipends for officials commissioned to oversee the incense tax and the wages of their attendants, with the silver disbursed directly from Tai’an Prefecture.

Another portion consists of copper cash, which by old precedent was delivered to the Ministry of Rites. In recent years, however, the ministry has ceased to collect it, and the cash now accumulates in the provincial treasury—truly, as the saying goes, “coins pile up in the millions while their strings rot.”

*Records of Mount Tai*, Scroll Fourteen: *Disasters and Auspicious Omens*
The Master said: Why record disasters and auspicious omens?

The Master said: It is because Mount Tai’s qi-transformations take precedence, and its disasters and auspicious omens bear an especially weighty relation to the realm and the state. In former times, the omens appearing on Mount Tai—such as the great stone that stood upright when Emperor Xuan of Han ascended the throne, or the auspicious clouds that formed palace gates when Emperor Guangwu revived the Han—were these not the most illustrious of blessings? And the startled crows whose nests burned when the Flying Swallow brought ruin to the Han, or the pheasant that intruded upon the imperial procession when Empress Wu overthrew the Tang—were these not the most conspicuous of calamities?

Yet if we speak of the true nature of disasters and blessings, what could surpass the five stalks of auspicious wheat depicted in the *Rui Mai Tu* during the Song dynasty’s Jiayou era, or the single stalk bearing five ears of wheat that appeared on Mount Tai in our dynasty’s Jiajing reign? Such omens sustain life. But when the imperial tent was swept away by floods, drowning over three hundred households, or when the gates of the Bixia Temple opened and closed, crushing sixty or seventy people to death in an instant, the calamity struck at life itself—far beyond mere portents or omens.

Are not the warnings of disasters and blessings manifestly clear? Blessings either confirm virtue or incite extravagance; disasters either admonish transgressions or refine through trial. Does Heaven’s intent ever waver? Only by treading the path of blessings with redoubled effort can one enjoy enduring felicity; only by heeding calamities and cultivating virtue can one ensure that evil does not prevail. This principle is not merely for sagely rulers and worthy ministers to ponder—every official entrusted with even the smallest office bears the duty to influence the transformations of qi. Let none dismiss disasters and blessings as mere trifles unworthy of reflection.

In the third year of the Yuanfeng era of Emperor Zhao of Han, during the first month of spring, a great stone on Mount Tai stood upright, fifteen feet high, with thousands of white crows gathering around it. A withered willow revived, and worms ate its leaves, forming the characters: “Gongsun falls ill; the tally is already set.” The magician Xi Hong interpreted the stone’s yin aspect as resembling the common people, foretelling that a commoner would become Son of Heaven. For this “seditious speech,” he was executed. Later, Emperor Xuan arose from among the people.

In the first year of the Heping era of Emperor Cheng of Han, a flock of startled crows burned their nest in the mulberry and catalpa trees of Mount Tai. The prefectural man Sun Tong and others heard the clamor of crows and magpies on the mountain. Upon investigation, they found the nest entirely fallen, with three fledglings burned to death inside. The tree measured four spans in girth, and the nest stood fifty-five feet above the ground. The *Book of Changes* states: “When the crow burns its nest, the traveler first barks, then wails in lament.” Jing Fang’s *Commentary on the Changes* says: “When the ruler is tyrannical, the crow burns its nest.” Later, the Zhao sisters, Flying Swallow and her kin, monopolized the emperor’s favor in the rear palace, killing any child born to him along with its mother. After the emperor’s death, they were executed—fulfilling the omen of the nest’s burning, the slaughter of offspring, and the subsequent wailing. Some say this was the consequence of Wang Mang’s greed and cruelty, which led him to usurp the throne, ultimately bringing about the dynastic catastrophe.

In the first year of the Jianwu era of Emperor Guangwu, clouds over Mount Tai took the shape of palace gates. In the second year of the Yuanhe era of Emperor Zhang, on the day *xinhai* of the second month, as the emperor visited Mount Tai, thirty yellow cranes flew over the ritual altar toward the northeast, circling above the fasting palace before soaring and descending. In the fourth year of the Taishi era of Emperor Wu of Jin, in the seventh month, a section of Mount Tai three *li* in length collapsed. Jing Fang’s *Commentary on the Changes* states: “That which descends from above is called a collapse. When the stones of Mount Tai topple from their summit, it portends that a sage will receive the Mandate, and the ruler will be taken captive.” Later, the last two emperors of Jin, Huai and Min, were both humiliated in captivity in the north, while Emperor Yuan revived the dynasty in the south—this was the fulfillment of the omen.

In the second year of the Taikang era of Emperor Wu of Jin, in the sixth month, a great flood on Mount Tai swept away over three hundred households and drowned more than six thousand people. At the time, the realm was at peace, yet Wang Jun, a meritorious official, was slandered and executed without cause; Xun Xu and Jia Chong, men of no strategic insight, were both lavishly rewarded. The emperor took five thousand women from Wu and Yue into his rear palace—this was the fulfillment of the omen.

In the eighth year of the Jianwu era of Shi Hu of Zhao, in the eighth year of the Xiankang era of Emperor Cheng of Jin, a stone on Mount Tai caught fire and burned for eight days before extinguishing. In the first year of the Daming era of Emperor Xiaowu of Song, in the seventh month, a white sparrow appeared on Mount Tai. In the third year of the Jingming era of Emperor Xuanwu of Eastern Wei, on the day *xinsi* of the eighth month, Mount Tai collapsed, and seventeen springs gushed forth. In the sixth year of the Wuding era, in the fourth month, sweet dew descended upon Mount Tai. In the early years of the Tianlong era of the Later Qi, a jade disk emerged from the Fengshan altar on Mount Tai.

Mount Tai is where rulers of new dynasties announce the transfer of the Mandate, and the jade disk is the ritual currency used in such ceremonies. Its spontaneous appearance foretold the rise of a ruler from a different lineage. Later, the Qi dynasty fell, and its lands passed to the Zhou. When Emperor Gaozu of Sui received the abdication of the Zhou and unified the realm, he burned offerings on Mount Tai and performed the Feng and Shan sacrifices—this was the fulfillment of the omen.

In the fourteenth year of the Kaihuang era of Emperor Wen of Sui, as he prepared to sacrifice on Mount Tai, he sent envoys to present an image of the deity at the shrine. Several *li* from the destination, wildfire suddenly arose and burned the image. At the time, the emperor, swayed by slander, grew suspicious and jealous. Prince Teng Zhan died of despair, and many meritorious officials were exterminated. Later, the crown prince, Yang Yong, was executed.

In the second year of the Yonghui era of Emperor Gaozong of Tang, in the seventh month, Mount Tai experienced a great flood. In the thirteenth year, on the day *wuz*i of the tenth month, a male pheasant flew tamely into the fasting palace on Mount Tai. The Feng and Shan sacrifices are the most solemn rites for announcing the completion of great deeds—yet a wild bird flew unchecked into the sacred precincts, a most inauspicious sign for the imperial guard.

In the first year of the Dazhong Xiangfu era of Emperor Zhenzong of Song, in the fifth month, sweet springs emerged on Mount Tai. On the day *dingchou*, the water of the Queen Mother’s Pool turned a reddish-purple hue. According to the records, in Qianfeng County, a peasant named Wang Yong dug in his field and found a boy playing with dozens of bronze coins. As the villagers scrambled to take them, the coins fell onto a stone altar, and when the stone was lifted, twenty-four springs gushed forth, their water exceedingly sweet. Another twenty-five springs surged from the dry riverbed of the Stone River, one of them rising atop a hillock. By the next day, the flow had grown so abundant that it branched into several streams, with pairs of fish leaping in the water. Fruits resembling small plums floated out, their taste remarkably sweet. The pacification commissioner, Wang Qinruo, collected the water and presented it to the emperor. On the day *gengxu* of the sixth month, the emperor bestowed it upon the hundred officials, ordered the construction of a pavilion, and bestowed upon it the name “Divine Liquid.”

In the sixth month, Wang Qinruo arrived at Qianfeng and memorialized that the Divine Liquid had sprung forth on Mount Tai, celestial writings had appeared, and there were thirty-eight thousand two hundred and fifty stalks of lingzhi. Now, the emergence of sweet springs and magic mushrooms is indeed a matter of natural principle. But as for the descent of celestial writings—whom would I deceive, if not Heaven?

In the twelfth month, the Jade Maiden of Mount Tai, the White Dragon, and the Divine Liquid of the Queen Mother’s Pool all manifested. On the day *dingyou*, the emperor bestowed the newly emerged Divine Liquid upon his ministers.

In the tenth month of the sixth year, construction began on Mount Tai. A bird resembling a crow, with a red beak and talons, would fly about whenever laborers rested, crying out to rouse them. When the workers saw it approach, they hastened with their baskets and shovels, and the bird would depart only after they resumed work. This became a daily occurrence, and the bird was called the "Labor-Spurring Crow."

In the seventh month of the third year of Emperor Renzong’s *Jiayou* reign, auspicious wheat from Mount Tai was depicted in five illustrations.

In the ninth month of the ninth year of Emperor Shizu’s *Zhiyuan* reign, Mount Tai was drenched by incessant rain, and the river overflowed, damaging fields, homes, and crops.

In the sixth month of the first year of Emperor Chengzong’s *Yuanzhen* reign, Mount Tai experienced a great flood.

In the ninth month of the first year of Emperor Wuzong’s *Zhida* reign, Mount Tai suffered another great flood.

In the third month of the first year of Emperor Renzong’s *Yanyou* reign, Mount Tai was blanketed in frost and snow for three days.

In the first year of Emperor Wenzong’s *Tianli* reign, Mount Tai endured a great flood.

In the first year of Emperor Shundi’s *Yuantong* reign, Mount Tai was deluged by relentless rain, the river overflowed, and a severe famine ensued.

In the second month of the sixth year of *Zhizheng*, Fengfu County on Mount Tai suffered a great famine, and the earth quaked for seven days; neighboring counties experienced the same.

In the second and third months of the twenty-first year of the *Chenghua* reign of the Great Ming, Mount Tai trembled repeatedly, and officials were dispatched to offer sacrifices.

In the third month of the sixteenth year of the *Zhengde* reign, a fire broke out in the eastern corridor of the Dai Temple.

In the eighth year of the *Jiajing* reign, locusts infested Mount Tai, and the same occurred in the ninth and tenth years.

In the sixth month of the sixteenth year, floodwaters on Mount Tai drowned hundreds.

In the eleventh month, a fire ravaged the prefectural city, burning dozens of households.

In the twenty-first year, locusts appeared on Mount Tai but caused no disaster.

In the twenty-second year, Mount Tai enjoyed a double harvest in summer, with wheat and millet bearing three to five ears per stalk. Magistrate Ma Fengbo presented these to the court.

In the twelfth month of the twenty-ninth year, a fire consumed the Dai Temple, burning the Heavenly Hall, its gates, and corridors. Many ancient trees and inscribed steles were also destroyed.

In the sixth month of the thirtieth year, a great flood struck Mount Tai, sweeping away the imperial tent and drowning many.

In the thirty-second year, Mount Tai suffered a severe famine, with people robbing one another and travelers unable to pass.

In the spring of the thirty-third year, Mount Tai endured another great famine. The starving lay strewn like grass, and the poor resorted to eating their flesh.

On the eighteenth day of the tenth month of the fourteenth year of the *Wanli* reign, at the Bixia Palace on Mount Tai, sixty-one pilgrims from all directions were trampled to death in a crush while burning incense. The provincial governor and censor-in-chief, Li, dispatched Sang Dongyang, the judicial commissioner of Jinan Prefecture, to oversee the recovery and burial of the dead.

*Dai History, Volume Fifteen: Records of Ascents and Sightseeing*

A Preface:

Why record the accounts of ascents and sightings? To preserve the writings of those who have ascended and beheld through the ages—be they odes, poems, prefaces, or records. I have heard Qiu Huzi say that pursuing external excursions is inferior to cultivating inward contemplation, and so Liezi claimed he knew not how to wander. Yet Su Shi of Meishan remarked that those bound by worldly affairs often roam much, while those transcending the mundane seldom do. Judging by these words, how can wandering be taken lightly?

How magnificent, then, is the wandering of Mount Tai! Ascending the lofty peaks of Sunrise View and Moon View, one may look down upon and rise above the cosmos. Searching for the wondrous stones of Dragon Vein and Tiger Mound, one may sharpen one’s noble aspirations. Facing the cascading springs of Heavenly Girdle and Sacred Waters, one may cleanse and invigorate the spirit. Exploring the secluded grottoes of Master Lü and Lou Jing, one may inhale the mists and clouds. Who is to say that external wandering does not aid inward contemplation?

The resolve to draw the bow and aim at the four directions, the aspirations of lofty scholars and enlightened minds—these remain unchanged from ancient times to the present. Among those who wandered and could give voice to their experiences, their words spread across scrolls and chapters, as if the myriad forms of peaks and valleys, so vivid and majestic, were rendered in cinnabar and ink. Moreover, their inspirations soar to distant heights, drifting beyond mere questions of mountains and waters. Did they merely seek to delight the eyes and ears, to indulge in literary flourishes, or to amass curios for pleasure? Surely, even those who advocate inward contemplation do not dismiss such pursuits.

When the Sage of Qiu composed his ode upon the hills, it became a song unmatched for a thousand years. Thus, it is placed at the forefront of these records to eternally illuminate the spirit of the mountain. From the Han and Wei dynasties to the present, all are arranged in chronological order.

**Poems and Odes**
*Zhou Dynasty*

**Confucius’ Ode on the Qiu Hills**

Ascending those hills,
I smooth their slopes.
The Way of benevolence lies near,
Yet sought as if afar.
Thus lost, never to return,
Ensnared in hardship’s coils.
With a sigh, I turn my gaze—
Behold Mount Tai!
Its towering peaks, rugged and grand,
Linked with Liangfu’s ridges.
Briars choke the path,
Gummed fast, no green remains.
To hew them without an axe—
The trouble spreads unchecked.
I can but sigh forever,
Tears streaming down.

*Dragging my staff, I chant at the gate:*
“Will Mount Tai crumble?
Will the ridge-beam collapse?
Will the wise man wither?”
*(See* Liji*:* Tan Gong*.)*

Zigong heard this and said:
“If Mount Tai crumbles, where shall I turn my gaze?
