The frozen Northern Dipper threatens to sink into Yan by night.
Pine shadows still drip with rains of Qin’s time,
Jade buds remain sealed in the years of Han’s emperors.

Best to borrow the west wind as wings,
Adorning the Five Peaks like a staff bearing the Milky Way.
The Giant Spirit’s ancient might stands towering,
Ten thousand divine lamps flicker beyond the misty vines.

The autumn gloom of the wilds stretches to the Bottle Gourd Ford,
The crisp air of hollow cliffs descends upon the Yellow River.
At midnight, the sun cradles the Gate of Heaven in its rise,
A hundred cascades fly down to the lower realms in abundance.

The seventy-two sovereigns are nowhere to be found,
Only their banners and canopies drift dimly through the mist.
Mount Tai coils vast to the east of the great sea,
Its pearl-like glow stirs the heavens with crimson.

Clouds and mist ever guard the Azure Clouds’ summit,
Dragons and tigers slumber near the Green Emperor’s palace.
Around my feet, stars and constellations gleam vividly,
The fragrance clinging to my robes—how thick and sweet!

Are immortals present or absent beyond the Weak River?
Death dissolves into the mists of the Western Pond.

**Li Xuedao, Prefect of Eastern Zhejiang, Paying Homage at the Dark Palace**
Above, the scenery of the Gourd Heaven is most splendid,
Cliffs bear lingzhi, stones bloom with flowers.
Clouds part to reveal the Jade Palace, gazing toward the Green Emperor,
Incense swirls around the Pearl Palace, embracing the Azure Clouds.

At my feet, the world’s dust is but an illusion,
Within the realm, sun and moon belong to the immortals.
I come to knock upon the mysteries of the Dao,
Silent before the vast sky, my thoughts drift far.

**Li Ren, Magistrate and Censor, Renowned for Qi Cultivation**
During his tenure as Prefect of Qingzhou, he paid homage to the south. On the appointed day, he dreamed of Mount Tai’s deity sending an envoy to welcome him, bestowing an official title. Later, he indeed died in Qingzhou. Strange indeed!

**Feng Dashou, Provincial Graduate of Songjiang, Two Poems on Ascending Mount Tai**
The Water Emperor’s Azure Yang Gate,
The Immortal’s White Jade Altar.

Amidst the vast expanse I wander, gazing down upon the racing sun and moon,
Misty haze obscures the Qin’s distant view, layered mists bring daytime chill.
Fellow travelers, like Xie Lingyun, feast their eyes on the lush hues,
Only upon the central peak’s summit does one grasp Mount Tai’s majesty.

Ridges and peaks lock the earth’s axis, stars and constellations hang at Heaven’s Gate,
The solitary stele of Qin’s First Emperor, the deep pool of the Jade Maiden.
Immortals traverse beyond the clouds, white cranes dance in graceful flight.

**Magistrate Feng Zhong of Taishan’s Summit Peak**
At dawn’s faint glow, the Altar of the Emperor’s Daughter,
A mighty wind chills the traveler’s coarse robe.
Suddenly, twin luminaries appear beneath the five-colored clouds,
A thousand li shared with a single horse in view.
White stones and azure fungi gathered from the earth,
High mountains and flowing waters—yet for whom to play?
Within the sacred precincts, purple qi ever comes and goes,
The merchant’s son first ascends, beholding the grand spectacle.

**The Scripture-Exposing Terrace**
The path winds, peaks recede—another realm unfolds,
A level stone mirror lies before the setting glow.
Hibiscus thrusts through emerald cliffs, ivy drapes the hanging waterfall.
Clouds brush the seat, revealing tadpole script,
Phoenixes, weary of the flute’s wind, drift by.
Suddenly, I long to leave the mortal world behind,
Drunk on peach blossoms, forgetting the years.

**Vice Commissioner Liu Zongdai of Licheng, Ascending Mount Tai**
Stone steps cleave the heavens, climbing by night to the Azure Clouds Palace.
Cliffside steles and grotto abodes mark the numinous,
Rain veins and cloud roots trace the work of creation.
June’s springs fly with misty vapors,
At midnight, the sun bathes in the crimson of Shao Hao.
Pity the Qin and Han’s ancient seals remain,
Yet jade tablets and golden mud endure unending.

**Pan Ziyu of Licheng, Ascending Mount Tai with Minister Tao Xinzhai**
Mount Tai, renowned for its splendor,
Draws travelers to climb through clouds and mist.
Jade tablets and golden seals pass through the ages,
Qin steles and Han inscriptions gleam in the endless blue.
Shaded cliffs hold snow through a thousand autumns,
Clear days bring thunderous winds through ten thousand ravines.
Inspired, we do not shun the summit’s heights,
Where heaven and earth lie empty before our eyes.

**Censor-in-Chief Liu Yu of Dong’e, Ascending Mount Tai’s Summit**
At Sunrise Peak, mist and fog swirl,
A biting wind stirs my robes.
Above, I grasp the stars—bright and near,
To the east, Penglai seems yet not to be.
Who sealed the golden cask in this ancient stone?
The clustered mountains, verdant, shrink below.
How I long for wings to ride the wind,
To soar from here to the Four Sacred Peaks and return.

**Provincial Graduate Zhao Bangyan of Dong’e, Ascending Mount Tai**
From Mount Tai’s summit, crows scatter below,
Phoenix banners and jade halls honor the Primordial Lady.
Layered cliffs and tiered crags dim the autumn sun,
Han stones and Tang steles exhale evening incense.
Misty rain descends, veiling a thousand homes,
Terraces and galleries stand serene amid ninefold skies.
When the wind stirs the Five Pines, cold waves surge,
The mournful strains of Qin’s pipes—unbearable to hear.

Winding crags hide the raven’s path, golden groves and jade tables shroud the divine light,
A single stone’s divine power births clouds and rain,
The primal qi of myriad peaks merges in boundless vastness.
Nine dots of Yu’s domain blend into one hue,
A thousand years of profound blessings flow to all lands.
Anqi’s alchemical cave holds leftover cinnabar,
Smoke trees stand verdant in the fading light.

Golden mud and jade tablets guard sacred writ,
This mighty peak, a divine gate, secures Lu’s borders.
Threefold peaks pierce the heavens, towering,
Six-dragon chariot paths stand amid verdant trees.
Collapsed cliffs hold snow a thousand years white,
Shaded grottoes bear peach blossoms fragrant in April.
At dusk, cups fly atop the summit,
Penglai’s five colors gleam over the eastern sea.

Mountain peaks swirl in purple and emerald,
Penglai and Yingzhou lie eastward, shrouded in azure mist.
The Radiant Palace shines upon a thousand cliffs,
The Phoenix Pool cradles five-colored clouds.
Qin and Yue’s rivers and mountains fill the cup’s view,
Heaven and earth’s spirit and beauty lie at the staff’s side.
The Jade Lad remains unseen, lost in the mist,
Leaning on the pine pavilion, I gaze at the purple haze.

**Secretary Zhu Weijing of Wan’an, Ascending Mount Tai**
At Sunrise Peak, I knock upon the Emperor’s Gate,
Upper realms’ towers and pavilions stand apart from the mortal world.
For a moment, I leave my brush behind in the Western Clear Palace,
Ascending to inscribe my name beneath the Northern Dipper.
Twelve Qi states lie within a glance,
Three thousand heavenly realms all within reach.
Since ancient times, lofty mountains inspire shared reverence,
I do not lament the arduous climb to Liangfu.

Towering, it truly touches the Great Clarity,
Following the immortal’s song, I ascend to Yingzhou.
Through a hundred thousand kalpas, its majesty endures,
Seventy-two rulers’ names—mere fleeting fame.
Mountain mists become clouds, obscuring Chu’s distant view,
Pine waves carry rain, sounding Qin’s lament.
Upon returning, do not marvel at the lack of new grass,
I do not seek the Qianfeng era’s extravagant praise.

**The Feat of Feng and Shan—What Was It Like in Those Days?**
Qin’s altars and Han’s time stand steeped in grandeur.
Where two travelers sought immortals,
A thousand officials once followed the imperial progress.
Sunlight gilds the white horses, the sky stretches like a belt over the Yellow River.
Shaking my robes, I tread straight to Heaven’s Gate,
Plucking stars at night, singing a boundless song.

Rocks glow, bursting toward the distant earth,
From ten thousand li, Fusang’s dawn mist arrives.
Elusive immortals seem to rise from the sea,
The Threefold Peaks stand staggered, leaning on the clouds.
Hua and Song stand side by side, hard to surpass,
The Milky Way’s midst reveals a single cup.
Jade tablets and golden statutes—mere illusions,
When the Han emperor questioned Penglai.

**Yu Shensi, Student of Dong’e, Ode to Gazing at the Sacred Peak**
For ten days, I traversed the winding slopes,
Yet all mountains seemed dull and unremarkable.
One morning, gazing at the towering heights,
Suddenly, my eyes were opened wide.
Flickering, faint—perhaps glimpsed,
Everlasting, the blue haze shines through the ages.
Divine beauty uniquely gathers in creation’s wonder,
Far-reaching, it greets Penglai and Yingzhou of Qi and Lu.

Before mulberry fields became seas,
Rites and music endured.
Not even the myriad peaks of Guifeng compare,
For here, a single peak thrusts into the heavens.
Before the seventy-two rulers’ seals,
How many years did heaven and earth guard its secrets?
Qin’s First Emperor’s jade tablets—vain effort,
The Han emperor’s golden mud—equally futile.

Yet the Han’s seals still bear traces,
Broken steles leave Qin’s jade brush behind.
The Three Luminaries have not aged, preserving Zong’s height,
The primal qi, boundless, remains ungathered.
Fortunate to tread here at dawn’s joy,
Above, pines lean and vines entwine,
Deer startle, gibbons leap.
Anqi and Xianmen—how I long to glimpse them,
I would ride a whale or leopard, hire Wang Qiao’s services.

At Sunrise Peak, lesser mountains lie below,
At midnight, the crimson sun casts its bright glow.

Do not say the sun and moon conceal their radiance,
Yet see how yin and yang cleave dawn from dusk.
No need to rush and pray at famed peaks—
One glance at Cloud Rock shatters all pride.

There will come a time to lean on a staff and pierce the clouds,
To soar on the wind and watch the waterfall, joy unbounded.
Mount Tai’s allure has drawn me through life’s vast sea,
Yet in dreams, I wander Song and Hua instead.

Piled moss and heaped stones fade from sight,
A decade of hardship still binds me to the eastern land.
The dusty world’s turmoil cannot hold me,
Often I invert the scene and rise above clear autumn.

They say Mount Tai is a native treasure,
Its primal transformation dissolving all cares.
From this lofty height, I gaze upon the distant sea,
Where sea winds arrive in rushing gusts.

The Heavenly Gate stands too high to reach,
Only jagged peaks and sheer cliffs meet the eye.
A thousand forms, ten thousand shapes—
I strain to take them all in, never resting.

Ascend the Moon Palace, open the Cloud Tower,
Below, clouds and mist churn like oysters.
At Horse Peak’s summit, the White Silk is clear,
Yet leaning sideways, the gibbons and monkeys still wail in sorrow.

Five Sacred Peaks on earth, Three Terraces in heaven—
What grandeur the Creator has wrought!
If a scholar reaches this place once,
Even *Wandering Boundless* cannot match its wonder.

Have you not seen?
Mount Hua’s collapse shames Heng and Huo,
Only the eastern peaks of sea and Tai endure through the night.
The eastern people gaze upon the mountain as upon a mother,
Each inch of cloud rising to answer their prayers.

A scholar, mired in dust, longs for pure waters,
Yet worldly ties remain—what can be done?
The hermit of Mount Tai’s noble wind endures,
So let us turn to the eastern peak, veiled in moss and vine.

*Inscribed after ascending Mount Tai in the footsteps of Emperor Yao*
Two days of climbing, lost in sublime delight,
Reaching the summit, my spirit soars wilder still.
The stars of the celestial sphere wheel high and low,
While crimson clouds below glow near and far.

On Jieshi Peak, the bright moon lingers,
From Penglai’s sea, the rising sun appears.
Alas, I, like a mayfly, remain frail,
Gazing toward Heng and Lu, I think of home.

**Huang Yigui, Vice Prefect of Fujian, ascends the mountain**
Driven by official duties, I follow the path of fame,
Traversing perilous cliffs where marvels gather.
Heaven opens palaces in the void,
While mortals glimpse Penglai beyond the world.

Dragon channels spew foam a thousand yards high,
Bird paths coil through ten thousand layers of mist.
Immortal youths, now feathered, ride cranes away,
White clouds and flowing waters seek forgotten traces.

**Guo Tian, Revenue Officer of Huguan, on the incense tax of Mount Tai**
Last year, I ascended Penglai Pavilion,
Today, I stand again on the Fengshan Altar.
The vast sea now offers tribute anew,
While Mount Tai hears the Son of Heaven’s six imperial flights arrive.

From all directions, fragrant offerings honor the immortal,
A dragon’s decree reveals the sage’s heart.
Ashamed, this humble servant oversees taxes,
Leaning on the clouds, I gaze vainly toward the Three Terraces.

**Yuan Qiang, Prefect of Huaiyuan, composes upon ascending Mount Tai**
From afar, the Heavenly Gate crowns Phoenix Summit,
A crimson ladder of ten thousand yards links the purple haze.
The east eternally preserves the Jade Maiden’s grace,
While the Fengshan records recall the years of Qin and Han.

Dawn’s light unveils the dark sea’s nearness,
A staff rests on the wind-carved cliff where sun and moon hang.
Further up the layered crags, I seek the sage’s aid—
To ascend with the immortals, I must surpass the world.

**Yang Wenzhang, Vice Prefect of Gu’an, ascends Mount Tai**
Who says the world is vast?
All is gathered within this fourfold gaze.
Peaks and ridges all bow to the north,
While sun and moon peer early from the east.

No earthly heights compare,
Here in mid-heaven, this is the mightiest.
Confucius alone walked this path,
His lofty wind revered through the ages.

**Gu Wenyao of Eastern Wu ascends Mount Tai**
Mount Tai’s towering heights press close to the jade capital,
From ten thousand miles, wind and clouds rise beneath my staff.
From the Han to this day, imperial aura lingers,
The pines of Qin still shrouded in spring’s warmth.

Visitors to heaven crane their necks toward the distant Heavenly Gate,
Their hearts, like the sun, hang upon Sunrise Peak.
In my palm, rivers and mountains are but grains,
Yet the wind-carved cliff still praises the peace of Tai’s steps.

**Su Meishan of Fujian at the Jade Emperor’s Summit**
The Jade Emperor’s lofty pavilion embraces the cinnabar mound,
Crane cliffs soar, gazing down upon a hundred regions.
As if nearing the Purple Tenuity’s stars,
One knows oneself to stand at the head of the five-colored clouds.

**Sunrise Peak**
Dawn’s light opens the peak before the fifth watch,
Fusang’s fire wheel rolls forth.
Crimson rays shoot beyond the azure sea,
In an instant, civilization spreads across the nine realms.

**Elder Peak**
Solemn and towering, this is the noblest,
All other peaks stand like children in pairs.
Through how many ages has the world turned?
Only heaven and earth preserve this ancient one.

**Peach Blossom Grotto**
Snowy cliffs and cloudy peaks, the jade grotto renewed,
Peach blossoms redder than those of Wuling’s spring.
Immortal rafts drift straight into Penglai’s bounds—
Why should a fisherman ask the way again?

**Ruan Zihua, Provincial Graduate of Anqing, ascends Mount Tai**
Morning returns before the Green Emperor’s aged mountain,
Scattered stars embroider the brocade eaves.
The Fengshan’s hues float like Sima’s ode,
Sea’s glow crimson as the First Emperor’s ships.

Pine pavilions and rock huts, divine as paintings,
Stone gorges and Heavenly Gates, lovely beyond compare.
In vain, I sought the eighth month’s raft—
Already my robe and sash enter profound mystery.

**Zhang Xin ascends Mount Tai**
A cinnabar ladder, misty, climbs to heaven’s gate,
The earth’s mountains and rivers lie within my grasp.
I long to brush the stars and touch the Emperor’s throne,
Yet fear the clouds and mist will lose the mortal world.

Who will ever clarify the golden mud and jade seat?
White stones and clear springs remain fragrant and serene.
Where is the bolt of silk from Wu’s gates?
Riding the wind, I would brush my robes and return.

**Chen Shen lodges at the Mount Tai official residence**
At dusk, I wander past the sacred peak,
Climbing winding steps, thousands upon thousands.
Before reaching the summit to savor pure delight,
Already the lesser peaks fade beneath the evening bell.

On a cold night, I briefly visit the cinnabar turtle,
The purple gate—will it allow the white clouds to seal it?
Leisurely, I borrow the moonlit stone bed,
How could I count this as Hua’s second peak?

**Guang Lu, Provincial Graduate of Licheng, ascends Mount Tai**
Leaning on a staff, I rise above the vast expanse,
Shaking my robes a thousand yards high, I wander alone.
The sea gate swallows and spits out wind and clouds,
The celestial realm encircles the light of sun and moon.

The stability of Lu and the might of Qi have faded,
The Fengshan of Qin and the Shan of Han dissolve into mist.
Yet heaven and earth grant me, the seeker of wonders,
To gather jade herbs and offer them to the sage king.

**Zhou Tianqiu of Wu Prefecture gazes upon the mountain**
Lush and verdant, the air at dusk is fine,
My robes bear the flying hues of kingfisher and jade.
From the four seas, a thousand mountains bow in submission,
While mid-heaven’s three terraces stand in layered splendor.

Where now are the traces of the Archer’s Fengshan?
Only Sima’s talents from those years remain.

Seeking the Five Pines upon the cliff’s face,
Where Qin’s winds and rains once hastened the call.
Ascending the peak—
Six dragons clear the path as nine clouds gather near,
Spiral steps coil upward, chasing the light’s glow.
Mid-slope, I fear the gale may sweep me away,
Below, faintly seen, cranes wheel in distant flight.
Clouds bury the clustered peaks, dwarfing their height,
Heaven splits the river, a single thread stretched long.
Leaning over the crag, I bend toward the setting sun,
How could I know this bolt of silk is my homeland?

I, Yu Weiyu, a scholar of Yuxue from Le’an, ascend Mount Tai—
To learn the Dao is like scaling Mount Tai’s sheer face,
Not a moment’s rest may slacken the climb.
Step by step, the stone stairs rise ever higher,
Passing through Heaven’s Gate into boundless space.
Watching the sun at third watch has its origin,
Dwelling among clouds in ten thousand valleys needs no gate.
Straight to Confucius’ place of ascent I press,
The Dao-eye should know the world’s eight bounds are narrow.

Sun Shi of Moling ascends Mount Tai—
For years, tales spoke of Mount Tai’s clouds,
Like lotus carved sheer, towering above the rest.
Layered ridges curve, hiding vast ravines,
The long river drifts like silk in the clear mist.
Rosy dawns retain the hues of ancient sacrifices,
Bronze and stone still bear inscriptions of bygone ages.
Today, standing here with far-reaching gaze,
I raise a cup of wine, lingering in the slanting glow.

Chen Xuan ascends Mount Tai—
Of heaven and earth’s five peaks, it stands foremost in the east.
Joyous deeds adorn its crimson stairs, its jade-green cliffs soar high.
Vast and hazy, I gaze toward Jieshi,
Standing alone, I compare it to Kongtong.
Below, clouds and mist stretch endlessly,
While above, the sun and moon share the same sky.
The mountain spirit once pitched its tent here,
This perilous land has heard countless wise decrees.
Snow falls before winter’s white,
At Sunrise Peak, dawn’s red has yet to glow.
Wandering through the Azure Emperor’s hall,
My spirit soars to the Azure Cloud Palace.
Its halls align with stars and constellations,
Divine radiance flashes like lightning and rainbow.
Its fame resounds beyond the civilized world,
Its sacred rites solemnly ascend to the heavens.
Response and resonance unite a thousand lands,
A single cry echoes through ten thousand fields.
Tribute flows like state taxes,
Its nurturing power rivals heaven’s work.
Han’s policies shine with Sima Xiangru’s brilliance,
Qin’s stele bears Li Si’s craftsmanship.
Immortal beings may yet appear,
Jade maidens meet daily here.
The primal transformation knows no separation,
Mountains and rivers endure from beginning to end.
My life is frail, my beard sparse,
Yet my wandering heart still burns with vigor.
I lodge here briefly to compose these verses,
Humbly inscribing my deepest sincerity.

Ji Xiang of Zhangzhou visits Peach Blossom Grotto—
Stepping into the path of Peach Blossom Spring,
The traces of Tiantai seem lost in the mist.
Once I came as Liu or Ruan’s companion,
Not as one who asks the way to the ford.

White Cloud Grotto—
Amid jagged rocks and perilous cliffs,
White clouds arise here.
Two or three maidens remain,
Leisurely reciting Yang Xiong’s classics.

Wu Xiang’s *Evening Glow on Mount Tai*—
At dawn’s chill, I follow the mounted retinue,
The slanting sun crosses twin peaks.
My gaze stretches into boundless blue,
Awe fills my heart.
Sun and moon illuminate east and west,
Mist and rosy clouds envelop far and near.
This sacred radiance, this rare sight,
Is seldom met in the mortal world.

Zheng Cai of Ansu ascends Mount Tai—
Mount Tai, loftiest of the five peaks,
Its grandeur spans heaven and earth.
Stars seem near enough to touch the clouds,
Sun and moon appear to float above the Bohai Sea.
The winding stairway coils through a thousand ridges,
Flying springs and waterfalls cascade down ten thousand streams.
With the bold spirit of one who climbs and gazes,
I let out a long whistle, brushing my robes beneath the heavens.

Song Cunde of Moling ascends Mount Tai—
For years, my tracks were lost in the dusty world,
Who would have thought I’d scale this famous peak today?
The winding path clings to green cliffs beyond,
The sunlit peak vanishes into white clouds.
I seem to hear dogs and roosters at heaven’s edge,
Suddenly startled by wings beating the vast sky.
Moreover, this splendid journey is shared with a fine companion,
How can I lack fine verses to record these layered peaks?

Wu Dao of Zhe ascends Mount Tai—
Thirty-six perilous bends—how treacherous the climb,
Yet at dawn’s ascent, weariness is forgotten.
Gazing at the summit, I doubt there’s a path,
Standing amid clouds, I lose all sense of self.
Are Qin’s Five Pines still here?
Tang’s thousand-foot cliff bears a polished stele.
The rise and fall of ages are but common affairs,
Yet golden tablets and jade caskets remain a great wonder.

Lu Jun of Tiantai ascends Mount Tai—
Heaven grants clear skies to speed my journey,
I scale the cliffs straight to the azure clouds.
Worldly dust fades as rivers and mountains brighten,
My heart embraces the autumn of the cosmos.
I seek the phoenix and qilin, thinking of Confucius and Mencius,
Gazing level with the Milky Way, I recall the imperial capital.
Who would fault me for coming late?
A thousand-year vow has now fermented into a smile.

Sun Zhang of Haining ascends Mount Tai—
Jade pillars rise steeply to touch the heavens,
I gather my robes and gaze, thoughts drifting far.
Two wheels of sun and moon draw near the twin peaks,
A unified realm stretches ten thousand miles long.
Even the remotest lands have lords who pay tribute,
No corner of the wilds fails to submit to the king.
Now is not the time to present memorials for eastern sacrifices,
For sagely rule and harmony surpass Han and Tang.

Wang Lian, Instructor of Taizhou, ascends the peak—
Reaching the summit, wonders upon wonders unfold,
The Azure Cloud Palace rests amid purple clouds.
From a thousand-fathom precipice, the distant seems within grasp,
Heaven’s nearness tempts me to peer into its depths.
How many can be called true Dao-eyes for seeing the great?
How many seek divine blessings through rites to the mysterious?
Thinking of eastern Lu, cradle of spirit and talent,
Surely worthy scholars will herald a flourishing age.

Zhu Juemin, Scholar of Gaoyou, ascends Mount Tai—
All my life, I’ve looked up to Mount Tai from Huaihai,
How fortunate now to ascend its heights!
Following in Confucius’ footsteps, I tread his path,
At the stone grotto, I may knock on the gate of profound cultivation.
Step by step, I rise beyond clouds and mist,
Amid towering crags, I stand amid snow and frost.
If the mountain spirit were to judge our heights,
Even the brightest mirror has no platform to surpass the world.

Zhu Hongji, Scholar of Yanggu, prays for rain—
Scorching heat like fire, the sun’s excess burns,
The earth’s fine crops wither on the verge of harm.
Ten thousand households fret, hearts heavy with care,
Yet one sincere plea moves heaven to send torrential rain.
Abundance and prosperity are celebrated with filled granaries,
Praise resounds for the governor’s goodness.
Virtue and merit naturally earn their reward,
The imperial summons first selects those who aid the court.

Wang Hua, Instructor of Lu’an, prays for rain—
The drought demon wreaks havoc with raging flames,
Burning crops and scorching stones, the people grow anxious.
The prefect earnestly emulates the mulberry and catalpa prayers,
The River Lord generously bestows a deluge of rain.
Hope is fulfilled as the three farms rejoice in praise,
Loyalty ensures nine taxes yield a bountiful harvest.
Reporting achievements to heaven brings highest acclaim,
A glorious name is inscribed beside the emperor’s throne.

Wang Tan, Registrar.

Ascending Mount Tai

Ten years of wandering, temples now streaked with silver,
Yet ever longing to gaze upon Mount Tai’s verdant heights.
Misty pavilions governed by the true sovereign,
Reverently inhaling wind and thunder, paying homage to the myriad spirits.
Who in the world decides gain and loss?
In heaven’s realm, I name myself an immortal.
Boiling stones and consuming rosy clouds—no subtle vehicle,
In solitude, I once more awaken to the truth of non-being.

Guo Rulin of Yongfeng, Ascending the Sacred Peak

Heaven preserved Mount Tai to inaugurate the eastern land,
With lofty resolve, I ascend as the sun shines bright.
Han sacrifices and Qin enfeoffments—all faded traces,
Only sheer cliffs and secluded ravines stir my emotions.
Towers and terraces embrace the azure mist,
Ancient trees, dragon-veined, bear the weight of sun and moon.
My exploration unending, I borrow a resting place,
Tonight, beneath the emerald haze, a single star gleams.

Zha Zhilong’s Record of Three Ascents to Mount Tai (as previously noted)

Before Mount Tai’s peaks, I pause my banner,
The Heavenly Gate’s imperial canopy clusters in the clear mist.
Mournful autumn unexpectedly meets the Double Ninth,
Having braved peril, I know the joy of threefold delight.
On the Eighteen Bends, sweat and blood intermingle,
Amid ten thousand layers of clouds, I sing without restraint.
The grand vista permits a step beyond Confucius,
A long whistle at parting—how satisfying it is!

In the Longqing reign’s ren-shen year, falsely accused, I was graciously exonerated; on my eastward return, I passed here.

Fifteen years later, I revisit this place,
The mountain’s radiance unchanged, yet my thoughts drift far.
The Old Man Peak’s cassia ages with heaven and earth,
The Jade Maiden Spring cradles the flow of sun and moon.
On the third summer’s scorching cliffs, I still clutch my spindle,
In the ninth heaven’s clearing skies, dew nearly soaks my robes.
Suddenly, clouds and rain stretch toward Yan and Ji—
The sagely ruler must ease the drought demon’s woes.

In the Wanli reign’s bing-xu year, summoned to office in the east, I journeyed in summer and, passing here, encountered rain.

In early winter, I ascend to the summit’s highest point,
The air as mild as spring, as crisp as autumn.
The moon now fills the Jade Pool,
The Sunrise Peak seems to rise from the azure sea.
At Dragon Gully, the three luminaries stand still in the wind,
At Rainbow Bay, ten thousand ravines’ mists wholly disperse.
Surveying heaven and earth, I return to maps and records—
Could it be the mountain’s spirit rewarding me?

In the Wanli reign’s bing-xu winter, dispatched by the provincial administration to compile the *Annals of Mount Tai*, I visited its wonders.

Zou Depu, Compiler of the Hanlin Academy, of Anfu in Jiangxi—Two Poems on Ascending Mount Tai

At dawn, Mount Tai’s crags press close to the Dipper’s enclosure,
Who in the world speaks of Kunlun’s existence?
Twin peaks of sun and moon encircle heaven’s bounds,
Half a couch of wind and thunder brushes the imperial domain.
Northward, cloudlight reveals the Purple Ultimate,
Eastward, the sea’s hues enter the clear goblet.
Where now are the Qin chariots and Han tripods?
A sigh—only fragments of stone remain in these empty mountains.

Ten thousand layered peaks encircle the Great Peak,
Green cliffs and sheer walls carved like hibiscus.
The mountain’s glow, hazy, recalls Wu Gate’s steeds,
The sea’s breath, faint, evokes Yue’s Watchtower Peak.
The clear horn’s notes seem to echo the emperor’s melody,
Where might one seek the red pellet’s immortal traces?
Brushing my robes, I stand alone in the azure firmament,
Light wisps of cloud drift past the Five Pines.

Guang Lu, Provincial Graduate of Licheng in Shandong—Ascending Mount Tai, Composed with Academician Zou

From birth, I bore the bones of an immortal,
Delighting in deep mountains to gather ferns and bracken.
Of the Five Peaks, Mount Tai is most revered,
An old friend invited me to venture from the imperial domain.
Together, we explored the mountain’s rear,
Precipitous cliffs and jagged ravines embroidered like brocade.
A bird’s path spirals upward a thousand fathoms,
At the perilous summit stands the Jade Emperor’s abode.
At Sunrise Peak, I shake my robes—white clouds descend,
The Heavenly Gate’s long rainbow trails in the pure breeze.
The pure breeze whispers like celestial music,
White clouds part—from the sea, the sun is born.
From the central peak, Qi and Lu’s boundaries shift,
Beneath the clear sky, the lower realm darkens with thunder and rain.
Before the fading light, emerald peaks stretch endlessly,
Jade-like blossoms gleam one by one.
The river flows like silk, hanging from Qin’s trees,
Rosy clouds scatter like brocade, brightening Zheng’s gardens.
Seventy-two sovereigns who performed the feng and shan rites—
Only weathered steles remain, overgrown with moss.
I long to seek truth and visit the Great Dao,
Truly beholding its wonders, I roam freely in seclusion.
Who opened the ancient cave to refine elixirs?
Five-colored misty light illuminates the upper terrace.
A half-acre boulder bears seal-script inscriptions,
The characters seem to emerge from Cang Jie’s hand.
I wish to beg a single pellet of immortals’ cinnabar,
Thus, as I’ve heard, the heart’s earth would expand.
Plucking bracken and fern—most delectable,
Unwilling to depart without becoming an immortal.
Yet the thatched hut of Tu Qiu is old, far from the world’s dust,
To return to the mortal realm by day—how sorrowful it would be!

Ascending Mount Tai with Academician Zou

A scholar’s pennants flutter as he departs the imperial capital,
Passing through the eastern peak, he embraces this wild man.
I pity the fragrant grasses that ever mark me as a traveler,
Yet rejoice that the green mountains never fail me.
Beneath the old garrison’s blossoms, my departing steed follows,
The river pavilion’s moonlight illuminates our revelry.
Relying on your twin swords, bright as autumn waters,
Would you let my lifelong aspirations stand alone?

Su Dan of Pucheng, Provincial Graduate—Two Poems on Ascending Mount Tai

My life has ever been as vast as lakes and seas,
Today, I ascend the foremost of mountains to behold its wonders.
Flanking the path, thick fragrance of crimson maple leaves,
From overhanging cliffs, chrysanthemums cast their dappled hues.
Beyond the faint traces of Yu’s Nine Provinces,
At a glance, the lands enfeoffed by Yao stretch a thousand li.
The stars and constellations seem within reach,
Deep in the night, jade pendants chime softly.

The west wind carries my climbing clogs,
With each step, my eagerness grows.
The Old Man Stone, kin to melons and gourds,
The floating and sinking of the Qin noble’s pines.
A secluded spring gently receives the midheaven moon,
A distant valley faintly transmits the upper realm’s bells.
Bathed in rain, wreathed in clouds, the emerald peaks are sharply carved,
As autumn arrives, heaven and earth age like hibiscus.

Su Yan of Pucheng, Judicial Reviewer—Two Poems on Gazing upon Mount Tai

Leaning on my staff, I come to heaven’s heights,
Below, I watch the floating clouds.
Riding the wind, I pay homage to Mount Tai,
Gazing at the sun, the azure isles seem distant.
Gathering herbs, I lose the immortals’ path,
Seeking scriptures, the water’s mouth is broken.
At dusk, the cassia grove’s tips are fragrant,
My distant thoughts cannot bear to linger.

I come to gather golden light grass,
Then ascend the Jade Maiden’s Pool.
Once, I spoke of ancient traces,
Threading paths to explore hidden wonders.
A broken stele bears Qin’s seal-script,
Rubbing the moss-grown characters...
Leaning on the railing for a single glance,
The Twin Peaks rise unevenly.

Liu Jin of Licheng, Provincial Graduate—Ascending Mount Tai

At Mount Tai’s summit, jade trees in autumn,
Brushing my robes, I let out a long whistle—thoughts drift far.
My eyes encompass the cosmos, three thousand realms,
The land’s majesty—one hundred and two provinces.
At the Heavenly Gate, stars and constellations draw near,
At Sunrise Peak, parrots cry as sea clouds float.

The meditation grasses of those years remain even now,
Yet how did Qin and Han become ancient mounds?

Zhang Qi of Songjiang—Two Poems on Ascending Mount Tai

Carved from empty space, a green hibiscus,
Rising straight through mist and sky, layer upon layer.
From Han times still lingers the emperor’s wu-tung tree,
While Qin’s enfeoffment has changed to the pine of the grandee.
My talent, unlike the meditative grasses, vexes the magistrate,
In this age of peace, I laugh at the First Emperor.
Once I wandered aimlessly to offer blessings,
Vermilion wrappings ever encircle Azure Cloud Peak.

Brushing my robes at a thousand fathoms, beyond the dust’s haze,
I lean on the evening ocarina—wild hues stretch boundless.
At Sunrise Peak, a mirage rises from the sea,
Ancient steles, wordless, press close to the stars’ script.
In an instant, the three thousand realms vanish beneath my clogs—
Who among the crowd knows the seventy-two lords?
My sleeves flutter, flutter, as if to take flight,
Unknowing that this body and life emerge from floating clouds.

Gong Mian of Wuxi, Provincial Graduate of Zhejiang—Composed with Military Examiner Tang Juexuan While Ascending Mount Tai, a "Song of Ascending Mount Tai" to Record the Splendor

I happen to ascend Mount Tai,
As the third month’s spring nears its end.
A long wind comes from ten thousand leagues,
Rolling clouds and mist through the empty sky.
In a moment, the mist clears to reveal the mountain’s hue—
Verdant, lush, towering...
Pressing against heaven, the sky so close,
Step by step, stone stairs ascend through the clouds.
Myriad ravines and thousand cliffs shift to the wondrous and strange.
The imperial road winds, the six dragons alight,
Sheer cliffs where flying springs hang as waterfalls.

From afar, a hollow blooms with crimson peach blossoms—
One might mistake it for the path to Peach Blossom Spring.

How lofty stand the Great Master Pines,
How towering the Wordless Stele.
Where now are the emperors of Qin and Han?
Only these pines, this stele stand unshaken.

Boulders like Zan and Ruan fill the mountain,
Their carved words long worn by time, moss floating in green.
Mystic traces and hidden marks defy pursuit,
Deep in the crags, dragons sometimes coil.

Shaking my robes, I ascend once more to the highest peak—
Mountains and rivers of the earth lie spread beneath my gaze.
My eyes embrace more than the "small world" below,
Alone I transcend antiquity to glimpse the primal chaos.

To the east, Penglai and Yingzhou seem but a footstep away;
To the west, Kunlun is near enough to tread.
Red City and Wangwu lie within a glance,
Mists and rosy clouds entwine my climbing clogs.

What joy to reach this sublime height!
I cry aloud to the heavens, and the Gate of Heaven opens.
From the clouds, jade maidens descend in pairs,
Straight I ascend to the golden and silver terraces.

Gazing at my own mortal form, dust-bound and frail,
I bow to the host of immortals, knowing I cannot compare.
Only this wish remains: to cast off my official’s cap and follow Xiangping,
To wander all famous mountains and dwell at Shaoshi.

*Miscellaneous Verses on Ascending Mount Tai*

**Stone Sutra Vale**
Emerald screens enclose the stone ravine—
Who inscribed the golden sutra here?
The years have worn the words to near oblivion,
Yet the sutra’s essence remains ever thus.

**Turning Horse Ridge**
The imperial road where six dragons passed,
Sheer cliffs where a thousand horses turned back.
The rites of imperial ascent are long forgotten,
Mountain paths now thick with moss.

**Great Master Pines**
Tall pines lean over the precipice,
Their verdant hues seeming to brush the sky.
Did they heed the Qin emperor’s command?
They are sealed by white clouds of their own accord.

**Imperial Tent**
Sheer cliffs soar to the heavens,
On all sides like a tent arrayed.
Here once the Son of Heaven rested,
His jade banners still faintly visible.

**Heaven’s Gate**
From distant heights, a ladder of blue clouds,
A single leap to the wind’s utmost reach.
A long whistle pierces Heaven’s Gate,
Jade maidens come to greet me from afar.

**Jade Maiden Pool**
The mountain pool’s waters are clear and blue,
Reflecting even a husband’s face.
Jade maidens come and go,
Scattering petals of colored clouds.

**White Cloud Grotto**
A stone grotto cradles white clouds,
Sealed with jade by Emperor Wu of Han.
Han Wu is long gone and silent,
But the white clouds endure through the ages.

**Wordless Stele**
Grand designs have faded to nothing,
Only an empty stele remains atop the mountain.
Its golden script is no longer seen,
Day and night, the wind of heaven blows.

**Sun-Viewing Peak**
I lodge the night on Sun-Viewing Peak,
Rising to watch the sun’s first rays.
From the sea’s depths, a luminous pearl surges,
Startling dragons from their caves.

**Confucius Cliff**
Long ago, the Master ascended here,
His profound vision transcending all bounds.
Now I come to gaze upon this place,
Suddenly my heart and spirit feel unbounded.

**Zhang Maozhong of Feixiang—Two Poems on Ascending Mount Tai**

**I**
Heaven’s Gate stands lush and verdant,
A bamboo staff from the west to pay homage to the vast void.
When was the jade tablet left by Han’s decree?
A thousand years later, Qin’s achievements are still read in stone.
The clear light points near to Fusang’s sun,
Purple qi stretches far to Jieshi’s winds.
The seventy-two sovereigns now lie silent,
Yet the waterfall’s veil never fades, wildflowers ever red.

**II**
Twin dragons wind a hundred turns, rinsing clear rapids,
Flanking the path, how many layers of verdant pines?
The bright moon levels the Three Observances,
White clouds rest motionless by the Five Pines, cold.
A long incense of sacred fragrance rises,
In secluded valleys, magic mushrooms trampled by deer.
A Daoist beyond the world, flowing like water,
Explores this famed peak to write of its wonders.

**Zou Depu, Compiler of the Hanlin Academy, of Anfu, Jiangxi—Composed on the Road to Mount Tai, Recalling Past Journeys**

In those days, we kept summer retreat at South Mountain Temple,
Our spirits soaring with the heavens and earth.
On rush mats, we sat through clouds veiling a thousand peaks,
Scattering fragments of cloud-light upon the world below.

Today, I meet you again at the headwaters of Clear Spring,
Hand in hand, we revisit Mount Tai’s ascent.
My heart swells at the summit’s final climb,
As if drifting among the azure skies.

You stride like a mountain, unshaken,
Swallowing and spitting winds and clouds, a hundred wonders of Tai.
Heaven and earth grant us health and joy,
Bestowing both noble friends and rare elixirs.

When shall we follow you to the Three Isles,
Treading immortal paths to gather jade herbs?

**Elder Peak**
Alone I stand on Elder Peak,
And suddenly the world seems small.
Looking up, the heights are boundless,
Looking down, not even a lone crow.

**Sun-Viewing Peak**
I scale a thousand fathoms’ ridge,
My gaze sweeping beyond heaven and earth.
A long wind carries the sea’s hues,
Chill upon my robes and belt.

**Jade Maiden Mountain**
Azure heavens hang in sheer cliffs,
Below, a recluse makes his home.
Cross-legged, he sits among white clouds,
All sounds stilled, the world serene.

**White Cloud Grotto**
This grotto is fit for secluded dwelling,
A brief retreat to rest one’s integrity.
Gazing down, white clouds billow forth,
Rumbling like thunder, raining upon the world.

**Stone Sutra Vale**
Who brought the *pattra-leaf* sutra,
To carve upon this massive stone?
The Sanskrit script is half-lost—
Yet enough remains to grasp the Dharma’s treasury.

**Peach Blossom Vale**
Sheer cliffs pierce the vault of heaven,
A frigid spring gushes at their base.
In all the world, who could compare
To this place of pristine purity?

**Wordless Stele**
At the summit stands an empty stele,
As if the ancients had a purpose.
For the highest teaching from the start
Was never meant to be confined in words.

**Great Master Pines**
You hold fast to your steadfast integrity,
What could the Qin court’s commands have meant to you?
Riding the wind, you let out a long whistle,
Envious of the magic mushrooms of Shang Rock.

**Composed on the Road to Mount Tai**

The ancient roads of the Central Plains grow lush with grass,
A thousand miles I wander west of the Wen River.
Glad to find green mountains to indulge my rustic whims,
I call the bright moon to share my rocky perch.

An empty river hangs with stars against blue cliffs,
Wild garrisons and winds darken the green streams.
The seventy-two sovereigns who performed the feng and shan rites—
Could any still be questioned with a staff and golden mud?

**Yu Lin, Professor of Jiangxi’s Xinfeng—Ascending Mount Tai**

Through perilous paths I pay homage to the Imperial Grandson,
The Azure Clouds Hall near the Purple Tenuity’s gate.
Heaven’s course bears sun and moon in dual radiance,
Earth arrays Song and Hua—only Tai stands supreme.

**Jade grasses and coral blossoms** form their radiant hues,
**Rainbow banners and lightning canopies** escort the refined souls.
Should you stand atop the **Golden Gate**, riding the wind,
The **nine points of the divine realm** lie within your grasp.

**Hall of Primordial Chaos**
To seek the **Three Yangs Temple**, I halt my carriage for another dawn.
Pine shadows carve the white sun,
Stone paths lean against the azure sky.
The **imperial brush** illumines the valleys,
**Sacred scrolls** seal the depths of silence.
Discoursing on the **Mystery**—truly a meeting of minds,
An old friend from my homeland extends the invitation.

**Yu Jun, Prefect of Songjiang—Four Poems Composed with Censor Li Changqing on a Late Spring Day Ascending Mount Tai**

**I**
To my lifelong wish—**famed mountains of the cosmos**—I remain true,
Once more donning spring robes with a **Daoist adept**.
Heaven turns, the great sea folds its clouds in thousands,
The Central Plains bloom like brocade encircling all.
The **towers of Qin** and **peaks of Cuiwei** fade into the past,
The **misty gates of Wu**—such tales now seem unreal.
Amid a thousand cliffs, temple bells chime in unison,
No need for **twin tablets** to descend the emerald slopes.

**II**
The peaks of **Qi and Lu** unfold before my gaze,
The **muddy Yellow River** and **clear Ji** coil in endless loops.
Here, the land’s grandeur surpasses all other mountains,
Its sacred spirit stirring the **nine heavens** since time immemorial.
These verdant cliffs once knew the **Yu Emperor’s** hunt,
These white clouds once followed the **Han sovereign’s** ascent.
In this sagely age, the **eastern feng rites** are set aside—
No need to ask **Sima Xiangru** for his ode.

**III**
At dawn’s first light, I linger east of **Sunrise Peak**,
Watching the morning hues seep into the **Han mist**.
**Auspicious clouds** do not guide the **Xihe’s chariot**,
**Turbid mists** instead obscure the **Sea God’s palace**.
In the abyss, **fish and dragons** slumber undisturbed,
While all other mountains, **gilded and jadelike**, dissolve into emptiness.
From afar, I pity the **Fusang’s** distant glow—
Would that I could escape the **Frost Terrace’s** keen scrutiny.

**IV**
As I tread the mountain’s waist, the sun nears dusk,
Returning to the **official pavilion** to savor its wine.
Beneath the eaves, the air shifts between cold and warmth,
Beyond the walls, the signs of life remain uncertain.
A **crow’s-path** of a thousand coils dangles precariously,
Yet my **dove-staffed retinue** needs no support.
The **mountain elder** points with a laugh at a towering trunk—
This was once the **Five Great Men of Qin**.

**Chen Wanyan, Censor of Nanhai—Inscribed at Sunrise Peak**
Before dawn, the **ten thousand households’ stars** still veil the sky,
Yet **auspicious light** already bathes the **Observation Peak**.
I know that from here, **Fusang** lies near—
Would that I could borrow a **celestial raft** to reach the sun’s edge.

**Small Heavenly Gate Encounters Rain**
Here stands the **altar of Emperor Wu’s** ancient rites,
Where **sacred springs** sometimes reveal a **white dragon’s** return.
The **heavenly wind** inverts the **curtain-spring’s** flow—
Yet still, travelers brave the rain to ascend.

**After the Storm**
Beyond the clouds, rivers and mountains encircle **Mount Tai**,
My homeland lost amid layers of emerald.
From this height, my gaze stretches far, stirring melancholy—
Mistaking **Luofu’s** **Bathing Sun Peak** for home.

**The Inscriptionless Stele**
I’ve heard it was **lashed from the eastern sea**,
Now crystalline, it gleams beside the **Azure Clouds Palace**.
Though untouched by carvings, its blankness remains—
Far nobler than those cliffs **shamed by self-aggrandizing praise**.

**Paying Homage at Mount Tai**
Since ancient times, the second month has marked the **imperial progress**,
Yet the **feng rites of later ages** fade into doubt.
**Jade seals** lie hidden where fragrant grasses entwine,
The **imperial canopy** once graced this peak, now **auspicious clouds** stand alone.
At heaven’s edge, **boats and oars** seek **immortal songs**,
While **lofty pines** at the horse’s head are hailed as **Great Men**.
Today, the **local magistrate** performs the **proper sacrifices**—
A hundred spirits hasten to the **green mountain’s** altar.

**Dreaming of the Famed Mountain for Years**
I’ve long dreamed of this mountain, soaring with **apes and cranes** in fleeting company.
Climbing sheer stone steps, I feared no path remained—
Then, turning through the clouded woods, another world appeared.
The **misty waves of the vast sea** unfold like a painting,
The **land of Qi’s** splendor swirls in my wine cup.
Yet, seeking the **Way**, I’ve not grasped the **elixir of immortality**—
I can only bow to the **jade immortals** atop the peaks.

**Lu Kun, Provincial Administrator of Shandong—Song of the Poisonous Grasses (with Preface)**
In the spring of **Wanli 16 (1588)**, Shandong suffered famine—two years of drought.
Tree bark and roots were stripped bare,
Then wild grasses became their sustenance, heedless of bitterness or raw hunger.
Faces blackened like **smelted ore**, yellowed like **dust**,
The emaciated littered the fields, beyond all recovery.
As I traveled through the region, I saw by the roadside two or three kinds of green grass,
Their flowers and fruits already formed, thriving alone.
I asked their names—
"These are **cat’s-eye**, **lamb’s-kill**, and **ghost’s-umbrella**," they replied.
"Highly poisonous, they can kill instantly if swallowed. How fortunate we are!
Some have eaten them, enduring vomiting, diarrhea, and torment all day,
Their livers and intestines splitting by night—yet they did not die.
The agony surpasses even death—who would dare consume them?"
I wiped my tears and sang in their stead:

The willow’s crown is gone, the elm’s bark scant—
Was this some **Shennong’s** lesson, each tasting a hundred herbs?
If only hunger could be delayed, even for a moment,
Who would care if the taste was bitter or harsh?
Alas, **poisonous grasses**—why does Heaven grow you?
You neither prolong our lives nor hasten our deaths.
A swift death would be hard enough—
But how can one endure **endless hunger**?

**Slaying the Village**
In **Changqing**, the sixth month’s crops still await rain,
Great and small households despair of the drought.
Grass roots are dug up, trees stand like winter,
Then they hunt fledglings and devour field mice.
In the sands, a child’s sobs verge on breaking,
While **Ah Ba**, a stranger, cannot speak.
Three years of relentless hunger, yet a breath remains—
What heart is left to care for sons and daughters?
In a ruined hut, an old man pounds elm bark.
I ask, "Why do you pound it?"
"The earth’s nature is heavy, the chaff’s is light—
Mixed with elm flour, it can be made into gruel.
I know this feeble life can last but a little longer,
Yet for now, it sustains my gut and stomach.
My wife and children fill the ditches, while I alone survive—
The dead rest in eternal bliss, the living starve.
Silent sobs, only tears remain,
Their gaunt bones rend my heart and spleen."
By the roadside, a temple houses a seated god,
Its body gilded, receiving incense and offerings.

**Bai Nanjin of Yongnian—Ascending Mount Tai**
At the gorge’s mouth, the **morning wind** clears the dawn,
Grasping vines, I stand in awe, my spirit stirred.
The **clamor of the world** lies far below the **Heavenly Gate**,
While **lush greenery** still cradles the **Sunrise Peak**.
**Crow shadows** of late autumn drift from the northern deserts,
**Pine blossoms** in a single night reach **Peng and Ying**.
Suddenly, I recall the **events of a thousand years**—
The **Han’s decrees** and **Qin’s feng rites** were but empty names.

**Xu Shouren, Assistant Magistrate of Qimen—Ascending Mount Tai**
The **majestic Eastern Peak** touches the azure vault—
Who carved the **crimson gate’s** stairway to the heavens?
My **bamboo staff** has crossed **Huangxian Ridge** in vain,
Clutching vines, I ascend straight to the **Azure Clouds Palace**.
The **era of Yao and Shun** seems near at hand,
Yet who now surveys the **lands of Lu and Qi**?
From the **thousand-fathom cliff’s** inscriptions, I take in all—
Then climb the summit to praise the **divine work**.

**Ascending Again**

Twice I seek the marvels of the **Sacred Peak**,
Where mountains swell with numinous grace, cradling **lotus blooms**.
Through clouds I tread the **Heaven’s Gate** path,
From **Sunrise Peak** I gaze afresh upon the sea.

Lichen creeps o’er **Han-dynasty** inscriptions,
Pine canopies overshadow **Qin’s imperial seals**.
Beyond the **three thousand worlds**’ faint haze,
Due north gleams the **Ninefold Radiance**.

---

**Yao Zifu of Wu County—Ascending Mount Tai**

At last I scale the **August Peak**, heart alight,
Where the **Eighteen Turns** unfold as stone-carved stairs.
The **Sima’s ode** on meditation still lingers,
Yet moss now buries the **First Emperor’s** carved decree.

Shaking my robes, I near the **Celestial Pivot**,
Leaning on my sword, I watch the **Dipper’s handle** dip low.
Craning toward **Wu’s gates**—where are they?
A **bolt of silk** in the clouds, lost to sight.

---

**Zhou Daonan, Student of Wu Commandery—Ascending Mount Tai**

Of all the **cosmos’ famed mountains**, **Tai** reigns supreme,
To this day echoing **Qin’s imperial seals**.
Low clouds drift past a thousand peaks’ crows,
Spray like snow battles ten thousand ravines’ dragons.

Might the **Sunrise Peak** reach the **Emperor’s throne**?
Does the **Heaven’s Gate** permit ascent by **jade steps**?
Gazing toward home—who now stands there?
A **bolt of silk** cannot bind the **evening pestle’s** glow.

---

**Yao Yingshi of Zheng County—Ascending Mount Tai**

A **wondrous sight** for all beneath heaven,
Where even the reckless laugh with bold delight.
Encircling streams weave slender ribbons,
A **pillar of heaven** stands towering high.

Like pellets they vie to be the **sun**,
Like autumn leaves they race to be **boats**.
At the mountain’s foot, rain falls from a **blue sky**,
In the maple groves, **white waves** surge like paintings.

A lone fortress rises, **stone-rolled** and sheer,
The **imperial gates** cradle a **scrap of cloud**.
The **wordless Qin stele** fades into illusion,
The **Han’s feng rites** clamor in history’s pages.

From **water eaves** a **waterfall** takes flight,
In **Dragon Gorge** a **divine turtle** leaps.
On **Xian Ridge**, yellow plums ripen,
From **cliff’s edge**, emerald brows arch.

Where **Azure Clouds** lie deep in seclusion,
The **Jade Maiden** refines her **elixir paste**.
At **Five-Flower Spring**, I bathe my face,
The **eight-hued phoenix** bears a **banner** in its beak.

A **crimson mirror** hangs in the sky,
Combing clouds into **four wild hats**.
**Lotus blooms** unfurl in **green bays**,
**Stars** gleam like **palace robes**.

**Girdle pendants** chime through a thousand ravines,
**Pipes and reeds** resound with myriad voices.
Weary of the **Three Tablets’ Bridge**,
The **imperial retinue** halts at **orchid’s** edge.

**Husband pines** congeal in **snow’s hue**,
The **sacred Dao** earns **heaven’s praise**.
**Cliff inscriptions** guarded by spirits and ghosts,
The **winding path** howls with **crows and apes**.

**Stone seals** bear **Vajra** script,
Waves rock the **Sunrise Peak’s oysters**.
Of **mountains and rivers’** hundred-two realms,
The **Dharma-realm** teems with ten thousand hosts.

I **ascend and let loose a long cry**—
**Fame and profit** are but **goose feathers**.

---

**Hu Xishun, Vice Prefect of Yuanwu—Ascending Mount Tai**

**Heaven and earth** stretch boundless as I stand,
Alone in **clear autumn**, thoughts unrestrained.
The **eastern sea** draws near with its **three isles**,
From the **Heaven’s Gate**, I gaze north to **five-layered clouds**.

The **Jade Maiden** treads mist in nuptial grace,
The **Azure Dragon** chants beneath the ravine’s rain.
Where now are the **seventy-two sovereigns**?
Only echoes of **golden tablets** remain.

---

**Zhan Yangbi, Vice Minister of Anxi—Two Poems on Ascending Mount Tai**

**Leaving the city**, I already love the clustered peaks,
Ascending the ridge, my eyes drink **ten thousand layers of green**.
From the ravine, cascades roar beneath the cliffs,
Distant monks strike the **visitor’s bell** as I approach.

**Turning crags** form a **heavenly canopy** over the **eastern sea**,
**Strange cliffs** hum with **wind and waves** like **ancient pines**.
This **supreme marvel** delights me—my **long-held vow** fulfilled,
Yet where might I meet **immortals** again?

**Han’s tablets** and **Qin’s seals**—empty echoes all,
**Bronze beams** and **golden tiles** honor the **Primal Lady**.
Sitting, I watch the **myriad forms** stretch endlessly,
Looking down, a thousand peaks blur into **indistinction**.

Beyond the wine, the **waning watch** and the **sea’s sun**,
Within the **seal’s enclosure**, night-long **stone tower clouds**.
Brushing my robes, I fail to shake my **misty obsession**,
So I **ascend the mountain** to escape the **world’s dust**.

---

**Chen Wenzhu, Minister of Justice of Mianyang—Four Poems on Mount Tai**

In the **palace’s divine chambers**, I glimpse **feathered wings**,
Weary of mountains, I long cling to **steadfast form**.
From **Wu’s gates**, a **bolt of silk** whitens the **Heaven’s Gate**,
From the **sea**, **flying waves** rest on my **palm’s blue**.

Since the **beginning of time**, **sun and moon** have parted,
Where **mist and clouds** blow, **stars** descend.
Why did **Zhang Heng** gaze eastward?
Could it be this mountain alone deserved an **inscribed stele**?

The **Sunrise Peak** stands **alone**, far from the crowd,
The **Incense Pavilion** exhales **swirling mists**.
**Pine voices** carry **Qin-era rain**,
The **altar’s shadow** flutters with **Han tablets’ clouds**.

In the **Dark Stone Grotto**, I seek the **Jade Maiden**,
Within the **Azure Clouds Palace**, I visit the **Primal Lady**.
Last year, I passed through **Linzi’s** lands—
Still I recall **Sima Xiangru’s** meditation ode.

**Freezing rain** pelts the mountain’s bell,
From the treetops, a **white dragon** suddenly hangs.
The **imperial gates** fade into the **northern pole**,
The **sacred court’s** rites lack an **eastern feng**.

A **jade elixir** of a thousand years may yet be found,
At a glance, **layered clouds** already fill my breast.
**Li and Du’s** lofty verses endure—
How could I meet them with **staff and clogs**?

With a **gesture**, I survey the **famed mountain** in **evening light**,
The **Central Plains** stretch **green and boundless**.
The **Yellow River** curves to embrace the **Three Streams’ Gorge**,
The **cold sea** births a **sixth-month sky**.

From this height, **Qi’s nine points of smoke** are discerned,
From this terrace, **Lu’s thousand-year road** is seen.
Even **Anqi** had his **divine elixir** scattered—
Might I obtain one to **hang at my elbow**?

---

**Records of Mount Tai, Volume 18: Accounts of Ascents**
**Tang Dynasty • Ding Chunze’s *Rhapsody on Sunrise Peak***

When the **sun** rises, it bathes the sea and graces the sky;
When the **peak** soars, it pierces the heavens and overlooks the frontier.
Those who **ascend high** reach the **ninefold firmament**,
Those who **cherish the light** wish to stand before ten thousand men.

Its **essence** is one, its **height** a thousand fathoms.
Since the **spirit-winds** have borne it,
There stands the **Sunrise Peak**.

Before the night’s watch ends, the dawn’s hues remain dim,
At the **utmost height’s farthest reach**, there is only advance, no retreat.
Before **dark and light** are distinguished, **heaven and earth** are already sundered.

Often I hear the **crows’ cries**, yet the gloom lingers unseen;
Suddenly, the **morning cock** crows, and the **pupils brighten** with love.

Thus it gradually emerges from **Yang Valley**,
About to leave the **earth’s bonds**.
The **cliffs and peaks** grow lush with grass,
The trees’ branches **breathe with vitality**—
All gaze upward in awe.

Its **gaze** is like a **torch**, its **illumination** impartial.
Of old, **emperors and kings** ruled the realm and established the **pinnacle**,
Extending their legacy through **feng and shan rites**—
All achieved their success here.

During their **spring tours**, without exception,
They ascended this **summit**,
Illuminating the **distant and vast**,
Contemplating the **meaning of nurturing warmth**,
And plumbing the **essence of creation**.

The sun is likened to the virtue of a single man, while the sacred peaks bear the title of the Three Dukes. Truly, the kings and lords set forth these perilous heights, that the barbarians might come to peace.

Now, with the realm governed by a single virtue and non-action, the Three Luminaries manifest their forms. All things, moving and still, flourish in harmony; the spirits and deities rejoice in sweet accord. A thousand auspicious hues arise, as if offering blessings to ascend in doubt; ten thousand valleys brim with spring clouds, longing to rise into the sacred precincts yet lingering in vain.

There is a traveler of talent, yet lacking in noble bearing. His heart soars and contracts, ever kindling the firefly’s zeal, yet he cannot fathom the boundless heights where the sun’s gaze dwells. He strains his neck through the night, his emotions lingering until dawn, as if awaiting the promise of a torch’s light—thus he hesitates, unwilling to depart.

**Postlude:**
The sun’s gaze stands peerless, solitary through the ages;
Ascend and look—no distance lies beyond its reach.
What is the sun’s purest essence?
None but it presides over all rising and setting.

**Rhapsody:**
To the southeast of Mount Tai, a peak opens into a chamber,
Its sides touching the celestial path, its base gazing down upon the dawn sun.
The jade hare of the moon’s haze stirs the faint glow of the Milky Way;
The crowing of the celestial cock heralds the sun’s emergence from distant mists.

At first, the twilight’s verdant hues enfold the cliffs, which cling to the void.
Alone, it rises beyond the clear and empty, far removed amidst the boundless blue.
Veiled in mist, it remains pale; crossing the sky, it reddens.
Grass and trees scatter like lamps; waves and tides melt into blood-red fusion.

Then, as the chill recedes from ridges and forests, the colors of mist and cloud shift.
The stars and river fade into their first vanishing; peaks and ridges stretch forth in gradual revelation.
Fire stirs atop the mountains, shifting across the water’s face.
It pierces the clear sky like a flying mirror, traverses the hidden windows like streaking lightning.

When the gates stand desolate and the frosty void lies deep,
The cold dawn unfolds its light, warmth lingering into the last hours of night.
It reddens the frost with crystalline radiance, scattering its rays like drifting sparks.
Dew and wind mingle in doubt; shadows sway and tremble.
Gazing to the farthest edge, life stirs from the earth’s surface.
The frail trees stand distinct, emerging from the depths of shade.
Ten thousand ravines withdraw their gloom; a thousand crags usher in the dawn.
It melts the morning snow on ancient steps, rouses the roosting crows in the cold courtyard.

The distant sky gleams smooth and clear, grieving the fleeting moments of the sun’s sliver.
The azure firmament rejoices in its embrace, watching the wheel of light gradually shrink.
Ascending, its colors grow resplendent; soaring, its shadow slants across the void.
Its qi disrupts the mountain’s glow; its light divides the water’s bloom.
The lone hue of the stone cliffs makes the spirit’s ford seem narrow;
The scorching radiance, though near, confirms the road to Chang’an is far.

Then, all things brighten in gradual approach, floating upon the round heavens without hindrance.
Veiled by water’s mist, pearls dim; beneath pine shadows, cliffs shatter into fragments.
The rosy hues gather their brocade; the sky’s winds carve like dark swords.
Parting the slanted heavens at the clouds’ edge, it clears the celestial gates to gaze down in full view.
Emerging from the eaves, it suddenly appears, as if answering the queries of Qi and Lu.
Passing the ridges in turn, it illuminates even the depths of grass and buds.

Thus, it hangs afar in the boundless, rising high beyond the deepest gloom.
All things blaze in its radiance; the six dragons soar and twist without rest.
How might one tread the echoing heights, hands grasping the azure void?
Lamenting the brevity of daylight, one wishes the sun’s crow might pause awhile.

In *Rongzhai’s Random Jottings*, Hong Mai of the Song records Ying Shao’s *Han Official Rites* on the Feng and Shan sacrifices of the Jianwu era, where the emperor is often called "the state." The account vividly depicts the steep and treacherous ascent, the toil and exhaustion of the climb—words I delight in reciting. Its summary states:

That morning, the emperor rode up the mountain, but the paths were often so steep that he dismounted, leading his horse on foot, alternating between walking and riding in equal measure. At the Central Observatory, he paused, gazing upward at the Celestial Gate as if from the bottom of a valley, looking up at a towering peak. Its height made it seem as if one were gazing at floating clouds; its sheer cliffs bore no trace of a path, their stone walls as smooth as jade. From afar, the figures of climbers appeared like drifting stumps or patches of white stone, or snow that had lingered too long—only after passing the trees could one recognize them as people. The ascent was utterly impassable. Scattered stones lay strewn about; even above, they relied on offerings of wine and dried meats. Springs flowed everywhere, yet they pressed on with difficulty. Upon reaching the Celestial Gate, they believed they had arrived, only to be told by those on the path that ten *li* still remained. Along the mountainside, the rocks and pines loomed in lush verdure, as if suspended in the clouds. Glancing down at the ravines, the depths were lost in obscurity, not even a *zhang* or *chi* visible. Ascending seven *li* straight up, they relied on the winding sheep’s-gut path called the Ring Road, where ropes could sometimes aid the climb. Two attendants supported the emperor, those ahead pulling him, those behind seeing only the soles of the ones before, while those ahead saw only the crowns of the ones behind—like a painting of stacked figures. At first, they rested every ten steps or so, then grew weary, their lips parched. Soon, they paused every five or six steps, stumbling and collapsing to the ground, heedless of dry or damp. If a dry patch lay ahead, their eyes fixed upon it, yet their feet would not move. It is also recorded that after the sacrifices were completed, the officials descended in order, with the emperor following. The next day, the imperial physician inquired after his well-being. The emperor replied: "Yesterday, ascending and descending the mountain, I wished to walk, but was pressed by those ahead. When I wished to rest, I was trampled by those behind. The path was steep and perilous—it was no labor for the state." It is further said that the eastern peak is called Sunrise View, where at the first crow of the cock, the sun is seen rising three *zhang* high. The Qin View overlooks Chang’an; the Wu View overlooks Kuaiji; the Zhou View overlooks Mount Song. All such finely crafted records exist, yet they have seldom been praised by past scholars. The Qin, Wu, and Zhou Views have never been put to use.

Here follows the translation:

---

Now, the supplementary texts are fragmentary, with only Liu Zhao’s annotations to the *Book of Han* preserving portions—yet even these are incomplete.

From *Shao Bowen’s Records of Hearings and Sightings*:
A guest recounted: "Once, after resigning my post in Yanzhou, I joined a few friends to offer sacrifice at Mount Tai. We ascended to the summit, traveling forty *li* before lodging in a rustic hut. Before it stood a medicine garden, where *ghost-arrow* herbs, *tianma*, and *xuanshen* grew in abundance. Just before the fifth watch, each of us took up our staffs and set out eastward. After one or two *li*, we reached the Tai Ping Peak. Amid the dense woods lay the remnants of the altar where the Zhenzong Emperor had performed the feng sacrifice. Wrapped in coarse cloth, we sat awaiting the sunrise.

After a long while, the stars gradually thinned. To the east, the land appeared as level as a plain, the horizon already bright, while below, all remained dark. Still later, several peaks emerged in the light, resembling oxen and carts in the distance. The stars vanished entirely, yet the depths below stayed shrouded. At first, I thought the sun would rise from the bright region. But after more time, it burst forth from the great darkness—a deep red orb surging upward for tens of *zhang*. Halfway into the light, half its radiance shone; fully emerged, it blazed in full splendor, though the land beneath remained dark. As the sun climbed, its hue shifted. By the third or fourth mark of the fifth watch, we passed the site where the Zhenzong Emperor’s tent had stood. The stone bore many square hollows. We then crossed the Dragon’s Mouth Spring, where a great rock formed an altar like a dragon’s maw, water welling from its center.

Next, we traversed the Eighteen Bends of the Heavenly Gate, where the peaks rose with exceptional grace. Gazing north, the mountains of Qing and Qi could be counted one by one. Truly, it was a magnificent sight under heaven."

From *Qian Boyi’s Record of Travels in Yingqing*, by the Prefect of Yingqing:
"On the twenty-fourth day of the ninth month in the *jihai* year of the Xuanhe era, I received an imperial edict in person, ordering me to hasten to Fengfu to inspect the mountain shrine. A month later, Boyan arrived from Yanzhou. The following day, I prepared incense and candles to announce the emperor’s decree, then concluded the jiao rites at the Hall of Meeting the True. Alone, I ascended the Pavilion of Auspicious Clouds and took morning tea at the travelers’ lodge. Later, I accompanied Magistrate Kou Xiang, Vice-Magistrate Lü Guangwen, Shrine Official Cao Qincheng, Magistrate of Laiwu Han Xi, and Daoist Su Yanbi to pay respects at the Taiyue Temple. We lingered by the pools, then passed through the Grotto of the Golden Mother to the Temple of the Azure Emperor, where we viewed the inscriptions left by the late Prime Minister Wenxi. Proceeding to the White Dragon Pool, we cast imperial jade sprouts into the water. Enamored of the beauty of the springs and rocks, we strolled with our staffs, returning via Rooster Cage Peak before resuming our sedan chairs. I personally walked to Mount Sheshou to inspect the altar for the she sacrifices, seeking out traces of the past. By evening, I entered the Qian Yuan Temple, taking a light meal at the Pavilion of Verdant Shade before returning."

From *A Brief Record of Prefect Zhang of Dongping’s Ascent of Mount Tai*, by Du Renjie of the Yuan:
"In the first year of the Zhongtong era, the emperor promoted learned scholars to pacify the ten circuits. The Prefect was among the first selected. His governance in eastern Hebei was exceptional, earning him the highest praise in the realm. The emperor summoned him to court, offering a goblet of wine and addressing him by his courtesy name—a mark of honor noted by all.

Four years later, the emperor again appointed him as Pacification Commissioner of Dongping, remarking: 'The court of the Supreme Sage in Qufu and Mount Tai, the sacred peak of the Central Plains, both lie within your jurisdiction. You must personally offer sacrifices.' On the third day before the Double Ninth Festival, he set out with solemn reverence, departing through the gate of Yizhou. The sky was overcast. The next day, he arrived at the Forest Temple, paying homage at the tombs of the Three Sages. The rain had not yet ceased when, after two nights, he reached the mountain shrine.

The following dawn, he ascended the Western Flowery Gate. The clouds shifted between gloom and brightness, the rain coming and going. The Three Views lay within a brushstroke’s reach, their forms changing a thousandfold in an instant. The Prefect said to his attendants: 'I dare not fix a time for the summit ascent. If it clears, we shall proceed. Otherwise, I fear wearying the men.' Soon after, as the fifth watch began, the darkness began to lift. Before the third drum call ended, the stars scattered like sparks.

Thereupon, Prefect Zhang Rulin of the prefecture, Magistrate Zhang Quan of Fengfu, Registrar Wang Tianting, and three hundred followers prepared sedan chairs for the ascent. Passing Yellow Ridge, they refreshed themselves at the Guardian Spring, then rested briefly at the Imperial Tent. Fifteen *li* from the Heavenly Gate, the path narrowed, the forest closing in. Gazing upward from the altar, the sky’s light was scarce, the greenery as dense as clustered pearls.

At dusk, they reached the summit. Looking east, the mountain shadows stretched boundless and black. Examining the stele of Li Si, only a few characters remained legible; the rest were worn beyond recognition. Descending from the Feng Altar, they passed the cliff inscriptions of successive dynasties, all similarly eroded. Only the *Inscription Commemorating Mount Tai* by Emperor Xuanzong of Tang remained—its characters as large as bowls, carved inches deep, their gold inlay still glinting in places.

Moments after sunset, the cold pressed in like midwinter. The attendants lit bonfires and gathered in a circle to await dawn."

At mid-morning, the Master rose and walked from the Jade Maiden’s Pool to the Sun-Watching Peak. The six directions parted like curtains, leaving the air solemn and utterly untainted. By the time the millet steamed, the eastern horizon glowed faintly, now separating, now merging. After the passage of an hour, half the sun emerged, as if entering a boundless realm of golden radiance—all who witnessed it could not but marvel.

Turning back, they beheld the western shadows stretching straight to the Dark Valley, as though vying in grandeur with Kunlun. The Master suddenly tossed his beard, cried out in astonishment, and declared: “On this journey I have seen the true face of Mount Tai thrice over—my wish is fulfilled!” Alas, since my crossing north in the ren-chen year, more than thirty years have passed, and in nine visits I have never before witnessed such wonders. Though I might wish to leave it unrecorded, how could I?

Of old, there was debate between heaven and man, the saying that man can reach anywhere, yet heaven permits no falsehood—truly these are wise words. The Master is a man of pure sincerity: wherever he goes, all harmonizes; whatever he does, his words are fulfilled; whatever he prays for, he receives. This extraordinary sight is but one instance.

Yet in the world, such fortuitous convergences are many. When Duke Wen of Wei attacked Xing, rain fell as his army marched—some say it merely coincided, but this is not so. Such things are purely the work of heaven; where is the role of man? As for wielding a sword to carve rivers or altering day into night, such principles may exist. When Han Yu parted the clouds of Mount Heng or Su Shi borrowed the spring of the Sea Treasury, these were but trifles for men like us—why marvel?

But because the Master came this time, I inscribe these words upon stone for those who come after.

Inscribed by Wang Zhen of Fenggao, engraved before the Yanxi Hall.

*Record of Li Yu’s Ascent of Mount Tai in the Ming Dynasty*

In the spring of the yi-you year of the Chenghua reign, my fellow graduate, Censor Li Jingxian, arrived to inspect Shandong. On the first day of the third month, we traveled together from Jinan to pay respects at Queli, then returned to Taizhou, where the Master hosted a nighttime feast. Jingxian said: “The weather is so mild—tomorrow, let us ascend Mount Tai together to behold its wonders. Would you agree?” I consented.

At dawn the next day, Jingxian and I rode side by side from the prefectural city. To the north, faint mist and pale haze girdled the mountain’s waist. By chance, Vice Minister Ye Gongchen and Assistant Commissioner Zhuang Shangyuan encountered us, and we proceeded together.

After two *li* north, we passed the White Crane Spring, where water bubbled from a stone altar like a boiling tripod. In drought, it dwindled to a trickle. A hundred paces to the west stood the Hair-Washing Tower, its origins unknown, now reduced to ruins.

Turning west and then north for another two *li*, we reached the Queen Mother’s Pool. Its waters were sweet and icy, clear and ever-flowing, neither depleting nor overflowing. The locals drew from it to pray for rain, with frequent success.

Fifty paces farther north lay Lü Rock, where a stone statue of Immortal Lü stood. A pavilion built in the Song dynasty had collapsed, leaving only crumbling foundations. Now enclosed by a stone wall, the site was overgrown with dense forests and lush grasses, their verdure shimmering in the light—quite a fine sight.

By the time we reached the mountain’s base, the sun had risen over Fusang, and clouds and mist blazed in vibrant hues. Before us, peaks jutted in jagged array, their forms varying with distance. The scenery was so extraordinary it seemed to gladden the heart.

From the base to the Horse-Turning Ridge spanned over ten *li*. Twin peaks faced each other, sharp as teeth, their ridges interlocking like jaws. Between them, a stream tumbled and surged, striking the cliffs to send up sprays of jade-like foam. Its roar was thunderous, its waters cascading and swirling for several *li* before joining the Wen River. Scattered along the way were boulders—some as large as wheels, others like screens, bowls, or cups—strewn across the path in countless numbers.

We urged our horses forward single-file, the trail growing ever more rugged until we reached the ridge. Below, the slope plunged sheer, impassable to carriages or horses. The four of us felt our spirits weary, and even the attendants showed signs of fatigue. We rested briefly on the rocks, then drank tea and switched to sedan chairs. Pushing through dense forests and skirting massive boulders, we traveled another four *li* until we reached a sheer cliff face split by a stone path. The ascent was steep and treacherous, but stone steps allowed us to climb.

After another two *li* or so, we arrived at the site where Emperor Zhenzong of Song had encamped before his eastern pilgrimage—the Imperial Tent. The land here was level and broad, shaded by flourishing trees whose dense canopies blanketed the ground. Ahead lay a deep ravine, its dark waters still and rich, reflecting the sky like a mirror. Jagged rocks jutted amid the stream like broken blades.

Small fish, dark green and black, swam near the surface. As we watched by the brook, a Daoist named Luo offered tea cakes. When we tossed crumbs to the fish, they gathered eagerly. Later, Shangyuan threw in a pebble, and they scattered at once.

After another three *li* or so, we reached Huangxian Mountain. During the Qin dynasty, five pines stood here, and the First Emperor of Qin ascended the mountain to confer upon them the rank of *Wudafu* ("Five Great Officers"). After they withered and died, people replanted trees for over a hundred years. Their aged trunks twisted like clenched fists, swollen and gnarled, resembling coiled green dragons poised as if about to take flight. The four of us rested beneath them, marveling at their extraordinary forms.

We walked another two hundred paces to the Hundred-Foot Cliff, where the rock face resembled a house large enough to shelter over ten people. Overlooking the path beside it, purple vines and green creepers swayed, entwining and draping the surface. Nearby lay a stone cave, dark and foreboding, its depth and breadth unfathomable—no one dared to peer inside, though curiosity lingered.

Passing through Stone Wall Gorge, we reached the Eighteen Turns, where two peaks stood facing each other like sheer walls. Between them, stacked stones formed a winding path leading to the Southern Heavenly Gate, nearly five *li* away. From a distance, it appeared like a ladder suspended against the mountainside—this was the most perilous stretch. We crossed a small bridge and followed the winding trail upward, entering the Heavenly Gate. Proceeding east for several *li*, we arrived at Zhaozhen Abbey, where we changed into ceremonial robes and offered sacrifices to the Purple Radiance Primordial Sovereign. When we asked the Daoist about the abbey’s origins, he said he did not know. Every spring, pilgrims from all directions arrived in droves, and those whose hearts were not sincere would invariably receive strange retributions. I had heard and witnessed this many times.

Behind the shrine, we examined a cliffside stele, which bore Emperor Xuanzong of Tang’s inscription commemorating his Feng sacrifice on Mount Tai. The characters were as large as a hand, but years of wind and rain had eroded many of them. A dozen paces to the east stood the stele of the First Emperor’s Feng sacrifice on Mount Tai, inscribed by Li Si. The stone was partially buried in the earth, standing five *chi* tall, its shape irregular—neither square nor fully formed. Its four sides varied in width. I scraped away the grime and erosion to examine it closely: it bore twenty-two lines, each containing twelve characters, though most were illegible.

Walking several paces west, we ascended to the Summit of Great Peace, the highest point of Mount Tai.
