Atop it lay a square slab of stone, a *zhang* in length, its edges still sharp and its surface smooth and lustrous. The four of us sat upon it for a while, but Gongchen and Shangyuan, weary and daunted, returned to Zhaozhen Abbey. Jingxian and I gathered our robes and pressed onward to the summit. Gazing down, the clustered peaks lay prostrate like mounds of earth, save for the two peaks of Divine Empyrean, which rose sharply, their crags glistening. Exquisite flowers and rare herbs bloomed in profusion—white blossoms spread in rows across the ravines and valleys. The cries of strange birds and beasts echoed in our ears, mingling with the wind, creating one of Mount Tai’s most wondrous sights.

A hundred and three peaks lay before us: Sun Viewing Peak, Wu Viewing Peak, and Zhou Viewing Peak. Jingxian and I let our eyes roam freely, the vastness stretching endlessly before us. The mountains Song, Hua, Heng, and Huo, as well as the rivers Jiang, Han, Yellow, and Ji, could all be pointed out in the distance—truly, no other mountain in the world could rival Mount Tai’s height.

It was midday, the wind gentle and the air crisp. After lingering for some time, we suddenly felt our spirits soar, as if we had left the mortal world behind, forgetting we stood upon the azure heavens. Descending a few dozen paces from the summit, we came to Jade Maiden Spring, its waters clear and sweet. The Daoists often drew from it to brew tea. Twenty paces east lay Dragon’s Mouth Spring, where a great stone altar resembled a dragon gaping wide. Water gushed from its maw with a rushing sound.

Following a perilous path south for forty paces, we reached a sheer cliff where ancient trees cast dense shadows. Peering down into the abyss, my hair stood on end. A strange rock, shaped like a great chest, teetered precariously on the cliff’s edge, swaying in the wind. When we asked the Daoist about it, he said it was called the Cliff of Abandoning the Body—a place where those cultivating the Dao would cast themselves off to enter the realm of the weary. Jingxian and I shuddered, calling it a deception that harmed lives. We ordered the local officials to block the path and prohibit passage.

To the west of the cliff stood five peaks, jagged and clustered like lotus petals, named Lotus Flower Peak. From the western foothills, winding eastward, tiered ridges emerged, and a spring cascaded over the rocks, plunging dozens of *zhang* in silken threads like a curtain—this was Water Curtain Spring. The peaks of Parrot Cage and the valleys of Peach Blossom held many numinous traces and marvels, but the paths were tangled with brambles and thorns, the ravines choked with undergrowth, making them inaccessible.

As the sun passed its zenith, Jingxian and I went to observe, where we met the two gentlemen for a shared meal. Each of us composed two poems and inscribed them on the eastern wall of the abbey. We descended along the winding path from the Heavenly Gate, reaching the mountainside by dusk. The setting sun cast its glow, the mountain’s hues shimmering, the waters rippling. Woodcutters and herdsmen moved faintly among the trees, as if figures in a painting. Wishing to prolong our delight, we considered visiting Zulai Mountain to seek the former site of the Six Hermits of Bamboo Brook and the retreat of Shi Shoudao, but the day had grown late.

Alas! In my youth, I once read the works of Master Meng, who wrote that Confucius, upon ascending Mount Tai, saw the world as small. The *Gongyang Commentary* speaks of Mount Tai’s clouds, which bring rain across the land before the morning is out. I pondered Du Fu’s poem *Gazing at the Peak*, with its lines: *"Nature’s spirit gathers in its divine beauty, / Yin and yang divide dusk and dawn. / My breast swells with layered clouds, / My eyes strain to follow the returning crows."* I imagined Mount Tai’s towering majesty, its peaks’ grandeur, its flora’s rare splendor, its immortal traces and strange wonders, and the reflections of sages and worthies through the ages who had ascended and left their marks. How could I have hoped to climb this mountain, to explore its vastness and witness its marvels firsthand? For over a decade, such dreams had filled my mind.

When Cheng Mingdao was young, he heard of the wonders of Mounts Tai and Hua while in Exian County but had not yet visited them. Upon ascending, he requested to serve as magistrate of that county to fulfill his long-held wish, later recording the experience. Now, I hold no official post, yet by good fortune, I serve as the regional inspector of this land. Moreover, the sagely virtue of our sovereign has spread far, sheltering the people like dew. I am further blessed that my companions are all former colleagues from court, and with this leisure, I have been able to witness these wonders, fulfilling a lifelong aspiration. How fortunate I am!

This, too, is the Dao of tension and release—how could it be compared to those who indulge in excess and forget to return? This account, along with Qiao Yu’s record, is among the finest in He Zhong’s *Records of Famous Mountains Ancient and Modern*.

**Minister Qiao Yu’s Record of Travels on Mount Tai**
In the fifth year of the Zhengde era, a great drought struck Yan and Qi, and the Grand Canal became obstructed. The Son of Heaven thus revived the ancient rites of prayer for rain, dispatching me, the Vice Minister of the Ministry of Revenue, Qiao Yu, to perform sacrifices to the Eastern Marchmount and the Eastern Sea on the fourteenth day of the fifth month. I had long cherished the wish to ascend Mount Tai, yet I feared this journey—undertaken to invoke the divine and succor the people—might prevent me from fulfilling it. If the rites concluded without rain, I would be unable to ascend at all. Thus, I purified my heart and set forth. Passing Dezhou, rain fell in torrents; crossing Pingyuan, Yucheng, Tai’an Prefecture, and the lands of Jinan, the rain persisted until dawn. Farmers rejoiced, and I, too, smiled, believing my wish would be granted.

On the twenty-ninth day, I bathed, changed into ceremonial robes, and inspected the sacrificial animals. At the third watch of the first day of the sixth lunar month, after the cock’s crow, I offered sacrifices at the Eastern Marchmount Temple. As the rites concluded, thunder and lightning erupted. I grew fearful: since the Qin and Han dynasties, seventy-two rulers had performed the feng and shan sacrifices on Mount Tai, their deeds recorded in the annals. Even the First Emperor, with his tiger-like might and a retinue of ten thousand chariots, encountered a violent storm on the ascent and could not complete the feng sacrifice. Now, with thunder and lightning upon us, if rain followed, weary men and emaciated horses would be unable to ascend. Yet I told myself: with utmost sincerity and reverence, would the mountain fail to take notice? In the end, I ascended with Messrs. Hu and Zhang. The sky was overcast, but after traveling five *li*, we entered through the Red Gate, and the clouds gradually dispersed.

Proceeding northwest along the stone embankment for twenty *li*, we crossed two ridges: Huima and Huangxian. After another five *li*, we reached the Imperial Tent, where we saw the Five Grand Masters Pines, the trees where the First Emperor had rested. Their divine roots and ancient trunks stood over two *zhang* tall. From the Imperial Tent upward, the path consisted entirely of stone steps. After a meal, we ascended the Great and Small Dragon’s Mouths. Winding for another fifteen *li*, we reached the Southern Heavenly Gate, then three *li* more to the summit—a total of fifty switchbacks. The weather shifted between clear and overcast, the climate assuming myriad forms, beyond description.

We paid homage to the Azure Clouds Primordial Sovereign. Ascending further, we came to the Eastern Marchmount Temple, where the cliffside stele stood. The stele, two *zhang* tall and one and a half *zhang* wide, bore the *Inscription on Mount Tai* composed by Emperor Xuanzong of Tang in clerical script, erected in the Tianbao era. We inscribed our names upon it. Further up was the highest point, the Jade Emperor’s Hall. To its south stood the First Emperor’s Fengshan Stele. Clearing the path to Shidian, we viewed the *Stele of Qin’s Meritorious Virtue*, over two *zhang* tall, its inscription enigmatic. Within the stone chamber of the inner hall lay a stone casket of Emperor Zhenzong of Song, containing sixteen jade tablets. During the Chenghua era, they were examined by the emperor and confirmed to be the *Text of the Sacrifice to the Earth of Mount Tai*.

To the east lay the Sun-Viewing Peak, marked by a small stele where we also carved our names. A few steps further stood the Sea-Viewing Stone, the site of the Yue View, from which one could gaze toward Kuaiji. The stone, perched at the summit, crouched like a living creature. Enamored, I climbed it and inscribed *Sea-Viewing Stone* in three large characters. Turning, we ascended the Immortal’s Bridge and Five-Flower Ridge. The sky was radiant, the azure heavens stretching boundlessly. Gazing in all directions, my heart expanded with boundless joy. To the northeast, the city of Jinan and Mount Hua appeared like tiny huts floating on water. Below, ethereal white clouds billowed from the mountainside, veiling the land beneath. To the north, I beheld the capital; to the south, Huaixu; to the west, Yan and Zhao; to the east, the sea. Beyond lay the realms of Kongtong, Danxue, Taiping, and Taimeng. Truly, this was one of the world’s most wondrous sights.

We dined at a Daoist abbey and inscribed our names and poems on a small stone stele. Lingering until dusk, we descended, leaving inscriptions on beloved cliffside rocks. By the time we reached the prefectural city, the second watch had already been announced.

**Lao Hongxian, Zhuangyuan of Jishui, on Confucius’s Ascent**
Mencius said: "When Confucius ascended Mount Dong, Lu appeared small; when he ascended Mount Tai, the world appeared small. Thus, those who have gazed upon the sea find other waters insignificant; those who have studied at the gates of a sage find other words inadequate." To observe water, one must watch its waves; the light of the sun and moon illuminates even the smallest crevices. Flowing water does not advance until it fills every hollow; the gentleman’s aspiration for the Dao does not flourish until it achieves coherence. This is why the sage’s greatness lies in constancy—one cannot act haphazardly. Though Confucius was the ultimate sage, he began his studies at fifteen, stood firm at thirty, and attained clarity at forty. Thus, the gentleman’s Dao is like ascending a height: one must begin from the lowly. No one has ever reached the utmost heights without adhering to the Mean.

Later, recounting You Ruo’s words: "Among the people, there is Confucius; to Mount Tai, Confucius is but a mound of earth. Yet to aspire to learn, one must engage in constant practice—lest one fail to achieve coherence." Grand Coordinator Zhu of Mount Tai, entrusted with governing the eastern lands, found no leisure amid his myriad duties. Yet he first honored the sage’s footsteps, seeking inscriptions from afar. Delving into antiquity and tracing the lineage of learning, he wished to inscribe these words beside the path for posterity to ponder.

**Li Xueshi, Hanlin Compiler from Pingdu Prefecture, A Brief Record of Ascending the Supreme Marchmount**
Alas, a mountain is but one. Before ascending, one gazes upward, and it seems ever higher; after ascending, the high appears low. Descending, the low seems high again. The peaks and mists differ, yet it is not the mountain that changes—it is the climber who changes. The mountain remains as it ever was. Those who gallop along the paths of fleeting clouds and transient wealth, their innate nature altered, may never comprehend this truth.

Viewing the floating clouds from Mount Tai, one mistakes the clouds for the mountain’s transformations—this is no different from those who, before ascending, first behold the mountain. Yet your sudden awakening, unswayed by the illusions of fleeting clouds, and your true attainment of the grand vista of Mount Tai—was this not the power of today’s ascent?

**Shang E, Assistant Administration Commissioner, A Brief Record of Touring Mount Tai**
In the spring of the *bingwu* year of the Chenghua reign, as the provincial and judicial officials prepared to inspect the prefectures and counties, I, along with Assistant Administration Commissioner Wang Shiyong of Liwu, Surveillance Commissioner Liu Tinggui of Qishui, and Pan Yingchang of Tiantai, agreed upon a date to travel together.

On the *dingmao* day of the first month, we departed from Jinan. That evening, Shiyong and I lodged at Lingyan Temple. On the *wuchen* day, we reached Tai’an Prefecture. On the *jisi* day, Tinggui and Yingchang arrived. At dawn on the *gengwu* day, we visited the Grass Umbrella Pavilion, paid homage at the Eastern Peak Shrine, and then took sedan chairs out of the city’s northern gate.

After five *li*, we reached the foot of Mount Tai. Taking the Red Gate route, we passed the Gao Laojiao Bridge, beside which lay the Water Curtain Cave. To its left stood the Steep Cliff Pavilion, where we rested briefly. Passing the Horse Shed Cliff and facing Horse Ridge, the path grew increasingly steep. After ascending further to the eastern slope of Huangxian Ridge, the trail leveled into a stretch known colloquially as "Three Joys." Winding upward, we reached the Imperial Tent, where Emperor Zhenzong of Song once encamped. Continuing in serpentine ascent, we traversed the Eighteen Bends and arrived at the Southern Heavenly Gate. Proceeding northeast for two *li*, we reached Zhaozhen Palace, where we paid respects to the Azure Cloud Primordial Sovereign. Just north of the palace stood the Eastern Peak Temple, its shaded grove bearing the Tang-era *moya* stele from the Kaiyuan reign. We then ascended to the Fengchan Terrace, where the Jade Emperor Hall stood, its front marked by a stone tablet over a *zhang* tall—some say it is Qin’s uninscribed stele.

Turning southeast, we reached Sunrise Peak, beneath which lay the Leap-of-Faith Cliff. To the west rose Moon Viewing Peak and Qin Viewing Peak; to the north, Lion Crow and Quail Crags. To the southwest stood the Venerable Man Peak, and to the southeast, Mount Zulai—these were the most striking, their emerald mists and soaring ridges joined by Horse Hoof and Peach Blossom Valleys, the Eastern and Western Divine Empyrean Mountains, the Great and Lesser Suspended Rocks, the Transcendent’s Shedding Grotto, Bright Moon Peak, Tortoise Shade Mound, and Yang’s Old Garden, all within a glance. Below, the Wen and Fu Rivers trailed like silk ribbons. It is said that on clear autumn days, the sea may be seen.

Shiyong, Tinggui, Yingchang, and I drank in a side chamber of Zhaozhen Palace, then again at Steep Cliff Pavilion. With wine warming our spirits and the sun dipping low, our thoughts soared—we rode the vastness of the azure void, our qi spiraling through the cosmos. Below the mountain lay Lü Rock Cave, the Queen Mother’s Pool, and White Crane Spring, but the late hour prevented us from visiting.

Other peaks, cliffs, forests, and ravines—secluded and wondrous places—held many fine sights, but I cannot record them all. On the *xinhai* day, we exited the city’s western gate to pay respects at the Jade Maiden Hall, once the Song-era Celestial Writ Observatory. We then proceeded to Gaoli Mountain to explore the Ghost Immortal Cave, where we had a Daoist priest light a torch to guide us. The yin qi was oppressive, and we dared not venture deep.

At the time, Tinggui had business in Dongyong, I in Qing and Lai, and Yingchang was to inspect troops in another prefecture. Only Shiyong remained to govern Tai’an. He spread mats, arranged music, and hosted a farewell banquet for the three of us. We drank joyously through the night before parting.

Touring scenic sites is a joy of past and present; Mount Tai is a marvel of the world. That four of us traveled together—provincial and judicial officials in harmonious virtue—demands this record. Such fine places are not eternal, nor are grand feasts easily repeated. Those who come again hereafter may well sigh with longing for these bygone days.

This stele stands at Steep Cliff Pavilion. In the ninth year of the Jiajing reign, Tanglingzi inscribed his name beside it: Fang Hao.

**Reflections After Returning from Mount Tai’s Summit**
Mountains and rivers remain unchanged, but where are the heroic spirits of old? How joyous, how sorrowful! Fifteen years later, a friend of Fang’s saw this and found it as flawed as Fang himself once was. Alas, Shang!

**Ye Fen, Surveillance Commissioner and Education Intendant, Inscribed "Gazing at the Sea" on the Stele**
In the Tang, Yan and Liu were renowned for their calligraphy, still praised today as "Yan’s sinew and Liu’s bone." Yet Yan’s loyalty and Liu’s remonstrances illuminated the cosmos—was their fame merely for their script? Master Zhang of Heartfast Hall, once a censor guarding the passes, petitioned Emperor Wu to return from his northern expedition, his demeanor resolute, a peer of the sages of old. He devoted himself to the study of characters, excelling in large scripts. When he dipped his brush and spread the paper, his qi settled and his spirit stilled, his hand responding to his heart. His writing was solemn and forceful, transcending the sinew and bone of Yan and Liu. Yet the world, while honoring his noble integrity and great deeds, dismissed his calligraphy as a lesser art. He himself regarded it as a minor accomplishment.

Now, his two grand characters "Gazing at the Sea," carved into Mount Tai’s cliffs, display the mastery I described. Ah! Calligraphy is the heart’s script. Master Zhang’s vast inner cultivation and unrestrained expression adapted to all circumstances, fulfilling every intent. That Mount Tai and the Eastern Sea vie in grandeur is fitting—but does their towering majesty not make them ill-suited for mere water?

*(The following is a marginal note:)*
While Master Heartfast’s character and integrity are rightly praised, to claim his calligraphy surpasses Yan and Liu’s is mistaken. Connoisseurs will recognize this.

**Yao Kui, Judicial Commissioner, Record of Touring the Stone Chamber**
A few *li* east of Mount Tai lies the Stone Chamber, said to be where the Azure Cloud Primordial Sovereign refined her truth. During my official leisure, I wished to visit it. My attendants warned, "The path is too narrow for a sedan." So I changed into a cloth cap and sable robe, tied my waist with a leather belt, took a short staff and straw sandals, and had the Daoist priest Liu Jiaohong lead the way. After two or three *li*, the trail vanished. Pushing through dense undergrowth, clambering over jagged rocks, we ascended by grasping vines. The higher we climbed, the more perilous it became. Below yawned sheer cliffs, and my back drenched in cold sweat.

After another three *li*, winding around a ridge, the Stone Chamber came into view. Four feather-clad Daoists, gaunt and solemn, approached me by a sheer ravine of verdant pines, guiding me forward and supporting me from behind—only then did I cross safely. Eyes wide, I gazed in all directions: above towered a thousand iron-hewn peaks, sharp as blades; before me lay a level clearing of half a *mu*. Entering the chamber, I found it profound in depth and luminous in emptiness. A crystalline stalactite, ten arm-spans in girth and over a *zhang* in height, gleamed like polished jade, its dripstone forming a translucent pillar. On the cliff face remained the handprint of the Primordial Sovereign, her jade-like fingers still discernible. Behind stood a stone bed, where she once reposed. To the left, a crevice pierced the summit; atop it sat a stone pavilion, large enough for three or four, its surface slick as ice—unclimbable. After ascending a few steps, I descended, my strength spent. Outside, another stone bed sent shivers through flesh and bone. Beside it lay the Sacred Water Pool, its waters clear, sweet, and ever-full, neither overflowing nor drying. Below stood the Stone Sun and Ox Heart, most wondrous to behold. Further down lay Crane-Washing Bay, its depths unfathomable; a crane cried above, its call echoing human voices in harmony. Truly a realm of immortals, utterly removed from the mortal world.

Those who visit Mount Tai often exhaust their journey at the roadside pavilion, rarely venturing here—who would know the Stone Chamber holds such marvels? For me, alas, the discovery came late. Yet sigh! Among the world’s heroic and talented, how many pass their lives without such an encounter? And if one meets it late, what harm is there?

*(The following passage, from "the roadside pavilion" onward, is adapted from Shu’s* Record of the Stone Chamber.*)*

**Li Rong, Instructor of Tai’an Prefecture, and Assistant Magistrate Yao’s Comprehensive Record of the Tai Shan Inspection**
Atop the summit of Mount Tai stands a divine shrine, the Temple of the Heavenly Immortal Jade Maiden, the Azure Cloud Primordial Sovereign. As the sacred peak of Dai nurtures spirits, its numinous power ascends to the celestial realm—such is the principle. From near and far, those bearing incense and seeking blessings from the deity throng the paths, their shoulders rubbing, wheels clattering, numbering tens of thousands each year. Upon arrival, they prostrate themselves in jubilation, vying to offer wealth, their gifts often totaling tens of thousands. The provincial and prefectural officials, from their salaries and stipends, allocate funds for all expenditures, sometimes drawn from here, sparing the people from levies. In times of hardship, this support brings relief, benefiting both state and populace—how could such a sacred trust be entrusted to the unworthy?

In the *yiyou* year of the Jiajing era, the governor and provincial administration selected honest and capable officials from the prefectures and counties to oversee these duties. At that time, Assistant Magistrate Yao of Shouchang, styled Xueyu, was chosen for the post of Chief Inspector. He declared: "Profit and power are the concerns of vulgar officials. For men like us, they should be as loathsome as crane droppings—how could we allow even a thread of corruption to stain us?" He assembled all functionaries, exhorting them with solemn oaths to devote their utmost care to meticulous management, ensuring that no schemer could exploit the slightest gap. From the first month of winter to the tenth day of the third month the following year, the total receipts of gold, silver, coin, and livestock amounted to approximately thirty thousand *guan*, not including pearls, jades, robes, and silks—an increase of one-third over previous years. These were registered and submitted to the provincial administration for annual use.

Magistrate Yao governed with integrity and leniency, his laws strict yet not harsh. He ruled his subordinates with severity but valued simplicity, treating scholars with respect and affection. During his six months as Chief Inspector, he took nothing for himself, relying solely on his family’s resources. From his Shouchang salary, he frequently drew funds to aid impoverished scholars, while his official travel stipend was exchanged for hay and beans to feed the government horses. Sacrificing his own interests for the benefit of others—ordinary men could not endure such hardship, yet he found it unburdensome.

Atop Mount Tai rises a precipice of over a hundred *zhang*, commonly called the Cliff of Self-Abandonment. Whenever a chaste widow, driven by fidelity, threw herself from it to her death, the vulgar hailed her act as heroic. Magistrate Yao strictly forbade and admonished against such deeds. The path to the Kuaihuo Ridge was level but narrow, with sheer cliffs below—many had lost their footing at night and perished. He donated his salary to erect wooden railings, saving countless lives thereafter. When the provincial and prefectural authorities entrusted him with legal cases, he adjudicated them swiftly and smoothly.

Near the city stood hundreds of inns for travelers. Previously, these had been subject to exactions. Magistrate Yao declared: "Should a snail moisten its own trail, only to be hunted like prey? I cannot bear it." The village elders, led by Zou Li, sought to engrave a stele to inspire future generations and commissioned me to record these deeds.

Master Yao was a native of Haicheng, named Kui, a man of vast learning and eloquent speech. In just a few months, he composed *An Account of Travels to Mount Tai*, spanning several thousand words. The eminent scholars of Tai’an—Li Wenhan, Ma Zaidao, and Gao Minyue—exchanged verses in response. Yet how could my humble and unrefined self do justice to their admiration? Though the music of *Shao* has faded, its lingering echoes remain; I write this in earnest to bequeath to them.

Record of Assistant Magistrate Gao Hui’s Journey to Mount Tai, the Fifth Year of Jiajing

Mount Tai stands north of the prefectural seat, towering over forty thousand *chi* in height and spanning nearly two hundred *li* in circumference. Its seventy-two named peaks rise in serried ranks, their verdant splendor too numerous to recount. Of old, it served as the Eastern Marchmount, and no mountain in the realm surpasses its grandeur.

In years past, I traveled to the capital for the spring examinations and took the route to pay homage, but the bitter cold and haste left me regretting that I had not fully beheld its wonders. In the winter of the *renwu* year of Jiajing, I was transferred from Qingcheng to Laizhou, and the Provincial Military Commission ordered me to oversee affairs at the Sacred Peak. On the sixth day of the third lunar month in the *guiwei* year, I set out by sedan chair from the northern city gate. Two *li* beyond a small bridge, the path was strewn with scattered boulders. Near the foot of the mountain stood a temple, a pond, and pavilions, all of imposing grandeur. To the left stood several ancient parasol trees, said to have been planted by Emperor Wu of Han during his eastern feng sacrifice.

After another two *li*, I reached the Red Gate, where the Flying Cloud Pavilion spanned the road, and beside it stood the Changing Robes Pavilion, its lintel inscribed with *Zongyue*. Along the streams grew medicinal herbs like *tianma* and *huangjing*, which locals gathered in baskets. Five *li* further on lay the Gao-Lao Bridge, where a shrine to the Marquis of Han Shou stood. Before the shrine, peach and plum blossoms were just beginning to bloom. A little ahead was the Water Curtain Cave, where a spring gushed from the Celestial Ribbon Cliff, cascading like a hanging silk, its sound clear and ringing. Below, a small stone bridge channeled the stream to the left, where the Yan-Yan Pavilion offered a commanding view of the scenery.

Four *li* onward stood the Horse Shed Cliff, and shortly beyond, the Turning Horse Ridge. The rocks grew steeper and more rugged, the forests deeper and more secluded. Along the cliffs and streams grew pepper, plum, apricot, and cherry trees, from which mountain dwellers profited. Another five *li* brought me to Yellow Ridge, where the path turned west before bending northeast, ascending and then descending, then west again before bending northeast once more—rising and falling in three undulations, each spanning a *li*. Weary travelers found respite here, and the place was thus named the Three Clouds of Joy. To the west, bamboo thickets flourished.

Five *li* further lay the Imperial Encampment, where Emperor Zhenzong of Song had once halted his retinue. Five towering pines, each several *zhang* high, stood there—Emperor Qin had sheltered beneath them from rain and named them the *Grand Masters*. A monk from the Bamboo Grove Monastery offered pine-flower cakes, fragrant and delicious, followed by a bowl of tea. When asked, he replied, “It is grown on the eastern cliffs, its flavor clear and slightly bitter.”

Another five *li* brought me to the Great and Small Dragon Mouths, where two sheer mountains faced each other, and water gushed from the stone gorge. Above stood ancient pines, their branches twisting like coiled dragons. Five *li* further lay the Eighteen Bends, where cliffs were adorned with wild crabapple, mountain pomegranate, and *mitao*, some in bloom, others bearing fruit. The stone steps clung precariously to the void, and looking up, the summit seemed unattainable—yet by leaning forward and clambering upward, one reached the high pass at the gorge’s end. The stone gate was inscribed *Southern Heavenly Gate*, and beside it stood the Temple of the Three Spirits.

Two *li* to the northeast lay the Temple of the Primordial Lady. To its left was a square stone pool called the Jade Maiden Spring, its sacred water sweet, cold, and crystal-clear. When drawn to brew tea, its flavor rivaled that of *Fuzhou*’s Dragon Spring. Further northeast stood the Temple of the Sacred Peak Inscription, behind which were two Tang-dynasty cliff inscriptions: one recording Mount Tai’s grandeur from the *Kaiyuan* era, the other a eulogy for the *Qianfeng* imperial audience. Nearby were many ancient poetic inscriptions, but moss had eroded them beyond legibility.

To the north lay the summit, where the Jade Emperor’s Temple stood. Before it lay a stone slab over a *zhang* long, known as the Qin Emperor’s Uninscribed Stele. Proceeding southeast, I reached Sunrise Peak, where, it is said, one can see the sun at cock’s crow. On the mountain was a stone coffer about a *zhang* square, said to have been unearthed by floodwaters during the *Chenghua* era, containing a royal tally that was presented to the throne and later restored to its original place.

To the west of this peak, a towering and precipitous outcrop was Qin Viewing Peak; slightly south, standing alone, was Yue Viewing Peak. Below the peaks, a cliff plunged ten thousand *ren*, known as the Cliff of Abandoning the Body. To its right lay the Heart-Testing Stone and the Immortal’s Bridge. Gazing in all directions, the vastness stretched boundless: to the east, the Yellow River cleaved the sky, while the Wen River coiled like a silver thread; to the southwest, myriad mountains lay prostrate, like scattered grains of rice—only Mount Zuolai and Confucius’s peak stood distinct, as if pointing to the spring.

The nearby peaks—some resembling elders, others crows in flight, lotus blossoms, the bright moon, or the Eastern and Western Divine Auroras—rose in tumultuous array, their forms vying for dominance. Those too humble to bear names of their own stood in attendance, bowing or clasping their hands in reverence, their demeanor solemn and obedient. Truly, it was a marvelous sight.

When I first arrived, the rocks were jagged and bare, the fierce wind howled, and the streams, bamboo, and pines seemed made for snow and moonlight. What stirred the senses most were the echoes of woodcutters’ axes, reverberating through the valleys. Later, as birds sang and flowers bloomed, spring filled every ravine. The springs were sweet to sip, the wild vegetables delicious to gather, and the mountain’s visage grew radiant—so too did the joy of those who beheld it.

As for the clouds that rose and vanished from the valleys, shifting between clear and overcast in the span of a conversation—this was the Creator’s play of expansion and contraction, its workings beyond human ken.

The mountain’s scenery is most wondrous in winter and most resplendent in spring, yet travelers often constrain themselves to brief visits, bound by the seasons. I, however, lingered here on official duty, ascending and descending at will. Thus, the mountain’s moods, the shifting vistas, and the ancient joys of wandering and gazing—all their fragrance I inhaled, and all their paths I trod.

This journey—could it not be called uniquely sublime? I record it here to mark the time.

*The Most Sublime Record of Ascending Mount Tai on the Ninth Day*
In the summer of the *yisi* year of the Jiajing reign (1545), I was stationed in Dezhou to oversee autumn defenses. On the first day of the ninth month, I learned that the Censor-in-Chief, Master Huang of Nanqu, having completed his southern inspection tour, was traveling north from Yi, passing through Yanzhou and Jizhou to reach Tai’an. I went to greet and attend him, but before his arrival, the Double Ninth Festival drew near. Magistrate Zhongyan Yong said: "Ascending heights on the ninth day is an ancient custom—let us revive it." I assented and told him: "The ninth day is the finest of heaven’s seasons, for even a single hill or mound suffices to mark the time. Mount Tai is the finest of earth’s peaks, and since antiquity, those who have scaled its summit—whether for imperial feng and shan sacrifices or otherwise—have all earned the right to count it among their grandest journeys. I hail from Min, yet on this very day I am granted the chance to ascend it—how could this not be a supreme blessing?"

Thereupon, we modified the mountain palanquin, and the magistrate joined us as we set forth. We explored every reach of the mountain, though all that has been said of it by ancients and moderns is already exhaustive. Yet on this day, the sky was bright and the air crisp, the golden autumn breeze brisk and refreshing. Seated atop the Jade Emperor’s Peak, we touched the weathered, inscription-less stele and beheld the crimson peaks arrayed like guardians, flanking the august chart of the Supreme Sovereign. The Yellow River bound the might of Qin and Zhou, while Kunlun offered a vista spanning China and the barbarian lands—all lay vast before us. As the sun hastened toward dusk and shadows deepened, the cries of a thousand spirits and the sighs of ten thousand winds arose. Yet leaning against the heights, I felt only a heart at ease and a spirit uplifted. Scenery and self merged, qi intermingled and flowed, as if in silent homage to the Great One, partaking in its mystery—how could this be dismissed as mere folly?

Descending to the guest lodgings, I returned at dawn the next day to climb Sunrise Peak. There, the eastern wilds stretched before me, the predawn darkness giving way to clarity. Black clouds surged upward, while golden clouds pressed downward, their swirling mists parting to reveal a fleck of crimson—like cinnabar, like leaping flame—piercing the dense fog to cast a radiant glow. A marvel indeed! Moments later, half the sun emerged from the clouds, the sky brightening as the light withdrew like a drawn bow.

On the descent, we passed the Water Curtain Grotto, where I heard that within lay stone scriptures inscribed by the hand of Wang Xizhi. Yet the mountain’s steepness and the valley’s depth made the path impassable. Setting down the palanquin, we climbed by grasping vines and descended with staff and sandals, only to find a massive boulder inscribed in bold, archaic script—solemn yet soaring in spirit, its characters gleaming after ten thousand years, as if in dialogue with the celestial patterns above, guarded by gods and spirits. Some of the summit stones had fractured and crumbled, others worn smooth by water’s erosion—did even the workings of creation harbor some aversion? The calligraphy, whether in standard, cursive, or small script, differed so starkly from the received tradition that some doubted its authenticity as Wang’s work.

Later, we visited the Bamboo Grove Chan Monastery to the west, but found little to observe. The valley leading to it, however, evoked the ambiance of the Orchid Pavilion in Shanyin: a spring cascaded down a hundred-zhang cliff like a trailing silk ribbon, which Master Zhai Tang had named "Waterfall." The abyss below, the White Dragon’s Pool, was the source from which water was drawn for rain prayers. I named it "Source of Abundant Rain." A slender stream meandered forth, fit for floating wine cups. Flanking the flow stood a solitary boulder, broad enough to sit upon, while mist and mountain light shimmered in the distance, inspiring verse. Beyond it lay another massive stone, smooth as white jade, though smaller than the Scripture Stone. I had heard of earlier inscriptions here but could not find them. As dusk approached, we departed with lingering regret, our hearts still unfulfilled.

Among my companions was the provincial graduate Liu Yizhu, who knew the location. The next day, he had a craftsman carry the stone tablet to me—it was a work from the Tianli era of the Yuan dynasty, left by a recluse of that time. Alas! As a southerner, I had come late to the capital’s wonders, yet in two nights I had savored the finest of heaven, earth, past, and present—was this not fate?

Magistrate Yong said: "Ascending Mount Tai on the Double Ninth—through all the records I have read, nothing compares. Should this not be recorded?" Thus, I wrote this account and composed a lyric to accompany it:

*O Mount Tai, so towering and steep,*
*On the ninth day, so pure and bright.*
*Scaling the summit, I stand alone,*
*Riding the celestial winds, awestruck.*
*Adorned with fragrant pendants, resplendent in hue,*
*I drink chrysanthemum wine till intoxicated.*
*Pursuing the traces of the ancients, now lost to sight,*
*I question the boundless void—profound, obscure.*

Hong Chaoxuan, Vice Minister and native of Tong’an, titled this *On the Pivot of Qian and the Cord of Kun*.

Someone asked me: "Why do you call Mount Tai the *pivot of Qian* and the *cord of Kun*?" I replied: "There is an explanation. The *Records of the Grand Historian*’s *Treatise on Feng and Shan* states that Qi is so named because it is *tianqi*—aligned with heaven—just as the navel is the center of the human body, from which the chest, abdomen, limbs, and all proportions are measured. The *Book of Han* says: 'The peace of the realm is like Mount Tai, secured by its four cords.' The commentator explains: *Cord* means *tie*; Mount Tai is singled out because it is the most stable, and adding cords only makes it more so. In modern parlance, Mount Tai is called the Eastern Marchmount, but this refers only to the lands within the seas. If we consider the Nine Provinces, China and the barbarians, the lands within and beyond the seas, then Mount Tai is the center. Today, it lies within the borders of Jinan Prefecture, and thus within Qi’s domain. Is this not the meaning of *pivot of Qian*? As for *cord of Kun*, it signifies stability."

Another asked: "To name Mount Tai as *pivot* and *cord* refers to the mountain itself, but can this be extended to the human heart?" I replied: "The center of the human heart is like the center of Mount Tai; the peace of the human heart is like the peace of Mount Tai. Yet the world fails to perceive this. Consider: benevolence is the heart of heaven and earth, the principle by which humans receive their nature at birth. Yet those who violate and destroy it go so far as to oppress others and harm all things."

The rites, righteousness, integrity, and shame—these are the four cords of humanity. Yet those who lose them go so far as to cast them aside entirely, chasing profit alone. This is what I deeply lament.

I observe those who ascend this mountain: their shoulders press together, their heels follow one after another. They come to avoid misfortune and seek blessings. Why not seek instead the Mount Tai within their own hearts? If one turns inward to seek it, then the means to preserve one’s benevolence and uphold the rites, righteousness, integrity, and shame will be found without stepping beyond the door—how could there be anything more secure? After I answered thus, the questioner had my words engraved upon stone to show those who come to climb the mountain.

**Record of My Visits to Mount Tai by Wang Shizhen, Vice Minister of Rites, of Taicang**

Between the years *wuwu* and *jiwei*, I made three journeys to Mount Tai, and of these, the second was somewhat worth recording.

On my first visit, it was the last day of the first month. After paying respects at the platform in Qingyuan and returning, I accompanied Elder Song Taiwu of the maritime route. We bathed at the official residence that night, rose at the third watch, and opened the northern door of the hall to gaze out. There appeared a length of silk being drawn from the foot of the mountain to its summit, or like fireflies gathered in a sack, their light flickering uncertainly. Upon inquiry, I learned this was the procession of men and women paying homage to the Yuanjun, their lanterns ascending in a line. The murmurs of their prayers could just be heard.

We entered the mountain at dawn, but the sky was overcast, clouds drifting in and out of sight. Beyond ten paces, nothing could be discerned—only the sensation of the sedan-bearer’s front foot rising higher than the palanquin’s front rail. Even at the summit, there was nothing to see, and the cold was severe. Song, eager to return, rested at Dengdu Palace. Several rounds of toasts were hurriedly raised before my skin ceased to shiver. I regretted the haste deeply.

On the first day of the sixth month, I set out with Imperial Censor Duan and others, who planned a three-day ascent. As the entourage was large and the sedan-chairs and horses could not bear the burden, I requested of Attendant Xu Wentong to precede Censor Duan by two days, and he agreed.

That evening, a heavy rain fell, but the next day it ceased. After traveling about two *li* from Tai’an, we entered the mountain. The wheat and millet had just ripened, their yellow and green hues interwoven like embroidery. A gentle breeze carried the scent of cakes and pastries. The dense shade of the treetops cast a soothing coolness, yet there were no elders to consult along the way. From then on, the path twisted and turned upward, the peaks looming as if about to bite one another. Beside the trail, streams and gullies rushed and tumbled, their waters murmuring mournfully, harmonizing with the sound of reed pipes.

After some time, we reached Huima Ridge, where we dismounted the sedan-chairs and switched to carrying bamboo litters. Four *li* farther, we arrived at Yuzhang Rock, also called Yuzhang—where the imperial retinue halted during the Song dynasty’s Yongding Emperor’s eastern sealing ritual. Before it lay a great ravine, its bed of white stone smooth and lustrous as jade. The cliff was steep; ascending it, we reached a temple. At its summit, the ground was fairly broad, shaded by fine trees, and the steps were charmingly adorned with moss.

After another three *li* or so, we reached Huangxian. No one knew the origin of its name. There stood two pines, the so-called "Five Great Men," their growth stunted by the rocks, their forms resembling coiled serpents. They were likely two or three hundred years old.

Before long, we came to the Hundred-Foot Cliff, its hollow depths like the interior of a house. Beside it was a stone cave, its mouth narrow, descending into impenetrable darkness. Having crossed the Stone Wall Valley, we reached the Eighteen Turns, where, as Ying Shao described, attendants supported us on either side, those ahead pulling, those behind seeing only the soles of the feet before them, and those ahead seeing only the crowns of the heads behind—like figures stacked in a painting. Yet now the path is better maintained, and there are even stairways for ascent.

They say one rests every five or six steps, panting and stumbling to the ground, heedless of dry or wet. If one steps where the ground is dry, the next moment one’s feet may sink into mud—yet to escape this is already fortunate. Three times we passed the Eighteen Turns before threading through the central tunnel called Heaven’s Gate.

Upon emerging, the wind blew fiercely, nearly knocking off my hat. Daoist priests in feathered robes, bearing instruments, came to greet us, appearing and disappearing amid the clouds—another marvelous sight.

After another *li* or so, we reached the Yuanjun Temple. No one knew the origins of the Yuanjun; some say she is the Jade Maiden of Mount Hua. Pilgrims from across the realm flock here to pray for blessings. The temple is grand and imposing, and the annual income from incense and offerings amounts to tens of thousands, used to supply the local officials rather than being allocated by the court.

To the right stood the lodging for the imperial censors. Behind it, a stone about three *chi* tall bore two lines of Li Si’s seal script. There was also a stone pool, roughly two *chi* in length, width, and depth, called the "Princess’s Hair-Washing Basin."

From there, turning left and ascending another *li* or so, we reached the Yue Emperor Temple, which was too crude to offer even a flicker of light. It was built against a sheer cliff.

To the left stood the *Inscription on Mount Tai* by Emperor Xuanzong of the Kaiyuan era, its Tang-dynasty clerical script nearly two *cun* in size, the strokes soaring as if in flight. Alas, the lower three *chi* had been scorched by the fires of winter travelers who lodged against the stele, leaving it incomplete. To the right was Su Xian’s *Eulogy on the Feng Sacrifice*, its characters elegant and graceful, still legible, though marred by the four large characters—*Filial Piety and Loyalty*—inscribed by the Fujian scholar Lin Huang. There was also an inscription by Yan Lugong, damaged by a poem by Fang Yuanhuan. Such defacement was far worse than the obscuring of moss and earth, which at least could be washed clean.

From there, ascending northward several hundred paces, we reached the summit, where stood the Jade Emperor Temple. Before the temple stood a square, yellow-hued stone pillar with fine grain, over a *zhang* in height—this was the so-called "Uninscribed Stele of the First Emperor of Qin." The stone was not native to the mountain; some say a stele had once protruded from its center. According to Li Yu’s record, the stone was buried in the earth, appearing roughly square but irregular in width on all sides. Upon close inspection, it bore no more than two lines, each with twelve characters, most indecipherable. Yet now it appeared otherwise. Still, Li’s account placed it a dozen paces east of the Kaiyuan inscription, so this could not be the same. I regretted not having once sought out the stele’s original caretaker, for its traces were now lost.

Turning eastward and descending a hundred paces, then ascending another hundred, we came to a stone chamber crowning the peak, as tall as the Jade Emperor Temple. Within lay a greenish stone, a *zhang* square, smooth and lustrous as a mirror—said to be the site where Emperor Wu of Han had buried his golden mud and jade seals. The legend that "white clouds rise from the sealed summit" referred to this place. The ground before it opened slightly, where the peaks known as Sunrise View, Qin View, and Yue View stood. Rising at the fifth watch to observe the sunrise made it the Sunrise View; gazing west to see Qin made it the Qin View; looking south to see Yue made it the Yue View. All other peaks pointed out by later visitors were mere fabrications.

The mist was thick, allowing no prolonged stay, and nothing could be seen. We descended in low spirits, as if it were spring. Just then, Master Xu arrived, and we called for wine, reciting poetry with great delight. We retired at the third watch, agreeing to rise at the fifth to watch the sunrise. Yet the bedding was so soft that we overslept, waking only at late morning. Disoriented and dissatisfied, I forced myself to rise with Master Xu, leaning on our staffs to retrace yesterday’s path.

The sky had just cleared, and the sun began to play with color. To the southeast, as far as the eye could see, a faint white shimmered like rippling water—was it the sea? Due north and west, a faint azure streak stretched like the Great Wall’s silhouette—perhaps the Taihang, Heng, or Song mountains. Farther off, the Zi, Mian, Ji, and Si rivers intertwined like sashes. The nearer peaks were mere mounds, save for Mount Culai, which stood somewhat taller, like a low table. After gazing for a long while, I remarked to Master Xu that the tales of the White Horse at Wumen were surely exaggerated, but even the claim that "all under heaven is small" was no lie.

Suddenly, each peak emitted a single wisp of white cloud, rising like smoke from a tomb. They swelled to the size of mats, hundreds of them, darting and merging upon meeting. Their ascent seemed endless, as did their wild chase and union. In an instant, they coalesced into a vast expanse of white jade. Looking down, the earth appeared thus; looking up, the sky gleamed with pure azure—a stark contrast. We called for wine and drank with Master Xu, marveling that this was the first such sight in our lives. Truly, this was the "wild horses of the steppe," as the ancients described.

Soon, word came that Master Duan had arrived, accompanied by Minister Zhang Xiju, Vice Commissioner Li Song, and Secretaries Wang Lin and Zhang Shifen. They set out wine at the temple hall and moved the feast beneath the wutong trees south of the Jade Emperor Temple. The record claimed Emperor Wu of Han had planted a thousand of these trees, the largest fifteen or sixteen spans in girth. Now, not even a tenth remained, and those were smaller—likely descendants of the originals.

After a brief pause in the drinking, we strolled to the Cliff of Abandoning the Body. The gap was about three *chi* wide, plunging into an abyss of mist thousands of *ren* deep. Minister Zhang recoiled, refusing to approach, while Secretary Wang, who had earlier spoken boldly of military affairs, also shrank back. Minister Zhang turned to him and said, "You clench your fists and speak of war—no one dares oppose you—yet you too hesitate?" I laughed and replied, "This is a matter of military strategy you gentlemen fail to grasp. There is no life in advancing, only in retreating—hence Secretary Wang’s hesitation." Secretary Wang burst into laughter, and we took our leave of Master Duan, agreeing to explore the mountain’s wonders the next day before descending.

At the fifth watch, a heavy rain began, continuing for days without cease. I had been sharing lodgings with Master Xu, but Minister Zhang and Secretary Wang’s quarters had collapsed from leaks, so they moved in with me. The water inside soon rose to nearly two *chi*, forcing us to lay out a long table and place our pillows and bedding atop it. The attendants, barefoot, served wine and roasted meat. We spoke of all things within the Six Directions, while Minister Zhang occasionally interjected with refined jests. Four days passed before the rain finally lessened.

That night, I was utterly exhausted, but Secretary Wang, like a cold cicada, recited poetry endlessly, often tugging my ear to share his latest lines. Unable to bear it, I forced myself up to look outside. The sky was as if freshly washed, and over a hundred stars, large as cups, hung clearly along the eaves—one could almost reach up and pluck them. At dawn, the rain returned in force. The prefectural supplies were exhausted, so we prepared to depart. After bidding farewell to Master Duan, we set off with the others. The cold was bitter; I wore five layers of silk and linen without removing any, and some even begged for a Taoist’s cotton robe. Descending the Heavenly Gate, the rain stopped, and the sun emerged. With each completion of the Eighteen Turns, we shed a layer of clothing. By the time we reached the Imperial Screen Cliff, we had stripped nearly bare.

Beside the cliff, waterfalls plunged over twenty *zhang*, their waves churning like snow, fierce as battling dragons spewing foam, jade scales scattering, pearls spraying. My spirits soared uncontrollably. I stood barefoot on the rocks amid the rushing streams, calling for wine and draining several large cups. I sang loudly, my voice echoing through the forest, and the others marveled. Some joined in, while others came to drink.

After some time, word came that Master Duan had arrived, and together we went to Dengdu Palace for a small farewell banquet.

The following year, on the first day of the fourth month, as I passed through Laiwu on an official tour, I learned that my father had matters to attend to at the frontier. Once those affairs were settled, I sought the numinous power of Mount Tai, intending to ascend it. Just before the fifth watch, I took my staff and climbed to the Peak of Sunrise Observation. Soon, the eastern sky began to pale, and when I turned, my companions’ and my own robes appeared a deep crimson—even the stone chambers and all the steles had taken on the same hue. Then, a single crimson thread rose from the dark eastern horizon. Yet when I looked back at the mountain’s far side, it remained shrouded in darkness, and the cocks had crowed only thrice.

Moments later, the sun’s disk rose slowly, its retinue of cloud lords and lightning marshals, golden banners and kingfisher plumes, vaguely in attendance. Then all the peaks and walled towns came into view, and the mountain’s spectacle became as the world’s. I have climbed it three times, yet only now have I exhausted its wonders—but only with my eyes and ears.

Its peaks are named for the Turning Crow, the Parrot’s Cage, the Lotus, the Bright Moon, the Old Man, the Solitary Beauty, the Eastern and Western Divine Empyreans. Its cliffs are called the Hundred-Fathom, the Horse Stable, the Wagtail. Its valleys bear the names of the Stone Scriptures, the Peach Blossoms, the Horse’s Hoof. Its rocks are known as the Ox Heart, the Dragon’s Mouth, the Sword-Testing, the Dragon’s Vein, the Tiger’s Mound. Its caverns are the Venerable Lü’s, the White Cloud, the Distant View. Its springs are the White Crane, the Water Curtain, the White Dragon. All these marvels, so often recounted in legend, I have yet to explore a second time.

Of the seventy-two sovereigns who performed the Feng and Shan sacrifices to declare their achievements, half lived in the age of knotted cords. The cultural splendor of heaven and earth flourished, yet even the most learned of later generations cannot recount it all—so I must briefly set it aside, for now.

Thus I departed. Seventeen years have passed since this journey began, yet what I have witnessed seems but a fleeting moment. Upon returning from Taihe, I undertook this account, and so I record it here. Later, I made two more visits, each yielding poems—seven-syllable regulated verse, ten in all.

Wang Shimei, Vice Minister of Rites from Taicang, wrote *Eastern Travels*. In the autumn of the dingchou year of the Wanli reign, during the intercalary eighth month, Wang Zichao, while in the capital, returned to his hometown via the old road. As he passed through his native village, he suddenly recalled the travels of his life that had long weighed on his mind—three in particular—and realized this journey could encompass them all at once.

Mount Tai, foremost of the Five Peaks, had been neglected: the previous year, he had ascended the summit of Mount Hua, yet for Tai, he had not even set foot upon its slopes. He feared the Azure Lord would reproach him. Having left officialdom nearly twenty years prior, he had never visited Queli, nor seen the state of the forests there—how could he reconcile this with the principle of filial duty? Moreover, his old friend Li Yulin had passed away eight years prior, and he had never personally offered wine to his spirit. As for Qiao Xuan, Cao Cao would surely clutch his belly in laughter at the omission. If he did not make this journey now, his illness might confine him to bed, and he would never again face north—he feared this regret would haunt him for life. Thus, he resolved: even in sickness, he must go.

Among his colleagues, some had already set out before him. By relay carriage, he reached Dezhou, where he inquired of the prefect, Lord Geng. The prefect said: "From here, enter Pingyuan, pass through Yucheng and Qihe, then turn south to Changqing—this is the road to Mount Tai. It lies slightly east of the old route, adding no more than a hundred li to the journey." Wang replied: "How can I burden the eastern lords with my baggage?" Lord Geng said: "The cost is the same, and it will spare you the detour to Su and Kong’s old path. The provincial administrators—Shu from Zhe and Chen from Ling—departed a day ahead of you." Delighted, Wang followed this advice.

Li Yulin’s home was in Lixia, and Qihe lay forty li from there. Wang wished to pay his respects at Yulin’s grave, but could not let the two officials know. He devised a plan: feigning frailty, he would travel alone in plain clothes. First, he sent a servant to inform Yulin’s son, Ju. Ju replied: "My father’s grave lies along the road to Changqing—no need for a detour." Overjoyed, Wang set out from Qihe, turning slightly east. He nearly lost his way when a messenger arrived, saying: "The grave is near. Rest at a villager’s home and wait for Ju." Ju arrived and led him to the site—a mere mound of earth, no larger than a foot, without walls or trees. Without Ju, it would have been unrecognizable. When asked, Ju said they planned to relocate the grave elsewhere.

Wang stood in silence, overcome. He prepared wine and offerings, bowed, burned the essay and poems he had written, and wept as he took his leave. By dusk, he reached Changqing and learned that Administrators Shu and Chen were still at Lingyan Temple. He sent a servant ahead to arrange a joint excursion. Soon after, Administrator Chen arrived with an invitation, and they hastened onward. That night, they lodged at Tushan Post Station. A fierce wind arose, and at dawn, they set out through the frost.

Administrator Shu’s messenger also arrived, and Wang rejoiced to learn the two officials would wait for him. Three li into the mountains, they reached the imperial road. A stone bridge spanned a ravine, grand and imposing. Gazing into the distance, the peaks stood in orderly ranks, a natural barrier. Monasteries emerged like floating islands from the verdant haze—truly a secluded realm under heaven.

Upon entering, they paid homage to the Great Being. Wang then joined Administrators Shu and Chen, reciting a poem to apologize for his delay. The two had already prepared a vegetarian meal. After dining, they readied sedan chairs, and two monks led the way. They visited every renowned site on the mountain.

At Lingyan, a stone hung downward, standing upright like a man—this was Langong Stone. A pool held still waters, sheltered by a pavilion. A trickle flowed from the Buddha’s foot, gathering into an outer pool before spilling down the mountainside, then vanishing underground—this was □□ Spring. In a separate courtyard called Damo, a bronze statue, gilded in gold, stood four feet tall and three feet wide. One side was smooth, the other carved with celestial patterns resembling rice paddies—this was the Iron Kasaya.

They ascended further, stopping at each site. Above, the summit seemed unreachable. Gazing upward, they saw a level plain on the peak, shaded by trees, with a temple emerging from the foliage. Beside it stood a hut, home to a monk and an attendant. For five years, they had carried water up and down. Wang surmised the monk was a practitioner of asceticism, and regretted not meeting him. After a long gaze, they descended.

Administrator Shu, unwell, rested at the Spring Pavilion while Wang and Administrator Chen descended leisurely. Returning to the temple, they admired the lush bamboo groves—unmatched anywhere east of the mountains. After a brief rest, they ventured out again. Before the lecture hall stood several Song-dynasty steles, which they read. Entering the kitchen, they saw another spring, brimming but not overflowing, surrounded by a gentle, murmuring stream. The monks said: "This is another source." Behind the kitchen lay the Two Crane Springs, as full as the kitchen spring but finer.

The mountain’s fame lay in its springs—some brimming, some flowing, some hidden, some visible, some intersecting, some severed. Even the monks could not name them all. The temple, one of the four great monasteries of the world, boasted magnificent halls. A three-story pavilion stood before it, and a seven-story pagoda rose to its right.

Wang and Administrator Chen ascended the pavilion’s summit. The pagoda was incomplete, rising only four levels. The surrounding mountains blocked the distant view. Descending, they turned south to Luban Cave. Above it, green cliffs overhung, and the monks had built two stone gates around the entrance. The interior was too narrow to enter—perhaps the burial site of the mountain’s founding monk.

Nearly all the mountain’s wonders had been seen, save the "Luminous Cavern." The monks said: "It lies two li beyond the mountain. A few steps to the side, and you can glimpse it from afar."

My elder brother Yuanmei once told me: "Lingyan is the most secluded and sublime spot on the northern face of Mount Tai. To visit Mount Tai without seeing Lingyan is no visit at all." I asked the mountain monks where my brother had inscribed his poems and found two steles, both in his own hand. Each bore a poem of twelve couplets—composed over two visits—from twenty years past.

That night, after supper, we retired to our chambers. I composed a regulated verse in the same meter as my brother’s. At dawn, I inscribed it on stone and entrusted it to the monks before departing. The next day, Administrator Shu, Administrator Chen, and I proceeded in single file. After the midday rest stop, we reached Tai’an Prefecture, where the prefect received us. When he inquired about our ascent, we replied that we would set out at dawn.

The Temple of the Eastern Peak’s deity stood within the city. We paid our respects there. The temple complex was vast, enclosed by walls. Three gates stood at the front, the central hall for offerings in the middle, and the rear hall for repose—an imperial residence in all but name. Towering steles and ancient wutong trees stood in solemn array, while strange rocks were scattered across the forecourt. The inscriptions, we noted, were all gifts from visitors who had crossed the sea during the Song and Yuan dynasties.

After bowing with my two companions, we surveyed the surroundings before descending. The prefect had already prepared a farewell banquet in a separate hall. Though the dishes were simple, the music and ceremony were refined. Pressed to stay, we took our seats, though I felt unworthy of such hospitality. After sitting for some time, I rose to change clothes and saw the summit of Mount Tai looming as if within arm’s reach—so near one could peer down upon it. All mountains, when viewed at night, appear thus, an experience neither of my companions had known. Hearing my words, they marveled. We dispersed after the second watch.

The next morning, we prepared for our journey. Administrator Shu, citing his medication, asked to follow later. Administrator Chen and I set out in sedan chairs, arriving first at the Changing Clothes Pavilion, where we dismissed our retinue and exchanged our chairs for lighter palanquins to ascend.

The day was clear, the wind still, and our spirits high. We saw the mountains embrace like arms, their ridges sharp as blades, while springs cascaded into ravines, their murmurs rising in misty veils. Gazing upward at the central peak, clouds swirled in competition, and only then did the true height of Mount Tai reveal itself. The porters, exhausted, descended to a terrace to rest. Below, the Wen River wound like a ribbon, and the surrounding peaks seemed mere mounds—save for Mount Zulai, which stood before us like a grand guest, refusing to yield.

Delighting in its majesty, Chen and I remounted our palanquins and continued upward, passing Gaolao Bridge. To our left and right, mounds rose, with slender streams trickling over them before spilling into the right-hand ravine—the famed Water Curtain. The rocks were low, the spring scant, and the sight unremarkable. Turning forward again, we saw the sheer cliff of Maple Shed looming to the right, a modest marvel. After ascending the steps for some time, the path suddenly leveled into a stretch of three *li* or so, which the locals called "Happy Three." I had once climbed Mount Hua, enduring every hardship, and though this mountain’s flat stretches were nothing by comparison, even its roughest paths were but a third as treacherous. In jest, I inscribed a quatrain:

*Once a guest on Mount Hua’s peaks,*
*Here too, "Happy Three" speaks.*
*No idle boast—truth it be.*

Further on lay the Return-Horse Ridge, where riding was no longer possible. After a long climb, we reached the Jade Emperor’s Pavilion. A pass stood before it, the mountain’s sole bottleneck. From the pavilion, we turned west and wound upward. The Second Heavenly Gate appeared, its twin cliffs rising sheer, their trees lush and tangled. Springs cascaded like strings of pearls, while massive boulders jutted defiantly, their waters tumbling in rippling streams through crevices. We sat among the rocks, playing with the springs, and looked up at the Imperial Tent Cliff—the most sublime spot. This was where Emperor Zhenzong of Song had encamped. Now a rest pavilion stood there, flanked by two pines, their roots barely clinging to the stone below. The monks claimed these were the Five Great Men of Qin, but the trees were no more than common shrubs on a barren mound, and I could not suppress a laugh.

After eating, we ascended further to see the Third Heavenly Gate, floating amid clouds and mist. Twin peaks stood like celestial gates, with two gorges to the east. The Eighteen Bends coiled like scales over ten thousand steps, and the porters panted, drenched in sweat, changing shifts repeatedly. Gazing up at the gorge’s mouth, it seemed to shift between near and far—a path fit for immortals. We alternated between riding and walking until we reached the Heavenly Gate, where the land suddenly flattened into a market of some thirty households. Beyond the dwellings stood the Palace of the Azure Clouds’ Primordial Lady. Before it lay a vast incense-burning ground, its fires unceasing day and night. Golden studs adorned vermilion doors, and tablets stood in solemn rows. This blessed land of heaven seemed no accident.

Chen and I rested briefly. He then donned his official robes, paid homage, burned offerings, and withdrew. According to Daoist scriptures, the Primordial Lady was the Jade Maiden of Mount Hua. I wondered if, amid her golden banners and kingfisher standards, she might recognize an old visitor to the Jade Well.

We waited for Administrator Shu, but he did not arrive. Taking Chen with me, we roamed the peaks. Cliffs and crags stood in ranks, their inscriptions too numerous to attend to. The grandest was the Cliff Inscription Stele, Emperor Xuanzong of Tang’s *liushu* script for the *Eastern Enfeoffment Ode*. Midway up the cliff was a stele by Su Xun, its traces still faintly discernible, though vulgar hands had since carved four large characters over it.

Further up, the northern summit rose. A small mound of surging stone formed its crest, sheltered by a hall and enclosed by a wall. At its front stood the high marker—the Wordless Stele of the First Emperor of Qin. The stele was broad and thick, its proportions even, topped by a canopy (though I suspect it was merely a marker for the summit’s height). The stone was smooth, white, and dense, unlike any native to the mountain. I could not fathom how such a mass had been transported—proof that moving mountains and stones was no idle tale.

After our survey, we turned south. The temples of the Sun, Moon, and Wu; the Cliff of Abandoning the Body and Stone Bridge; the ancient Enfeoffment Terrace; and the site where the jade tablets of Song’s eastern enfeoffment had emerged—we visited each in turn, lingering and pointing. Our delight complete, we gazed southward. The Wen River flowed far from Laiwu, blocked to the north by Mount Zulai, its tributaries converging in a serpentine course, vanishing westward without end. Grass-covered peaks crouched and arrayed themselves like courtiers, while a few wisps of blue smoke marked scattered hamlets.

Where our gaze reached, it passed beyond the southern bounds of Mount Zulai—here, the mountain stood as but a single peak guarding the gateway, no longer assuming the deferential posture of a guest. Turning west, the sunlight dimmed, veiled at times by drifting clouds and haze. Distant waters glinted like scattered stars, and only after prolonged scrutiny did we discern they were indeed waters. At the farthest edge of the sky, a radiant orb glowed steadily, poised as if about to descend—not the illusory vision we had seen before. The crowd murmured in astonishment; it was a star, for Mercury had set before the sun, a sight rarely witnessed.

As the ascent grew steeper, Master Chen and I found a rock to sit upon. To the north of the mountain, a solitary peak rose abruptly amid ten thousand ravines, its slopes cradling a verdant monastery half-hidden in embrace. Surrounding huts suggested a secluded retreat worth exploring. We inquired, and a Daoist adept replied: "It is but a refuge for mud monks, nothing extraordinary." Thus, we let the matter rest.

At that hour, not a breath of wind stirred, the warmth as gentle as spring. When Master Shu failed to arrive as promised, we retired to a lodge on the peak’s flank. The sun had already dipped low; hastily, I called Master Chen to the parapet to watch. A luminous half-disk sank into Yu’s Abyss, no different from any other sunset—save that, once fully set, it re-emerged like a platter of agate, veiled in gossamer mist. Truly, it seemed to rise and fall from the water’s depths, a rare and wondrous spectacle.

Just then, Master Shu completed his rites to the Primordial Lady. He and Master Chen arrived in a palanquin, its curtains swaying gently. Upon dismounting, Master Chen took charge as host. We sat, but a chill wind arose, and suddenly our hands and feet felt encased in ice. We rose at once to change into warmer robes, then called for wine to ward off the cold. Later, we recounted the day’s marvels to Master Shu, who smiled and said: "I yield to you both the title of this mountain’s greatest wonder—I have already claimed the first." When pressed, he revealed it was the broken stele of Li Si, an artifact kept in his residence. We clapped in delight. After the wine was finished, we dispersed, agreeing to rise at cock’s crow to ascend the Sun-Viewing Peak and watch the dawn.

Before dawn broke, I arrived ahead of the two masters, only to find screens, wine, and fruit already prepared. Soon after, the two arrived, their faces barely discernible in the gloom. Though sheltered indoors, a biting wind pierced our backs; we hastily shielded ourselves with screens. Master Chen, wrapped in a blanket, still shivered uncontrollably. We called for large cups of wine, drinking deeply to steady ourselves. Gradually, the sky brightened enough to distinguish shapes. A great crimson orb emerged half-veiled in the sea of clouds—no different from the sunset’s reappearance—then vanished again. After a long while, it rose once more, only to be obscured by dark clouds, yielding no further marvels.

I returned to the lodge to record our journey’s names on paper, intending to carve them into stone. But my wrists were so stiff with cold that I could not even withdraw my hands from my sleeves; only with the aid of fire did I manage to write. The high-altitude chill was unlike anything below. Once the record was complete, I went to Master Shu’s quarters to view Li Si’s small-seal script. The right side of the stone casket remained embedded in the cliff, with several lines still legible—a treasure preserved by heaven’s protection. Nearby lay the Jade Maiden Pool, marked by a stele, another scenic spot.

Master Shu proposed continuing yesterday’s excursion. The two of us followed as he led us through various sights, then returned to the lodge to prepare a meal. I acted as host, serving a simple vegetarian fare, for the two masters were occupied with ritual offerings. After the meal, they went to burn incense. With nothing to detain me, I decided to descend at once and proceed to Qufu.

Suddenly, the sky filled with mist, thick as cotton against our faces. As we descended to the Imperial Tent Cliff, Master Shu twice boasted of his strength in pulling us along—for he was the steward of the Stone Sutra Gorge. Reluctantly, I halted at the gorge to wait. Less than half a *li* from the road, the stone could seat hundreds. Its surface bore clerical script carvings of Buddhist sutras, popularly attributed to Wang Xizhi, though this was false. The calligraphy was not Tang in style; it was surely the work of an Song-dynasty hand. Above the stone, the cliff rose some three or four *chi*, where a spring cascaded straight down. Vice Minister Wan had inscribed the characters "Water Curtain" in bold strokes, the carving over an inch deep. The water flowed into the grooves, creating a striking effect. Wan had also built a stone pavilion beside the spring. To its left, a smoothed cliff face towered two *zhang* high, where he had carved an inscription—a fitting tribute to this peerless site.

I sat in the pavilion, drinking alone by the stream. After some time, the two masters arrived. Music was played in a small chamber, and as dusk fell, we lingered until the first watch before descending. Lanterns lined the path, guiding us in a winding procession into the city. The night watch had already marked two *ke* when we arrived.

Concerned that our provisions might burden the local populace, I dismissed all unnecessary attendants, planning to depart for Qufu at first light. Master Chen wished to visit Ningyang for an engagement and asked the two of us to wait for him in Qufu. The next morning, we set out, traveling south from Mount Zulai for less than a hundred *li* before Qufu’s outriders gathered. We crossed the Wen and Si Rivers as dusk fell, and the magistrate came to welcome us. He was a hereditary official of the Kong clan.

Entering the city, I saw its streets quiet and wells deserted, yet the provisions for our reception remained unabated—a source of deep embarrassment. At dawn, Master Shu and I donned ceremonial robes and prostrated ourselves before the ancestral temple of Confucius. Its grandeur surpassed all others in the world. Gazing upon the revered images, we extolled the Master’s virtues, lingering in the shadowed hall to bask in his lingering influence. Below, the courtyard trees were all of such girth that they could only be embraced by several men, their branches reaching toward the clouds. The so-called "hand-planted cypress," however, was no thicker than an embrace, its trunk withered and bare. Its grain spiraled leftward, its color and texture ancient. Reading its stele, I learned it had twice revived—even in later ages, its vitality endured. I marveled, speechless. I do not speak of wonders lightly, yet how could such transformations not belong to the realm of the Daoist and Buddhist traditions?

Han-dynasty steles stood in rows outside the ceremonial gate, their surfaces remarkably free of erosion. Their forms were archaic and strange, rivaling only the Stone Drums of the Imperial Academy as the finest examples I had seen in my travels. After paying our respects, we withdrew with reverent steps, walking a hundred paces to the Temple of Yan Hui. Its scale was smaller than the Confucian temple, yet every detail was meticulously arranged. We bowed solemnly and departed.

To the right of the gate stood a deep well, over which a pavilion had been built. The surrounding neighborhood was named "Humble Lane," a designation with some basis, though its origins could no longer be traced.

Passing the gate of the Duke Yansheng, we found the Duke still away and not yet risen. Thus, I accompanied Master Shu out of the northern city to pay respects at the Kong Forest. The forest lay five *li* from the city, its approach a straight thoroughfare like a drawn bowstring, flanked on both sides by towering cypresses—truly magnificent. At the forest’s eastern and western ends stood two gatehouses. No sooner had we entered than we heard the mournful notes of a *suona*, and a post rider announced the arrival of Minister Chen. Master Shu and I drew near the gate, then entered the burial grounds together to await Minister Chen’s change of attire. We bowed at the pavilion and proceeded to the offering hall, which stood neglected and overgrown, its disrepair unaddressed. I remarked that this was the Duke Yansheng’s responsibility—why wait for the imperial court? Behind the hall, we came upon the scholar tree planted by Zigong, twice the size of the others, its trunk withered yet unrotted, a tree of ancient dignity. Turning slightly north and then west, we reached the tomb of Zisi; further north lay that of Boyu. Veering west again, the northern courtyard gradually widened, revealing the tomb of the Master himself. Beside it stood a hut bearing a plaque: “Site of Zigong’s Vigil.” The Master’s tomb rose high and broad. After bowing, I ascended its summit, where grasses and trees grew thickly, many beyond recognition. We then paid respects at the two other tombs and surveyed the enclosure, finding it free of brambles—this, indeed, was extraordinary. The land stretched flat and open, without great hills or lofty mountains, yet distant peaks clustered like scattered stars, with Mount Fengshan arrayed to the south like an emerald screen serving as its desk. I could not name the full measure of its concentrated beauty.

Exiting the forest, we gazed southeast to see a hall with turquoise tiles, where the Duke of Zhou was enshrined. We went to pay our respects, learning that this site marked the ruins of the Lu Lingguang Hall. Thus did I realize that the present city could not compare to even half of ancient Lu. Leaving the Zhou temple, we reentered the city from the east and rested at the official residence. Soon after, the Duke Yansheng arrived, describing his ailments. Master Shu and I returned his bows and offered brief words of praise. The Duke spoke graciously, then informed us of his journey to Zou before taking his leave.

That night, we reached Zouming. Passing by Mount Fengshan, my thoughts lingered, though weariness from travel weighed upon me. Even so, I could not put it from my mind. Upon arriving at Lincheng, I chanced upon Fan Dache, the Chief of Protocol, and recounted my journey’s highlights. Fan asked, “Have you climbed Mount Fengshan?” I replied, “Not yet.” He sighed and said, “How could you forgo such a marvel? Its rocks are intricately hollowed and exquisitely delicate—only the ascent is somewhat arduous.” Now I regret it deeply, a regret that cannot be undone.

After returning home for some time, I prepared to take up my brush and record these travels. Just then, my old friend from Jiaozhou, Master Lü, visited and spoke of Mount Tai, saying, “It is a pity you did not explore the northern slopes. The rocks there are extraordinarily magnificent, far surpassing those on the southern side.” When I asked the location, it was precisely the Buddhist temple I had pointed out earlier. Thus did I sigh over this journey: though I had secured an official carriage, seized a respite from official duties, and, despite illness, fulfilled my lifelong wish to pay homage, still there remained such regrets. I record them here to inform future travelers.

**Vice Commissioner Liu Zongdai of Licheng’s Account:**
