In the summer of the *jiyou* year of the Jiajing reign (1549), I first climbed Mount Tai and saw the Five Grand Masters Pines, which the First Emperor of Qin had ennobled, standing tall and proud. A little over a *li* further, I came upon a pine sprawling like an umbrella, called the Scholar Pine. It had only recently been named by Jiang, who inscribed it thus because it had not been honored by the Qin. Alas! From the Qin to the present, how many thousands of years have passed? People know only of the Grand Masters Pines, not the Scholar Pine—do all things have their appointed times of prominence and obscurity?

By the spring of the *wuchen* year of the Longqing reign (1568), I climbed Mount Tai again. How brief the span of years! Of the Five Grand Masters Pines I had seen before, only two remained, while the Scholar Pine stood as before, unharmed. Do all things have their destined cycles of flourishing and decay? Or is it that those who preserve their simplicity meet good fortune, while those who seek favor often fail—do things bring their fates upon themselves? Now, Magistrate Zhai of Anyang has renamed the Scholar Pine the Independent Grand Master, as if it had once received Qin’s ennoblement. Alas! The survival or demise of the Five Grand Masters Pines is of little consequence, but I lament that the Scholar Pine should suffer such misfortune in its naming. Ah!

**Secretary Wu Tongchun of Gushi’s Record of Climbing Mount Tai:**
I had twice passed through the eastern provinces without ascending Mount Tai. In the tenth month of the *guiwei* year of the Wanli reign (1583), I received orders to take up a post in Taiyuan. My parents still lived in my hometown, and I wished to visit them. Calculating the distance, traveling east would shorten the journey by four hundred *li* compared to going west. As my home lay in the southeast of Henan, closer to the eastern provinces, I set out from the capital on the second day of the eleventh month, reaching Dezhou on the eighth. The eastern route was two hundred *li* shorter than the western one, and Mount Tai lay along the eastern path. I sighed, “Most of my fellow townsmen travel west from the capital. Now, by taking the longer route west and the shorter one east, I still save two hundred *li*—how could I not climb Mount Tai?”

My colleague Wu Zideshi, who was serving as an inspector in the eastern provinces, had an old friend, Li Youqing, then serving as prefect of Jinan. Upon reaching Dezhou, Zideshi sent a messenger to meet me in Pingyuan, and Youqing also dispatched an envoy. I was accompanied by Master Shen, a native of Cixi who tutored in my household. On the tenth, we lodged at Wandui. From Wandui to Tai’an was seventy *li*, and from there to the summit, another forty. Locals said, “From the front of the mountain, it is forty *li* to Taohua Valley and another forty to the summit. From the rear, the scenery is finer, but those who ascend from the front must descend the same way, so few travelers take that route.” I replied, “Even if the front were shorter, I would still take the rear.” On the eleventh, we rose at dawn and traveled twenty *li* to Diantai, then turned southeast toward Taohua. We ascended and descended more than ten slopes before reaching Taohua, where Prefect Jia of Tai’an had overseen the laborers’ arrival. After dining, four porters arrived with a mountain sedan, a chair-like litter without wheels, which rested on the ground when not in use.

From there, the path was flanked by mountains. Following the valley eastward, we turned northeast after several *li* and reached Jiangni Village. The mountain seamed the path to our left, while cliffs sheltered us on the right. The rocks of Leimai Terrace and Stone Canopy arched like bows or clasped hands, beckoning us forward. Only the Central Military Plain ran counter to our sedan’s path. The road beside the valley twisted and wound like a snake or a snail’s shell. At first, I could recall a turn or two, but soon, the route faded from memory.

After several more *li*, we sat at Iron Buddha Temple by Mo’er Rock to drink wine. A few rounds later, we reached Haiyan, where Shen tossed a stone into the water, its deep resonance lingering. Five *li* on, we arrived at Sixiang Ridge, then another ten-odd *li* to Master Gui’s Hermitage. Ten thousand pines merged with the clouds, the hermitage nestled halfway up the crags. As we walked and drank, we looked down upon Tiger’s Den and inscribed on the rock: *"Finest vista west of Mount Tai."*

From there, we ascended Wind Ridge, passing Cloud Terrace. The path to the hermitage grew ever steeper and more treacherous; the sedan could no longer proceed, so we continued on foot. After another ten *li*, we reached the summit—forty *li* in all, half of which I had walked. Before the summit stood the Temple of the Primordial Lady; to the right, the official residence. Prefect Jia awaited me at the gate. He had once been my subordinate; now, with dusk falling and snow beginning, we forwent paying respects to the Primordial Lady and instead observed Jade Maiden Pool behind the residence. Together with Prefect Jia, we traced the Qin-dynasty seal-script stele embedded in the rock, of which only thirty characters remained. Song-dynasty scholar Liu Da once claimed to have counted 220 characters; now, half the stone was missing. Upon close inspection, faint traces of characters could still be discerned along the right edge. Liu had noted that the stele was entirely inscribed and that its sides varied in width—this proved true.

When the wine was finished, we stepped out to stand at the cliff’s edge. All four directions merged into a single vast expanse, white clouds like floss swirling about our beards and brows, clinging to our sleeves. From every side, pilgrims bearing incense and paper money raised a clamor like thunder, shaking the cliffs and valleys. Lamps hung from the inns and temples above and below, markets bustling as if for the Lantern Festival. Taking leave of Prefect Jia, I retired to sleep, rising at the fifth watch to observe the sunrise. Prefect Jia had instructed his attendants to pay respects to the Primordial Lady at the fourth watch. At that hour, he presented sacrificial offerings, and several scholars arrived. I composed a text for the occasion, and after the rites, Shen and I turned west from the temple, then north to Sunrise Peak. Prefect Jia and the four scholars followed. Clouds billowed like a screen before us.

I asked, *"Shall we not see the sunrise?"*
Prefect Jia replied, *"Let us wait a while."*

Soon, the eastern sky paled, and the clouds in other directions thinned. I fixed my gaze eastward, but the light appeared in the southeast—where the sun would emerge from *xun*. Moments later, the clouds rolled upward like a mat unfurling, stretching into a band like the Great Wall. Beneath it, radiance flickered, its brilliance piercing the distance, mingling with faint mist as if viewed through sheer curtains. To the south and north, the sky blazed with rainbow-hued light. Suddenly, the peak’s summit wore a crown of molten purple, and the people below seemed clad in robes of the same hue.

As we sat to watch the sunset glow, the sun had already risen from the sea. I asked a Daoist where the sea lay, and he pointed to a shimmering thread of light below. I looked—it seemed no more than a flash of lightning, then vanished. At first, the sun appeared golden, gradually whitening as dawn advanced. Initially as large as a wheel, it shrank as it rose. At first, the sea’s waters were visible in the distance; up close, they seemed no larger than a mat. Soon, all peaks emerged, while the sea’s surface darkened, its vastness beyond reckoning.

In the past, I had admired a painting of sunrise by a certain master, often unrolling it for contemplation. The scene today differed slightly—though the sky was clear and the air crisp, floating clouds obscured the view. Prefect Jia said, *"If we do not drink here, what of the sea’s fruitfulness?"* Together, we crouched upon Watching-the-Sea Stone, raising our cups in great drafts. Drunk, I said to Prefect Jia, *"The three divine mountains of the sea may not lie beyond what I have seen. If only some immortal would lift Mount Tai and place it above them, so I might sit at the sun’s gate, gazing upon its rising and setting, sparing me these wayward clouds before my eyes!"*

We descended to test Heart-Testing Stone, then turned to view the Song-dynasty Enfeoffment Terrace—a stone coffer, a *zhang* in size. Some said it was the Treasure Repository, housing golden texts and jade tablets. To the south, we descended to Abandoning-Life Crag, its sheer cliffs plunging ten thousand *ren*. As I leaned forward to look, Prefect Jia had several men grasp my robe in alarm.

I scoffed, *"No need—truly, I do not wish to abandon this!"*

Turning southeast, a stone arched upward, bearing the sea and sun’s wondrous spectacle. I inscribed it in large characters. Further down lay East Heavenly Gate. Turning right, we climbed Immortal’s Bridge—two stones standing apart, suspended over the sheer cliff. Ascending west, we reached Flat-Top Peak, where Prefect Jia had prepared a feast, with singing girls to accompany our cups. Eager to see the other peaks, I grew impatient with Prefect Jia’s insistence on finishing the opera. After three toasts and as many departures, I finally took leave, setting off alone toward the peaks. Prefect Jia sent the singing girls to follow.

From Qin’s Viewing Peak, we ascended to the Jade Emperor’s Temple—the very summit of Mount Tai. Before it stood Qin’s Wordless Stele, several *zhang* tall, its surface a mix of yellow, white, red, and purple. Some said it was Qin’s stone marker. From the summit, gazing south, the pilgrim’s path descended in a long ribbon to the mountain’s base. The Wen River coiled like silk around the prefecture’s south, while Liangfu, Meng, Feng, Yun, Yiyi, and Niqiu appeared as mere mounds. Only Zulai, An, and Niu were obscured by the mountain’s foothills, their peaks hidden. To the east, the great ridge coiled like a dragon, following the old Great Wall’s trace to the sea’s west, where Lingyan and Bie Mountains stretched toward the Taihang range. Huabuzhu, Chang, and Bai ridges trailed across Licheng, encircling the north like a halo. Truly, this was the *"mysterious and secluded realm, the sky’s most transcendent boundary."*

Beside the summit stood Qin’s Enfeoffment Terrace. Formerly, the Jade Emperor’s Temple had crowned the peak, but Wan of Nanchang relocated it to the north, leaving the summit’s stones bare. I inscribed beside them: *"Summit of the Primordial Peak."* From the summit, a *li* west, we climbed Elder Peak, then another *li* to Stone Horse Mountain. The horse reared three times, each time halting as if reluctant to advance. The Daoist said, *"Below, to the northeast, lies Yao’s Viewing Terrace, beneath which is Jade Maiden Cave—where the Jade Maiden once cultivated the Dao."* Too distant to visit, we turned left from the summit, observing Broad Life Hall and the Primordial Lady’s sleeping palace. Turning southwest, we ascended Confucius’ Cliff—where Confucius had declared the world small—a bronze statue enshrined in a cramped chamber. The temples of Mount Tai gleamed in splendor, while Confucius’ shrine was modest. I sighed in lament.

Passing the Eastern Peak Temple, we viewed the Tang-dynasty cliff inscription. The stele bore Emperor Xuanzong’s *ba fen* calligraphy, each character five *cun* tall, its brushwork vigorous and admirable.

Beneath it lay Peach Blossom Spring, its waters sweet and pure, unlike any other spring. Exiting the shrine and ascending the cliffs to the east, we found many ancient inscriptions, though more than half had been defaced—deeply regrettable. At the fourth watch, we worshipped the Primordial Lady, but before we could take in the view, we returned to the shrine, where the golden and azure hues dazzled, truly befitting a sacred abode. The Five-Flowered Cliff curved to the right in an embrace, known as the Shared Viewing Peak.

Taking leave of the deity, we descended southward, then turned west for several hundred paces to reach White Cloud Cave, which overlooked a sheer precipice. To the north of the cave rose Phoenix Mountain. Turning northwest for two *li*, we ascended the Overlooking Peak, which flanked the summit of Mount Tai, gazing left at the drifting white clouds. Below lay the Western Heavenly Gate. The peaks to the west stretched out in clear succession. We raised our cups several times, and voices joined in song, the sound piercing the clouds.

Descending south from the First Heavenly Gate, we traversed the thirty-six bends—known as the eighteen upward and eighteen downward turns. The path was precipitous, forcing us to proceed step by step, supported by two attendants. After five *li*, we reached the Great Dragon’s Maw, and after another five *li*, the Lesser Dragon’s Maw, where strange rocks encircled us, and pines and bamboos intermingled in verdant splendor. We rested briefly, then passed the Imperial Tent Platform and descended to Morning Sun Cave. Magistrate Jia’s men, escorting Zideyi, met us with torches at the Mid-Mountain Pavilion.

Three *li* farther lay the Second Heavenly Gate, where we saw the Grand Master Pines. Two pines stood by the path’s left side—one too thick to encircle, the other not quite. Some say these were the Five Grand Masters ennobled by the Qin, but who can confirm it? Below this, the place was commonly called "Happy Three," though some explained it as a mispronunciation of "Mountain," with "Happy Mountain" later carved into the rock. To me, this level stretch seemed aptly named, for three *li* below, the path grew rugged again, as the locals affirmed.

From Yellow Manifest Ridge, ten *li* brought us to the Turn-Back-for-Horses Slope, where to the north stood the Jade Emperor’s Temple, and ahead, the Ascending Immortals Pavilion. Another five *li* led to the Rest-for-Horses Shed, where cliffs plunged several *ren* high. Atop them, three inked strokes remained unblemished by wind or rain, deemed the work of immortals. Five *li* farther was the Water Curtain Cave, where water cascaded from the overhanging cliff like a hanging curtain. Fifty paces from the waterfall, we turned east for five *li* to reach Stone Sutra Valley, where snow lay deep. Having long heard of the valley’s wonders, I pressed on through the snow.

A level stone, half a *mu* in size, bore the engraved *Diamond Sutra*, each character roughly a *chi* in diameter. Brushing away the snow, I read haltingly, tracing the strokes. Here, jade-like streams flowed high, and mountains vied in marvelous beauty, forming a realm apart. Another ten *li* brought us from Old Gao’s Bridge to Red Gate, known as the First Heavenly Gate, where we finally exited the mountain gorge. Several *ke* had passed since sundown.

From the First Heavenly Gate to this point, towering pavilions and secluded halls bore grand ancient seal-script inscriptions. At first, I recalled a few, but soon they faded from memory. The roads wound tortuously, indescribably rugged. Descending forty *li* straight down, half the distance I had walked. Then, firelight flickered and voices grew loud as we neared the carriages—Magistrate Jia had ordered great torches to welcome us at the mountain’s base.

Turning right, we reached Mount Tai’s Shrine, where we sought the wutong trees planted by Emperor Wu of Han. Only a few remained, their dragon-scale bark nearly stripped away. Proceeding to the Queen Mother’s Pool, twin cliffs rose on either side, the sound of rushing water filling the air. Crossing a wooden bridge, we viewed Lord Lü’s Cave, then entered the Ritual Mount Shrine through the Enfeoffment Gate. The shrine was magnificent and grand, rivaling an imperial palace. Amidst the flickering torchlight, we glimpsed ancient and modern steles. At the third watch, we returned to our lodgings and retired.

The next day, we left the city to the south to view the Yin-Character Stele, then bid farewell to Magistrate Jia at the Three Peaks. Confucius said: *"Whenever I visited the various temples of Mount Tai, I could not help but sigh for a long while."* Ah! Wu was where I once served as an official; Yue was where I had traveled during my postings; and Qin I had toured the previous year, climbing the very summit of Mount Hua. As for Zhou, I had passed through it last year, sitting atop Mount Song to gaze at the old course of the Yellow River. In over thirty years, my footsteps had covered nearly half the realm, yet in three years I had ascended the Three Peaks—all in mid-winter. Such encounters are indeed extraordinary.

Zhong Yuchun of Songjiang, *An Account of a Pilgrimage to Mount Tai*

I, the unworthy one, was born in a thatched hut in the southeast, and as I grew, I sharpened my sword and journeyed to the capital, admiring Sima Qian’s bold travels. I longed to fulfill Xiang Ziping’s wish to visit the Five Peaks, but worldly affairs bent me like a chime stone, and I could not. In the *guiwei* year, I left the capital by boat, reaching Qingyuan, and resolved to begin my travels at Mount Tai. I sought the Dao, climbing straight to the mountain’s base.

The peach and willow trees bloomed beautifully, pheasants and wheat sprouts greeted the morning—all delightful to the traveler’s eye. After a hundred *li* or so, the mountains coiled like dragons, stretching across the horizon. The lofty Horse’s Head Peak, known as *"the green of Qi and Lu unending,"* seemed to drip with fresh verdure. Soon, distant brows of dark green emerged from the clouds and sky. Upon inquiry, I learned it was Mount Tai. My traveler’s spirit stirred ever more deeply.

Along the road, many children and sturdy women gathered tender shoots in baskets, saying they had no food and would mix them with wheat bran. Tasting it, I was moved with pity. The people’s strength was exhausted, and with the long drought, they despaired of the wheat harvest, praying fervently to the trees for rain. I mused: *"Great mountains send forth clouds that do not rise even an inch, yet in a single morning, they bring rain to the whole world. Why does the mountain’s spirit so harshly deny this single patch of cloud?"*

A few *li* from the prefecture stood a mountain like an overturned bowl, housing an ancient immortal’s cave, dark and unfathomable. Two boys arrived with torches, and following the creaking stairs, we descended several paces to a slightly more spacious area where we could stand upright. Turning, the path grew even narrower, forcing us to crawl like snakes. Where our feet touched, a foul wind assailed our noses, and we hastily retreated by the original route.

We reached the prefectural seat for the midday meal, then left by the north city gate. Crossing a small bridge after two *li*, we found the road lined on both sides with jagged rocks, the horses’ hooves stumbling. Near the base of the mountain stood a temple, pond, and lodge, all well-maintained. The ancient wutong trees cast deep shade—said to have been planted by Emperor Wu of Han during his eastern enfeoffment tour. The scene had a rustic charm.

After another two *li*, we arrived at Red Gate, where the Flying Cloud Pavilion spanned the road. From its side, we reached the Changing Garments Pavilion and switched to a bamboo sedan chair. Winding for five *li*, we came to Gao Lao Bridge, where a shrine stood—dedicated to the Marquis of Han Shou. Peach blossoms were in full bloom, lush and radiant as brocade, making me wonder if this were the Peach Blossom Spring of Wu Ling. Who could say how much sesame had flowed from that cave’s mouth? Ahead lay the Water Curtain Cave, its cloudy springs said to originate from Heaven’s Girdle Crag. After rain, the cascading waters would spray like ten thousand *hu* of luminous pearls. But with the long drought, the spring’s pulse was faint, and I could not witness this marvel.

A small stone bridge carried the stream across the ravine. To the left stood a cliff, where a single ramie plant offered a fine vantage. Another four *li* brought us to Horse Stable Cliff, and shortly beyond, to Turn-Back Horse Ridge. Here the mountains grew ever steeper and more wondrous, forcing the horses to retreat. Five *li* farther, we reached Yellow Gap Ridge, where the path coiled east and west, ascending and descending in three loops—though the terrain was fairly level, earning it the name "Three Joys." I laughed and said it should be called "Tortuous Valley" instead.

Another five *li* led to the Imperial Canopy, also known as the Imperial Staff—where Emperor Zhenzong once halted his procession. Two pines stood there, among the five ennobled as "Great Officers" in Qin times; these were two of them. Their branches spread wide, scales and manes weathered by wind and rain, as if poised to take flight. A century-old relic, perhaps—unless later hands had planted them, or they were but offshoots of the originals. I lingered to admire them and composed a poem, *Five Pines*:

*Qin’s ennobling knew no private grace,*
*The imperial canopy now marks an empty trace.*
*The emperor’s road lies desolate, far removed—*
*Year after year, wind and rain renew the offshoots.*

Turning onward, we came to Hundred-Fathom Cliff, its hollowed rock face like a stone chamber, spacious enough for dozens of wicker chairs. Vines and creepers draped it like tattered robes hung to dry. Nearby gaped a cavern, its depth and breadth rivaling the ancient immortal’s grotto—another marvel.

Five *li* more brought us to the Great and Small Twin Dragon Valleys, where stone altars shaped like dragons stood. Striking their mouths, water gushed forth. An ancient pine pavilion rose nearby, its form like a canopy chariot, named the Scholar’s Pine—perhaps some enthusiast’s whim, as if evading the Qin. I mused: this lord, with his hoary face and silver hair, weathered and proud amid the ice and crags—what care had he for the Qin’s disgrace? Even the title "scholar" meant nothing to such a one.

We ascended to the Sunrise Cave, climbing the Eighteen Turns, where—as Ying Shao once said—those behind saw the heels of those ahead, and those ahead saw the crowns of those behind. At the Three Heavenly Gates, another eighteen turns led to the Palace of the Azure Mist Primordial Sovereign. By now, flickering lanterns glowed, and the Northern Dipper hung level above. We lodged in the public quarters. That night, wind shook the cliffs and valleys, roaring like ocean waves or the Milky Way pouring down. I wondered if I had boarded Zhang Qian’s raft to ask the way of the Oxherd and Weaver Girl, or if I soared like Liezi riding the wind.

At the third crow of the cock, I rose, dressed, and went to Sunrise Viewing Platform to watch the dawn. But clouds and mist veiled the scene, the great sea stretching boundless. I could not, like Han Yu at Mount Heng, part the clouds to glimpse the hundred-yard surge of flying radiance as the golden wheel bathed in Fusang’s waters. The next morning, I saw an inscription: "Eastward, observe the sunrise; westward, behold the Yellow River’s source"—and my spirit soared.

Then we descended to pay homage to the Azure Mist Sovereign. Pilgrims seeking blessings numbered in the thousands. A local guide received incense offerings, amounting to tens of thousands yearly, all supplied by the county magistrate.

Carrying wine in a coarse cloth bundle, I took a small drink before departing. To the right of the palace lay Jade Maiden Spring, crowned with a pavilion. Legend holds it was the basin where the Jade Maiden washed her hair—its waters clear and icy, seeping into one’s bones. I composed a verse on the spot:

*How cold and pure this hollow in primordial chaos,*
*Breathing its qi to commune with immortals.*
*Where now the golden stem that wearied the heavens?*
*Here, at last, I leave the dusty world behind.*

To the right stood a stone, some three feet tall, bearing Li Si’s broken stele—an artifact from before the Qin. Turning northeast, I reached the Peak God’s Shrine. Behind it, a cliffside inscription recorded Emperor Xuanzong’s *Inscription on Mount Tai*, its grandeur striking. The Tang clerical script, though weathered and incomplete, retained a soaring, dynamic force. Nearby, Su Dongpo’s *Eulogy on the Eastern Sacrifice* rivaled the imperial text in sanctity, while the preface bore the signature of Yan Lugong. Alas, all had been defaced by vulgar hands of recent times.

Ascending several hundred paces north, I found the ancient *Feng* Altar, where white clouds were said to rise from its center. Further up stood the Jade Emperor’s Palace, perched at Mount Tai’s summit. Here, azure lotus clouds brushed the heavens, and breath itself touched the imperial throne—only this place could bear such majesty. Truly, it inspired one to soar, to push open the gates of heaven and cradle the sun and moon. Before the palace lay a stone like a jade tablet: the Qin’s wordless stele. Some say a stele lies within, sealed in stone—likely so.

Turning east and descending a hundred paces, I saw a stone slab a *zhang* square. It was said that during the Chenghua era, a jade tally was found here and presented to the emperor, who later ordered it returned to its original place. Reflecting on the seventy-two sovereigns—thousands of chariots and riders thronging the skies—all now vanished into wild mist and weeds, indistinguishable as a bird’s fleeting shadow across the void. What endures, from ancient times to now? I inscribed the wordless stele:

*This relic stands a thousand feet high,*
*Blank beneath the vast white of Qin clouds.*
*The mighty winds of thirty-six peaks—*
*Both dynasties reduced to dust.*
*How could they match this single stone atop the peak?*

Chanting the lines for some time, I turned west to Qin’s Viewing Peak, towering and solitary. Nearby stood Zhou’s View, Wu’s View, and Yue’s View, each named for the lands they overlooked. One was Moon View, its name derived from the *geng* direction of the moon’s essence—apt indeed. Gazing toward the Wu region, I saw the Five Threads of Silk stretching below. Earlier, I had dismissed them as mere wild horses’ manes, but now I wondered: were these the celestial steeds of the Gate of Heaven?

Behind the palace rose the Elder Peak, its sheer cliffs evoking a thousand-*ren* wall. Below lay the Immortals’ Palace, where a stone likeness of Confucius stood. I bowed in reverence. Nearby, a cliff bore the inscription *Where Confucius Declared the World Small*—above, the azure heavens; below, the boundless expanse. Overwhelmed, I lost myself in the moment. Reciting Du Fu’s lines—*"Yin and yang cleave dawn and dusk," "Chest swept by layered clouds,"* and *"Birds vanish into flight"*—I found them even more sublime. I composed another verse:

*This lotus of jade pierces the sky,*
*Confucius at my side, a single glance.*
*Star envoys turn back, the Silver River near,*
*Poems inscribed on the white clouds’ seal.*

About to seek the Jade Maiden’s cultivation site, I suddenly saw several sedan chairs emerge through the clouds. Drawing closer, I recognized Qian Deqing, the filial scholar; Cai Youjun, the mountain recluse; and Feng Xianfu, the filial scholar. They hailed me with delight. Returning to the Jade Emperor’s Palace, we raised our cups in toasts. The three marveled at the wonders of my journey, then produced poems from their satchels—dozens in all. Xianfu had composed a piece on Mount Tai’s summit and, upon meeting me, demanded a long verse in response, as if our words were opposing armies. I let loose a song to match his, much like Xiang Yu battling at Julu.

Just as they urged me to compose *Mount Tai’s Loftiness*, I said: *"This is but our frog-in-the-well view. How do we know there are no soaring immortals who see the six directions as mustard seeds and Mount Tai as an anthill? Or that we are not the sea to their fleas? Such boasting is laughable."* We clapped our hands and let the matter rest.

Gazing northeast, I saw Yellow Flower Cave, where the Royal Maiden cultivated the Dao. The locals named it for the yellow blossoms that filled the valley. The winding path to the palace was faint, and the stone chambers within seemed not of this world—a true sanctuary for immortals. Xianfu and Youjun followed, eager to shake out their robes and explore, but the winding black paths were unsuited for sedan chairs. Deqing, with his weak stomach, was no master of rugged terrain, so the plan was abandoned.

Turning back, we reached *Gather-Body Cliff*. I edged forward to peer over the precipice, but two Daoist priests hastily pulled me back, fearing a gust of heavenly wind might send me plunging. Deqing cried out in alarm, and I scoffed: *"When Han Yu climbed Hua Peak, he wept and could not descend. What sort of man is this?"* Glancing at the stone inscription—*Love-Your-Body Rock*—I recalled the foolish commoners who had once thrown themselves from it in deluded sacrifice. Thus, I took pity and admonished them.

I said: *"To relinquish is not truly to relinquish, how much less to cling to the ephemeral? Neither relinquishment nor attachment—thus should one contemplate."* The two Daoist priests could not respond, and led us to the *Sincere Heart Stone*, where we gazed intently for a long while.

The *Immortal’s Bridge* stretched far beyond the peaks of Kuocang and Nanming, a stone beam spanning the void. Descending, we returned to our night’s lodging. After a modest drink, the three gentlemen proceeded ahead to *Stone Sutra Gorge*, where they sawed stone in wait for me. Upon arrival, I grasped the vines and made my way forward, spying several ancient plum trees—sparse shadows slanting sideways, strikingly like the place where Master Lin Hejing of West Lake once released his crane. Passing through the plum grove, we came upon a broad expanse of stone, spacious enough for hundreds, akin to the *Preaching Stone* of Tiger Hill but thrice its size. Once called *Sun-Sutra Stone*, it bore, at some unknown time, the *Diamond Sutra* carved in *bafen* script, each character as large as a peck measure. Later, some enthusiast added more sacred texts to enhance its splendor—truly a marvel to behold.

At the stone’s end stood a pavilion. Above it, and beside it, was a rock named *Listen-to-the-Spring Pillow*. To the left, the sound of rushing water cascaded straight from the rock’s belly, like jade being rinsed or silk being drawn out. Below, it divided into a pool, its still waters so clear one could see the reflection of brows and hair. Behind the pavilion rose a mountain, inscribed with *Lofty Mountain, Flowing Water*. Below it was a record, written in the bold strokes of Wanliangxi, its prose most admirable.

The three gentlemen raised their cups again, and without tally, they turned to me, asking where I had obtained two qin. They wished me to play one, that all the mountains might resound, summoning the mountain spirits and the seventy-two immortals to hear the music of heaven. I sang: *"Mountains and waters have their pure tones—why need strings or bamboo?"* The three gentlemen howled in delight. Xianfu composed a poem on the *Sun-Sutra Terrace*, and I, inspired, wrote one on *Stone Sutra Gorge*:

> *I come to feast on wonders rare,*
> *Where flowing water spills o’er stone.*
> *Washing my cap-strings, I see ancient script,*
> *Dim as stars yet bright, half-lost in time.*
> *Seal characters part from crow-tracks’ trace,*
> *With rustling leaves, the wind’s own rhyme.*
> *This stone outlasts the First Emperor’s fire,*
> *Dragon’s vault opens to Elephant Hill’s prime.*
> *Pillowed on stone, I listen to the spring’s delight,*
> *‘Lofty Mountain, Flowing Water’—suddenly, I wander alone.*
> *Is carving not but vain fancy’s flight?*
> *Nodding, I grasp the stone’s intent—elusive, unknown.*

I remarked that of this journey’s sights—*Sunrise Peak* and the other summits—each was wondrous, and all could speak of them. Yet *Stone Sutra Gorge* was an unlooked-for discovery, and the unplanned gathering of the three gentlemen was yet another marvel. Creation begrudges those who take too much—should not our travels end here?

But the three gentlemen’s enthusiasm for travel burned still. They wished to rest, yet the adjacent chamber was dilapidated, and the fine rain had dampened the mossy traces. They considered following the path of Zulai to seek the former haunts of the *Six Recluses of Bamboo Creek* and the *Study Hall of Shi Shoudao*, gazing south toward *Cloud-Cloud Pavilion* or north to *Spirit Rock*. But the sun had already sunk behind the mountains. Some wished to stay for ten days, exhausting the wonders of the seventy-two peaks; others said this journey was but the beginning.

Clear springs and white stones—let us pledge another day. Ah, heaven!

The wanderer said: *"A lofty mountain—how my heart yearns for it!"* I traveled to Changping, where White Horse Mountain rises some twenty *li* high. White clouds could be grasped in the hand, often flying forth from one’s palm. White elms stood distinct, their branches within reach. In grandeur, it rivaled Kuocang, yet the mountain folk took pride in it alone.

How does it compare to when Han Yu climbed Mount Tai, which rises forty *li*, with two White Horse Mountains added to its height? Yet of the immortal grottoes, blessed lands, and cave-heavens—how can one recount them all? And why is Mount Tai alone revered as their patriarch? Is it because the emperor emerges from the east, the direction of *zhen*, where all things receive life’s beginning?

From here, the peaks of Zulai and other mountains seemed as near as a table’s edge. In the distance, Mount Taihang, Huabuzhu, and Kongtong appeared and vanished like mirages. The surrounding mountains, humble and bowing, resembled children crouching in reverence, like a host of dragons hastening toward the Purple Tenuity. Their forms were beyond description. Leading all the peaks in homage to the Supreme Perfected, with the Yellow River a mere brushstroke and the Wen and Si rivers like sashes, while the vast seas lay at one’s elbow—swallowing and spouting with the tides—this, too, was wondrous.

As for the marvels of its peaks, caves, and springs; the *tianma*, *huangjing*, pepper, cassia, peach, apricot, plum, wu-tong, cliff pines, and crag bamboo; its suitability for snow and moonlight; the transformations of dawn and dusk, mist and haze—these are too subtle and fleeting to recount.

Ah, this *qi*! It bears the soul as a pivot, rising and vanishing like the great sea. It is like the cicada, which clings to a leaf and sings, yet within, it has already shed its skin. At times, this is no journey—what is seen is nothing but mountains, yet nothing is not mountains. This is the journey of gathering the true. If one clings to a single hill or ravine, even crossing the Weak Water to sit atop Kunlun’s peak, is one truly a traveler of mountains? How then can one glimpse the so-called *smaller world*?

In the Wanli era, during the *shangzhang* and *xieqia* months, when the moon was waxing bright, Wang Heng of Taicang, on the second day after the Double Ninth Festival, recorded his ascent of Mount Tai.

For I, born in obscurity among hills and ravines, raised amid reeds and rushes, have long yearned to see mountains. Of mountains, none are greater than the *yue*, and of the five *yue* within the seas, their ranges sprawl in distant corners, the nearest no less than several thousand *li* away—beyond the leisure of a scholar. Yet Mount Tai is somewhat closer to the Yellow and Ji rivers, perhaps within reach of visiting my parents and catching a glimpse of it—but I dared not hope.

This year, on the eighth day of the ninth month of *bingxu*, I set out northward. My boat docked at Jining, where my grandmother was to perform rites at Mount Tai. I accompanied her, and my joy was beyond measure. Zhou Rusheng also joined us. On the day of departure, we lodged in Jianning. All night, the wind roared, and rain lashed the cabin door. By dawn, the rain’s trailing threads still lingered, so we delayed our departure, thinking it unwise to proceed. As the sun set, the clouds began to drift, and by midday, the sun emerged. The mountain mists displayed their hues, though yellow dust still clung to the horses’ saddles—truly what Du Fu meant by *"soaked and drenched, how fine!"*

Several times, the carriage driver pointed out what he claimed to be Mount Tai, yet none aligned with due east. Halfway up the sky, a wall of deeper blue than the heavens themselves made me suspect it might be so. When I mentioned this to my fellow travelers, they all dismissed it as nonsense. But soon, clouds brushed past the gorge’s edge, and there it stood—indeed Mount Tai. Only then did they gasp in awe.

I bought a donkey and rode it, glancing left and right, fearful of losing sight of the mountain. As dusk fell, the mountain grew ever more radiant, as if responding to my admiration. After another night’s rest, we reached the prefectural town, changed into climbing attire, and after ten *li* arrived at the Red Gate at the mountain’s foot. From the Red Gate to the Small Heavenly Gate, the path to the left was a ravine trail—deep, wide, and vast. Small stones lay scattered, rushing to cradle the springs. From the side, they seemed to listen; from above, to rinse; some stood tall, others jostled for the way, each assuming strange postures.

Five-colored trees adorned the path: peach, apricot, maple, pear, pine, and chestnut; among the grasses were creeping fig, red arrowroot, *tianma*, and *huangjing*. Without selection, all were beautiful. Mount Tai, a skeletal mountain, owed its flesh and adornment solely to these.

A few *li* farther stood the Bridge of the High Elder, where the flat bridge spanning the cliffside ravine was especially fine. Crossing another short bridge, we reached the Water Curtain Cave. The left cliff rose sheer for dozens of *ren*, two great stones leaning together like a hanging curtain, beneath which water flowed. Though the stream was dry, droplets trickled down. I imagined it in spring, and though I dared not compare it to Mount Lu, it must surely rival its lesser peaks.

Turning from the cave for several *li*, we came to the Horse Shelter Cliff, where the overhanging rock could shelter a herd. A few *li* more brought us to the Horse-Turning Ridge, where our companions left their mounts and proceeded on foot. Soon we reached Huangxian Ridge, winding several times steeper, the view beyond vast. Proud Lai Mountain, which had faced Mount Tai’s shoulder to the north, now bowed its head. I sighed at the height’s boundlessness.

From there, the path descended and ascended three times, each stretch measured in *li*—three in all, known as the Three Joys of Delight. From Horse-Turning Ridge to Huangxian, the sedan-chair bearers led the way high, while I leaned forward, my head and heels nearly striking theirs in precarious pride. Here, the path grew slightly more level, and the trees and ravines even more wondrous. At last, I could tuck my hands into my sleeves, tilt my head back, and whistle freely—truly a joy.

A little farther on, we rested at the Jade Emperor’s Pavilion, the mountain’s midpoint. I prepared tea for my elderly kin, who beamed with delight. She pulled me to look up and down for a long while, and after some scrutiny, we made out the Southern Heavenly Gate, faint as a red star’s glimmer.

Pressing on, we reached the Imperial Tent, where the late Song emperor Dingling had once stayed. Nearby stood two of the five pines Qin had ennobled as *Great Officers*; the other three were lost. Though somewhat stunted, they were said to be thousands of years old—I dared not confirm it. Beyond the tent, the path narrowed to a struggle between towering cliffs and sheer walls. Soon we ascended the Small Heavenly Gate, where jagged peaks clustered, their heights nearly touching, encircling us entirely. To the left, the ravine’s rugged texture held water that shimmered like mica or green jade.

A gap in the mountain’s face opened before us, and my companion and I cried out in astonishment. Someone offered us wine; as we drank, a gust of wind rushed in, toppling the cups. I poured the spilled wine onto the ground and pressed on.

Farther up lay the Small and Great Dragon Valleys, leading to the Eighteen Bends. The stone steps clung to the void, flanked by sheer gorges. In the western gorge, dozens of pines clustered, their green canopies overlapping. The locals called it *Facing Pines*—another marvel. Beyond the valley, only pines remained, their verdant boughs standing alone. At last, the bends ended, and the Heavenly Gate appeared—a wall of iron split down the middle, forming a path and a gate. Though man-made, it seemed as if heaven had cleaved it for travelers.

Winding several *li* from the gate stood the Temple of the Primordial Lady, grand in scale. After paying respects with my elder, I changed clothes in the official lodge. Behind it lay the Jade Maiden’s Hair-Washing Pool, its waters clear and deep. A broken stone stele bore two lines of Li Si’s seal script.

Turning left and ascending, we reached the Temple of the Sacred Mountain Emperor. Behind it, a cliff bore Emperor Xuanzong’s inscription, elegant in its praise. Nearby were many refined carvings, though the patrons had changed. From the worn remnants, I pried out a few characters, polishing and exclaiming over them for some time.

Farther up stood the Jade Maiden’s Sleeping Palace. The foolish folk here had stuffed the statue’s outer robes with padding—ridiculous to the extreme. Continuing upward, we reached the summit. Though its peak was hollow, fit to see all, they had built the Jade Emperor’s Temple there. Before it stood a stone tablet over a *zhang* tall, said to be Qin Shi Huang’s wordless stele—no one knew its meaning.

Looking around, Tai’an City lay before us like a sash or a brooch. Thousands of mountains paid homage, some like upturned halberds, others like overturned vessels. All could form toes but not calves—only Mount Zulai stood ahead, as if within reach. Pointing to a few prominent peaks, I asked a Daoist priest their names, but he could not say. Disappointed, we descended.

We reached Sunrise Peak, behind which stood the Sea-Viewing Tower. The so-called Qin, Zhou, and Wu Observatories all lay there. Looking back at our path, threads of sky hung in the misty distance, while the ravine’s trees and stones intertwined like embroidered treasure. The winding, shifting waters below—were they the Wen River or the Yellow River?

Gazing southeast, I searched for the sea’s trace but found none. The Daoist priest said the haze had obscured it—perhaps so.

We then turned toward Sheer Sacrifice Cliff, where a massive rock jutted forth. My feet could dangle two inches beyond its edge, yet my eyes dared not look straight down. Glancing sideways at the abyss below, all who stood there crouched low to the ground. Descending slightly to the west, we came to the Immortal’s Bridge, where two cliffs parted and a stone beam spanned the gap. Here, the marvels of undulating ridges and crystalline pools were most abundant. The Daoist priest pointed out peaks named Solitary Beauty, Gentleman, and Lotus, but I merely murmured acknowledgment, my gaze fixed on the Eastern Gate of Heaven—steeper still. The priest marveled, and I thought it far surpassed the Southern Gate of Heaven, yet without stairs, it served only as a den for badgers, pheasants, and hares. Truly a pity.

West of the bridge, a Daoist recluse in patched robes and a palm-leaf hat sat motionless as visitors arrived. Unsure whether he might be a worthy conversant, I persuaded the others to spare him their coins. From there, we began our return as the moon waxed slender and pale. My companion Zhou and I urged the Daoist priest to lead us to the Temple of the Eastern Peak, where we would spend the night.

Moonlight and starlight—whether veiled by mist or not—felt near and intimate. The boundless sky shimmered in floating blues and drifting whites, stirring a restless ecstasy beyond control. The cold bit deep, raising gooseflesh, so I returned to check on my elderly parents’ rest before retiring to the Daoist’s lodging. His thatched hut measured barely ten feet across, half of it occupied by a well-curb. With no space left, we curled our legs outside.

The mountain’s silence was broken only by the midnight procession of men and women ascending, their murmured prayers rising in a ceaseless hum. My eyelids never met, and the night watch had long since ended when I roused Zhou to throw on his clothes and peer into the courtyard. The stars had vanished, and the darkness felt ominous. We rose at once, staffs in hand, and made for Sunrise Peak. The cold gnawed at our toes and teeth, chattering in unison, when the prefectural envoy arrived with wine and charcoal to delay us. Delighted, I raised my cup three times. After a long while, a faint crimson glow appeared in the darkness—above and below, the clouds were black, but a gap of a finger’s width remained between them. How fortunate.

Soon, the light tightened like a clamp; then it flushed red, then purple. The radiance swelled to half the sky, and the drifting clouds ignited into dawn’s hues, dazzling and overwhelming, as if spilling over our faces. The mountain peaks emerged faintly, like grains of rice transmuting into cinnabar. At the farthest edge of the sky, three golden blossoms floated upward. The Daoist priest said, “This is the sun embracing the sea, about to rise.” Moments later, it emerged—molten gold in color, as large as a carriage lamp.

Beneath the sun, a shimmering streak rippled to the right, as if the sea itself were leaping and barking. But the sun had already entered the clouds, and the golden banners and kingfisher plumes of its retinue vanished in an instant. Disappointed, we turned back. Winding our way to the Jade Emperor’s Pavilion, we beheld sights beyond all previous experience, yet the Daoist priest—crude and unrefined—seemed blind to them even when they stood before his eyes. From the pavilion’s rear, we gazed northward at the mountain’s forested slopes, where verdant bamboo groves nestled in secluded hollows. A place called Stone House lay there, and I felt certain it held wonders. But my elderly parents, citing the distance and the impossibility of a litter, urged our return. We merely circled westward to glimpse the so-called Sage’s Peak—unremarkable in the end.

Descending further, we visited White Cloud Grotto, its jagged cliffs riddled with cavities. The Daoist priest explained that on overcast days, clouds seeped from the holes like threads, then billowed and surged until the entire mountain was enveloped, the strands weaving into one—harbingers of rain. I regretted not witnessing this. To Zhou, I remarked, “We came at dawn hoping for clear skies, yet now we wish for rain. Such is the insatiable nature of the human heart.”

We bade the Daoist priest farewell and descended the Eighteen Bends. The stone steps were sheer, and the four men carrying my litter could not maneuver freely, so they lowered me upside down, my palms and soles slick with sweat. My eyes bulged as I stared at the porters’ thighs. Zhou, however, guided his two bearers with the ease of a serpent gliding over level ground. Amused, I asked to switch places at the mountain’s midpoint. Looking up, we had already lost sight of the Heavenly Gate—much to my chagrin.

From then on, my gaze remained fixed on the mountain. Between the midpoint and the Three Joys, we lost sight of the Heavenly Gate repeatedly, only to glimpse it again just as often. Each time it reappeared, I called to Zhou, and together we waved farewell, our hearts lingering. Descending to Water Curtain Cave, we passed through flowing springs and trees before climbing eastward to Stone Sutra Vale. The vale was a stone platform spanning over ten *mu*, its surface smooth as jade. Engraved in clerical script was the *Diamond Sutra*, each character a foot in size, magnificent in scale. Though more than half had been eroded by water, this was far preferable to the damage wrought on the previous two steles by boorish hands. A spring cascaded from above, its splashing waters resounding. Prefect Wan had inscribed “Water Curtain” on the cliff face and carved his *Record of High Mountains and Flowing Waters* into the stone wall—fitting indeed.

A few *li* farther down, the mountain’s summit culminated in Linghan Stronghold, its brow raised as if straining to support the heavens. The mountain seemed barely equal to the task, while Mount Zulai, Mount Haoli, and the surrounding peaks arrayed themselves like courtiers. From there, we returned home, sleepless and without appetite, unable to speak of Mount Tai.

After several stages of the journey, I would turn back to gaze at the mountain again and again, believing I had exhausted its wonders. Yet in Ningyang, I eagerly pored over the *Records of Mount Tai* and realized that what I had seen was merely the perfunctory pilgrimage of village women and children—scarcely a glimpse of its grand halls. Of its hidden chambers and secluded pavilions, I had witnessed perhaps a tenth. Even the golden sun I had seen was but a fleeting moment in the valley; the splendor that followed was entirely different. I was left feeling hollow and lost.

Still, it had been a magnificent sight for a lifetime. Had I been granted half a month, with all clouds and mist dispelled, and even a modest shelter atop the mountain to lodge my elderly parents, I would not have left so hastily. If I had brought the *Records of Mount Tai* and my young, vigorous friends had leapt forward with staffs, undeterred by brambles, searching the mountain according to its map, our discoveries would have been far greater. Yet now, all was lost. Was I simply unskilled in travel? Or did the mountain’s spirit guard its secrets, unwilling to reveal them lightly?

In my youth, I was fond of travel, journeying to the mountains of Gusu, Wulin, Dongting, and Yixing. Each range’s wonders eclipsed the last, and in turn, each dispelled the memories of those before. Once, the two Dongting peaks lingered in my heart like thorns, yet now even they have faded. I wonder, in the span of my life, where lies the mountain that might replace Mount Tai? I shall record these impressions in detail, however shallow, to inspire those who follow in my footsteps.

This is *An Account of Ascending Mount Tai* by Yu Shenxing, Minister of Rites from Dong’e.

My home lies at the foot of Mount Tai, and I once accompanied my nephew Cong on two excursions there. Both times, however, were in late autumn. Had it been the third or fourth month, when pilgrims from all directions—hundreds of thousands of men and women—flock to worship the Primordial Lady of the Sacred Peak, the mountain at night would have blazed with lanterns like swarms of fireflies, their glow filling the slopes. Processions would wind like ants or fish in single file, their voices rising in a clamor like a cauldron boiling or thunder rolling, shaking the valleys. Yet even then, one could barely find foothold to ascend. The springs ran full, but the trees were bare of leaves, and thus the mountain’s rare and wondrous beauty remained nine-tenths concealed, further obscured by the crowds. I surmised that between the fifth and sixth months, when water and foliage flourish, the scenery must be even more extraordinary. But I never ventured forth, deterred by the summer heat; and even when I did, I had no companion, only gazing eastward in longing.

In the sixth month of the year *xinsi*, while I was convalescing in the mountains of Gucheng, Magistrate Zhu Keda of Guangchuan moored his boat and came to visit. We lingered among the misty clouds and eastward-flowing streams, and as I gazed at the vaporous breath of Mount Tai—seeming as if it clung to my very robes—I resolved to journey there with him. On the nineteenth day of that month, we set out from the springs of Dongliu. After two days of traveling through mountains and rivers, we arrived at the foot of Mount Tai.

Our host courteously ushered us into lodgings. At dawn the next day, we dressed in formal attire and paid homage at the Yue Temple, which stands in the northwest of the prefectural city, its halls backing the mountain like the celestial palace of the gods. Exiting through the Dengfeng Gate and proceeding three *li* or so, we reached the mountain’s base, where twin pavilions stood—called the First Heavenly Gate. We dismounted our carriages and boarded sedan chairs, ascending a stairway of overlapping stone, its path winding like a dragon’s spine. Looking up, the peaks loomed like the light of Yao’s reign, their sheer cliffs defying footing, as if they were clouds hanging from the sky—so precipitous they seemed utterly unreachable.

After several *li*, we arrived at Huima Ridge, where the Jade Emperor’s Temple stands, its peak named Auspicious Immortal Crag. Before the temple lies the Pavilion of Ascending Immortals. Leaning against its railings, the surrounding peaks appeared beneath our feet, yet beyond them rose even more towering summits. The stairway grew steeper, its twists and turns more treacherous. Four men carried the sedan chair like a pole, with the chair itself slung in the middle. The feet of those ahead trod upon the heads of those behind, while the hands of those behind rested upon my shoulders. The two men at the rear mirrored the front, just as Ying Shao described: "like stacked figures in a painting." From this point onward, sweat streamed down, breath came in gasps, and my eyes had no leisure for anything else. Yet on either side, crimson cliffs and emerald crags gleamed with radiant hues, their colors shifting with the weather. Waterfalls plunged from the rocks, their waves churning like snow, their roar now thunderous, now delicate, like jade pendants chiming in the wind to welcome visitors. The spring from the Water Curtain Cave cascaded down the mountain, flooding the ravines before vanishing into crevices, only to reappear elsewhere. Stone bridges and wooden beams braced the path, and as my senses strained to take it all in, I feared missing even a moment, daring not to let my gaze wander. Only then did I realize how little of the mountain’s marvels I had seen before.

After several more *li*, we reached Huangxian Ridge, where the path turned west, then northeast, ascending before descending, then turning west again before veering northeast—now descending, now climbing. Further on, we came to the Imperial Tent Platform, where the people of Qin once sheltered from wind and rain. Situated halfway up the mountain, it nestles against the central peak, with ridges encircling it on both sides. A stone pool of several *mu* lies there, its waters flowing over the edge with a resounding crash. Emperor Zhenzong of the Song once encamped here, leaving behind a pavilion. Before it stand two pine trees—officially titled the Five Great Men by Qin, though they grow from rock crevices, neither tall nor thick. Their bark resembles dragon scales, suggesting centuries of age. Clearly, they are not the original Qin pines, though the location is undoubtedly the same. I suspect later enthusiasts planted them there.

My attendants claimed there were once five pines, three of which were struck by lightning. This is incorrect. The "Five Great Men" refers to a rank in the Qin bureaucracy; why would there need to be five trees? If they had been titled the Seven Great Men, would there then be seven? My attendants had no answer.

Beyond this point, the peaks grew ever more perilous, their cliffs soaring skyward like walls. The rocks were heaped in chaotic piles—some round, some square, some jagged, some elongated—each crag and precipice defying description. Between their edges, colors shifted endlessly, dazzling the eye. The crimson and ochre formations became halls and houses; the azure streaks like ribbons were the light of heaven; the dark green wisps were ancient pines; the white patches like brushstrokes were moss. The sheer cliffs, resembling birds’ nests suspended from a thousand fathoms, were the dwellings of feathered immortals. Looking down, the valleys below were a blur, their depths indiscernible.

Several *li* further lay the Great and Small Dragon Mouths—ravines where flying springs gushed forth like dragons spouting water. Beside the Small Dragon Mouth stood the Doumu Temple. From here upward, the clefts and grottoes were the abodes of immortals, spirits, and gods, arrayed on either side—what is known as the Divine Chambers Grotto-Heaven, the Palace of a Hundred Spirits.

After several more *li*, we reached the Eighteen Turns, called the Ringing Path in ancient times. Gazing up, the Heavenly Gate appeared no larger than an arrow’s nock, perched at its summit, the winding ascent like a green wall. Relinquishing our sedan chairs, we climbed the steps, leaping upward three hundred times, our sweat pouring like rain.

Emerging from the Heavenly Gate, turning left to reach the Azure Cloud Palace, one finds the shrine dedicated to the Primordial Sovereign of Mount Tai. It stands with its back to the Jade Emperor Peak, cradling the Five-Flowered Summit before it, the Sun-Watching Peak to its left and the Venerable Man Peak to its right. Its scale rivals that of the Sacred Mountain’s central temple, its golden and azure hues resplendent, its halls and gates radiant—indeed, a sight surpassing all others.

To its west lies the official guest lodge, where the Jade Maiden’s Pool rests. The pool’s waters, thick as ointment and dark as indigo, hold a solemn stillness, suffused with numinous *qi*. Nearby, upon a cliff, stands the seal script of Li Si, barely a dozen characters remaining. There, Yu and Ke-da changed garments before ascending the altar to pay homage to the Primordial Sovereign, as feather-clad Daoists chanted invocations.

Stepping out to gaze upon the Five-Flowered Summit, the ridges and hills sloped in tangled profusion, embracing streams and valleys. Cloud-wreathed temples and palaces lay beneath the sun’s gaze, while below, the prefectural city appeared as a mere box of mist, the Wen and Guang Rivers like threads encircling it. All around, the land curved and folded, green hills entwined with white waters, a realm of a million households compressed into a single glance.

From the palace, turning left and ascending, one reaches the Azure Emperor’s Palace. Behind it, sheer cliffs rise for dozens of *ren*, where Emperor Xuanzong of Tang’s *Inscription on Mount Tai* is engraved. Further north, after several hundred paces, stands the Jade Emperor’s Palace, perched at the summit of Mount Tai—the ancient altar of imperial sacrifices. Here lies the First Emperor of Qin’s stele, its stone pale and smooth, towering two *zhang* high yet bearing no inscription, its words erased by time. Some say it was the Qin’s marker for Mount Tai, and that beneath it lies a stone casket containing jade tablets—an account not implausible.

Circling behind the Jade Emperor’s Palace and leaning against the railing, one looks down to see the surrounding mountains dwarfed like crawling serpents, their ridges coiling like dragons in the distance. Below, the seventy-two peaks stretch out, their branches and tails clearly countable, extending all the way to the sea. On the mountain’s far side lie the Peach Blossom, Bright Moon, and Stone House Grottoes, where the immortal realms of golden couches and jade tables, as recorded in the annals of the transcendent, seem almost within reach.

The records state that the Celestial Grandson’s Mount Tai spans three thousand *li* in circumference. Yet from this vantage, it appears scarcely half the size of three prefectures.

Turning eastward for about a *li*, one finds the Han dynasty’s sacrificial altar—a stone slab roughly a *zhang* square, its surface jade-green and lustrous. A shelter stands over it, where white clouds gather in constricted swirls.

Further east lies the Sun-Watching Peak, though the dawn’s observation was forgone. Instead, we ascended southward to the Platform of Self-Abandonment, a summit jutting abruptly, its flat expanse no more than a few *chi*, yet plunging a thousand *zhang* below. Three stones brace it, their span said to be a mere *chi* and a few *cun*—the so-called Immortal’s Bridge. Crossing it to sit upon the platform, the sun sank westward, its afterglow clinging to the cliffs. The wind tore at our hats and robes with a sound like splitting stone. There, we raised our cups in toast, gazing southwest to see what seemed a thousand households gathered, bordered by a great embankment, its darkness boundless. Upon closer inspection, it was but clouds—smoke-like at their base, tapering upward. From the plains, one sees only their tops; here, we beheld their crowns.

Soon, the moon rose, and feather-clad Daoists played their reed pipes, their songs harmonizing with the night. We drank to deep intoxication before descending, the mountain’s chill air seeping into our bones, our pillows and mats damp, sleep impossible. Stepping out upon the altar, the moonlight glimmered faintly, the stars near enough to brush. Below, the myriad ravines merged into a single sea of mist, vast and white, its silver waves and jade ripples indistinguishable—what the Daoists call the Three Officials’ Void-Heaven.

Having heard that the mountain’s sunrise is best viewed at midnight, we asked the feather-clad Daoists, who replied that in the fifth and sixth months, dawn breaks at daybreak, so one should set out in the fifth watch. Thus, we sat until the fifth watch, then took up candles and donned furs to ascend. Below, clouds poured from the mountain’s ravines like wisps of smoke, rising and billowing, now gathering, now scattering, swift as galloping horses, vanishing in an instant. Ke-da cried out in amazement, declaring it a sight unseen in his lifetime, while our attendants huddled in awe.

As we turned eastward, several peaks emerged, resembling oxen and carriage canopies, then gradually faded. Crimson clouds filled half the sky, their radiance dazzling, their hues shifting between azure and green, casting reflections that dyed our robes. Suddenly, from the flat earth rose a crimson disc, like a lotus blossom adrift upon the waves, its splendor beyond description—was this the sun? Then another crimson disc, twice the size of the first, appeared beside it, swaying as if two long ropes were drawing it upward. After the space of a meal, it steadied. The feather-clad Daoists announced the sun’s ascent, explaining that the earlier lotus-like vision was but the sun’s reflection in the sea, not the sun itself.

As the sun climbed higher, the crimson clouds and reflections receded, revealing below a gleaming white coin, flickering in and out of sight. The Daoists said, “The sea.” Soon after, a dark streak appeared to the sun’s left, faint as a distant mountain range or the Great Wall. Ke-da pointed it out to me, saying, “This must be the Six Turtles’ burden!” The Daoists replied, “Clouds.” Ke-da scoffed, then strode to the Sea-Viewing Stone, poured wine, and drank deeply before falling into a deep sleep. Our attendants shivered and called out, but I glanced at them with disdain, my spirit unshaken.

Returning to stand upon the Immortal’s Ascent Platform, we gazed west at the Venerable Man and Moon-Watching Peaks, so near they seemed within arm’s reach. Turning to Ke-da, I said, “Today, we have climbed the Venerable Man Peak—one could gaze upon the Taihang Mountains from there. Come, let us go together.” But Ke-da, exhausted, could not follow. Leaning on my staff, I descended, the air growing ever more invigorating. Along the path, the stones grew ever more wondrous, the waters and trees ever more splendid. Every few *li*, we sought out fine trees and flowing springs, or peaks and solitary stones, sitting in their embrace to raise our cups. To Ke-da, I said, “Is this not Boya’s Rain-Sheltering Rock? High mountains and flowing waters—what music could surpass this for you?”

Descending further, we rested at the Cliffside Pavilion. Turning aside, we entered the Scripture Terrace, located a *li* or so to the path’s left. A stone altar slanted against the mountain’s curve, several *mu* in area, inscribed with a volume of the *Heart Sutra* in characters as large as *dou*, rendered in ancient seal script. To the altar’s north stood a cliff, from which a waterfall cascaded, streaming over the precipice and flooding the scripture platform before flowing onward.

Gazing upward from the water’s surface, each seal-script character remained legible. On the cliff face were carved several large characters, their forms framed by the cascading water as if by a curtain. On my second visit, I had never before reached this place—its seclusion off the main path had kept it hidden from the crowds. Thus, passing the Scripture Terrace and descending, we emerged at Heaven’s Gate, then turned left to find the Terrace of Mount Tai, where Emperor Wu of Han had planted parasol trees. A short distance further, we came upon the Pool of the Queen Mother, its waters spanning over a *mu*, fed by a waterfall twice as grand as that at the Scripture Terrace.

Soon after, we arrived at our second stop, mounted our carriage, and departed the mountain, traveling along the streams. As dusk fell, torches were lit, and from within the carriage, the sound of water beneath our feet could be heard. After several *li*, we reached our lodging. The next day brought heavy rain, and our host urged us to visit the Three Yangs Abbey. We set out in haste, arriving at the foot of Hundred-Fathom Peak, where we beheld several waterfalls cascading from the mountaintop as if descending from heaven. These lay to the southwest of the mountain—unlike those we had seen before.

A local scholar remarked: “Examining ancient maps and the veins of the land, I see how the terrain rises and falls. The northern range of mountains, bearing the earth’s yin meridians, runs alongside the Taihang and Wangwu, extending through Mount Chang to Mount Yiwu, where it ends. The southern range, bearing the earth’s yang meridians, connects Mount Tonggu and Mount Xiong’er, stretching into Ou and Yue, from which the Yangtze, Huai, Yellow, and Ji Rivers flow eastward like a great ridge. Mount Tai stands by the sea, facing west to receive the homage of the surrounding peaks. The Wen, Guang, Yi, and Si Rivers issue from its foothills, winding like a sash before emptying into the sea—truly, it bears the weight of the clouds. In ancient times, the Yellow River flowed through Yan and Qi, skirting Mount Tai’s north, then joining the Ji to enter the sea. Now, in the present era, it has shifted course, emerging south of Mount Tai to merge with the Huai before reaching the sea—a transformation in the earth’s veins.”

From the mountain’s summit, I gazed northward toward the Nine Rivers, their ancient courses now lost in the vast expanse, merging with the horizon.

Recorded on the twenty-fourth day of the sixth month.
Lü Kun, Provincial Administration Commissioner of Shandong, from Luoyang, *Observations at Sunrise Peak*

Sunrise Peak lies a hundred paces east of Mount Tai. Legend holds that at cock’s crow, the sun first pays homage to the eastern lands before ascending the Fusang tree. Its golden light scatters like gravel, while purple qi swirls in the air. The azure waves of the sea stretch a thousand *li*, and crimson clouds unfurl like brocade across half the sky. When molten gold first emerges, how swiftly does it become so wondrous?

I remarked on this to Tian, the Ministry of Works official, who laughed. Zhushan replied: “I have indeed visited the Orchid Pavilion.”

The so-called "winding stream" here is enough to bring shame—before it stood a counterfeit Orchid Pavilion, where channels were dug to divert water, barely redeeming Wang Xizhi’s disgrace. Otherwise, it would remain but a single forgery across the ages.

Alas, beneath every famous name lies emptiness; such is the way of all things. Those who chronicle prefectures and counties all list their "Eight Scenes," each claiming marvels, each boasting wonders. Yet heaven and earth take no notice, the sage feels no astonishment, and only the innovator is struck with awe. In the end, it is all but swallows’ stones.

Fearing that later visitors might be misled by hearsay and repeat my regrets, I composed *Observations at Sunrise Peak* and turned my carriage back at Carved Rock. From Literary Peak, a few dozen paces away, one reaches Yellow Flower Grotto, the starting point of the journey. The path is treacherous and perilous; I inscribe this to warn those who come after.

Yellow Flower Grotto lies fifteen *li* behind Sunrise Peak. Locals say it was once the cultivation site of the Perfected Lady of the Yuan. The grotto is just tall enough to brush one’s hat, and too narrow to fit even a single table. Water drips from the stone crevices like rain from an eave, sufficient to cook for ten. Before the grotto stretches a clearing several *zhang* wide, atop which stands a shrine—its thatched roof supported by a few pillars, home to two or three elderly nuns. Around the grotto, thousands of young pines encircle the cliffs in fresh green, offering a slight marvel; beyond this, there is nothing extraordinary.

Descending northeast from Elder Peak for five or six *li*, one may still reach by carriage. Beyond lies a ravine of jagged stones, where a single misstep sends one clinging to sheer cliffs like a thread. The towering boulders glow with the yang energy of earth and fire. During the Longqing era, a carriage plunged here, injuring several; only those inside were spared by rescue. Moreover, this place offers no lodging or food. If one sets out at dawn, there is no returning by dusk. Once, travelers caught in rain and snow at dusk huddled together, necks drawn in, crouching in the ravine—half without intact clothing, some frozen or starved to death. Both gentlemen...

I was not in office when the precious mirror was newly polished—this, the world’s great wonder. I read of it in records and dreamed of it, yet had no means to see it. In the dinghai year of the Wanli reign, I was honored to govern the eastern principality and serve as the incense-officer for Mount Tai, a joy beyond measure. Upon arrival, I ascended thrice and lodged thrice. Yet at dawn, clouds and mist would rise thickly, much to my frustration.

The locals say there are three chances to witness the sunrise. The first: in the first month, if there is no rain, the sea’s halo does not rise. The second: in late autumn, when the air is crisp and the sky newly cleared of dust. The third: after midwinter snow, when dawn breaks without clouds or mist. These three chances occur but a few days in a year. A woodcutter here—now old—has seen them often, for he rises early on clear days.

Delighted, I asked, “What does it look like?”
He replied, “Have you seen the setting sun?”
“Yes,” I said.
“You have already seen it—what more is there to describe?”
I sighed in realization: “Have the records deceived me?”
Then I thought: What does a woodcutter know? He has only mortal eyes—what does a woodcutter know?

I wished to stay two nights in anticipation, but the mountain mist once more rose thickly, forming an impenetrable veil. Thus I turned back—this was the ninth month of that year.

Eleven months later, I returned to the foot of Mount Tai on official business and ascended once more. The great clouds had just cleared, and the mountain wind cut to the bone. By the fifth watch, my sedan-bearers were stiff with cold, their jaws clenched so tightly they could not rise. I forced them to their feet, and together we made our way to the base of the peak.

At first, a crimson glow emerged from the sea’s depths, gradually rising. Certain that something extraordinary would appear, I fixed my gaze without blinking. After a moment, the sun ascended like a mirror—larger than the midday sun yet not dazzling to the eye, as red as the setting sun but without the trailing radiance. This was what the woodcutter had been unable to describe.

On this journey, I fell ill with cold for several days, nearly unable to recover. Only then did I realize that those who delight in marvels fabricate wonders to astonish others, while those who come later, finding reality less grand than what they heard, fabricate even greater exaggerations to astonish future generations. Yet the rising sun is seen by all—those who wish to speak of it need only ask the mountain folk, for they know it best.

Formerly, Wang Lindan traversed the peaks of Mount Tai, yet even he did not set foot in this very ravine. Upon his return, he expressed regret. Let me send word to the old man of Linzhou: there is no need for regret.

Wu Tongchun, Vice Commissioner of the Surveillance Bureau from Gushi, *A Continuation of the Record of My Journey to Mount Tai*

I first traveled to Mount Tai in the winter of the *guiwei* year—seven years have now passed. The famous sights were recorded in my earlier account, so I shall not repeat them here. Instead, I note those places I had not yet visited or had visited without sufficient detail.

On the twentieth day of the sixth month of the *jichou* year, having concluded my duties in Tai’an, I wished to complete what I had left unfinished from my previous journey. There was an old friend, Li Zidan, a native of Tai’an, whom I had not seen for over a decade. We had once promised to climb Mount Tai together, but when I arrived, he was serving in Zhao. Now, at last, we met again, sorrowful at how swiftly time had passed yet grateful for this long-awaited reunion.

We set out at noon through the Dengfeng Gate, traveling over ten *li* to reach Pu Zhao Temple. A little farther on, we passed the ravine where Minister Hu Wendin once cast his letter, a stream flowing down from Ling Han Peak.

After another five *li*, we reached Sanyang Hermitage, perched precariously on a cliffside path so narrow and treacherous that the place felt profoundly secluded and serene. Below the meditation cave flowed an inexhaustible spring, modest in size. Above stood the Temple of the Dark Emperor and the Jade Emperor Pavilion, with a small hermitage nearby where several Daoist adepts pursued their cultivation. Two of them seemed rather refined, though not particularly enlightened.

Before long, Li Zidan arrived, and we shared a simple meal of bean-leaf porridge. Zidan engaged the Daoists in conversation, but their answers rarely matched his questions. I sighed and remarked, "Is it that they truly have not plumbed the depths of the Way, or is it that the Great Dao is not lightly imparted?" I then observed that we Confucians, too, have our own methods of self-cultivation—though most remain unaware of them. Confucius spoke of the longevity of the benevolent; Master Zeng said that virtue nourishes the body and a broad mind expands one’s frame; Mencius described how the countenance of the cultivated shines with purity, visible in the face and radiating from the back. These are true forms of self-cultivation. To abandon what is inherently ours and beg for scraps from others, to forsake what is universally known—how misguided! Late that night, behind closed doors, we discussed Master Yangming’s teaching: "To cast aside one’s own inexhaustible treasure and go door-to-door with a begging bowl, imitating the poor and destitute." Zidan and I shared another sigh.

At dusk, we left the hermitage and traveled over ten *li* eastward, hurrying toward Gaolao Bridge. The path wound up and down the mountain ridges several times, clinging to sheer cliffs—places rarely trodden by travelers. As we passed the southwestern face of Mount Aolai, the Daoists pointed out the Celestial Sash Spring and the site of the Immortal’s Shadow. To the northwest, a mountain rose in clustered peaks like lotus petals, appearing deceptively close yet impossibly distant—this was Nine Maidens Peak.

From Gaolao Bridge to Stone Sutra Ravine, Zidan eagerly rushed ahead to admire the spring. Soon, we clasped hands and traced the source of the water. After walking about a *li* northward, the rocks grew too slippery to tread, yet the spring bubbled forth from among the jagged stones, its currents mingling in a melody like the notes of a reed pipe. The dense forest teemed with the cries of crows and magpies, stirring a deep resonance within me—far more vivid than anything I had witnessed in previous years. Zidan, however, chattered endlessly about old colleagues, while I could recall only half of what he said, leaving me sighing for some time.

As nightfall crept upon us, we hurried from Stone Sutra Ravine toward the summit, arriving at the Imperial Tent Platform. There, we lit torches to examine the inscriptions on the walls—bold, sweeping calligraphy left by myself and other scholars during a previous visit. The bright moon and the murmur of water enhanced the beauty of the scene.

By the second watch, we reached the New Stone Terrace, constructed by the provincial governor Lü Shujian. During the spring festivals, so many pilgrims came to offer prayers that the old terrace could not accommodate them, and many were pushed over the cliffs. Lü thus expanded the site, creating a fire pit and aligning the western ravines with the old terrace to form the New Stone Terrace, making the journey far more convenient. The porters claimed the old path was slightly flatter, but I insisted on taking the new route. By the time we reached the guesthouse, it was the third watch.

Zidan and I retired to rest, rising at the fifth watch to witness the sunrise—no different from what I had seen years before, so I shall not describe it further. Yet all that we had traversed seemed as vivid as yesterday; how could it feel like a dream? After returning to the guesthouse for breakfast, I revisited the Qin dynasty seal-script stele and the inscription left by Liu’s postscript. Some discrepancies troubled me, but the stele was embedded in the cliff on three sides, making it impossible to examine fully. I ordered several strongmen to pry it free, only to discover that barely half of Liu’s account remained. Liu had mentioned seven lines on the front face, but now only three on the right side survived. The other three faces, intended to be mounted against the rock, had been roughly hewn to fit—none retained their original form. After washing the stele, I traced its faint characters, but the true surface of the stone, like the famous "Characterless Stele," was smooth and lustrous. Outwardly, it appeared coarse and unrefined, yet its inner nature was thus—how many things in the world are like this? I had the strongmen re-embed it in the cliff, fearing that this relic of millennia might suffer further damage at my hands.

Zidan’s feet were weary, but I explored the wonders of Five-Flowered Cliff, sitting at its heart and gazing down into the ravines below as if they lay upon my table. At noon, we traveled about eight *li* to the right of Confucius Cliff, reaching the Scattered Stone Gully. After another *li*, we arrived at the One-Foot Terrace, where over a thousand wild cranes circled above the mountain peaks—this was the famed Crane Mountain. A few *li* farther on lay Yellow Flower Cave, the very place where the Jade Maiden had cultivated the Dao—a site I had glimpsed from afar in previous years but never visited. The cave lay to the northeast of the summit, halfway up Crane Mountain, its path far more treacherous and narrow than Sanyang’s, yet its seclusion and beauty surpassed it many times over.
