A spring gushed from the mouth of a rainbow, twice the size of Sanyang’s, and the cave itself was twice as vast. Only a few elderly nuns resided there, and the temple’s incense and lamps amounted to less than a third of Sanyang’s. Thus, few travelers ventured here; had I not taken this circuitous route, I might never have come.

Zidan and I sat cross-legged, and I pressed him to share what he had gained over the past decade. He seemed full of conviction, recounting the essence of his insights, which leaned heavily toward Chan Buddhism. He claimed that his discussions with various eminent scholars had never failed to resonate. I sighed and said to him, "This cannot be fully explained in a moment. The world follows Chan, yet I alone do not—why do I, too, seem to lean toward Chan while suddenly rejecting it? Those who follow Chan achieve results in an instant, while I, gaining nothing, abandon it. Can you truly trust my words?" Zidan pondered this: "From the Two Emperors and Three Kings to Confucius and Mencius, there was no Chan in their time. Does this mean that without Chan, one cannot enter the Dao? Were the sage-kings and Confucius and Mencius all outsiders to the Way?" I had once craved Chan as one craves food and drink, yet now I suddenly rejected it—had I grown more distant with time? People can truly practice Chan without ever truly studying Confucius and Mencius, so they see no progress in Confucian learning and dismiss it as insufficient for entering the Dao. But if one were to apply the mind of Chan to Confucius and Mencius, would not the path to the Dao become nearer and easier? You say Chan can empty the mind, yet the *Analects* speaks of 'emptiness that is truly empty.' Have we Confucians ever been solely about substance? We seek emptiness that can become substance. You say Chan can bring stillness, yet the *Great Learning* teaches that only after attaining stillness can one achieve tranquility. Have we Confucians ever been solely about movement? We seek tranquility that can give rise to movement."

Those who speak of the Buddhist concepts of emptiness and stillness have merely appropriated and embellished our Confucian teachings—clever and strange as they may be—claiming Buddhism offers a swift and sudden enlightenment. Yet of the tens of thousands of volumes in the Buddhist canon, nineteen out of twenty are of this sort. Could the Buddha truly be a sage from the West?

Even Master Yangming, who attained enlightenment through reading Buddhist texts, did so only by chance encounter—like grasping the essence of calligraphy upon seeing a porter quarrel over a path or watching Madam Gongsun’s sword dance. Yet calligraphy is not the porter or Madam Gongsun themselves; without observing their quarrel or dance, one could never comprehend the art. This argument runs deep. Zidan, you do not outright reject my words, nor do you readily accept them as truth.

Noticing my emaciation, Zidan once again taught me methods of nourishing life. Though his teachings differed from those of the yellow-robed Daoists I had met before, they did not contradict my earlier principles of self-cultivation. We then parted ways. Turning left from the cave, I traveled fifteen *li*, passing Small Ox-Heart Stone and Great Ox-Heart Stone, until I reached Double Phoenix Ridge, nestled between two mountains. Ascending five *li* straight up, the twin peaks curved like soaring phoenixes. Asking a mountain dweller its name, I found it had none, so I inscribed "Double Phoenix Ridge" in large characters upon the cliff’s face.

One peak rose several hundred *ren*, rivaling Mount Tai. To its left lay Jieqiu, and to its right, Shiyin—this was the very Jieqiu of legend. Sitting midway up the mountain, I gazed upon the Ming Hall and the eastern ranges beyond, as if they lay upon my table. Descending fifteen *li* to the north, I wound through the foothills, passing no fewer than dozens of turns where the path clung to the cliffs. This was Aiyu Valley. Beyond it, the terrain leveled slightly, and after another fifteen *li* through forests and ravines, I lodged at Foyu Temple, also known as Gushan Temple. Though the temple stood deep in the mountains, the path was neither treacherous nor narrow, and the setting was secluded and sublime. A great ravine lay nearby; after rain, the rushing waters roared like serpents, while in fair weather, springs trickled coldly from between the stone altars.

Two monks in black robes struck the bell to welcome me. I had expected a splendid monastery, but upon arrival, I found only two dilapidated halls and a few side chambers, all thatched with straw—half of it torn away by the wind. Searching for a monk’s bed proved futile, so I pushed two tables together to sleep. The monks’ poverty far surpassed that of the yellow-robed Daoists at Sanyang, and even rivaled the nuns of Huanghua Cave.

The next day, after five *li*, I emerged from the valley to reach Tai’an, thus completing the eastern route to the provincial capital. From the high villages of Nanbei and Gaozhuang, crossing Yuhuang Ridge and Budongkou, I arrived at the capital. Descending from the summit via Mount Tai’s northern path, I traversed a route seldom trodden by travelers.

---

**Record of My Journey to Mount Tai**
*By Chen Wenzhu, Minister of the Court of Judicial Review, from Mianyang*
In the year *wuyin* of the Wanli era (1578), while serving as Left Assistant Administration Commissioner of Shandong to oversee grain transport, my boat passed through Jining. I had arranged to meet Li Chengcheng of Pu Prefecture for the journey, but he fell ill and could not come. Disembarking, I continued overland.

On the first day of the ninth month, I arrived at Tai’an Prefecture. The Yue Temple stood within the city, its towering steles and ancient tripods shaded by verdant pines and emerald parasol trees—objects not of this mortal realm. The courtyard brimmed with strange rocks, all inscribed by scholars of the Song and Yuan dynasties. After paying my respects, I exited through the Dengfeng Gate and ascended the foothills to the First Heavenly Gate. The Flying Cloud Pavilion stood upon a terrace of white stone, its base connected to a beam path. The stones lay stacked like fish scales, and a spring murmured softly. Gazing upward, the mountain’s grandeur was veiled in mist, while the surrounding peaks appeared as mere foothills—only Zulai stood prominently before me.

Passing the Doumu Hall, I reached the Gao Bridge, where a shrine to the Marquis of Han Shou stood. Beyond it lay the Water Curtain Cave. Water cascaded from thousands of *ren* above, plunging into the abyss like a coiling dragon or slithering serpent—vanishing in an instant, then bursting forth again. A jade rainbow hung to the earth, a silver river fell from the heavens, soaking my robes and sash.

A few dozen steps farther lay the Stone Sutra Valley, where the rock could seat hundreds. The *Diamond Sutra* was inscribed in *bafen* script, each character as large as a peck measure. A spring divided into pools, so clear one could see every hair reflected. Vice Minister Wan Gonggong had named it "High Mountains and Flowing Waters" and composed a record—truly a convergence of three perfections.

Beyond the Yanyan Pavilion, I came to the Horse Shed Cliff, where three characters in ink remained unblemished by wind and rain, said to have been written by Lü Dongbin. The Jade Emperor Temple stood on Ruixian Cliff, where I rested briefly. Beyond the Return Horse Ridge, palanquins could go no farther. The path wound three or four times before reaching Huangxian Ridge, where the ascent grew even steeper. Travelers advanced in single file, each step connecting heel to toe. After navigating the rugged terrain, the path suddenly leveled for three *li*—dubbed "Joyous Three *Li*."

Ascending the Second Heavenly Gate, I reached the Imperial Tent Flat after a few more *li*. This was where the people of Qin sheltered from wind and rain. Emperor Zhenzong of the Song had once encamped here, leaving behind traces of his sojourn. The twin cliffs towered like stacked cicadas, overgrown with lush vegetation.

The Five Pines Pavilion marked the site where the First Emperor of Qin had enfeoffed a pine as a *daifu*. Only two trees remained, not from Qin’s time but planted later. The wind howled from all directions, and the pines’ sighs echoed like an old dragon’s song, as if they might take flight. I called for wine to savor the moment. To the north, an ancient pine twisted like a coiled canopy, surpassing even the *daifu* pines. Someone had inscribed it as the "Recluse’s Pine," likening it to Lu Zhonglian. I composed a poem in its honor.

Passing the Chaoyang Cave, I came upon two temporary palaces, the Greater and Lesser Dragon Valleys, and a stone gorge where countless waters and springs cascaded like dragons spraying mist. The Qiankun Tower stood beside the valley. Vice Minister Hong Chaoxuan had inscribed it, writing: "Those who speak of the world’s stability compare it to Mount Tai. Yet the four corners of the earth are bound by cords—why should the human heart lack its own restraints? Propriety, righteousness, integrity, and shame are the four cords that secure one’s person. Drawing from Mount Tai, this teaching eases the heart."

After several *li* through the valley, I reached the Eighteen Turns—a winding sheep’s-gut path. Gazing upward at the gorge’s mouth, the sky’s light congealed like a strand of green pearls. Truly, this was the divine abode of the immortals, the palace of a hundred spirits.

Exhausted from the winding ascent, I reached the Three Heavenly Gates. Gazing toward White Cloud Grotto, its ethereal vastness seemed as though it could rain across the land before noon. Within the gates lay a level plain, where market stalls and dwellings numbered in the dozens. The former Heavenly Gates of Mount Tai had no structures—these were built by Daoist Master Zhang.

I lodged at the Yangzhi Pavilion. The next day, I paid homage at the Temple of the Azure Clouds, where the original stone statue of the Yuanjun had stood in Jade Maiden Pool before the Song dynasty replaced it with jade. Li Kua’s *Record of the Jasper Pool* recounts how the Yellow Emperor sent the Jade Maiden, a Daoist immortal clad in feathered robes and yellow crown. Some claim she is the consort of the Eastern Peak, but this is mistaken. Donations of gold, pearls, and horses from all directions arrive without cease throughout the year.

There is a Jade Maiden Pool, where over ten characters of Li Si’s inscription commemorating the First Emperor’s virtue remain legible. A short distance farther lies the Eastern Temple, its scale inferior to the shrine. A cliffside stele bears the *Inscription of Mount Tai*, written in the Tang Taizong’s vigorous and elegant *ba fen* script. To its right stands Su Xian’s *Eulogy on the Eastern Sacrifice*, with Yan Lugong’s signature—its calligraphy reminiscent of the Jin dynasty—though both have been defaced by human hands.

Turning north, the Palace of the Green Emperor matches the temple in scale. There stands the First Emperor’s "Wordless Stele," said to conceal a stone casket holding golden tablets and jade scrolls, a mystery across the ages. The stone’s yellow-white hue shines with an extraordinary luster, unlike any native to this mountain.

Ascending to the Supreme Summit of Great Peace, I beheld the ancient altar of the Feng and Shan sacrifices, where the founding emperor’s inscribed stele still stands. The Yellow River appeared like a sash, while the Zi, Mian, Ji, and Si rivers coiled around it. The Taihang, Hua, Buzhu, and Kongtong mountains lay like objects upon a table.

To the west, I climbed the Hermit’s Peak, its cliffs rising ten thousand *ren* in sheer majesty. Below, the Immortals’ Palace nestled in the gorge. I paid respects to the statue of Confucius, then reached the "World in Miniature" vantage. Above, the Milky Way seemed within grasp; below, the Bohai Sea stretched into twilight. Only Confucius’s feet could bear such a view—had he not once asked whether Mount Tai was not greater than Lin Fang? Yet Mount Tai and the burial mounds of commoners are of the same kind. The sage emerges from his kind, and thus none honor Mount Tai more than Confucius and Mencius.

The prefectural officials hosted a banquet, and the Daoist masters poured wine until I grew deeply intoxicated. Never had I witnessed such grandeur. I lodged again at the Yangzhi Pavilion. As the sun set, mist rose in waves; at midnight, the moon shone bright. The Yue, Qin, and Wu observatories appeared as though painted. At the fourth watch, the Daoists urged me to witness the sunrise. We ascended Sunrise Peak, where a sudden gale arose, bitterly cold. The Daoists removed their robes to clothe me.

All below was a boundless expanse of white, like solid jade. The round heavens and square earth could not be distinguished. Then, a crimson glow surged thrice over the Eastern Sea, and the sun’s radiance gradually emerged. All things were renewed; the seventy-two peaks revealed their verdant splendor. Truly, the vastness of the cosmos reaches its zenith here.

I climbed the Sea-Viewing Tower, then stood at the Cliff of Self-Abandonment, where the Daoists frequently restrained me from leaning too far. Descending via the Flat-Top Cliff, I returned to the Palace of Great Clarity. There, I read the stele of Wan Gong’s *Memorial on the Ridge* and sighed: "How magnificent the prose!" Mount Tai commands the reverence of the Four Sacred Peaks; its summit stone commands the reverence of Mount Tai. To build upon it, to tread upon it, is to honor it fittingly. When its peak is adorned, the mountain spirit exhales its qi, and the world is illuminated. Of the seventy-two sovereigns who performed the Feng and Shan sacrifices, many left with regret.

Following the old path down the mountain, I reached the prefectural city at dawn and contemplated visiting the Forest of Confucius. The waters of the Mi and Si rivers connect with the mountain’s veins, so near to the sage’s dwelling—how could this not be the work of Mount Tai’s numinous power? To observe the rhythms of heaven above and the patterns of earth below—what is this if not the spirit of Mount Tai? To behold Confucius is to see Mount Tai as the equal of heaven and earth.

In the thirty-fifth year of the Wanli reign of the Great Ming, the cyclical year *dingwei*, on an auspicious day of the first lunar month, Zhang Guoxiang, the Orthodox Successor of the Zhengyi Tradition, the Devout and Dao-Attaining, Mystery-Unfolding, Teaching-Propagating Great Perfected Master, who oversees the affairs of all Daoist teachings under heaven, respectfully collated and printed this text.
